# L
## L&P
 
 <i>abbr.</i> Lemon and Paeroa (See [Lemon and Paeroa](../L#lemon-and-paeroa))

>  The land's owner soon began bottling the water and a version with lemon added called Paeroa and Lemon. Now branded as L&P and made in Auckland. <i>(Dec 30, 2004,Dominion Post,C2</i>)

>  L&P is the fifth largest-selling carbonated soft-drink brand in New Zealand, and the equivalent of 18 million 600ml bottles of L&P are sold every year. <i>(Feb 24, 2007,Press, The,D2</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> The guy across from me had a blanket over him and his blue Addidases poking out in the aisle. He drained an L & P, flicked through a throwaway, killed his light. <i>(1985,G'day Country,50</i>)

>  I love the buses that come late, the brightness of the crisp grey sky on endless rainy days, and cold L&P at the Viaduct on the occasional sunny one. <i>(Aug 1, 2006,NZ Herald,E5</i>)

>  Then it's the L&P "sponsored mentioned theme" and over to the L&P boat on the harbour. <i>(Mar 13, 1982,Listener (New Zealand Listener),78</i>)



## L.O.B.
 
 <i>abbr.</i> Left out of battle [ORIGIN: acronym]

>  <b>WILSON</b> Our companions are not really dead. They are - what is the term most suitable? Round the corner? No. L.O.B. That is it - L.O.B. Left Out of Battle. <i>(1951,Brave Company,49</i>)



## la(la)
 
 <i>n.</i> (informal) toilet [short for lavatory]

>  la/lala toilet; euphemistically short for lavatory <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,68</i>)



## la-la
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  New Zealand First is the only real centre party, which would not take gladly to the extreme of either the loosely left la-las of the Alliance or the un-right acid dries of ACT. <i>(Sep 19, 1999,Sunday Star Times,A10</i>)



## La-Z-Boy
 
 <i>n.</i> a proprietary brand of armchair [also as lazy boy?]

>  "Christmas! Who needs it!", I barked at Gav, dumping the last of my Warehouse shopping bags and parking tickets on the La-Z-Boy. <i>(Dec 20, 2001,Evening Post,6</i>)



## Labor
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  The presence in Parliament of a Labor Party will have a steadying influence on those politicians who have so far chiefly sought to use Labor , and whose administrative acts have in the main only crippled and maimed trades [sic] unionism. <i>(Aug 20, 1910,NZ Truth,269/4/3</i>)

>  <i>(1971,Speeches & Documents,301</i>) [NOTES: (1919) 1971 Speeches and Documents 301 quoting Maoriland Worker 3 Dec This seems the contemporary house style:? from the IWW literature.]



 
 <i>n.</i> [AND has LABOR, short for Australian Labor Party 1911.] The Labour Party, a political party of NZ: `she voted for Labour last election.'; LABOR in early names of political or Union organisations:]

>  This move is represented primarily by two schools of thought, some finding expression in the New Zealand Federation of Labor and the Trades and Labor Councils. The former may be said to represent the international and revolutionary section; the latter the parochial and advanced Liberal, though calling itself Labor. <i>(Jun 2, 1911,Maoriland Worker,13/2/3</i>)

>  LABOUR DAY/ CHANGE DESIRED/ NOVEMBER SUGGESTED <i>(Nov 16, 1933,Evening Post,14</i>)



## Labour
 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  The work of organisation is far from complete, but the greatest success so far achieved was the adoption at the annual conference of the Trades and Labor Councils, at Auckland, of the Labor objective and platform. <i>(Aug 27, 1910,NZ Truth,270/1/2</i>)

>  This move is represented primarily by two schools of thought, some finding expression in the New Zealand Federation of Labor and the Trades and Labor Councils. The former may be said to represent the international and revolutionary section; the latter the parochial and advanced Liberal, though calling itself Labor. <i>(Jun 2, 1911,Maoriland Worker,13/2/3</i>)



## Labour Day
 
 <i>n.</i> [1899 Act made it a public holiday on the 2nd Wednesday in Oct; until 1910 when the Public Holidays Act changed it to the 4th Monday in October.]

>  Where in any Act.. reference is made - (a) To Labour Day, such reference shall hereinafter be deemed to be to the fourth Monday in October, and not to the second Wednesday in October. <i>(1910,NZ Statutes,1 Geo.V. 71/363</i>)

>  An Act to constitute a Public Holiday to be known as Labour Day... The second Wednesday in the month of October in each year shall be known as "Labour Day". <i>(1898,NZ Statutes,63 Vict. 17/50</i>)

>  We have a Labour Day established, we have it gazetted, and we have everything almost except the part of making it universal, as we propose to do in this Bill... I know Labour Day is fixed locally by the districts... I believe a change is needed in order to do away with the inconvenience caused by having different days in different parts of the Colony for the same event. <i>(1899,NZ Parliamentary Debates,110/455</i>)

>  The second Wednesday in the month of October in each year shall be known as Labour Day. <i>(1908,NZ Consolidated Statutes,III 93/222</i>)

>  Mr Guinness.. asked the Premier whether he intended.. to move its adjournment.. to enable members to attend the Labour Day Demonstration. <i>(1899,NZ Parliamentary Debates,110/449</i>)

>  LABOUR DAY/ HOLIDAY AS USUAL <i>(Aug 21, 1940,Evening Post,8</i>)

>  REMIT PROPOSED / LABOUR DAY CHANGE ... the Wellington Labour Day Committee has again discussed the desirability of having this statutory holiday changed to a more seasonable time of the year. <i>(Nov 10, 1947,Evening Post,8/8</i>)

>  LABOUR DAY TRUE TO FORM AGAIN Wellington's first Labour weekend softball tournament was abandoned this morning ... <i>(Oct 22, 1951,Evening Post,8</i>)

>  Labour Day public holiday, in NZ the fourth Monday in October. <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,417</i>)

>  <b>JOSEPH</b> ... waiting to be loaded with gear, liquor and groceries he would need for the long week-end holiday. For Monday would be Labour Day ... <i>(1962,Pound of Saffron, A,31</i>)

>  <b>MACRAE</b> But it was still Labour day. <i>(1988,Awful Childhoods,49</i>)

>  LABOUR DAY MEETINGS <i>(Oct 20, 1945,Evening Post,12</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Other severe losses were the gate takings at Newtown Park. The Labour Day Committee now faces further trouble in arranging to run off the events at some other venue ... <i>(Oct 28, 1947,Evening Post,8</i>)

>  REMIT PROPOSED / LABOUR DAY CHANGE ... the Wellington Labour Day Committee has again discussed the desirability of having this statutory holiday changed to a more seasonable time of the year. <i>(Nov 10, 1947,Evening Post,8/8</i>)

>  LABOUR DAY ORIGIN AND GROWTH The Labour Day holiday on the fourth Monday in October is taken for granted ... <i>(Oct 24, 1935,Evening Post,17</i>)

>  WEATHER INTERFERES/ LABOUR DAY HOLIDAY <i>(Oct 25, 1943,Evening Post,3</i>)

## laburnum
 
 <i>n.</i> Sophoraspp., =kowhai.

>  <b>ANGAS</b> Along the banks of rivers, the `kowai' or `native laburnum,' displays its pro-[52]fusion of golden blossom. <i>(1866,Polynesia,51</i>)

>  <b>LINDSAY</b> <i> S.tetraptera</i>, Aiton, var. <i> microphylla</i>, Jacq. ... The `Kowhai' of the North Island Maori; the `Kowai' of settlers, - or as the word is variously corrupted and spelt by them, - `Goai' or `Ghoai,' `Goa' or `Ghoa.' According to Hector, the type is known to Otago Maoris as `Houma;' while the term `Kowhai' is applied to var. <i> grandiflora</i> in common with its other varieties. Var. <i> microphylla is also sometimes designated by the colonists the Native `Laburnum' or `Mimosa,' from the similarity of its foliage, in the arboreous condition, to that of these familiar ... European trees. <i>(1868,Contributions to the Early History of New Zealand,73</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> [1860 <i> New Zealand laburnum</i> BGN, 1909 HW (Andersen 5 entries)]

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> The [kowhai] tree is sometimes called the <i> New Zealand Laburnum</i>. The Maori name is said to mean <i> yellow </i>(the colour of the flowers). <i>(1907,Plants of NZ,214,1940, P. 225 ed.</i>)

>  <b>WHITE</b> The New Zealand Laburnum (<i> Sophora tetraptera</i>, (Yellow Kowhai) is a splendid deep yellow, with much larger flowers and calix than the English Laburnum. <i>(1905,My NZ Garden,92</i>)

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i> Sophora microphylla</i> NZ laburnum <i>(1908,My NZ Garden,C-14</i>) [NOTES: Report on a Botanical Survey of Waipoua Kauri Forest]

>  <b>TURNER</b> <i> Sophora microphylla</i> NZ laburnum <i>(1909,My NZ Garden,C-11</i>) [NOTES: Botanical Examination of the Higher Waimarino District]

>  KOWHAI TREE. - New Zealand laburnum tree. <i>(1917,New Zealand At the Front,xii</i>) [NOTES: Glossary]

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> The yellow kowhai (<i> Sophora grandiflora</i> and <i> S. microphylla</i>) has been fitly termed the New Zealand laburnum. <i>(1910,NZ Plants,37</i>)

>  <b>LAING $ BLACKWELL</b> The tree is sometimes called the New Zealand Laburnum. <i>(1940,Plants of NZ,225</i>)

>  lace fern [=] ring fern [s] <i>(1969,Standard Common Names for Weeds,43</i>)

## lacebark
 
 <i>n.</i> 1. See houhere. 2. any of various unrelated NZ trees having many-layered inner bark; spp. of the genera Hoheriaand <i> Plagianthus</i> of the family Malvaceae: Moore and Irwin <i> The Oxford Book of New Zealand Plants</i> (1978) 74 [get gen. quot.]; =ribbonwood, =thousand-jacket. 1. a. (esp. or chiefly NI) Spg also <i> lace bark, lace-bark. Houheria</i> spp., esp. <i> H. populnea</i>, =houhere.

>  No imported tree surpasses in lavishnessof white blossom the New Zealand hoheria,variously called ribbonwood and lacebark,nor equals in beauty of shape or colourits intricately dentate and translucentgreen leaf. <i>(Oct 12, 1935,Christchurch Star-Sun,14</i>)

>  Houhere (Hoheria pupulnea (Lacebark) was used in Mother Aubert's remedy Marupa, which she used for asthma, bronchitis, sore throats and other respiratory throats. <i>(Aug 24, 2002,Dominion Post,F16</i>)

>  <b>FEATON</b> On account of the fibrous nature of [<i> Plagianthus Lyallii'</i> s] inner bark, it is popularly known in the Middle Island as one of the `Ribbon Wood' or `Lace Bark' trees, as is the Hoheria populnea, and its varieties in the Northern Island. <i>Ibid.</i> 55 Hoheria... It is asserted that at one time "Lace-bark" bonnets were quite fashionable in Nelson... HOHERIA POPULNEA (<i> A.Cunn.</i>) ... The `HOUI' or `WHAU-WHI'.- ... grows abundantly in both Islands... Owing to the chaste [snow-white] appearance of its flowers, it has not inaptly been termed by some the `New Zealand orange Blossom'. The inner bark is peculiar, and consists of layer upon layer of laced fibre, ...From this peculiarity it is called by the settlers the `Thousand Jacket', `lace-bark', or `Ribbon-wood'. <i>(1889,Art Album NZ Flora,54</i>)

>  Cabbage trees invade the redwood forests, lacebark is troublesome around San Francisco, and ngaio trees are the "most invasive wildland pest" in southern California and Baja California. <i>(Nov 3, 2003,Dominion Post,B5</i>)

>  A curiosity which attracts a great deal of attention is a hat or bonnet made from the "lace-bark tree." <i>(Oct 11, 1879,Auckland Weekly News,11</i>)

>  (Wairarapa) ... a beautiful lace-bark tree. <i>(1848,NZ Journal,8/71</i>)

>  <b>KIRK</b> [Native name Houi .. [Settlers' name] Lace-bark .. [Family] Malvaceae <i>(1886,Settler's Handbook of NZ, The,121</i>)

>  Houhere (lacebark) .. Hoheria populnea .. Timber soft and of a perishable nature; used in cabinet making <i>(1890,PWD Catalogue of Timbers,9</i>)

>  Broadleaf [fn <i> Griselinia littoralis</i>.], five-fingered Jack [fn <i> Panax arboreum</i>.], lacebark [fn <i> Hoheria angustifolia</i> and <i> Plagianthus betulinus</i>.], matipo [fn <i> Pittosporum</i>.], red matipo [fn <i> Myrsine urvillei</i>.], New Zealand coffee [fn Species of <i> Coprosma</i>.], titoki or native ash [fn <i> Alectryon excelsum</i>.], and the totara pine [fn <i> Podocarpus totara</i>.] jostle one another in the dense dry bush. <i>(1900,Canterbury Old & New,184</i>)

>  <b>WHITE</b> Its vernacular names are `Ribbon Wood' and `Lace Bark,' for when the bark is pulled off and cut into strips it reminds one of insertion lace. <i>(1905,My NZ Garden,90</i>)

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> The varied names given by the Maoris in different districts to this plant, are all said to spring from the same root. <i> Whau</i> [252] signifies <i> wrapped about, netted, - like lace</i>, and the tree is so called from the character of its inner bark. The settlers name it lace-bark, ribbon-wood, [1940 edn, no hyphens] or thousand-jacket. <i>(1907,Plants of NZ,251,1940 p. 264 ed.</i>)

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> The various species of trees known as lacebark (<i> Hoheria populnea, H. sexstylosa</i>, and <i> H. angustifolia</i>), are, in their season, dense masses of snowy flowers. <i>(1910,NZ Plants,37</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i> Hoihere ...Hoheria populnea, lacebark. =houhere, houhi, houi. </i> <i>(1917,Dictionary of the Maori Language,66</i>)

>  <b>ANDERSON</b> <i> houhere</i>, a tree (Hoheria populnea), ribbonwood, lacebark, thousand-jacket. One of the three or four deciduous Maori trees. <i>(1946,Journal of the Polynesian Society,55/149</i>) [NOTES: Maori Words in English]

>  <i> lacebark</i>: tree,hoheria. <i>(1947,Reeds' School Dictionary: NZ Suppl.,174</i>)

>  <b>LEVY</b> Lacebark, wineberry, konini, mahoe, and water ferns characterised the secondary growth in the gullies and on the flats. <i>Ibid.</i> 87 ... such species as lancewood, karamu, toro, ramarama, koromiko, kanono, kohuhu, kawakawa, hangehange, rangiora, five finger, patete, toru, tree daisies, broadleaf, kamahi, mahoe, tarata, karo, lacebark, wineberry, fuchsia, kowhai, with tree ferns, weki, and mamuku. Toetoe, phormium, cabbage tree, with swamp coprosma and hupiro may figure as a developmental phase on wet soils. <i>(1951,Grasslands of NZ,16,1970 ed.</i>)

>  <i> lacebark</i> One of the names of a small New Zealand tree, also called `ribbonwood'. <i>(1958,Whitcombe's Modern Junior Dictionary (8th edn),233,8th ed.</i>)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> Lacebark .. .. houhere, houhi .. .. <i> Hoheria </i> <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/705-12</i>)

>  <i> lace-bark</i> n. the ribbonwood tree. <i>(1966,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),619</i>)

>  <b>HENDERSON</b> ... picturing the start of the track and lacebark or ribbonwood in flower bringing thoughts of white glimpses of miners' sudden canvastowns. <i>(1981,Exiles of Asbestos Cottage, The,131</i>)

>  <i> lacebark</i> n. a New Zealand tree with fibrous underbark. <i>(1982,Collins Concise English Dictionary,627</i>) [NOTES: 1986 NZPOD 417; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 275]

>  <b>WOOLASTON</b> When I told Eddie ... about our three trees and their names as I knew them, he said lacebark was incorrect, it should be ribbonwood ... [my parents] would have none of it. Lacebark it was and would ever be. <i>(1980,Sage Tea,43</i>)

>  <b>DAWSON</b> The species ... are not confined to such sites, but some are most frequent there, including ribbonwood (<i> Plagianthus regius</i>), and species of lacebark (<i> Hoheria</i>) of the family Malvaceae ... <i>(1988,Forest Vines to Snow Tussocks,154</i>)

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> called Ribbonwood by the colonists. <i>(1940,Plants of NZ,170</i>)

>  <i>(1916,NZ Association of Nurserymen, Trade Register</i>) [NOTES: And]



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> mountain ribbonwood, wild cherry. [sub-alpine SI]

>  <b>FEATON</b> On account of the fibrous nature its inner bark, it is popularly known in the Middle Island as one of the `Ribbon-Wood' or `Lace Bark' trees, as is the Hoheria populnea, and its varieties in the Northern Island. - <i>(1889,Art Album NZ Flora,54</i>)

>  <b>KIRK</b> <i> Gaya Lyallii</i> lace-bark <i>(1889,Forest Flora</i>) [NOTES: And.]

>  <b>CHEESEMAN</b> The Maoris apply the names <i> hoihere</i> or <i> houhere</i> to varieties [of <i> Hoheria populnea</i>] indifferently; the European settlers usually call all the forms `ribbon-wood' or `lacebark', names which are, unfortunately, also used for <i> Plagianthus betulinus</i> and <i> Gaya Lyallii</i>. <i>Ibid.</i> 80 <i> [G.Lyalli =Hoheria Lyallii</i> Hook.] <i> G[aya] Lyallii, J.E.Baker ... Lacebark.</i> <i>(1906,Manual NZ Flora,79</i>)

>  <b>NAIRN & SON</b> <i> Gaya Lyalli</i> lace bark <i>(1912,Catalogue (Nairn & Son)</i>)



 
<b>3</b> <i>n.</i>

>  Ribbonwood, P.betulinus, ... Whauwhi, Maori name, according to Hector; lace-bark tree, settlers' name according to Buchanan. <i>(1876,TrNZI,IX/175</i>) [NOTES: Blair]

>  <b>POTTS</b> The soft, bright-foliaged ribbonwood (lace-bark, Plagianthus) ... <i>(1882,Out in the Open</i>)

>  <b>FEATON</b> Hohere [Maori name] .. Lace-bark [Settlers' name] .. Plagianthus Lyallii .. Small tree <i>(1889,Art Album NZ Flora,177</i>)

>  <b>CHEESEMAN</b> <i>(1906,Manual NZ Flora,79</i>) [NOTES: see cit above]



## lacebark tree
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>MEREDITH</b> My camp [on a Wairarapa Sheep-run] is in a small patch of `leather-jacket' or `lace-bark' trees. ... [28] Four corner posts (round leather-jacket) were sunk in the ground ... <i>(1875,Adventuring in Maoriland in the Seventies,27,1935 ed.</i>) [NOTES: 1872 Oct in c1875 Meredith Adventuring in Maoriland]

>  <b>MANSFIELD</b> she had walked away back ... through the orchard, ...along the path by the lace bark tree and so into the spread tangled [sic] garden. <i>(1916,Aloe, The,91,1982 O'Sullivan ed.</i>)

>  <b>ALPERS</b> In the garden some tiny owls, perched on the branches of a lace-bark tree, called: `More pork; more pork.' And far away in the bush there sounded a harsh rapid chatter: `Ha-ha-ha ... [sic] Ha-ha-ha.' <i>(1984,Stories,232</i>) [NOTES: 1920 Mansfield Stories (1984 Alpers)]



 
 <i>n.</i> Plagianthus spp. esp. P. lyallii

>  <b>HETLEY & RAOUL</b> <i> Plagianthus Lyallii,</i> lace bark tree <i>(1889,Fleurs Sauvages</i>) [NOTES: And.]

## lacey-bark
 
 <i>n.</i>

## lactated
 
 <i>adj.</i> [An international sports term? Roger Robinson says yes, but this may still be the first recorded use.]

>  It's a major adrenalin rush which Cox (22) says can leave paddlers lactated and in oxygen debt. <i>(May 18, 1997,Sunday Star Times,B16</i>)



## lactoferrin
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Nutriceuticals are purified proteins which have some sort of useful biological activity in addition to their food value. Best known is lactoferrin, an iron-binding protein with antibacterial properties which has been approved for use on meat as a means of reducing bacterial spoilage... But the real beauty of lactoferrin.. is that removing it from milk has no effect on milk quality or properties. <i>(Mar 2003,NZ Geographic,62/54</i>)

>  <b>KITTO</b> Before letting down the ladder you should examine the bottom tumbler and hangers to see they [sic] are all right. <i>(1900,Practical Dredgeman's Manual,16</i>)

## ladies a basket
 
 <i>phr.</i>

>  <b>SCOTT</b> Ghastly sort of thing. Men two shillings a head and `ladies a basket'. <i>(1953,Breakfast at Six,33</i>)

>  <b>DARE</b> Admission was generally `gentlemen 2s. 6d., ladies a basket', which puzzled me considerably at first, until I found that ladies took a basket of refreshments ... and this exempted them from paying. <i>(1956,Rouseabout Jane,44</i>)

>  <b>COWAN</b> `A dance will be held in Paewhenua Hall on ... Good music. Good floor. Gents, 2/6; Ladies, 1/6 or Basket. Otorohanga Patrons - Metalled road to the door.' That was the notice the other day in a King Country paper. `Ladies, a basket' is a customary dance-night formula in the outback, and a convenient arrangement it is. Those liberal baskets of home-made dainties outdo anything you can buy for the money in your town shops. <i>(1935,Hero Stories of NZ,158</i>)

>  <b>BENNETT</b> Visitors reading notices in New Zealand papers, might be puzzled by phrases like `Rooms with tray' and `Ladies bring basket'; in both cases the container stands for the thing contained-breakfast and afternoon tea delicacies respectively. <i>(1943,English as it is spoken in NZ</i>)



## ladies a plate
 
 <i>phr.</i> request for women to bring a plate of food, to a party etc. [[AND 1961: OED(S b 18h.] to provide food as (part of) an admission fee to a function.] (See [plate (bring a plate)](../P#plate-(bring-a-plate)))

>  Others react to the ladies-a-plate politics. <i>(Oct 2, 1993,Listener (New Zealand Listener),24</i>)

>  <b>MITCHELL</b> When going to social functions make your [16] wife take a plate. Every immigrant will assure you that his wife did this in response to the `ladies a plate' instruction, ... <i>(1972,Half-Gallon Quarter-Acre Pavlova Paradise, The,15</i>)

>  When going to social functions make your wife take a plate. Every immigrant will assure you that his wife did this in response to the `ladies a plate' instruction, though readers of Groupie might think the injunction scandalous <i>(Jun 25, 1972,Sunday Times (NZ),35/2</i>)

>  <b>McCARTHY</b> ... we decided to hold a dance, a golf ball, at Ikamatua. As times were hard it was decided at two shillings for men and `ladies a plate'. <i>(1973,Listen...!,49</i>)

>  <i>(1979,Heinemann NZ Dictionary</i>)

>  <b>LELAND</b> ladies a plate (gents a crate): It's a party (invitation) with the female guests providing the food and, most likely, the male guests providing the drink. <i>(1980,Personal Kiwi-Yankee Dictionary, A,59</i>)

>  Newcomers to New Zealand country districts have been embarrassed by a failure to detect [a] semantic development in in the advertisements for country dances, used to ensure that the right amount of suppewill be provided and the hire of the hall paid - `Gents 2/- - Ladies a plate'. Since New Zealand countrywomen are renowned for their cooking of fancy cakes, an empty plate shows up rather poorly. <i>(1966,English Language in Aust. & NZ, The,48</i>)

>  <b>HILL</b> The Dansant, to be held in the Wharetapu Hall, ... May 18, 1954, ... Ladies a Plate; Euchre for Non-Dancers. <i>(1988,More from Moaville,48</i>)

>  plate refreshments contributed to a gathering, usually homemade cakes or sandwiches or sausage rolls or savs and pavs, usually by women, hence phr. `Gents $5, Ladies a plate.' NZA. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,85</i>)

>  <b>HOLCROFT</b> It was a relief ... to read the manuscripts of stories and poems instead of reports from country correspondents on council meetings or dances (`Ladies a Plate'). <i>(1986,Sea of Words</i>)

>  Ladies-a-plate is a local tradition far more profound than commercial sausage rolls. <i>(Mar 9, 2002,Listener (New Zealand Listener),42</i>)

## lady moth
 
 <i>n.</i> [??Is this a real word or a trope.]

>  <b>ANDERSON</b> tawhiri, a tree (Pittosporum tenuifolium); so called because small branches were broken off to wave welcome while calling out haeremai to approaching visitors: <i> tawhiri</i> means to beckon. The tree has smallishchocolate-coloured flowers very fragrant in summer evenings and nights, when the bowers of the trees too are a-flutter with the visiting lady-moths: also called ko[o]hu[u]hu[u] and tawhiwhi. <i>(1946,Journal of the Polynesian Society,55/146</i>) [NOTES: Maori Words in English]

## lady's bedstraw
 
 <i>n.</i> cleavers

## lady's thumb
 
 <i>n.</i> redshank

>  <b>HILGENDORF</b> REDSHANK (<i>Polygonum persicaria</i>) is also called persicaria, spotted persicaria, knot weed, smart weed, willow weed, and lady's thumb. It is an annual weed of gardens, crops, and wet pastures. <i>(1926,Weeds of New Zealand,59,1967 ed.</i>)

## lag
 
 <i>n.</i> type of physical punishment

>  "sometimes she'd say, if you don't stop following me, I'll tell them to take you for a lag'," Tawa said. <i>(Jan 27, 2006,NZ Herald,A6</i>)

>  The court heard that they assaulted the boy using a process they referred to as giving him a "lag". . . . It was said they would pick the toddler up, throw him over their shoulders, and "body slam" him on to a mattress. . . . Lags were also said to include regular punching and kicking of the boy. . . . just about every day the men would take the child into his bedroom and give him a lag. . . . The lags became punishment for when the boy was "naughty" and resulted in him crying. <i>(Oct 13, 2005,NZ Herald,A5</i>)

>  He told the court Tito witnesssed some of the lags he and Wharewera gave the boy. ... Asked by prosecuting council how she would have known what a lag was, Tawa said, "Because sometimes she'd say [to her son], 'If you don't stop following me, I'll tell them [Tawa and Whakawera] to take you for a lag'". <i>(Nov 15, 2005,NZ Herald,A2</i>)

## lagger
 
 <i>n.</i> [Newbold says (p. 250) LAGGING/LAG (n) Prison sentence. From laggings (leggings), the leg-irons in use last century to secure prisoners on convict ships or in outside working parties, or those undergoing punishment.]

>  <b>NEWBOLD</b> Bob.. and I were the two longest laggers in the joint... I just slipped into the routine immediately and into the main clique in the prison which was dominated by the long-laggers. <i>(1982,Big Huey, The,107</i>)

>  <b>SANSOM</b> In several letters written in 1898 by Burr Osborn who called himself `Yankee jack the lagger ..., I see that it was John Hunter who befriended this runaway sailor in 1845. <i>(1982,In the Grip of an Island,76</i>)

## lagoon
 
 <i>n.</i> This common word has wide contextual usage in NZ - essentially a smallish, shallowish body of water - not necessarily or usually salt: indeed it can have salt (see x) or fresh (see y), muddy, or mineral, or clear; frozen or cold ( ) or boiling. It can be deep enough to drown in, or shallow enough to be merely an overloaded swamp ( ), or `sheet of water', or can even have no water ( ). It can be of some square miles (see Lake Grassmere) or small as a pond; early NZ uses follow this standard sense: get geog. as well as temporal coverage. [-->[ 1873; AND cf US and DAE freshwater usually still 1797, 1801.]]

>  <b>Capt. W. Symonds R.N.</b> On the S.E. Coast of this island are several immense lagoons, into which flow rivers [the salt Lake Grasmere, Marlborough] <i>(1838,Journal,422</i>) [NOTES: c1838 Journal of Capt. W. Symonds R.N. quoting 1837 June 14 W.C. Symonds Notes on Cloudy Bay 422]

>  <b>BEST</b> In the Neighbourhood of the head of the bay are two Lagoons one upwards of a mile across the other much smaller. [ed fn] The large lagoon covering 213 acres of Miramar flat, originally called Roto Kura, then Para Lagoon, then Burnham Water, was drained in 1874. <i>Ibid.</i> 268 The first remarkable feature is a sand bank which divides a large brackish lagoon from the sea. ... Around the Lagoon is a belt of swamp constantly subject to inundation either from freshes or from eruptions from the sea. <i>(1840,Journal,230,1966 ed.</i>) [NOTES: 1840 June Best Journal (1966)]

>  <b>HEAPHY</b> The quantity of land in Chatham's Island ... exclusive of lagoons, which, when at their height, cover about 50,000 acres. <i>(1842,Narrative of a Residence in  Various Parts of NZ,120</i>)

>  The Hekeau is a small stream with lagoon at beach. <i>(Mar 6, 1849,Torlesse Papers,64,1958 ed.</i>)

>  <b>MONRO</b> ... a lagoon ... quite a lake in fact <i>(1844,Notes of a Journey,255</i>)

>  <b>HOCKEN</b> ... a useless lagoon. <i>(1898,Contributions to the Early History of New Zealand,204</i>)

>  [Terms of purchase issued by the Land Purchasers' Committee of Mangement] 12.Every allotment not fronting upon a river, road, lake, lagoon, or coast, must be not less than 300 yards in width, and not less that half a mile distant from a river, road, lake, lagoon or coast. <i>(Feb 22, 1851,Lyttelton Times,2</i>)

>  ... at certain notorious lagoon, pool, or deep-water swamps, frequented by ducks, lying about three miles from Dannevirke, and not far from the bridge over the Manawatu. <i>(1896,TrNZI,28/87</i>) [NOTES: Colenso]

>  I went down to the lagoon for a bathe. <i>(Feb 5, 1940,Otago Daily Times,18</i>)



 
<b>4</b> <i>n.</i> [?orig. a SI use]

>  A small dab-chick (Podiceps rufipectus) is common in our freshwater lagoons; and a largecrested grebe, hitherto undescribed, inhabits the lakes of the South. <i>(1869,TrNZI,1/III/19 ed.</i>) [NOTES: Buller Ornithology]

>  Most of the ridges are rounded on the tops, and many of them are covered with extensive peat bogs, dotted over with numerous lagoons. <i>(1915,TrNZI,47/121</i>)

>  <b>DIEFFENBACH</b> In several places behind these sand-hills lagoons of fresh water are found, ... <i>(1843,New Zealand (various authors/titles),1/134</i>)

>  <b>STRAUBEL ed.</b> He has located himself on the margin of a freshwater lagoon about a quarter of a mile from the beach [Of Sunday Island]. <i>(1954,Rhodes Whaling Journal,35</i>)

>  <b>MATHEW</b> There is a Lagoon of fresh water at a distance of perhaps two miles, whose waters appear to feed a small Creek ... [fn Lake St.John's] <i>(1940,Founding of New Zealand, The,165</i>) [NOTES: 1840 F.Mathew Report in 1940 Founding of NZ]

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> There are several freshwater lagoons, in which eels abound, even on the highest table-lands. <i>(1848,Handbook for New Zealand, The,210</i>)

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> Lagoons differ from lakes in being generally formed by surface water gathering in low grounds during the winter, from which there is no outlet. <i>(1849,Colonial Surveying,59</i>)

>  <b>ARNOLD</b> We ... walked along the river to a small lake or lagoon about 2 miles off. <i>(Oct 10, 1848,Letters,91,1966 ed.</i>)

>  <b>MacDONALD</b> Washed [the animals] in the lagoon. <i>(1933,Pages Past</i>) [NOTES: [1855 Trolove's Diary]]

>  <b>PHILLIPS</b> Paradise Flapper on a Lagoon. <i>(Dec 27, 1856,Journal,60</i>)

>  <b>JUNE BUTLER</b> [The Rangitata Valley bottom] consisted of a large swamp from which arose terrace after terrace up the mountains. ... The country is, as it were, crumpled up in an extraordinary manner, so that it is full of small ponds in lagoons - sometimes dry - sometimes merely swampy - now as full of water as they could be. <i>(1860,Forest Creek MS,36,1960 ed.</i>)

>  At my feet [at Butler's Mesopotamia on the Rangitata] there is fine undulating country spotted with small lakes or lagoons and streams. <i>(Jan 8, 1862,Chudleigh Diary,76,1950 ed.</i>)

>  <b>PASCOE (ed)</b> ... from the excessive rain it [the forest] was one large lagoon; we walked on all day above the knees in water ... <i>(1863,Over the Whitcombe Pass,41</i>) [NOTES: 1863 Lauper in 1960 Pascoe]

>  Rushy lagoon <i>(1864,Digger's Notes,43</i>)

>  ... near Forest Creek we pass, in order to reach the higher lines, across a swampy tract of country, in which we meet numerous lagoons, the whole having the character of an ancient river-bed running along a lateral moraine ... <i>(1864,AJHR,11</i>) [NOTES: Report on the Formation of the Canterbury Plains]

>  <b>HAAST`</b> Next morning, ... after passing a succession of lagoons and [122] ponds, [we] reached at last the upper end of Lake Coleridge. <i>(1879,Geology of the Provinces of Canterbury & Westland,121</i>)

>  The plateau, or elevated plain which forms the top of the Lammerlaw and Rock and Pillar Mountains, contains a large morass of 3000 acres in extent, besides numerous smaller swamps, with bogs and lagoons. <i>(1881,TrNZI,14/96</i>) [NOTES: Arthur]

>  ... in the grassy bed of a dry lagoon near Lake Guyon, ... <i>(1884,TrNZI,16/65</i>) [NOTES: Meyrick]

>  .... and, on top of the mountains, lakes and lagoons similar to other New Zealand Alps ... [189] In August I measured the ice in one of the lagoons on top of the Alps ... <i>(1885,TrNZI,17/188</i>) [NOTES: A. Reischek]

>  This is an extensive tableland, about 2,000 ft. above sea-level, lying between the Hokianga and Kaipara districts. It is covered with forest, and here and there are several lagoons. <i>(1905,TrNZI,37/351</i>) [NOTES: Walsh]

>  The specimen, which I now exhibit, was shot on a lagoon near Greytown North, in the Province of Wellington ... <i>(1907,TrNZI,39/137</i>) [NOTES: Iredale]

>  Most of the ridges are rounded on the tops, and many of them are covered with extensive peat bogs, dotted over with numerous lagoons. <i>(1915,TrNZI,47/121</i>) [NOTES: Popplewell]

>  In the town of Wyndham there is a lagoon called Pipi-a-Manawa, and it is fed by a spring called Matatiki ... <i>(1921,TrNZI,52/56</i>) [NOTES: Beattie]

>  Lagoon, n. Any tarn, pond, or open water too small to be called a lake. (C.) <i>(Nov 4, 1933,Press, The,15</i>) [NOTES: Acland Glossary]

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> There were innumerable lagoons on the plains in those days [c1860s] - mostly storm water, and not deep. <i>(1939,First White Boy Born in Otago,120</i>)

>  A shallow lake joined to a large area of water by a narrow channel. <i>(1958,Whitcombe's Modern Junior Dictionary (8th edn),233,8th ed.</i>)

>  lagoon ... NZ & Austral. freshwater pond, esp. stagnant one. <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,418</i>) [NOTES: nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict]

>  found that they were not ready and they will wash now in some Lagoon on their own side. <i>(Nov 29, 1861,Puketoi Station Diary</i>) [NOTES: Hocken TS]

>  This plant [Typha angustifolia] continues encroaching and filling up lagoons, ... <i>(1869,TrNZI,1/188-189</i>)

>  <b>BULLER</b> PHALCROCORAX BREVIROSTRIS.(WHITE-THROATED SHAG.) ...<i> Native name.- Kawau-paka. ... The White-throated Shag, which appears to be confined to New Zealand and the Chatham Islands, frequents the freshwater rivers and lagoons in all parts of the country. <i>(1873,Birds of New Zealand,330,1888 2nd edn 2/168 ed.</i>)

>  <b>REEVES</b> On the other hand, `lagoon' has replaced the English `pond', except in the case of artificial water. <i>(1898,Long White Cloud, The,403</i>)

 <i>n.</i>

## lahar
 
 <i>n.</i> a flood of mud and water

>  During the evening a lahar burst from the side of Mr Ruapehu's crater lake. This flow of mud and volcanic debris rushed down the Whangaehu River and swept away the railway bridge at Tangiwai, shortly before the overnight express from Wellington to Auckland was due. <i>(Jan 11, 2003,Listener (New Zealand Listener),31</i>)

>  [He]assumes that another Mt Ruapehu lahar will inevitably cause another disaster... [The engineer] told me that the destruction of the rail bridge at Tangiwai was only because its piles were not sunk deep enough to resist the underscoring action of the lahar. <i>(Nov 22, 2003,Listener (New Zealand Listener),9</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  Crying "racist!" didn't work, and neither did the lahar of backdowns. <i>(Apr 3, 2004,Listener (New Zealand Listener),15</i>)

>  <b>RENAE</b> Fostering her creativity had more to do with assisting Frank and Ernest in slugging back a daily liquid lahar of a certain beverage rather than nurturing Tit in her struggle to release the Frances Hodkins in herself. <i>(1998,Yin & Tonic,27</i>)

>  This brought the usual lahar of denialfrom Mr Peters. <i>(Nov 24, 1995,Dominion,2</i>)

## lair up
 
 <i>v.</i> to dress in a vulgar exhibitionist style [X-ref only at lare]

>  Derek Parsons, of Westport, wants to know the derivation of the verb lair up, which he hears used by people planning a party... Lair has a long history.... As the centuries passed it became a sideways look or leer, producing leery as a synonym for distrustful, still in proper use today. Moving right along, leery became lairy, meaning flashy, eventually producing lair for a person who dresses in a vulgar, exhibitionist style. Properly, to lair up is to dress in this style. If you want to have a sleazy party you should use lair it up. <i>(Oct 6, 2001,Dominion,12</i>) [NOTES: Frank Haden]

## lamb
 
 <i>n.</i> A lamb carcass comes from a sheep under 12 months of age, and ceases to be classed as a lamb for meat purposes after September 30 in the year following that in which it was born.

>  Lamb: A lamb carcass comes from a sheep under 12 months of age, with the proviso that a lamb ceases to be classed as a lamb for meat purposes after September 30 in the year following thatin which it was born. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,34</i>)

>  <b>OGONOWSKA-COATES</b> Ages of sheep. Although there are many variations, the following names are usually used: A sheep is a lamb until weaned. After weaning a lamb is called a hogget until shorn. A male lamb is a ram lamb until castrated, then a wether. A female lamb is a ewe lamb. After shearing, a ewe or ram lamb is usually called a shorn hogget until cutting the first two teeth. It is then called a two-tooth. Some use the term two-tooth as soon as the lamb is shorn. Next year the sheep is a four-tooth, then a six-tooth and then an eight-tooth or full-mouthed sheep. It is full-mouthed as long as the teeth remain perfect but then becomes broken-mouthed and gummy. <i>(1987,Boards, Blades & Barebellies,94</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a station or farm cadet from Britain [mainly S.I.]

>  <b>BARRER</b> Colonel Western, her brother-in-law, had three cadets who were known as "Western's Lambs", because they were so unlike lambs. They were a terror to the district .. the dinner disappeared and so did the 'lambs'. <i>(1939,Brave Days: Pioneer Women of New Zealand,238</i>)

>  <b>STUDHOLME</b> They [cadets] were commonly known as 'the lambs' and were a fine lot of young fellows. <i>(1940,Te Waimate,94</i>)

>  <b>THORNTON</b> Their [the cadets'] collective nickname was "the lambs" for they had come from various leading English public schools. <i>(1986,NZ Heritage of Farm Buildings,189</i>)

>  station cadet from England: a lamb. <i>(2001,Questionnaire Respondents (DB)</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>v.</i> [Heard in October 2000 on Radio NZ farming programme in a remark like "farmers are lambing late this year".]

>  <b>McNAUGHTON</b> Jim, the runholder, persuaded Bluey to stay on and give a hand to lamb the paddock ewes. <i>(1970,Tat,89</i>)

>  <b>BUTLER</b> The all-engrossing topics [in Canterbury] seemed to be sheep, horses, dogs, cattle, English grasses, paddocks, bush and so forth. I was rather startled to hear one gentleman ask another whether he meant to wash this year, and receive the answer `No.' I soon discovered that a person's sheep are himself. He does not wash his sheep before shearing, but he washes; and, most marvellous of all, it is not his sheep which lamb, but he `lambs down' himself. <i>(1863,First Year in Canterbury Settlement,Ch. III</i>) [NOTES: (1860 Jan 28)]



## lamb break
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) an escape by a lamb from a mob of ewes and lambs [often causing a mass escape.] (See [break](../B#break))

>  <b>HALL</b> When a lamb peels off from a mob, it's called a lamb break. This is most likely to occur when they've become mismothered - that is separated from their mothers in the mob which, as you can imagine, makes them pretty upset. Lamb breaks are dreaded by shepherds because they can start a chain reaction, with the entire mob rapidly getting out of control. <i>(1987,Shepherd's Year,  A,42</i>)

>  <b>ASPINALL</b> Once the sheep are mobbed care must be taken to avoid .. a lamb break. <i>(1993,Farming Under Aspiring,150</i>)



## lamb down
 
 <i>v.</i>

>  Lamb (l&abreve.m), v. t. [Prob. identical with LAM, v. t., to beat soundly. See LAM, in the Dictionary.] To knock, in a peculiar figurative sense (see below); - used only in the phrase to lamb down. To lamb down, (a) To knock down; - as to lamb down a check. See To knock down a check, under KNOCK. (b) To entertain (a person) at a public house (esp., to supply him with liquor) and to receive a bank check as payment in advance, on the understanding that the guest's credit is to cease when the value of the check is exhausted; - said to the keeper of a public house, esp. in the Australian bush. In the second sense, which is the usual one, the phrase commonly implies extortion and fraudulent charges on the part of the innkeeper. <i>(1898,Merriam-Webster Third International,2025</i>)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> Lamb down, v. tr. (1) To knock down a cheque or a sum of money in a spree. There is an old English verb, of Scandinavian origin, and properly spelt lamm, which means to thrash, beat. <i>(1898,Austral English,258</i>)

>  <b>LYON</b> The result was always the same, and the guileless one went back [to the bar] to be duly lambed down which, in common parlance, meant he was allowed to shout for all hands, was soon moneyless and found himself on the verge, if not quite in the delirium tremens stage, ... <i>(1952,Faring South,161</i>)

>  ~ v. to spend money lavishly on a spree, or to encourage lavish spending, as of bush hotel-keepers (lambers down). <i>(1966,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),619</i>)

>  lamb down v.i. spend money lavishly on a spree, encourage lavish spending, as of bush hotel-keepers lambers down. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),12</i>)

>  A Maori pioneer was 'lambed down' lastweek. <i>(Jul 25, 1917,Chronicles NZEF,ii/23 : 251</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>v.</i> (Rural) to shepherd at lambing time.

>  <b>Butler, S.</b> He does not wash his sheep before shearing, but he washes; and, most marvellous of all, it is not his sheep which lamb, but he 'lambs down' himself. <i>(1863,First Year in Canterbury Settlement,32</i>)

>  This 1550ha farm is located 25km from Whakatane and finishes 1200 bulls, 500 other cattle and lambs down 3000 ewes (140%) and 900 hoggets. <i>(Apr 27, 2002,Dominion,48</i>)

>  Most marvellous of all, it is not his sheep which lamb, but he 'lambs down' himself. <i>(Jan 28, 1860,Joan, Betty and the Seagull,32</i>) [NOTES: in BUTLER First Year (1863)]



 
<b>2</b> <i>v.</i> (Rural) to spend all one's wages on alcohol.

>  <b>MARTIN</b> Swaggers were notorious in the popular imagination for their disreputable nature and alcoholic binges, during which they would 'burst' or 'lamb down' their pay cheques in public houses. <i>(1990,Forgotten Worker, The,29</i>)

## lamb sheet
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a length of scrim or netting used for gathering in lambs for docking/tailing.

>  <b>McLEOD</b> In some districts things called `lamb sheets' are used. These are long strips of scrim or calico about three feet wide and twenty yards long with a cord through the top. When the sheep approach the yard and the danger of a / break begins, three or four of these sheets are run outbetween the men, making a sort of false yard of flapping white sheets which frightens the lambs back into the mob and enables the pressure to be put on to force them into the yard. <i>(1951,High Country,25</i>)

Perhaps we'd be happier to call it by the biological name if we knew that in north-west England "lamb's fry" usually means testicles, skinned, halved and fried in butter. <i>(Jul 18, 2001,Listener (New Zealand Listener),42</i>)

## lamb's quarters
 
 <i>n.</i> [1770 Banks 2/8 [see FATHEN] [cf Beaglehole's note or OED.]] (See [fathen](../F#fathen))

>  <b>FORSTER</b> Near all the places where the Indians have their huts, there grows a kind of <i>Scurvy-grass <i> or <i> Lepidium <i>, which is an excellent antiscorbutic. The wild <i> Sellery [Apium australe] was not in flower & we could not determine what Genus of Umbellated plants it belongs. The common <i> Sow-thistle [Sonchus asper <i>] is likewise in great plenty ..., together with a kind of <i> Lamb's-Quarters. [Gnaphalium trinerve <i> Forst. f. ?] <i>(Jun 8, 1773,Resolution Journal of J.R. Forster, The,2/297</i>)

>  <b>FORSTER</b> Near all the places where the Indians have their huts, there grows a kind of <i>Scurvy-grass </i> or <i> Lepidium </i>, which is an excellent antiscorbutic. The wild <i> Sellery [Apium australe</i>] was not in flower & we could not determine what Genus of Umbellated plants it belongs. The common <i> Sow-thistle [Sonchus asper </i>] is likewise in great plenty ..., together with a kind of <i> Lamb's-Quarters. [Gnaphalium trinerve </i> Forst. f. ?] <i>(Jun 8, 1773,Resolution Journal of J.R. Forster, The,2/297</i>)

>  So Kerewin's next meal featured a salad with fourteen different greens in it, all plucked within a one-mile radius of the Tower [West Coast]. Sorrel, wild turnip, lambsquarters, dandelions, pikopiko curls, puha, cress, young yarrow ... <i>(1983,Bone People, The,110</i>)



## lamb's tongue
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [broad-leaved plantain](../B#broad-leaved-plantain),[hoary plantain](../H#hoary-plantain))

>  <b>HILGENDORF</b> BROAD-LEAVED PLANTAIN (<i>Plantago major </i>), also called ratstail, birdseed, lamb's tongue, and greater plantain, is common on roadsides and trodden places throughout the country and in pastures in the King Country. ... From the middle of the rosette ascends the flower stalk ... , which afterwards give[s] place to seeds, and produce[s] the ratstail appearance that gives the plant one of its trivial names. The long spikes of seed pods are often used for feeding caged birds. ... PLANTAIN (Plantago lanceolata) is commonly called rib grass, lamb's tongue, horse plantain, and soldiers. It is common in pastures and waste places throughout. It forms a rosette of lanceolate leaves about 6 inches long, with prominent or converging veins, giving the name `rib grass'. From the rosette rises the slender flower stalk a foot or more in length, with a crowded mass of tiny brown or black flowers at its tip. These heads are used by children for playing `soldiers,' and so in Scotland the weed is called `Carl Doddie'-that is `Charles and George'- a souvenir of 1745. <i>(1926,Weeds of New Zealand,161,1967 ed.</i>)

>  <b>HILGENDORF</b> HOARY PLANTAIN (Plantago media), is also called lamb's tongue and swamp plantain; it is found in fields and waste places, but appears nowhere very common. <i>(1926,Weeds of New Zealand,163</i>) [NOTES: nf. 1967]

## lamb-marking

>  <b>JARDINE</b> Of all the jobs ... on a high country property, that of lamb-marking provides the greatest scope for the occurrence of unseen events. <i>(1978,Shadows on the Hill,108</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> [AND 1882 (?earmarking); 1948 (tailing and castrating).] (See [marking n](../M#marking-n))

## lambie

## lambing down
 
 <i>n.</i> see lamb down v.2 [[AND as ppl aj 2.a. the process of spending one's earnings on liquor 1870 b. inveigling the spending ... 1886.] (Cf. do down; land-shark n2..)]

>  A case cropped up at the S. M. Court onSaturday that serves to remind one of theold "lambing-down" days... An oldminer... placed aGBP60 in the hands of thelandlord he put up with. He was arrestedon Friday for drunkenness... He had spentaGBP36 12s for drink, while his board costhim aGBP4 10s. <i>(Jan 25, 1904,Grey River Argus,2</i>)

>  A deplorable case of "lambing down"occupied the attention of Mr. Kenrick,S.M., at the Inglewood Magistrate's Courta few days ago. <i>(Sep 25, 1912,Otago Witness,11</i>)

>  `Lambing down' in Invercargill [i.e., a Publican's cashing in on a drunk shearer's cheque] <i>(Jun 9, 1880,Evening Post,3/1</i>)

>  <b>BELL</b> And if a good deal of `lambing down' was done in his house [sc. a rough and rowdy hotel] and he were charged with it, he would say, with his foreign accent, `If a fellow vill knock down his cheque and spendt his moneys in drink, vy, he may as vell and betters do it in mine house as inanoder man's'. <i>(1899,In the Shadow of the Bush,23</i>)

>  It was a case of alleged `lambing down' ... at the Caversham [Hotel]. ... he had somewhere about eight `quid'. <i>(Jun 23, 1906,NZ Truth,53/7/3</i>)

>  A specific charge was laid against Burke of what is classically known as `lambing down,' a common enough business in the olden days but little heard of now, except in the backblocks of Australia after shearing. The process is simplicity itself. A shearer comes along with a chequefor 80 or 100 [pounds], gives it to the publican, gets rapidly drunk, and then drunker, and then D.T.'s and then wakes up and goes back into the never-never to rake up another cheque. For a publican in these enlightened days to be accused of this kind of business is a very serious thing,... <i>(Oct 3, 1908,NZ Truth,172/4/5</i>)

>  <b>MOORE</b> The little process by which the publican is fattened and the bushman skinned is facetiously called `lambing down' <i>(1890,New Zealand For the Emigrant,217</i>)

>  <b>FOSTON</b> Lambing down' was a frequent occurrence. <i>(1921,New Zealand At the Front,150</i>)

>  One flagrant case of what was styled [c1860s] `lambing down' occurred during my stay at Cromwell. <i>(1925,Random Recollections,36</i>)

>  <b>MACKAY</b> In these modern times, `lambing down', a term that passed for good Australian slang, has become a little out of date. It was born of the experience of the average bushman or shearer, accustomed to an annual ... spree at the nearest shanty hotel. Over the counter went his hard-earned chequeand, when that cheque was `cut out' ..., the celebrant was kicked out. <i>(1961,Puborama,12</i>)

>  I hope to hear of fewer "lambing down"cases in future. <i>(Jun 7, 1918,Chronicles NZEF,IV/45 : 200</i>)

## lammie
 
 <i>n.</i> (informal) a lamb

>  <b>BAUGHAN</b> "And then, there's the lammies and the chicken.. for company." <i>(1912,Brown Bread,110</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> Mainly West Coast: a waterproof flannel coat worn for work in the open. [cf Part: `ineligible - mainly nautical term F. and H.; sup. 3. A blanket :c.: from ca1885 : B and L: ex the nautical S.E. sense. Farmer and Henley Lammy. A blanket: originally a thick quilted frock, or short jumper made of flannel or blanket cloth, worn by sailors as an outside garment in cold weather. (Gentlemen's Magazine 1866) W3 Lammie, -y; lambie, -y. [Nf OEDS?; EDD [The Ll-spelling might be back-formation from llama (OED b. The wool of the llama or a material made from this - 1882); c. llama - stuff 1851, llama-cloth 1871 HWO]]

>  [Advt] Men's Navy Lammies, doublethroughout, guaranteed rainproof; ridingand walking styles - 39/6 each. <i>(Jan 18, 1929,Grey River Argus,3</i>)

>  [Advt] The Workers' Friend. Llamys.I have pleasure in announcing that I amsole agent for Westland's famous LLAMYS -the garment used by Miners, StockmenFarmers, Roadmen and Railway Workers. Awarm, weatherproof pure wool garment towork in... Their Character and GeneralExcellence remain unchanged after 50years of unprecedented popularity andwidespread demand... H. Hamer, Kumara. <i>(Jul 9, 1938,Grey River Argus,2</i>)

>  [Advt] 26/-; Men's Heavy Double Kaiapoi <i>(Feb 20, 1924,Grey River Argus,7</i>) [NOTES: Citation incomplete; check original]

>  [Advt] The "Lamby" Bushshirt. A legendthroughout the land. Made from quality100% wool weave, wool lined, lined hoodand lace-up front... Lamby bushshirt$139.00. <i>(Jul 1993,NZ Outdoor,36</i>)

>  Out west, there's the Nugget Cafe, a one-shop renaissance for the former mining village of Mangarakau, and hairy farmer with ripped lammies and hearts of gold. <i>(Jan 29, 2001,Evening Post,18</i>)

>  The fame of the West Coaster's `own' garment for outdoor workers - the ubiquitous miner's `lammie' - has spread far afield. This week a trial parcel of lammies was dispatched on request to members of the Malayan Police Force at Singapore who indicated in a letter to the Kumara firm which has made a specialty of supplying lammies since the gold rush days that they had heard of the unique qualities of the garment. The full story of the request from Singapore is not known, but it is believed that Auckland Chinese market gardeners may have been responsible. For many years Chinese gardeners have adopted the lammie as almost an `official uniform' and supplies for Auckland are regularly forwarded from the small business in Kumara. But what is a lammie? In the first place it is a strictly utilitarian garment with no claims to attractiveness in appearance. The original type - still in use after 70 years - was a loose-fitting almost shapeless shirt buttoned in front and with elbow-length sleeves made of flannel and water-proofed. It is this proofing quality which has established the popularity of the lammie. It was an ideal garment for outdoor workers in the West Coast climate, enabling them to keep drywithout the encumbrance of an overcoat and it was soon in general use on the gold fields, later to be adopted by bushworkers, navvies and many farmers. At first the lammie was made of moleskin, then grey flannel and over the past 20 years of navy flannel, but the proofing formula has remained unchanged. In recent years there has also been developed a modified style, double breasted Coast fashion, but the original shirt style is still popular. Apart from the fact that the historic gold-mining town of Kumara is regarded as the home of the lammie in New Zealand, the story of its origin has been lost. It is thought, however, that it was probably brought to the goldfields by the first Welsh diggers and that it may have been spelt `Llamy.' It was soon almosta universal garment among diggers, including big contingents of Chinese miners who found it ideal both for utility and for economy. <i>(May 12, 1952,Evening Post,9</i>)

>  [corresp. col.] Sir, - In reference to your article on the West Coast shirt called a `lammie,' when a girl over 58 years ago I used to visit a couple named Tait who lived in Dillsmanstown. Mr. had a sluicing claim, and a draper named Todd used to get special flannel by the bolt for Mrs. Tait, and she made these `lammies.' She waterproofed them by dipping them in a special solution two or three times and hanging them on a line dripping wet, to dry each time. They were sold for 2 [pounds] 10/- to 2 [pounds] 15/- but I'm sure she never sold one to a Chinaman. There were hundreds there working tailings. It was Mrs. Tait's own invention I am sure. - I am, etc., <i>(May 1952,Evening Post,13</i>)

>  [For Sale Column] Llamys, double breasted all sizes ... Hamer's Drapery Kumara (Phil Duffy, Prop.) <i>(Aug 8, 1952,Grey River Argus</i>)

>  <b>HUNT</b> ... our favourite overcoat those days was a lambie. It was made of wool and really turned the water. It had no shape ... just the armholes; it fitted anyone. <i>(1981,Speaking a Silence,29</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a shapeless raincoat, usually sleeveless and ideal for active outdoor work.

>  <b>HUNT</b> Our favourite overcoat those days was a lambie. <i>(1981,Speaking a Silence,29</i>)

>  <b>MACNICOL & TROTTER</b> There was a brand of raincoats we called Lammies. They were made of flannel like cloth and steeped in sugar of lead and something else. [lamby; lambie] <i>(1987,I, Duncan from Skippers,23</i>)



## lamp

## lamprey
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [also blind eel](../A#also-blind-eel))

>  <b>McDOWALL</b> The lamprey (<i>Geotria australis</i>) is eel-like at first glance but closer examination soon reveals that it has no jaws, no paired fins, no bones, and no gill covers (but seven small external gill apertures)... Adult lampreys migrate into freshwater during spring, mostly at night. <i>(1984,NZ Whitebait Book, The,24</i>)

>  <b>FORSTER</b> We got some other new fish, viz. a small Lumpfish [[ed. fn] <i>Cyclopterus pinnulatus</i> of <i>Descr. Anim</i>., p.30], a small Blenny, both caught under the rocks at low water mark; some Starfish, Coats of mail water spouts, Centipus, Muscles & Turbines, besides in the Evening a Lamprey ... [[ed. fn] <i> Petromyzon cirratus</i> of <i>Descr. Anim</i>. p.112] was caught. We described all this and drew a new Sole.[ed. fn] <i>Pleuronectes scapha</i> of <i>Descr. Anim</i>. p.130] <i>(Apr 8, 1773,Resolution Journal of J.R. Forster, The,2/250</i>)

>  <b>AYLING</b> <i>LAMPREY</i> (Kanakana) <i>Geotria australis </i> <i>(1982,Collins Guide,39,1984 ed.</i>)

>  <b>PAUL</b> Lamprey <i>Geotria australis An eel-like animal, the lamprey of New Zealand waters also occurs in southern Australia, Chile and Argentina; ... <i>(1986,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,23</i>)

>  <b>DRUMMOND & HUTTON</b> The lampreys breed in the rivers <i>(1902,Nature in NZ,75</i>)

>  <b>THOMSON</b> The lamprey makes an annual visitation up the rivers in the spring months, usually about October. <i>(1922,Naturalisation of Animals and Plants,187</i>)

>  In the Waitera, a species of lamprey is taken in considerable quantities. <i>(Nov 23, 1842,Letters from New Plymouth,66,1968 ed.</i>)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> the lamprey I find is a sea fish wh[ich] comes up the river, it is like an eel except the head which is more like a leech but very ugly & forbidding <i>(Jun 10, 1843,Journal,30</i>) [NOTES: Journal 1842-1843 [TS:ATB]]

>  <b>JOLLIE ed.</b> [In 1846, the Maoris] used to visit Molineux to exchange mutton birds for `kune kunes' or lampreys, which an old maori living there used to catch in large numbers ... <i>(1873,Reminiscences,14</i>) [NOTES: MS]

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> The finest <i>Lampreys</i>, too, are taken in many of the rivers during freshets, by means of weirs very ingeniously erected by the natives. <i>(1848,Handbook for New Zealand, The,161</i>)

>  <b>POWER</b> The swamps, ponds, and lakes abound with eels and lampreys of various forms and sizes, some of them hideously ugly and snake-like. <i>(1849,Sketches in NZ,78</i>)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> Fam. <i>Petromyzidae, (Pihapiharau</i>,) the Lamprey, which is found in most of the rivers to the south. <i>(1855,Te Ika a Maui,627</i>)

>  <b>HURSTHOUSE</b> These, with the smelt, lamprey, whitebait ... are all the fish ... <i>(1857,New Zealand (various authors/titles),1/122</i>)

>  <b>THOMSON</b> The lamprey, Pipiharau, is, properly speaking, a salt-water fish which enters rivers to spawn. <i>(1859,Story of New Zealand, The,1/30</i>)

>  The lamprey, I may mention for the benefit of those who have not seen it, is not unlike a huge leech. It holds on to the rocks by a sucker, and by this means ascends the perpendicular rocks. Its colour and shape otherwise are not unlike the eel. <i>(1867,Rambles with a Philosopher,89</i>) [NOTES: Griffiths Collection]

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> The principal ones are the <i>tuna</i>, eel, the <i>pipiharau</i>, lamprey, the <i>kokopu</i>, and <i>inanga</i>;... <i>(1870,Te Ika a Maui,498</i>)

>  <b>HUTTON</b> 139. GEOTRIA CHILENSIS. Gray. Lamprey. <i>(1872,Fishes,87</i>)

>  <b>HAY</b> In the freshwater streams are eels, lampreys, and whitebait; ... <i>(1882,Brighter Britain,2/231</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i>Piharau</i>, n. <i>geotria chilensis</i>; lamprey. <i>(1892,Dictionary of the NZ Language,134</i>)

>  <b>TREGEAR</b> Lampreys (<i>piharau</i>) were caught in loose mats of fern laid in small streams ... <i>(1904,Maori Race, The,108</i>)

>  <i>Geotria australis</i> Gray. The lamprey is usually described as a fresh-water fish .. <i>(1913,TrNZI,45/235</i>) [NOTES: Thomson, Otago]

>  <i>Geotria australis</i> Gray. Lamprey. Maori: Korokoro, Piharau. <i>(1929,TrNZI,61/139</i>) [NOTES: Young]

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i>kanakana, ... 3. Blind eel</i> or <i>lamprey</i>. [so 1957/93 <i>Geotria australis, lamprey. = korokoro</i>.] <i>(1917,Dictionary of the Maori Language,110</i>)

>  GEOTRIA AUSTRALIS. <i>Lamprey</i>. Called `piharu' [sic] and `korokoro' by the Maoris. <i>(May 1922,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,V/97</i>) [NOTES: PHILLIPPS & HODGKINSON : Further Notes on Edible Fishes]

>  <b>BEST</b> The other principal species formerly taken were the inanga and kokopu ( <i>Galaxias</i> and <i>Retropinna</i>), upokororo ( <i>Prototractes</i>), and panoko ( <i>Cheimarrichthys</i>); while the lamprey (piharau and korokoro) and fresh water crayfish (koura) were taken in considerable numbers in some districts. <i>(1929,Fishing Methods,71</i>)

>  <i>Geotria australis</i>: [European Name] Lamprey [Maori Name] Korokoro or kunakuna. <i>(1936,Handbook for New Zealand, The,73</i>) [NOTES: ANZAAS Conf. Jan. 1937]

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> <i>Geotria australis</i> Gray. Lamprey. Korokoro. <i>(1938,TrRSNZ,68/400</i>) [NOTES: Fishes of Otago Harbour]

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> LAMPREY (KOROKORO) <i> Geotria australis</i> Gray <i>Ibid.</i> 397 Lamprey <i>Geotria australis</i> Gray (Syns. <i>G. allporti </i> Gunther and <i>G. saccifera</i> Regan) <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,58</i>) [NOTES: 1955 Whitley Namelist in 1956 Graham NZ Fishes]

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> Lamprey .. .. korokoro, pihapiharau, piharau, pipiharau .. .. <i>Geotria australis </i> <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/705-12</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> LAMPREY see <i>Hagfish </i> <i>(1981,Fisherman's Bible,142</i>)

## LAMS
 
<b>1</b> <i>abbr.</i> (Rural) Livestock Auction Management System, an information technology stock sales system.

>  The new system was installed at the Waiareka saleyards in Oamaru on Monday, the first saleyards in Otago to have the Livestock Auction Management System (Lams) operating. Lams is effectively the transaction processing part of the system which will enable tax invoices, sale notes, delivery dockets and vendor declaration details to be produced on sale day at the sale. <i>(Jul 7, 2000,Otago Daily Times,12</i>)

>  49 38 Irons - .. 8/- ea. ... 14 Lances - .. 8/- ea. ... 1 Cutting Spade - .. 8/- ... 2 Boat Spades - .. .16. 4 Boat axes - .. 4/- ea. ... 5 Boat Knives - .. 2/- ea. ... 3 Pughs - .. .9. ... 2 Blubber Knives - ... 8. ... 1 [Boat] Traveller - .. .4. <i>(1837,Piraki Log</i>) [NOTES: List of Gear Lost]



## lance fern
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Finger ferns (species of <i>Grammitis</i>), hanging spleenwort, sickle spleenwort (<i>Asplenium polyodon</i>), lance fern (<i>Anarthropteris lanceolata</i>) and fork ferns (species of <i>Tmesipteris</i>) are also common on tree-fern trunks or hanging from the branches of forest trees. <i>(2001,NZ Geographic,No. 49/76</i>)

>  The lance fern (<i>Anarthropteris lanceolata</i>) produces new plantlets directly from its roots and on wet rock faces colonies of plants can often been seen linked together in a long string. <i>(Jan 2001,NZ Geographic,No. 49/69</i>)

## lancewood
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Pseudopanax crassifolium</i>, a tree noted for its slender trunk and varying leaf forms at different stages of growth; =horoeka, =ivy tree, also grass-tree (juvenile), also called horoeka, umbrella tree. [AND timber trees [f. West Indian wood] 1861. 1872 Tr NZI - 1925 DD2 (Andersen 24 entries)]

>  Lancewood, bog pine and marble leaf with different juvenile and adult leaf forms are abundant. <i>(Jun 12, 2004,Dominion Post,E16</i>)

>  <i> Pseudopanax crassifolium </i> <i>(1872,TrNZI,v. 4:</i>)

>  <i> Pseudopanax crassifolium </i> <i>(1877,TrNZI,v.9:</i>)

>  <b>HAY</b> There is the cabbage-tree palm, with bare shank and top-knot; the nikau palm, with weird and wondrous frondage; the lancewood, ...; the fern-tree, .... There is the twisting, squirming rata; the gaunt ... kahikatea; the golden kowhai; the dark velvet-covered rimu; the feathery red tawai; the perfumy mangiao; ... <i>(1882,Brighter Britain,2/187</i>)

>  [Native name] Horoeka .. [Settlers' name] Lancewood .. [Family] Araliaceae <i>(1886,Settler's Handbook of NZ, The,122</i>) [NOTES: [Native name] Horoeka .. [Settlers' name] Lancewood .. [Family] Araliaceae]

>  ... from the branches hang bunches of that wonderful lichen, `old man's beard', and slender lance-wood emerges ... from the convolvulus-leaved but thorny `lawyers' and the low manuca scrub. <i>(1888,Antipodean Notes,152</i>)

>  (And) <i> Pseudopanax crassifolium </i> lancewood <i>(1889,Forest Flora</i>)

>  <b>MATTHEWS</b> (And) <i> Pseudopanax crassifolium </i> lancewood <i>(1890,Descriptive & Priced List</i>)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> <i> Lancewood </i>, n.There are many lancewoods in various parts of the world. The name, in Australia, is given to <i> Backhousia myrtifoli </i> a, Hook. and Harv., N.O. <i> Myrtaceae </i>; and in New Zealand, to <i> Panax crassifolium </i>, Dec. and Plan., N.O. <i> Araliaceae </i>, known as <i> Ivy-tree </i>, and by the Maori nameof <i> Horoeka </i>(q.v.). <i>(1898,Austral English,259</i>)

>  <b>DENDY</b> <i> Pseudopanax crassifolium </i> lancewood <i>(1900,Canterbury Old & New</i>)

>  <b>MATTHEWS</b> <i> Pseudopanax crassifolium </i> <i>(1905,Tree Culture in NZ</i>)

>  <b>CHEESEMAN</b> <i> P[seudopanax] crassifolium, C.Koch. Horoeka; Hohoeka; Lancewood. </i> <i>(1906,Manual NZ Flora,235</i>)

>  Report on Botanical Survey of Kapiti Island <i>(1907,AJHR,C-8</i>)

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>(1908,AJHR</i>) [NOTES: Botanical Survey Tongariro National Park]

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>(1924,Cultivation of NZ Plants, The</i>)

>  <i>(1925,Duncan & Davies' Catalogue</i>)

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> <i> Pseudopanax crassifolium </i> (The Lancewood)... Maori name <i> Horoeka. </i> <i>(1940,Plants of NZ,324</i>)

>  <i> lancewood: tree. </i> <i>(1947,Reeds' School Dictionary: NZ Suppl.,174</i>)

>  <b>LEVY</b> ... such species as lancewood, karamu, toro, ramarama, koromiko, kanono, kohuhu, kawakawa, hangehange, rangiora, five finger, patete, toru, tree daisies, broadleaf, kamahi, mahoe, tarata, karo, lacebark, wineberry, fuchsia, kowhai, with tree ferns, weki, and mamuku. Toetoe, phormium, cabbage tree, with swamp coprosma and hupiro may figure as a developmental phase on wet soils. <i>(1951,Grasslands of NZ,87,1970 ed.</i>)

>  ~ A name applied to different trees in Australia and New Zealand. <i>(1958,Whitcombe's Modern Junior Dictionary (8th edn),234,8th ed.</i>)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> Lancewood .. .. horoeka .. ..<i> Pseudopanax crassifolium </i> <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/705</i>)

>  <i> lancewood n.</i> a New Zealand tree with slender leaves showing different configurations in youth and maturity. <i>(1982,Collins Concise English Dictionary,630</i>) [NOTES: [1986 NZPOD 419; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 276]]

>  <b>WILSON</b> <i> Pseudopanax sp </i>. aff. <i> colensoi </i> FIVEFINGER ... Threefinger ... Orihou ... Shrub or small, rounded tree. Leaves ...with 3-7 unstalked `fingers' (mostly 5) ... <i> Pseudopanax crassifolius </i> LANCEWOOD ... Horoeka ... Grass tree (local name for juveniles) <i>(1982,Stewart Island Plants,82</i>)

>  Lithograph of lancewood (horoeka) foliage. <i>(1890,PWD Catalogue of Timbers,21, 322</i>)

>  The pseudopanax species, which include lancewoods, attract a variety of birds. <i>(Nov 5, 1999,Dominion,12</i>)

>  <b>HAWES</b> When he stirred the jars with the worn old lancewood pole they made a warm 'scrump' noise that you could feel inside your hands. <i>(2000,Dream of Nikau Jam, The,12</i>)

>  The woods used are totara burr, wairangi, kauri, lancewood, puriri and kohutuhutu. <i>(Apr 23, 1887,Auckland Weekly News,18</i>)

>  <b>BARKER</b> I can give you no idea of the variety among the shrubs: the koromika, like an Alpine rose, a compact ball of foliage; the lancewood, a tall, slender stem, ... with a few long leaves at the top, turned downwards like the barb of a spear, and looking exactly like a lance stuck into the ground; the varieties of matapo ... each leaf a study, with its beautiful tracery of black veins on a yellow-green ground; the mappo, the gohi, and many others ... `Lance-wood', a tall slender stem, straight as a line, with a few long leaves at the top, looking exactly like a lance stuck in the ground. <i>(1866,Station Life in NZ,94,1870 ed.</i>)

>  Ivyworts... include the lancewood, cultivated in many New Zealand gardens, and five-fingers, a small tree, particularly plentiful on the West Coast of the South Island, where cattle prefer it before any other plant in the forest. <i>(Oct 5, 1922,Auckland Weekly News,17</i>)

>  <b>STEWART</b> She picked up Miss Hooper's venomous lancewood cane. <i>(1983,Springtime in Taranaki,38</i>) [NOTES: 1991 edition]



 
<b>3</b> <i>n.</i> tingahere, cabbage-tree, prob. in error or confusion with grass-tree name or poss. shape.

>  <b>HAY</b> The Tingahere, or `lancewood' (<i> Cordyline stricta </i>), is another species of the same family. It is of very singular appearance, its head resembling a tuft of copper-coloured hair. <i>(1882,Brighter Britain,2/192</i>)

 <i>n.</i>

## land agent
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (historical) one who seeks to buy Maori land, = (Crown) agent.

>  The Government land agent had been here during my absence trying to convince them that it would be greatly to their advantage [11] to sell. <i>(Mar 17, 1851,Pioneer Missionary Among the Maoris, A</i>) [NOTES: T.S.Grace Journal in Grace Pioneer Missionary (1928) 10-11]

>  The war at Taranaki commenced by a land agent inducing a Chief to cut a boundary line (for the purpose of sale) through land of which he was, at most, a part owner only! <i>(Mar 24, 1858,Pioneer Missionary Among the Maoris, A,77</i>) [NOTES: T.S.Grace letter in Grace Pioneer Missionary (1928)]



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> [OED 12. also agent for the sale of land, or estate agent; W3 precisely the NZ sense.]

>  <b>50</b> [NZ sense sells houses etc.] <i>(1959,Gun in My Hand, A</i>)

>  <b>GRIFFITHS</b> He is carrier, ...gold buyer, slaughterman, ... land agent, station-holder, ... etc. etc. <i>(1971,King Wakatip,83</i>)

## land boom
 
 <i>n.</i> [AND 1827]



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) inflated land prices due to competitive land-seeking.

>  NZ Farmer April 345 I have seen land booms collapse just at a time when the collapse was hardest to bear. The number of those who have been ruined by land booms is painfully heavy. <i>(Mar 1908,NZ Farmer,345</i>)

>  In last month's number you infer that a land boom is setting in in Taranaki. Such is not the case. The land boom passed away away four or five years ago. <i>(Aug 1888,NZ Farmer,291</i>)

>  Land booms are fatal to farming prosperity ... <i>(Jan 1893,NZ Farmer,12</i>)

## land claim
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>MANSON</b> ... there is much distress in the Colony on account of non-settlement of land claims and a great deal through drink. <i>(1971,I Take Up My Pen,25</i>) [NOTES: 1842 S. Retter in 1971 Manson I Take Up My Pen]

>  <b>HOCKEN</b> In a turbary deposit by the river's mouth is the spot where moa bones were discovered [c1849] by Dr.Mackellar, Mr.Percy Earle, and Mr.Walter Mantell. Perfect specimens were sent home by the latter ... to his father, ... in 1849, whilst engaged as commissioner for the settlement of native land claims. <i>(1898,Contributions to the Early History of New Zealand,45</i>)

>  <b>REED</b> He had further aroused the hostility of the leaders of the settlement by his decisions on certain complicated `pre-Adamite' Banks Peninsula land claims, and by his support of Sir George Grey's cheap land policy. <i>(1947,Story of Canterbury, The,183</i>)



## Land Commissioner
 
 <i>n.</i> (historical)

>  <b>TUCKETT</b> There are nearly twenty other Europeans residing here [at Molyneux], most of whom have enclosures of cultivated land. ... If any claim has been advanced by any of these squatters, none has been approved by the Land Commissioners; yet they will consider themselves aggrieved if ejected from their dwellings without compensation; ... <i>(Apr 28, 1844,Diary</i>) [NOTES: 1844 Apr 28 Tuckett Diary in 1898 Hocken Contributions 213]

>  He had also the opportunity of personal communication with Mr. Spain upon the duties of a Land Commissioner; ... <i>(1949,Selected Documents,Vol. LXXXI col. 947 14A page 2</i>) [NOTES: Parliamentary Debates (G.B.) in 1949 Rutherford ed. Select Documents]
