## Land Court
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [Native Land Court](../N#native-land-court))

>  Riki Tatahunga, better known as ~King Dick', died recently at Tauranga. ... his illness was ascribed to witchcraft, brought on because he appeared as advocate against his own tribe in a Land Court case. <i>(1905,TrNZI,37/35</i>)

>  Judge Johnson opened the Land Court. He examined Rereti and Ngatau his mother. <i>(Mar 6, 1907,Chudleigh Diary,439,1950 ed.</i>)

>  <b>MULGAN</b> There were always a few natives about. ... Some of them were in town on land court business. <i>(1934,Spur of Morning,44</i>)

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> The war having broken the Maoris' will to resist, the Land Courts then quietly separated them from their lands. The function of the courts was to ascertain Maori title and to issue a Crown freehold title to the owners who, now that Crown pre-emption was abolished, were free to sell either to settlers or to the Government. <i>(1959,History of New Zealand,143</i>)

>  <b>STEAD</b> <i>(1960,Short Stories (various authors),248</i>) [NOTES: Hilliard in C.K. Stead Short Stories]

>  <b>IHIMAERA & LONG (ed.)</b> A Judge of the Land Court / By your so-called democratic policies / Claimed for himself our land and wealth. <i>(1982,Into the World of Light,89</i>)

>  <b>BAUCKE</b> At a sitting of the Land Court, Puke was awarded 1000 acres. <i>(1905,Where the White Man Treads,242</i>)

>  <b>STEAD</b> ?? <i>(1966,Short Stories (various authors),248</i>)

>  'Well... we have to write to the Land Court, find out.'<i>Ibid.</i> 14 'When we get home, you write to the Land Court, find out about that land.'<i>Ibid.</i> 19 'Letter today from the Land Court.' <i>Ibid.</i> 21 'Yes, that's the Land Court writings on the outside'. <i>(1960,Mate,5/13</i>)

>  It is a little noisy when the Maoris collect here to a Land Court. <i>(Feb 26, 1887,Auckland Weekly News,8</i>)

>  The Land Court which was to be held at Cambridge has been postponed from time to time. <i>(Mar 15, 1879,Auckland Weekly News,Suppl. 2</i>)

>  It is Land Court Day. <i>(Jan 5, 1938,Auckland Weekly News,32</i>)



## land district
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  A major land division of New Zealand used for both survey and land administration purposes. <i>(Oct 1957,NZ Timber Journal,73</i>)



## land fever
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>WAITE</b> This land fever [after 1840] has been described as `sharking' or `grabbing', and `bona fide speculation'. <i>(1940,Port Molyneux,15</i>)



## land girling
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) the working of women on farms during the Second World War.

>  "There are three things synonymous to me with land girling," says Susi Robinson Collins, poet and writer, who worked on an old-established Canterbury sheep station... Land girling was damned hard work - and went largely unrecognised, then and since. <i>(Apr 9, 1983,Listener (New Zealand Listener),15</i>)



## land grab
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a rush to purchase land.

>  We were decapitalised by the land-grabs that took place. <i>(Aug 17, 1987,Radio NZ / National Radio</i>) [NOTES: National Radio Stephen O'Regan Ngai-Tahu spokesperson]

>  <b>CHAMBERS</b> The urban man has now joined the 'land grab' in much bigger numbers than ever before. <i>(1977,Way of the Farmer, The,21</i>)

>  <b>TEMPLETON</b> In the land grab of the 1850s, the great squatters had taken over the 'waste lands' . <i>(1997,Problematic Journey, The,77</i>)

>  .. I still have a few plain, commonsense ideas about the matter which, I think, if carried out, might help to frustrate the game of land grab at present being carried on. <i>(Jul 27, 1900,Hawera & Normanby Star,4</i>)

>  .. the astounding developments and settlement throughout New Zealand, notwithstanding the heavy handicap of past land-grab and landlord legislation. <i>(Feb 21, 1905,Wanganui Herald,2</i>)

>  This means that he [the Premier] holds himself at liberty to acquire, at his own price,all the land worth having that yet remains to the Maori people, and if he pursues the policy of 'land grab' which has marked his whole tenure of office ... <i>(Nov 3, 1898,Evening Post,4</i>)



## land grabber
 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural, historical) a speculator who squatted on and/or purchased large tracts of land [often by spotting or gridironing].

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> Long after the time of the `land-grabbers' of the 'thirties and 'forties of last century, we have pioneer documents giving Maori names. <i>(1944,Maori Place-Names of Otago,73</i>)

>  <b>MILLER</b> The Orbells have been referred to as land-grabbers. It is a description that is as unfair as it is inaccurate. The land-grabbers were the men who acquired large holdings by stealth and cunning. <i>(1958,Sturdy Sons,55</i>)

>  <b>MCLAUCHLAN</b> Stories of .. people facing rogue maori bands lashing back at lang-grabbers usually turn accounts of what must have been great courage and self-sufficiency into melodramas. <i>(1981,Farming of NZ,99</i>)

>  There are many thousands of acres still in the grip of the land-grabber. <i>(Dec 1, 1917,NZ Truth,3</i>)

>  .. I helped in a small way to prevent the tools of land-sharks and land-grabbers from obtaining power in this Dominion for some time ... <i>(Sep 13, 1909,Wanganui Herald,2</i>)

>  Nor is he a budding land grabber, wanting to put high country farmers out of business. <i>(Aug 7, 2006,Otago Daily Times,6</i>)

>  Working men look and study the above tyranny and don't vote for land grabbers. <i>(Nov 27, 1893,Taranaki Herald,2</i>)



## land holder
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> In Taranaki and on the East Coast there were sporadic Maori feuds, during the fifties, between the land sellers and the `land holders'; but it seems probable that the great majority of Maoris, south of Auckland, sympathized with the latter party. <i>(1959,History of New Zealand,113</i>)



## land hunger
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a desire to obtain and farm land [particularly late 1800s-early1900s].

>  The land hunger is pretty keen here (says a Waitara correspondent of the Taranaki Herald) ... <i>(May 29, 1909,4</i>)

>  <b>WILSON & FRASER</b> There were something like 110 sections of an average size, which works out at 180 acres each, to offer to those who suffered from the prevalent land hunger. <i>(1936,Book of the Pioneers, The,9</i>)

>  <b>BROWN</b> But there was a land hunger [in the 1890s] and forest clearance could not await the prior milling of timber. <i>(1966,Rural Land Administration in NZ,62</i>)

>  Land hunger: the land fever is now raging at full height here: about every second or third place is either sold or for sale. <i>(Oct 1902,NZ Farmer,564</i>)

>  Land hunger: There is a young man in Hastings who has applied for sections of land at nearly every land ballot held in the North Island during the last two years. <i>(Feb 1906,NZ Farmer,125</i>)

>  The land hunger is stretching itself far and wide ... <i>(Apr 1908,NZ Farmer,295</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> And nearly all the land-hungry bought more of these acres than their capital warranted; as a result there were many failures; but land-hunger still increased. <i>(1955,Land of My Children,3</i>)

>  <b>PASCOE</b> He [Colenso] was needed also to uphold their cause against the land hunger of settlers. <i>(1959,Great Days in NZ Exploration,39</i>)

>  <b>SHADBOLT</b> And though European greed and land hunger was eventually to make a cruel mockery of the [Treaty of Waitangi] document, a certain amount of its original spirit survived and still survives. [land-hunger; earth hunger] <i>(1976,Shell Guide to NZ,29</i>)



## land incorporation
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Representatives of Maori land incorporations which have land leased to the forest service are understood to be studying their options on land that is to be transferred ... on April 1. ... But the incorporations are now understood to be reviewing the changeover and looking at newoptions for their land. <i>(Jan 30, 1987,National Business Review,1</i>)



## land jobbing
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a corrupt gaining of land by early European settlers.

>  <b>SEWELL</b> All the valuable spots will be jobbed away. River banks will be seized on. In short all the Land-jobbing vultures will be down upon their prey. <i>(Mar 8, 1853,Journal,184</i>) [NOTES: (1980)]

>  <b>PORTER</b> The following year J D Lang's book appeared in which he accused the CMS missionaries of extensive land jobbing. [land-jobbing] <i>(1974,Turanga Journals,146</i>)



## land law
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>WALLACE</b> <i>(1891,Rural Economy of Aust. & NZ,229</i>)



## land league
 
 <i>n.</i> (historical)

>  Of late there has been formed a League amongst various of the tribes on Cook's Straits for resisting further alienations of land to the Europeans ... The Church of England missionaries labour under a strong suspicion of fostering and even originating this Land League ... People feel certain that [Bishop Selwyn] will do his best to patch up a hollow truce and to shield the murderous Land-Leaguers. <i>(1855,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,1/177</i>) [NOTES: C.W. Richmond]

>  <b>THOMSON</b> To keep this spirit alive, an Anti-landselling League was formed among some of the Taranaki tribes. ... One tribe, not belonging to this League, was induced ... to offer a quantity of land for sale in 1854 ... <i>Ibid.</i> 2/227 SETTLER OPPOSE LAND-LEAGUE [heading] <i>(1859,Story of New Zealand, The,2/225,1974 ed.</i>)

>  Land Leagues are the reasonable protest of an unrepresented majority agaist an aggressive minority. <i>(May 8, 1860,Selected Documents,1949 ed.</i>) [NOTES: G.A. Selwyn Memorandum to Governor Gore Browne]

>  <b>BUDDLE</b> About the same time [c1852], and connected with the subject, arose the Taranaki land league. <i>Ibid.</i> 15 The objection ... arose from the foolish idea that is entertained by the Maories that opening roads will certainly lead to the alienation of their lands; as their main object clearly was the formation of a land league to prevent all further sales, ... <i>(1860,Maori King Movement,4</i>)

>  <b>GORST</b> If we had educated the natives in civilisation ... as British subjects ... nothing would have been heard of `land-leagues' and `king-movements'. <i>(1864,Maori King, The,39</i>)

>  <b>MACDONALD</b> The `Maori Land League' and the `Maori King Movement' were growing into consistency and power. <i>(1910,Reminiscences,65</i>) [NOTES: (TS:VUW)]

>  <b>LANCASTER</b> The Taranaki Maoris were likely to require some facing presently, having established their own Land League ... <i>(1938,Promenade,194</i>)

>  <b>COWAN</b> This growing feeling culminated in 1852 in the formation of a confederation of tribes which came to be called the Land League, binding themselves, so far as Taranaki was concerned, to put a stop to all sales of land northward of the Bell Block ... <i>(1940,Sir Donald Maclean,11</i>)

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> In later years the settlers came to believe that a conspiracy was hatched against them at Manawapou; that the Maoris formed a `land-league ... a war league, a league of blood anddeath'. In fact, according to a missionary and a government land purchase agent, who had early news of the meeting, no agreement was made. <i>(1959,History of New Zealand,109</i>)

>  <b>McNEISH</b> A month before, Matthew Whitehouse, the Superintendent, had told Polson in a letter that the natives of the north were forming land leagues; in Taranaki they were arming themselves and preparing to drive the settlers from their farms. <i>(1970,Mackenzie,20</i>)



## land maggot
 
 <i>n.</i> sheep [used also in limited use in Aust., USA, UK]

>  Sheep are banned. "We call them land maggots," the Marshalls say. <i>(Jan 12, 2006,NZ Herald,A2</i>)

 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>KING</b> [Hdg] Maori Land March <i>(1983,Whina,206</i>)

>  <b>KING</b> [After the meal I [Whina, at a meeting in1975 at Te Puea Marae] said, "Why don'twe march to Parliament... a sacred march- for a sacred purpose, to hold on to ourlands?"] <i>(1983,Whina,207</i>) [NOTES: Quote from 1975]

They passed in marching order,appearing to mimic the soldiers. Theywere a very ragged mob, and caused greatlaughter. They were supposed to be goingto a feast at Waianga.A further notice appeared in the 1863 NZ Advertiser 29 Aug. 2It may be true that not more than forty-two armed natives passed through Greytownlast Monday, of whom not more than three-fourths were armed with guns, but was notsuch an exhibition calculated to alarm heisolated settlers, and to prevent themfrom carrying on their usual avocationsin a quiet manner.]

>  The fishing nets will go out at Te Hapuatonight to feed the people who willgather at the township over the weekendbefore setting out on the 700-mile Maoriland march to Wellington on Sunday. <i>(Sep 12, 1975,NZ Herald,Sect. 1, p.3</i>)

>  Whatever people might say in retrospectabout the 1975 Maori land march whichWhina Cooper led, it was still the mostdramatic gesture of the Maori protestseen. <i>(Nov 23, 1983,Evening Post,38</i>)

>  Maori people need to cooperate more ifthey are to consolidate the gains of theMaori land march, Dame Whina Cooper says. <i>(Sep 16, 1987,Nelson Evening Mail,6</i>)

>  Predictably the timing and implicationsof the great Maori Land March were notappreciated by the Minister of MaoriAffairs. <i>(Nov 22, 1975,Listener (New Zealand Listener),00</i>) [NOTES: Check page]

>  [The armed Maoris that went to theWaihanga runanga returned yesterday inbroken order. About 30 in number passedthrough Carterton, a portion took thecoast, Teretahi direction, and theremainder proceeded towards Masterton;their bearing was civil, and.. subdued.] <i>(Sep 8, 1863,NZ Independent,2</i>)

>  [The armed Maoris that went to theWaihanga runanga returned yesterday inbroken order. About 30 in number passedthrough Carterton, a portion took thecoast, Teretahi direction, and theremainder proceeded towards Masterton;their bearing was civil, and.. subdued.] <i>(Sep 8, 1863,NZ Independent,2</i>)

After theSuperintendent had with some hesitationgiven the permission asked.. Matiaha andhis friend readily accepted his Honor'sinvitation to lunch. <i>(Sep 10, 1863,NZ Independent,7</i>)

## land of ferns
 
 <i>n.</i> New Zealand (See [Fernland](../F#fernland))

>  <b>YATE</b> One of the most peculiar features in the character of this country is the fern, which everywhere flourishes most luxuriantly ...[note] 57 species ... have been discovered <i>(1835,New Zealand (various authors/titles),15</i>) [NOTES: Griffiths Collection]

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> Though there are no other varieties which cover so wide an area, yet the ferns form such a prominent feature in the Flora, that New Zealand is often termed `The Land of Ferns,' and a fern frond has been taken as its emblem. <i>(1907,Plants of NZ,7,1940, P. 7 ed.</i>)

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> The land of the `Maori and Moa,' as a poet has called our land, has long been famous from both the ethnological and zoological standpoints. ... But when it comes to the question of the plant-life there is a pause. To be sure, New Zealand is known as the land of ferns, and not without truth; yet this admired group is found nearly all the world over, and is really much less important than are plenty of the other indigenous plants. <i>(1910,NZ Plants,1</i>)



## Land of Sacks
 
 <i>n.</i> New Zealand [Used by Dalmatian immigrants]

>  <b>BATISTICH</b> Sack hut, sack bed, even your patches were sack. No wonder that those who fled home as soon as they could spread the story that New Zealand was The Land of Sacks. <i>(1987,Sing Vila in the Mountain,105</i>)

## Land of the Long White Cloud
 
 <i>n.</i> New Zealand

>  You, too, in the Land of the Long White Cloud, will have a thought for us ... <i>(1917,New Zealand At the Front,54</i>)

>  ... a memento ... treasured, by relatives and friends in `The Land of the Long White Cloud'. <i>(1918,New Zealand At the Front,xi</i>)

>  <b>HYDE</b> Land was in sight, just a faint blue line ..., above it floating the band of silver which made the old Maori canoe-explorers call New Zealand Ao-Te-Aroa - Land of the Long White Cloud. <i>(1936,Passport to Hell,286</i>)

>  <b>LANCASTER</b> `E-a! My country! Ao-tea-roa ... [sic] Land of the Long White Cloud. The pakeha god is stealing from you all your own old gods....' <i>(1938,Promenade,64</i>)

>  A hundred and fifty miles were certainly no hardship to those [Maori] lads, and at the finish [of the service-car journey] it was the others who were staring gaunt ... upon the land of the long white cloud. <i>(1950,Listener Short Stories,23</i>) [NOTES: R.A.Copland]

>  <b>GOLDBLATT</b> The milk bars, the rendezvous of the `Land of the Long White Cloud', are interspersed and barnacled to the Picture House. <i>(1957,Democracy at Ease,12</i>)

>  land of the Long White Cloud [transl. of M AOTEAROA] New Zealand. <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,419</i>) [NOTES: nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict]

>  <b>OVENDEN</b> Then it's no trouble for Intelligence to ... push him off to anew life, lost in the land of the long white cloud. <i>(1986,O.E.,67</i>)

>  <b>JACKSON</b> In due course more men arrived from the Land of the Long White Cloud. <i>(1943,Passage to Tobruk,23</i>)

>  <i>Land of the Long White Cloud</i> with or without the initial capitals) has itself become a familiar variant of the country's name, sometimes appearing as <i>Land of the Great White Shroud</i>, or in shortened form simply as <i>the Long White Cloud. DNZE</i> also records the ironic, punning deflations of this romantic image that have been coined: <i>(Land of) the Long White Shroud</i> (from the 1940s on) and (more recently) <i>Land of the Wrong White Crowds</i> (a 1980s graffito and <i>Land of the Long Black Cloud</i>. <i>(Oct 2000,NZWords,4 : 2</i>)

>  In the Land of the Long White Cloud thou shalt study the Dole. <i>(Dec 21, 1942,NZEF Times,II/78 : 11</i>)

>  Now, 40 years away from my homeland, I call it the Land of the Long White Lie. <i>(Feb 28, 1987,Listener (New Zealand Listener),17</i>)

>  The cigarette smoke haze is now gradually lifting from the Land of the Long White Cloud. <i>(Jun 1999,Metro,13</i>)

>  Something which makes me feel very New Zealand is when I'm overseas and there's a Maori concert party performing songs about the arrival in the land of the long white cloud. <i>(Jan 1992,North & South,83</i>)

>  Right now the land of the long white cloud is broke and the people are bitter. <i>(Aug 5, 1968,Evening Post,13</i>)

>  ...the selling of the country's natural wonder off the back of a fantasy film - land of the long white digitally enhanced cloud, free-market scenery capital of the world. <i>(Jan 25, 2003,Listener (New Zealand Listener),4</i>)

## Land Office
 
 <i>n.</i> In most colonies there are land-offices, in which the sales of new land are registered, and warrants issued for the location of land, and other business respecting unsettled land is transacted.

>  I slept in Cass's bed at the Land Office, he having been unable to return from Lyttelton. <i>(Mar 13, 1851,Torlesse Papers,206,1958 ed.</i>)

>  The Land Office is a remarkably neat and I should think commodious building ... the upper part used as a Police Court. <i>(Feb 4, 1853,Journal of Henry Sewell,1/126,1980 ed.</i>)

>  <b>MACKIE</b> Finding that most of the religious denominations have had grants from government of pieces of land - ... we went to the land office to make some enquiry on the subject but being the day called `Good Friday', the offices were closed. <i>(Mar 25, 1853,Traveller under Concern,93,1973 ed.</i>)

>  <b>HART</b> At the Land Office [in Chch in 1852], which was really the Government Office, all the settlers were congregated ... <i>(1886,Stray Leaves,29</i>)

>  The Commissioner's office is known as the principal land office, and in some of the larger districts there are one or more local offices. It is with these land offices the selector has to transact all business, ... <i>(1902,Settler's Handbook of NZ, The,63</i>)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> Some of the Central runs had been applied for at the Dunedin land Office late in 1857 and others during 1858. <i>(1939,First White Boy Born in Otago,118</i>)

>  <b>ACLAND</b> <i>(1930,Early Country Runholders,11,4</i>)

>  <b>McNEISH</b> It was only a race with a missionary to reach the Land Office. <i>(1970,Mackenzie,16</i>)

>  <b>ACLAND</b> This completes all the Class III runs.. except one which I once saw listed in an old manuscript book that used to be among the Land Office records. <i>(1930,Early Canterbury Runs,349</i>) [NOTES: Pagination from 1946 edition]

>  A man applied at the Land Office for a likely piece of country which he had seen on his travels... If no one else established a prior claim the Waste Lands Board gave him the run <i>(1930,Early Canterbury Runs,13</i>) [NOTES: Pagination from 1946 edition]



## Land Purchase Commissioner
 
 <i>n.</i>

## land question
 
 <i>n.</i> (historical)

>  <b>SELWYN</b> When ... the events at the Wairau, and the Land Question in general had made a breach between the two races; then the doors began to be shut in the faces of the natives, and language peculiarlyoffensive to them was in common use among the lower classes: ... <i>(Jun 23, 1848,New Zealand (Selwyn),Part V (Church in the Colonies XX) 38</i>)

>  The next step of Sir George was to place the `land question' on a satisfactory footing, then no easy job. <i>(1852,Saunders' Monthly Magazine</i>) [NOTES: Vol I in 1976 Canterbury Colony 20]

>  <b>BUDDLE</b> Second - The land Question. To prevent further alienation of Native lands is the great object of the league, and on this point the kingites carry with them the sympathies of the majority. <i>(1860,Maori King Movement,60</i>)

>  <b>SWAINSON</b> The native land question remains to be considered, and on its satisfactory solution the ultimatefate of the Maori race ... materially depends. <i>(May 7, 1860,Selected Documents,108</i>)

>  <b>GRACE</b> Then followed a long discussion on the land question, all the arguments in common use amongst the Maoris being [144] brought forth. <i>(Mar 5, 1865,Pioneer Missionary Among the Maoris, A,143,1928 ed.</i>) [NOTES: T.S.Grace Journal in Grace Pioneer Missionary (1928)]

>  <b>BAINES</b> Ah! `that land question' was always a source of trouble in New Zealand, like anything else when the ownership is uncertain. <i>(1874,Edward Crewe,77</i>)

>  <b>SENIOR</b> Old colonists in New Zealand ... are divided amongst themselves upon the land question ... <i>(1880,Travel & Trout,236</i>)

>  ... a struggle for control of the land, and the land question involved the native question and the system of precarious military alliances establihsed by Fitzroy. <i>(1958,Early Victorian NZ,149</i>)

>  <b>HAWTHORNE</b> Most of the natives were Hauhaus of 1865, who had become once more exceedingly disaffected, owing to the non-solution of the land question. <i>(1869,Dark Chapter from NZ History,14</i>)



## land rail
 
 <i>n.</i> [aka mohopatatai; moho-pereru; banded rail; usually but not exclusively <i>Rallus philippensis assimilis</i>, or <i>Rallus assimilis</i>]

>  The landrail also is another example of a slow flying bird with considerable expanse of wing for its weight, but with short primaries. <i>(Aug 19, 1872,Internet - see Notes</i>) [NOTES: http://rsnz.natlib.govt.nz/volume/rsnz_05/rsnz_05_00_000730.html Transactions and Proceedings of the Royal Society of New Zealand ART. XVI ..By CAPTAIN F. W. HUTTON, C.M.Z.S. page 143[Read before the Auckland Institute, 19th August, 1872.]]

>  The following are proposed to be added to the list of birds to be protected:- Kaka, native thrush, fantail, wren, tomtit, robin, fernbird, cuckoo, paradise duck, blue heron, kingfisher, oyster-catcher, redbill, landrail and swamprail. <i>(Sep 23, 1905,Southland Times,3</i>)

>  Our representative of the restricted genus <i>Rallus (R. assimilis)</i> resembles closely an Australian species, but is distinguishable [18] by the pectoral band and rufous colouring of the head and neck being less prominent. This is the Land Rail' of the colonists. <i>(1869,TrNZI,1/III/17-18</i>) [NOTES: (Buller Ornithology)]

>  RALLUS PHILIPPENSIS. (BANDED RAIL.) ... <i>Native names</i>. Patatai, Popotai, Mohotai, Moho-patatai, Moho-pereru, and Puohotata; `Land-Rail' of the colonists. <i>(1888,Birds of New Zealand,2/95</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i>Pepe ... Rallus philippensis assimilis, banded or land rail. <i>Ibid.</i> 311 Pu[u]ohotata, Rallus philippensis, banded or land rail, a bird.... Pu[u]ohotata ... Rallus philipensis assimilis, banded or land rail; a bird. <i>Ibid.</i> 322 Puohotata, ... Hypotaenidia philippensis; land rail, a bird. </i> <i>(1892,Dictionary of the NZ Language,277</i>)

>  <b>FALLA et al.</b> BANDED RAIL <i>Rallus philippensis Other names:</i> Landrail, Striped Rail, Mohopereru, Mioweka. (The old sealers' name for the Macquarie Island race was Wigeon!) <i>(1966,Birds of New Zealand,104</i>)

## land ring
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  The proposal for the disposal of the Maori lands.. would play into the hands of the land rings... Sir George Grey's statements and accusations about the Canterbury land ring were a fallacy. <i>(Oct 25, 1879,Auckland Weekly News,16</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a group of early land speculators.

>  When land rings got large tracts of the country into their hands, settlers, when they wanted to buy it, had to pay a very stiff price ... <i>(Aug 1884,Northern New Zealand Settler,245</i>)



## land sales
 
 <i>n.</i> (historical) with reference to the Land Sales Act of WW2.

## land scrip
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a document providing land rights to settlers arriving with the NZ Company prior to 1850.

>  <b>MORRELL</b> To compensate its settlers for the trials and disappointments they had endured, it [the NZ Co.] gave them the right to select more land. These rights were in the form of documents called 'land scrip'. <i>(1951,Short History of New Zealand Life, A,245</i>)

 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> In Taranaki and on the East Coast there were sporadic Maori feuds, during the fifties, between the land sellers and the `land holders'; but it seems probable that the great majority of Maoris, south of Auckland, sympathized with the latter party. <i>(1959,History of New Zealand,113</i>)



## land settlement
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Until statesmen take the place of politicians, this sort of thing is likely to continue and land settlement is as far off as ever. <i>(Jun 19, 1930,NZ Truth,6</i>)

>  I claim that what is wanted to relieve the present stagnation is progressive land settlement. To get the people back on to the land should be the chief aim of the Government. <i>(Aug 7, 1930,Evening Post,6</i>)

>  REHABILITATION .. Some 16,000 men had been demobilised in recent months, and only 25 of them had been placed on the land. Something should be done to hurry things along. New Zealand had not for many years had a Minister of Lands who understood land settlement, and possibly that was why there was not one word in the Budget concerning land settlement. <i>(Jun 17, 1943,Evening Post,4</i>)

>  Mr. Massey said: - "The Dominion may rest assured that the policy of the new Ministry will be both liberal and progressive in the highest and best sense of the terms. We intend to push land settlement for all we are worth. ..." <i>(May 11, 1925,Evening Post,9</i>)

>  Not the slightest attempt has ever been made to bring into existence a decent land settlement scheme and to bring to the Dominion the type who would settle in the country. <i>(Oct 20, 1927,NZ Truth,4</i>)



## Land Settlement Scheme
 
 <i>n.</i> (historical) an 1890s scheme to induce people to break in bushland

>  RETURNED SOLDIERS .. Establishing of Businesses. -The association asks that opportunity be given to suitable returned soldiers to establish themselves in business. In this, as in all other schemes, the keynote is the suitability of the individual. It is considered that this is the natural complement of the land settlement scheme at present in operation. <i>(Sep 3, 1917,Evening Post,2</i>)

>  It is obviously ridiculous to say; that in a country like New Zealand, which is producing an increasing volume of wealth, each year, a definite and comprehensive land settlement scheme is out of the question. <i>(Mar 8, 1928,NZ Truth,6</i>)

>  The chief causes of discontent have been the failure to inaugurate the land settlement scheme on which the Party pinned its faith to solve the unemployment problem, the futility of purchasing high-priced estates to cut them up at uneconomic figures, and the uninspiring compendium of previously existing legislation placed by the Government on the Statute Book last session in the name of a Land Settlement Act. <i>(Mar 13, 1930,NZ Truth,1</i>)

>  The government-sponsored scheme was the 'brainchild' of Minister Sir William Hall-Jones and became know[n] as 'The Land Settlement Scheme'. <i>(Dec 2001,NZ Memories,No. 27/38</i>)

>  Practical Experts Must Administer Land Settlement Scheme. <i>(Nov 7, 1929,NZ Truth,6</i>)

>  If the land settlement scheme were going to be a success, as he hoped it would, roads were the primary thing to be considered in the settlement of the country. <i>(Nov 1, 1901,Poverty Bay Herald,2</i>)

>  Prior to the last election Mr. D. V. Bryant, who inaugurated the Waikato land settlement scheme, discussed with members of the Government the tenure that should be offered to soldier settlers. <i>(May 2, 1944,Evening Post,4</i>)

>  THE GOVERNMENT'S LAND SETTLEMENT SCHEME: THE PRINCIPLE INVOLVED. A LECTURESHIP PROPOSAL. AUCKLAND, March 9. In addressing a deputation which waited upon him to bring forward the matter of acquiring a block of land at Raglan for special settlement purposes, the Hon Mr Ballance gave some important information regarding the Government proposals for the acquisition of private lands for settlement. <i>(Mar 9, 1887,Star, The,3</i>)

>  A deputation from the unemployed waited upon the Hon Mr Seddon and asked for relief works until the land settlement scheme were in force. <i>(Apr 8, 1891,Grey River Argus,4</i>)



## land shark
 
 <i>n.</i> an unscrupulous publican (here, connotes kg. `a shark who lives on land' rather than n1. `a shark which swallows land voraciously'); unscrupulous business person, money-grubber (?the orig. of the main NZ use [[AND 1836 or ? n1..]]

>  <b>BATHGATE</b> The way some of these land-sharks [sc. publicans] will keep an unfortunate man in a state of intoxication for weeks, so long as he has any money .... then when his money is done they will kick him out remorselessly. <i>(1881,Waitaruna,271</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) [used elsewhere but sign.in NZ] a person who exploited others in the rush to claim land [mid-late 1800s.]

>  As an interesting and comforting fact to Pakeha-Maoris, land sharks, and all that class of gentry who look upon the Maori as a natural prey designed for their especial benefit by a beneficent Providence ... <i>(Aug 25, 1880,Hawera Star,2</i>)

>  <b>MEIKLE in GIFKIN</b> Land agents who were also house agents (always called 'landsharks' by my parents.. ) varied. <i>(1988,Through the Looking Glass,127</i>)

>  <b>THOMSON</b> Land was to the early settlers in New Zealand what gold was to the Spaniards in Mexico. Out of this scramble sprang up the 'land-sharks,' a class of men who bought land from savages, and took advantage of their childish ignorance regarding its value. <i>(1859,Story of New Zealand, The,II 31</i>)

>  <b>PRATT</b> This man had a morbid dislike to the resident land purchasers. Whom he was in the habit of designating 'land sharks' ... <i>(1877,Colonial Experiences,26</i>)

>  Why do not the Government put land into the market to satisfy somewhat the great craving for cheap land, instead of consigning it into the capacious maw of professional land-sharks? <i>(Jan 20, 1893,Egmont Star,2</i>)

>  Like all liberal provisions, however, they soon opened the way to land sharks who simply dummied the sections until they could find a purchaser. [land-shark] <i>(Dec 6, 1904,NZ Farmer,1006</i>)

>  Although the result of the Tauranga election is not by any means a surprise, the return of Mr John Sheehan is not one of those events which was very desirable. First and foremost, Mr Sheehan is an agent for land-sharks. <i>(May 26, 1885,Wairarapa Daily,2</i>)

>  Sir George Grey talks about the land-sharks of the North, and they believe him in the South. <i>(Mar 15, 1879,Auckland Weekly News,Suppl. 2</i>)

>  He bitterly assailed the land-sharks of early colonial days. <i>(May 24, 1879,Auckland Weekly News,17</i>)

>  While the land-shark fattens on the soldiers' blood, the soldiers' children starve. <i>(Jan 1, 1887,Auckland Weekly News,8</i>)

>  He [the Maori] will in no long time be thoroughly exploited by the Government land shark, the legal shark, and the native land agent, and when he has squandered the samll cash remainder of value that will be considered due to him, he will become by swift descent a hopeless and helpless dependent upon the charity of the State. <i>(Nov 3, 1898,Evening Post,4</i>)

## land the mustard
 
 <i>phr.</i>

>  As the ex-leader of the Liberal party, he must know the strength of that party, and he must be aware of the dangers which threaten, and the possibilities of the breach being widened should `Moses' Massey `land the mustard'. <i>(Mar 30, 1912,NZ Truth,353/1/3</i>)



## Land Transfer Office
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  We talk of the title or certificate of title, in the concrete sense of a document which is a copy of the registry entry in the Land Transfer Office. <i>(Jan 1999,NZWords,1</i>)



## land transfer system
 
 <i>n.</i> a system introduced in 1870 whereby title to land, and to interests in land, is conferred by registration in a public register, guaranteed by the state and evidenced by a copy of the register entry known as a of <i>certificate of title</i>. [Jim Cameron writes : This soon became the predominant form of land title, and since about 1950 almost the sole form, replacing the former <i>deeds system</i> introduced in 1842. The land transfer system was based on the <i>Torrens system</i> first introduced in South Australia in 1858. That term (which NSOD records) is occasionally used in NZ as a synonym. In England <i>registered land</i> has broadly the same status.]

>  The land transfer system and land transfer title are at the heart of New Zealand conveyancing law. Their social as well as legal importance is considerable, but the terms do not appear in any general dictionary. They are seldom encountered today, simply because the land transfer system has become to all intents and purposes the sole conveyancing system in New Zealand. In Australia the same system is known as the Torrens system after its South Australian originator. <i>(Jan 1999,NZWords,1</i>)



## land transfer title
 
 <i>n.</i> [Jim Cameron writes : Mainly historical (also <i>land transfer land</i>). Before the system became universal, sellers of land might stress that it was held under "land transfer title". Three such advertisements (1903, 1910 and 1926) are reproduced in W G Chapman and K M Wood <i>Karori Streets 1841 to 1991</i>.]



## land use capability classification
 
 <i>phr.</i> [1989 Charles Ransom notes: Land use capability classification - Not found elsewhere and this is likely to be defined in regulations.]

>  Land use capability classification- A systematic arrangement of different kinds of land according to those properties that determine its capacity for permanent and sustained primary production. <i>(1979,SANZ Glossary Building Terminology,68</i>)

 <i>n.</i> (historical)

>  'Court' means the Land Valuation Court. <i>(1953,NZ Statutes,II 94/1084</i>)



## Land Wars
 
 <i>n.</i> = New Zealand Wars sense 1 q.v.

>  "The land wars are over, so the consent of the tangata whenua is required before customary title can be extinguished. Otherwise it is a confiscation, and is likely to breach international law." <i>(Jul 5, 2003,Listener (New Zealand Listener),14</i>)

>  <b>Ihimaera</b> ...by wearing the feathers, Roimata was acknowledging her ancestral links with Parihaka, the village that had been the great site of resistance during the Land Wars. <i>(2000,Uncle's Story, The,324</i>)

>  <b>BAXTER</b> The river... weeping from the hills/ For what was lost in the Land Wars. <i>(1971,Collected Poems,517,1979 ed.</i>)

>  <b>FINLAYSON</b> Kapi is probably referring to the land wars between Maoris and European Troops in the 1860s. <i>(1973,Brown Man's Burden,13</i>) [NOTES: Pearson (ed)]

>  <b>FRY</b> After landwars and terrifying feasts/ the country settled down to farm,/ <i>(1978,Brushed Words,11</i>)

>  They sheltered from possible Maori attack near the church while violent thunderstorms raged....And even the land wars were postponed because of bad weather. <i>(Jan 12, 1983,Dominion,5</i>)

>  Land war cross in auction [headline] A rare New Zealand Cross, awarded for bravery duringthe New Zealand land wars, is the centre-piece of a major auction of coins and medals in Melbourne later this month. The cross, described as a non-military version of the Victoria Cross, is one of only 23 awarded to the militia, volunteers and armed constabulary during the wars, ... <i>(Jul 3, 1984,Dominion,6</i>)

>  <b>BELICH</b> .... the idea that the seizure of Maori land was the main British political and military objective has been so widely adopted that the conflicts are often called `The Land Wars'. <i>(1986,NZ Wars, The,77</i>)

>  <b>IHIMAERA</b> Ah yes, if your grandmother had been in the Land Wars, she would have been in the front line of the attack. <i>(1986,Matriarch, The,25</i>)

>  <b>ROBERTS</b> There were considerable official misgivings ... about land deals between Maoris and pakehas ...; in addition, the internal security of the country was shaken by land wars. <i>(1987,Politicians, Public Servants & Public Enterprise,21</i>)

>  The only time I remember the Oakland protesting was when climbing the Razorback, part of the Pokeno Ranges, a road built during the Land Wars. <i>(Aug 2002,NZ Memories,37/16</i>)

>  A man must have what might be called a 'land-breaking' temperament. <i>(Nov 5, 1953,NZ Farmer,5</i>)

>  <b>YEREX</b> During the 1870s 'land grabbing' was rife and large areas had been locked up in the hands of wealthy individuals ... <i>(1989,Empire of the Dairy Farmers,31</i>)

## land-hungry
 
<b>1</b> <i>adj.</i> (Rural) [of settlers and immigrants] keen to acquire land. [esp. 1860s and 1870s.]

>  <b>WILSON</b> And nearly all the land-hungry bought more of these acres than their capital warranted; as a result there were many failures; but land-hunger still increased. <i>(1955,Land of My Children,3</i>)

>  <b>CAMPBELL</b> Land hungry people turned their attention to European estates in more accessible areas ... <i>(1972,Evolution of Hawke's Bay Landed Society,72</i>)

>  <b>GASCOIGNE</b> There was the growing pressure of land-hungry immigrants, measured in an increasing antagonism to the native, all too evident in colonial press and government decree alike. <i>(1965,From the Top of a Hill,82</i>)

>  <b>STEVENS</b> .. some 48,000 acres of excellent arable and pastoral land became available for the land-hungry group of potential small farmers. <i>(1970,Pyne Gould Guinness: the jubilee history,42</i>)

>  <b>SHADBOLT</b> From land-hungry colonists, confined and frustrated in Auckland, soon came calls for Maori blood ... <i>(1976,Shell Guide to NZ,29</i>)

>  <b>ALLEN</b> His early life in Central Otago had given him a 'yen' for farming and, as our mother said, he had always been land hungry. [land hungry] <i>(1980,Farm on the Wanganui River, A,7</i>)



## land-jobber
 
 <i>n.</i> [[AND 1835.] Although attested in English before, (a 1748 Swift-OED) not necessarily pejoratively, in NZ between 1839 and 1842 it was used almost as a popular (political or journalistic) catchphrase, mainly in a bad sense against the Official Government and Missionary party in Auckland, by the NZ Company settlers and colonists. It took on most of the connotation of land-shark. qv. The Bay of Islands and Auckland saw much early private speculation in land.]

>  Having by these methods obviated the dangers of the acquisition of large tracts of Country by mere Landjobbers, it will be your duty to obtain, by fair and equal contracts with the Natives, theCession to the Crown of such Waste Lands as may be progressively required for the occupation of Settlers resorting to New Zealand. <i>(Aug 14, 1839,Speeches & Documents,13</i>) [NOTES: Instructions from the Secretary of State to Captain Hobson, C.O. 209/4 pp.251-81 in 1971 Speeches and Documents 13]

>  ... the acquisition of large tracts of Country by mere Landjobbers, it will be your duty to obtain, by fair and equal contracts with the Natives, the Cession to the Crown of such Waste Lands as may be progressively required for occupation of Settlers resorting to New Zealand. All such contracts should be made by yourself, through the intervention of an Officer expressly appointed to watch over the interests of the Aborigines as their Protector. <i>(Aug 14, 1839,Speeches & Documents,13,1971 ed.</i>) [NOTES: Speeches and Documents 13 quoting Aug 14 Instructions from the Secretary of State to Captain Hobson.]

>  <b>LANG</b> ... a matter of question as well as of considerable interest among the land jobbers of the Bay of Islands to whom Barrier Island belonged. <i>(1839,New Zealand (various authors/titles),22</i>)

>  By converting the cupidity of land jobbers and squatters into a source of remuneration and profit, the necessity for ... a large and expensive force, would ... soon be diminished. <i>(Jun 28, 1840,GBPP,(No.311) 111,1841 ed.</i>) [NOTES: Thomas Bunbury's Report]

>  .. the industrious colonist ..... is placed in a much worse position than the mere land-jobber. <i>(1842,NZ Gazette,2/79</i>)

>  <b>DIEFFENBACH</b> A few land-jobbers raised the price thus high, having bought the ground in all the best situations. <i>(1843,New Zealand (various authors/titles),1839-41 1/11</i>)

>  <b>TUCKETT</b> Land jobbers at home, I suppose, considered that the culled residue would be anunmarketable commodity; ... <i>(1844,Letter</i>) [NOTES: in 1898 [??] Hocken Contributions 228]



## land-jobbing
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>SEWELL</b> All the valuable spots will be jobbed away. River banks will be seized on. In short all the Land-jobbing Vultures will be down upon their prey ... <i>(1853,Journal of Henry Sewell,I:184,1980 ed.</i>)

>  The gradually increasing demand for land ... cannot for one moment be attributed to speculation, land jobbing with us being an unknown style of business. <i>(Aug 12, 1857,Lyttelton Times,4</i>)

>  <b>THATCHER</b> This wonderful township belongs, you must know, To some grasping land-jobbing faker, And the price that he gave for it some time ago, Was about twenty shillings an acre. <i>(1862,Dunedin Songster,3/10</i>) [NOTES: Little Dunedin in Dunedin Songster]

>  This mania for land-jobbing is by no means confined to Europeans, but has extended to the natives, ... <i>(Feb 20, 1840,GBPP</i>) [NOTES: Hobson to Normanby in GBPP House of Commons 1841 (No.311) 12]

>  After the Office, comes land jobbing, in which I shall be able to do a little with profit from time to time. <i>(1841,Letter</i>) [NOTES: Revans to Chapman [?date and page] (Wellington)]

>  <b>CHAPMAN</b> A considerable quantity of the capital imported into New Zealand from the Australian colonies ... is invested in land, - `speculating in the real estate.' as the Americans call it, or `land jobbing', as its enemies designate it, being a favourite occupation with the Sydney folk. <i>(1843,NZ Portfolio,40</i>)

>  <b>LANG</b> Whether the New Zealanders remain in that unconverted and heathen state, or adopt such a mere nominal profession of Christianity as they are likely to arrive at under the influence of land-jobbing missionaries, the prospect for that unfortunate people is gloomy in the extreme. <i>(1839,New Zealand in 1839,45</i>)



## land-leaguer
 
 <i>n.</i> [1855 see cit. LAND-LEAGUE] (See [also leaguer](../A#also-leaguer))

>  His court of appeal was at first the Land League, after 1860 the King - ... the active interference of the Land-leaguers with the Taranaki land question ... <i>(Mar 27, 1899,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,2/615,1960 ed.</i>) [NOTES: A.S.Atkinson]

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> The Government had come to the conclusion that the Taranaki feuds, the shortage of lands for settlement, and therefore the danger of war, were all due to the illegitimate interference of non-owners, whether kingites, land leaguers', or chiefs, in land sales. <i>(1959,History of New Zealand,123</i>)



## land-order
 
 <i>n.</i> [AND 1838]

>  <b>WOOD</b> A Company's land-order is a clog to freedom of action; ... <i>(1843,Twelve Months in Wellington,61</i>)

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> The purchasers then received [in London] land-orders entitling them to choose one Town-section and one rural section for each land-order, in the rotation governed by the number drawn for it; and the native reserves took their chance with the rest. <i>Ibid.</i> 317 [`Terms of Purchase of Land in the Settlement of Otago' agreed on by the `Otago Association' and the Company] 16 In exchange for each sum of 120l. 10s. ... each Purchaser to receive ... three separate Land-Orders, namely, for the Town quarter-acre, the ten acres of Suburban Land, and the fifty acres of Rural Land, respectively. These lands to be severally selected according to priority of choice, to be determined by Ballot. <i>(1848,Handbook for New Zealand, The,175</i>)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> The holders of the company's land orders were called, in the infancy of the colony, `sectionists,' and regarded as great men, being the only possessors of land. <i>(1868,Past and Present of NZ,275</i>)

>  <b>ACLAND</b> <i>(1930,Early Country Runholders,20</i>)

>  <b>GUTHRIE</b> This [being beaten for good land] had already happened more than once, for his `land order' (drawn by ballot) being a high number he was obliged to wait till those with lower ones had made their first choice. <i>(1930,NZ Memories,76</i>)



## land-seeker
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a person keen to acquire land [1800s.]

>  <b>GREENWOOD</b> One of the earliest land-seekers in the Waimate County was Miss Jeanie Collier whose applications for land were gazetted on Feb 1. 1855. Miss Collier was the only woman land-seeker in South Canterbury. She was about 62 years old when she reached Canterbury with her three young nephews, Leslie, James and Andrew Thomson, and she set about establishing them on the land. <i>(1986,Te Waimate,17</i>)



## land-sharking
 
 <i>n.</i> [1839 Oct 2 Colonial Gazette 627 [? Aust. ref. check: is in OEDS]]

>  Your Directors will not withhold from you their opinion, that the practice of land-sharking, or the acquisition of land from the barbarous natives by private persons, without any reserves for the use of the natives, or indeed any sort of regard for their just rights ..... <i>(1840,NZ Company Reports,1/31</i>)

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> This mode of acquiring land from savages [sc. by fraudulent blank contract forms] is now well known as land-sharking; a name which implies preying on the weakness of childish ignorance. <i>(1848,Handbook for New Zealand, The,54</i>)

>  <b>CHAMBERS</b> [Contracts for the sale of land were unknown before 1814. Marsden brought over a legal deed with blanks for the names of chiefs and places. A vast mumber of documents were drawn up by adventurers, sailors etc, in imitation.] The signatures of the chiefs were purchased for the merest trifle and sailors, with the earnings of a year, became mighty landlords. The system grew into disrepute among the conscientious settlers, and has become famous as land-sharking. <i>(1851,Papers for the People: NZ,4</i>)

>  The Constitution Act prohibits all private agreements for purchase or occupation of lands owned or occupied by the natives in common as tribes ... inasmuch as such agreements favour land-sharking. <i>(Apr 28, 1855,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,1/162</i>) [NOTES: C.W.Richmond]

>  <b>THOMSON</b> Out of this scramble [for land] sprang up the `land-sharks', a class of men who bought land from savages, and took advantage of their childish ignorance regarding its value. Land-sharking was not, however, limited to persons whose sole object in life was money-making ... <i>(1859,Story of New Zealand, The,2/31,1974 ed.</i>)

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> The acquisitions of the lately-arrived adventurers were mere land-sharking transactions. <i>(1848,Handbook for New Zealand, The,71</i>)

>  In short, it is "Land Sharking," and not the "legal opinions," which has brought about the "painful retrogression down the road to ruin." <i>(Jun 19, 1855,Daily Southern Cross,3</i>)

>  This is how the organs of the land sharking fraternity deal with the question of settlement. <i>(Oct 20, 1890,Wanganui Herald,2</i>)

>  OFFICIAL LAND SHARKING. <i>(Apr 22, 1867,Hawke's Bay Weekly Times,93</i>) [NOTES: Headline]

>  MORE LAND SHARKING .. An English Syndicate has purchased 40,000 acres of land at Taupo for aGBP32,000. the negotiations were conducted by cablegrams, which alone cost aGBP750. <i>(Apr 7, 1884,Hawera & Normanby Star,2</i>)

>  I cannot allow myself to think that this individual, ranked amongst those born to greatness, had become so unprincipled as to lend his name and even his presence, to a mere land sharking expedition! <i>(Oct 5, 1842,New Zealand Gazette & Wellington Spectator,2</i>)

>  The Herald points out that 30 per cent. ad valorem duty will tend to reduce the price received by the natives for their land, but will not prevent land sharking. <i>(Jun 11, 1877,Evening Post,2</i>)

>  The Company was, therefore, say its advocates, 'forced into the adoption of 'land-sharking'". <i>(1853,New Zealand and Its Six Colonies,26</i>)

>  I would tell the honourable gentleman to have a good look, and see whether some of his relatives here were not amongst the champions who outlawed "land-sharking" from the Maoris. <i>(Nov 21, 1945,People's Voice,3</i>)

>  He [sc. Tom Russell] had a finger in most land-sharking pies. <i>(Sep 10, 1904,NZ Observer,4</i>)

>  The most strenuous regulations were made against land sharking and speculative builders. <i>(Feb 25, 1913,NZ Observer,2</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) the acquiring of land with ease from unsuspecting owners.

>  <b>BAGNALL</b> Ludham contended that the maximum price [per acre] of ten shillings but Fox, now Commissioner, explained that the higher figures were to discourage land-sharking. <i>(1976,Wairarapa : An Historical Excursion,161</i>)

>  <b>THOMSON</b> Land-sharking was not however, limited to persons whose sole object in life was money-making, as several missionaries, Mr Wentworth, an eminent Sydney lawyer and the British Resident, were to be found among them. <i>(1859,Story of New Zealand, The,II 31</i>)

>  <b>THOMSON</b> The secrets of land-sharking were laid bare by the Commissioners. <i>(1859,Story of New Zealand, The,32</i>)

## landing chair
 
 <i>n.</i> [goldmining]

>  <b>McARA</b> `Clips', `keps', or landing chairs: A solid, dead flush, landing platform for the cages was required at each level and at the surface for getting full trucks in and out of cages. This platform was provided by a steel frame mounted on heavy bearers two or three feet below the level of the chamber floor so that it could be tilted into the shaft by a lever and make a strong support beneath the cages. It was balanced by a counterweight to make it easy to operate. To ensure that the two cages in the shaft worked exactly opposite each other when the chamberman `changed gear' to wind ore from a different level, he rang the change signal of five bells on the steel-wire knocker-line suspended in the shaft compartment, and the engine-driver placed the `free' cage on the surface `clips' (or `keps') and disengaged it on the engine drive-shaft. The chamberman then signalled to lift the `fast' cage on the level where he was working just clear of the clips, and pinned these back clear of the shaft. He entered the `fast' cage and rang the number of the level to which he wished to change, and was taken there by the driver. On arrival at the level he placed the clips in position in the shaft and rang the change signal, whereupon the driver placed the `fast' cage on the clips and put the `free' cage (which was sitting on the surface clips) in gear on the engine drive-shaft. Thus, when the cage was raised from the level to the surface, the cage from the surface would be exactly at the level from which the winding was taking place. Great care had always to be taken to ensure that the clips were not left protruding into the shaft as a serious accident could result if the cage crashed into them. <i>(1978,Gold Mining at Waihi (Glossary),316</i>)

## lantern fish
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> a common fish worldwide, family Myctophidae

>  <b>PHILLIPPS</b> <i> Myctophum humboldti </i> (Risso). LANTERN-FISH.<i>Ibid.</i> 197 LANTERNFISH ...<i> Other names: Myctophus sp.</i>; jewelfish, myctophid. <i>(1927,Bibliography of NZ Fishes,16,1971 ed.</i>)

>  <i> Myctophum Humboldti </i> ... Lantern Fish. <i>(1911,Records of the Canterbury Museum</i>) [NOTES: WAITE]

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> Lantern Fish <i> Protomyctophum tenisoni </i> (Norman)... Lantern Fish <i> Protomyctophum normani </i>(Taning) ... Lantern Fish <i> Protomyctophum andersoni </i> (Lonnberg) <i>Ibid.</i> 401 Lantern Fish <i> Myctophum novaeseelandiae </i> (Steindachner) <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,400</i>) [NOTES: 1955 Whitley Namelist in 1956 Graham NZ Fishes]

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> Lantern Fish <i> Hierops subparallelus </i> (Taning)... Lantern Fish <i> Electrona antarctica </i> (Gunther) ... Lantern Fish <i> Electrona carlsbergi </i> (Taning)... Lantern Fish <i> Hygophum hanseni </i> (Taning)... Lantern Fish <i> Lampadena parvimanus </i> (Gunther) ... 400 Lantern Fish <i> Scopelus hookeri </i> Whitley <i>Ibid.</i> 401 Lantern Fish <i> Gonichthys barnesi </i> Whitley ... Lantern Fish <i> Diaphus (Lamprossa) ostenfeldi </i> Taning ... Lantern Fish <i> Collettia perspicillata </i> (Ogilby) (Syn. <i> Diaphus danae </i> Taning) ... Lantern Fish <i> Lampanyctodes hectoris </i>(Gunther)... Lantern Fish <i> Serpa peccatus </i> Whitley and Phillipps ... Lantern Fish <i> Serpa australis </i> Taning ... Lantern Fish <i> Serpa conspicua </i> Whitley ... Lantern Fish <i> Ceratoscopelus townsendi </i>(Eigenmann and Eigenmann) ... Lantern Fish <i> Gymnoscopelus piabilis </i>(Whitley) ... Lantern Fish <i> Notoscopelus elongatus ejectus </i> Waite ... Lantern Fish <i> Notoscopelus longipinnis </i> (Regan)... Lantern Fish <i> Scopelopsis caudalis </i> Whitley <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,400</i>) [NOTES: 1955 Whitley Namelist in 1956 Graham NZ Fishes]

>  <b>WILSON</b> The pretty little Lanternfish comes in about 40 varieties in our waters but is usually only ever found when it is dead and washed ashore. It ... gets its name from the fact that it has rows of phosphorescent light spots along its undersides. <i>(1981,Fisherman's Bible,142</i>)

>  <b>AYLING</b> The most abundant species in this area is probably <i> Lampanyctodes hectoris </i>, a lantern fish that ranges in size from 5 to 7.5cm. <i>(1982,Collins Guide,132,1984 ed.</i>)

>  <b>PAUL</b> <i> Lanternfish Lampanyctodes hectoris </i> <i>(1986,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,54</i>)

>  [species name] lantern fish .. [scientific name]<i> Myctophidae </i> .. [other name(s)] -- ... [species name] lampanyctus sp. .. [scientific name] <i> Lampanyctus </i> sp. .. [other name(s)] type of lantern fish <i>(Jul 23, 1986,Codes for Species in NZ Waters</i>)

>  <b>PAUL et al.</b> [index to common names 283-293] lanternfish 618-704 LANTERNFISHES 614-708 <i>(1987,Fishes: A Bibliography</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> = witch

>  CAULOPSETTA SCAPHA (Forster). <i> Megrim </i>. Known as `witch' or `lantern-fish' at Napier. Taken by trawlers in sandy localities as far north as Hauraki Gulf. Not popular as a food fish. ... Distribution: New Zealand coasts (unknown North Auckland Peninsula). <i>(Jun 1921,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,IV/121</i>) [NOTES: Phillipps Notes on the Edible Fishes]

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> NEW ZEALAND MEGRIM or WITCH (MAHUE) <i> Caulopsetta scaphus </i> (Bloch and Schneider). In Napier it is known as Lantern-fish, but for what reason I am unable to say, unless it is from the peculiar transparency when it/is held up to a strong light. Another name given to this useless fish by local fishermen, in Port Chalmers at least, was that of Cadger's-fish. <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,186,2nd ed. ed.</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> MEGRIM ... Sometimes called Lantern-fish (because a lantern could be easily shone through it) and known to the Maoris as `mahue' - the fish that is left behind. Known to Christchurch fishermen in early years as the Bludger's Fish. It was the fish they handed out to well-known bludgers who came down to the docks to cadge fish. <i>(1981,Fisherman's Bible,74</i>)

## larrikin
 
 <i>n.</i> (derogatory) a street rowdy; a young hooligan; a mischievously destructive youth (cf. larry). [DISCUSSION -->the earlier contemporary synonym in US and British (as well as NZ) Englishes was probably ROWDY: in US use (OED(S A. sb ...Orig. a backwoodsman, ... a rough disorderly person a. in American use or with reference to America1808b. in general use 1865 B adj 1819) larrikin, and its cognates and analogues, are well attested in English dialect: (a) EDD supp: larrikin a mischievous and frolicsome youth Warwickshire, Worcestershire; 1882 Dr F.W.P. Jago Ancient Language and Dialect of Cornwall Larrikins Mischievious [sic but recheck spg] young fellows, larkers. `Mischievious [sic] larrikins who pull the young trees down'. (Baker 1942) (b) EDD:larack v. wYks, with `about', to lark about: `goas laracking about, ower mich fur my fancy'; also a possible larrick adj. Yks `careless'; (c) EDD lurry v1, sb3 2. to create a disturbance Durh; ??lallack vb and sb. [la.l k] Nhb, Lakel Yks Worc 1. v. to idle about, go gossiping from house to house 3. sb. pl. play, frolic Nhb. Hence lallacking or lalecking (1) unrestrained junketing or scampering. 2. ppl.a. boisterous.; lorriker tongue, mouth Lakel. s.v. lallicker. [?= loudmouthing, yahooing]]

>  The coining of the word "larrikin" iscredited to an Irish sergeant of thepolice in Melbourne... "What's he beendoing now, sergeant?" said theMagistrate. "He was a larrikin (a-larking) about in the streets, yourWorship." "A what?"... "A larrikin, sor." <i>(Mar 16, 1912,NZ Free Lance,14</i>)

>  This time the brand is Tau Henare, larrikin, cowboy, fighter. <i>(Sep 19, 1999,Sunday Star Times,C3</i>)

>  <b>MARSHALL</b> He said it was vicious interference from that cheeky young larrikin in the Labelling Department ... <i>(1985,Secret Diary of a Telephonist,10</i>)

>  <b>HALEY</b> When Dad was a young larrikin he and all his mates used to race up and down the tideline in their old stripped cars. <i>(1989,Transfer Station,51</i>)

>  <b>BUTLER</b> They visit the asylums, .... hold classes for the larrikins or city Arabs ....and take part in other such work. <i>(1886,Glimpses of Maori Land,57</i>)

>  <b>McHUTCHESON</b> (Griffiths Collection) [Frisco] ... the home of the `larrikin' Notes <i>(1888,New Zealander Abroad, The,78</i>)

>  <b>BELL</b> Let clodhoppers plough, then, and harrow;/ Let larrikins loiter at flax. <i>Ibid.</i>124 The soulless larrikins of your street-corners - the blackguard barrackers of your football fields; for these are they. <i>(1889,In the Shadow of the Bush,14</i>)

>  Lounging Leary Larrikins Lashed - the Decay in British Manhood. Have you ever made a study of the hooligan of the Antipodes, our own binging, leary, larrikin <i>(Sep 2, 1905,NZ Truth,11/7/3</i>)

>  This [Dunedin] individual began to deal out what the larrikins call a `stouch` to the two unfortunate females. <i>(Oct 21, 1905,NZ Truth,18/5/2</i>)

>  <b>BAUCKE</b> The larrikin [in Queen Street] stoppeth to invent appropriate blasphemy; ... <i>(1905,Where the White Man Treads,293,1928 p. 298 ed.</i>)

>  It is only fair to state that prominent members of the Melrose Club entirelydissociate themselves from the tactics adopted by the half-a-dozen larrikins on this occasion, ... <i>(Jul 14, 1906,NZ Truth,56/3/4</i>)

>  The larks of the leary larrikin are becoming a menace to the peace and comfort of citizens and a source of annoyance and loss to city restaurant keepers. <i>(Jul 21, 1906,NZ Truth,57/4/6</i>)

>  It [Hooligan] at first typified a cross between a London rough and a Cockney tough, which are two terms for two but slightly different things, which have only the semblance of a shade of a shadow of difference, and are both akin to our Australasian larrikin. A larrikin is not necessarily a loafer or a criminal, though he is often, if not invariably, a member of a push which may, and usually does, contain a certain criminal element, and a still larger element verging towards criminality. <i>(Jun 18, 1910,NZ Truth,260/1/1</i>)

>  <b>MANSFIELD</b> And where did a girl like that go to in a place like this. ... She supposed Alice had picked up some horrible common larrikin and they'd go off into the bush together. <i>(1922,Collected Stories,457,1984 Alpers ed.</i>)

>  <b>ARD FAIRBURN</b> ... the glory of war, pretending that soldiers are heroes, instead of brainless larrikins ... <i>(Jul 21, 1926,Letters,5</i>)

>  larrikin, larakin n. a rough; a disorderly person; - a. rowdy.- larr'ikinism n.rowdy conduct. <i>(1929,Whitcombe's Etym. Dict. Aust-NZ Suppl.,7</i>)

>  <b>LEE</b> Everything conspired to render me happy, even a pretty girl called Mona. She was no larrikin, neither did she hold aloof. <i>(1934,Children of the Poor,202,1949 ed.</i>) [NOTES: [of a girl]]

>  <b>MULGAN</b> I wanted a son like you, and I've got instead two larrikins who haven't an idea in their heads except spending money. <i>(1934,Spur of Morning,318</i>)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> the larrikins secured a real vicious one and put it in the police camp where Mr Arnott slept. <i>(1947,Pioneer Recollections,19,1956 ed.</i>)

>  <b>PARK</b> ... the white larrikins jammed on their flat hats and scurried out of there like Maori-bugs. <i>Ibid.</i> 134 ... some of the younger larrikins accomplished the long-desired feat of climbing the immense red-painted lattice tower that held the town firebell. <i>(1951,Witch's Thorn, The,43</i>)

>  larrikin ... n. hoodlum, hooligan, rowdy; whence larrikinism, behaviour oflarrikins. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),12</i>)

>  The man who once described himself as alarrikin is promising to return to theracetrack as a level-headed jockey. <i>(Apr 8, 1993,Evening Post,35</i>)

>  Some [business barons] have a cavalier manner that suits the New Zealand psyche well. We like a touch of larrikin. <i>(May 31, 2003,Listener (New Zealand Listener),32</i>)

>  <b>McGill</b> He could see nothing except some black marks on the road. Probably larrikins doing wheelies. <i>(1995,Gold in the Creek,124</i>)

>  There were sailors aplenty, a rough gang called the larrikins, and not enough police. <i>(May 2005,NZ Geographic,73 / 40</i>)

>  <b>PARK</b> The white larrikins jammed on their flat hats and scurried out of there like Maori-bugs <i>(1951,Witch's Thorn, The,43</i>)

>  ... a local inspector .... told Constable E. [to go] down to a nursery garden, whose owner complained that the larickins ... ne'er left him an apple. <i>(1866,Canterbury Rhymes,102,1883 ed.</i>)

>  <b>HARPER</b> We are beset with larrikins [in Hokitika] who lurk about in the darkness and deliver every sort of attack on the walls and roof with stones and sticks. <i>(Feb 5, 1885,Letters from Canterbury, NZ,123,1914 ed.</i>)

>  The people of Otago have no intention whatever of spending their revenue in educating Auckland larrikans or supporting indolent officials in Wellington. <i>(Jul 17, 1875,Otago Daily Times,Editorial</i>)

>  <b>HAY</b> The Auckland `larrikin`is a growing nuisance, but he is neither so numerous nor so objectionable as his folloe in Melbourne or Sydney. Unlike the street-arab, he is either a schoolboy, or earns his living somehow, or he is a truant from work of either kind. He hasexuberant spirits and an inordinate love of mischief ..... He has a sort of organisation of his own, and seems to revel in uncurbed liberty of action. <i>(1882,Brighter Britain,1/39</i>)

>  The chance mispronunciation of a colloquial word, by a Melbourne police officer, has added a new term to our language in the colonies. Who amongst us has not heard of the `larrikin'? He is essentially colonial, English cities may have `gamins` and the Pacific Slope can boast of `hoodlums` ... Though everybody speaks and writes about the `larrikin', what is he? ... I presume, I may say, he is of the male sex and that his age ranges from 12 to 21. He is oftown origin. His amusements are various. He likes to be in the streets, and rather prefers to be about theatres. Smoking is his pastime. As to his actions the larrikins may be classed under two headings: 1) The mischievous larrikin 2) the criminal larrikin. <i>(Aug 4, 1883,NZ Educational Monthly,7</i>) [NOTES: R.Stout]

>  <i>(Mar 30, 1885,Otago Daily Times</i>) [NOTES: letter by John Cassells, former policeman with Dalton (Griffiths Collection)]

>  <b>HOPEFUL</b> The youth of both sexes have far too much run of the streets, and the `larrikin,' as the rising young man is called, is often a mischievous specimen of later manhood, and his street liberty degenerates into vice. <i>(1887,Taken In,x</i>)

>  There is also a decrease in the number of offences that might be classed as acts of larrikins, and larrikinism ... is not so prevalent. <i>(1897,AJHR,6</i>) [NOTES: Police]

>  LaraZ.ri-kin (l&abreve.raZ.r&ibreve.aZ.k&ibreve.n), n. [Cf. prov. Eng. larrick lively, careless, larack to frolic, to romp. See Note below] 1. A street blackguard; a rowdy. 2. A hobbledehoy inclined to rough horseplay and practical joking. 3. In a general sense, an ill-bred or low-minded person; - often used merely as a contemptuous epithet. 4. A neglected boy who frequents the streets; a street Arab; a gutter snipe. Marcus Clarke. In senses 1 and 2 this word has become common inEngland. Larrikin is thought to have come into use about 1870. Its origin, though much disputed, is commonly explained by the following anecdote : An Irish policeman at Melbourne, on bringing a notorious rough into court, was asked by the magistrate what the prisoner had been doing, and replied, "He was a-larriki' [i. e., a-larking] about the streets." This story lacks confirmation, but the word may not improbably be connected with lark to frolic, to which, indeed, the Prov. Eng. larrick and larrack are perhaps related. <i>(1898,Merriam-Webster Third International,2025</i>)

>  <b>CROWTHER (ed.)</b> Larrikin groups, though not common, were also observed [in Cathedral Square, Christchurch, 20 Apr 1956]. Numbers of noisy youths and girls congregated for short [6] periods and engaged in loud conversation and laughter. <i>(1956,Street Society in Christchurch,5</i>)

>  [Hdg] TWO LARRIKINS PUT ON PROBATION. `This is not an ordinary case of burglary, but rather irresponsible larrikinism,` the Magistrate told the youths. <i>(Sep 10, 1963,Evening Post,16/7</i>)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> Brought from England by the early settlers or flowing thence in later days .us;are: quid for a pound sterling, larrikin and hooligan for roughs, getting the wind up, for donkey's years, and quod for prison. <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,2/860</i>) [NOTES: Wall NZ Speech]

>  ~ n. a boodlum, a hooligan, a rowdy; whence larrikinism, the behavious of larrikins. <i>(1966,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),619</i>)

>  <b>MITCHELL</b> larrikin: Young people of whom you happen not to approve. Note that all the terms of disapprobation for rumbustious youth left the rest of the world when youth took it over and crawled off to die in New Zealand where the old have such a strong hold. Expressions still used are `milk-bar cowboy', `flagon wagon gang' and even occasionally `Teddy boy.' <i>(1972,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),183</i>)

>  <b>CLEARY</b> ... but what can one old woman do with night coming down and nobody to protect her from these larrikins. ... In two minutes each of these bikies has a heavy hand on his neck. ... <i>(1975,Pocketful of Years,22</i>)

>  <b>LELAND</b> larrikin - larrikinism: A larrikin is a WILD YOUNG MAN. Larrikinism is what such a young fellow might engage in: VANDALISM ( see: yahoo, yob-yobbo) <i>(1980,Personal Kiwi-Yankee Dictionary, A,59</i>)

>  <b>MANTELL</b> Lucky we found it [an abandoned car] first ... Before some larrikins commandeered it for a joy riding spree.' <i>(1980,Murder or Three, A,78</i>)

>  larrikin n. Austral. & N.Z. sl. a. a hooligan. b. (as modifier): a larrikin bloke. [C19: < E dialect: a mischievous youth] <i>(1982,Collins Concise English Dictionary,633</i>) [NOTES: 1986 NZPOD 421; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 278]]

>  <b>BINNEY</b> Some larrikin had gone and bust the cigarette machine out in the hall. <i>(1985,Long Lives the King,25</i>)

>  <b>JONES</b> Gilmore worried that they were ... frightening off custom with their larrikin presence. <i>(1985,Gilmore's Dairy,33</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> [as name of a horse (Larrikin) etc.]

A larrikin is not necessarily a loafer or a criminal, though he is often, if not invariably, a member of a push which may, and usually does, contain a certain criminal element, and a still larger element verging towards criminality. <i>(Jun 18, 1910,NZ Truth,260/1/1</i>)

>  <b>MANSFIELD</b> And where did a girl like that go to in a place like this. ... She supposed Alice had picked up some horrible common larrikin and they'd go off into the bush together. <i>(1922,Collected Stories,457,1984 Alpers ed.</i>)

>  <b>ARD FAIRBURN</b> ... the glory of war, pretending that soldiers are heroes, instead of brainless larrikins ... <i>(Jul 21, 1926,Letters,5,1981 ed.</i>)

>  larrikin, larakin n. a rough; a disorderly person; - a. rowdy.- larr'ikinism n. rowdy conduct. <i>(1929,Whitcombe's Etym. Dict. Aust-NZ Suppl.,7</i>)

>  <b>LEE</b> Everything conspired to render me happy, even a pretty girl called Mona. She was no larrikin, neither did she hold aloof. <i>(1934,Children of the Poor,202,1949 ed.</i>) [NOTES: [of a girl]]

>  <b>MULGAN</b> I wanted a son like you, and I've got instead two larrikins who haven't an idea in their heads except spending money. <i>(1934,Spur of Morning,318</i>)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> the larrikins secured a real vicious one and put it in the police camp where Mr Arnott slept. <i>(1947,Pioneer Recollections,19,1956 ed.</i>)

>  <b>PARK</b> ... the white larrikins jammed on their flat hats and scurried out of there like Maori-bugs. <i>Ibid.</i> 134 ... some of the younger larrikins accomplished the long-desired feat of climbing the immense red-painted lattice tower that held the town firebell. <i>(1951,Witch's Thorn, The,43</i>)

>  larrikin ... n. hoodlum, hooligan, rowdy; whence larrikinism, behaviour of larrikins. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),12</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> Applied (often humorously) to a child (esp. if a healthy, thriving, noisy boy); or to a lively adolescent; a `young devil'.

>  This was great for a young larrikin like me as it gave me an immense amount of freedom to roam the hills and rivers and create all sorts of adventurous pastimes, some real, most imagined in my highly ceative mind. <i>(2001,Good Bastards: the larrikin's guide to success,16</i>)

>  <i>(Feb 7, 1870,Napier Daily Telegraph,2/3</i>) [NOTES: Melbourne]

>  <b>BAKER</b> ... the sound of children's voices .... not the melodious voices of angelic children .... but the voices of Colonial `larrikins', healthy, loud, and penetrating. <i>(1884,Daughter of King,194</i>)

>  <b>DUCHESS OF BUCKINGHAM</b> The `larrikins', as we called the young and giddy portion of this crew [who had landed to explore the creeks], found the bush impenetrable. <i>(1897,Glimpses of Four Continents,130</i>)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> The word has various shades of meaning between a playful youngster and a blackguardly rough. Little street-boys are often in a kindly way called little larrikins. (See quotations), 1870 and 1885.) Archibald Forbes described the larrikin as `a cross between the Street Arab and Hoodlum, with a dash of the Rough thrown in to improve the mixture.` (`Century.') The mostexalted position yet reached in liteature by this word is in Sir Richard Burton's Translation of the Arabian Nights' (1886-7), vol. i. p. 4, Story of the larrikin and the Cook; vol. iv. p. 281, Tale of First Larrikin. The previous translator, Jonathan Scott, had rendered the Arabic word, Sharper. -->There are three views as to the origin of the word, viz.-->(I) That it is a phonetic spelling of the broad Irish pronunciation, with a trilled rof the word larking). The story goes that a certain Sergeant Dalton, about the year 1869, charged a youthful prisoner at the Melbourne Police Court with being ` a-larrr-akin' about the streets.` The police Magistrate, Mr Sturt, did not quite catch the word- `A what, Sergeant?` -`A larrikin', your Worchup.` The police court reporter used the word the next day in the paper, and it stuck. (see quotation, `Argus,' 1896.) This story is believed by 99 persons out of 100: unfortunately it lacks confirmation; for the record of theincident cannot be discovered after long search in files by many people. Mr Skeat's warning must be remembered - -->`As a rule, derivations which require a story to be told turn out to be false.` -->(2) That the word is thieves' English, promoted like swag, plant, lift, etc., into ordinary Australian English. Warders testify that for a number of years before the word appeared in print, it was used among criminals in gaol as two separate words, viz. leary('cute, fly, knowing), and kinchen(youngster), `learykinchenshortened commonly into `leary-kin' and leary kid'. Australian wardens and constables are Irish, almost to a man. Their pronunciation of `leary kin' would be very nearly `lairy kin,' which becomes the single word larrikin. (See quotation, 1871.) It is possible that Sergeant Dalton used this expression and was misunderstood by the reporter. (3) The word has been derived from the French larron (a thief), which is from the Latin latronem (a robber). This became in English larry, to which the English diminutive, kinwas added; although this etymology is always derided in Melbourne. <i>(1898,Austral English,259</i>)

## larrikinism
 
 <i>n.</i> rowdy, objectionable public behaviour; also humorously applied to children's or adolescents' behaviour. [-->[AND 1870.] a. [Morris AC 1870]]

>  In actuality, it is the day when.. the boss searches for and finds his latent larrikinism just under the skin. <i>(Dec 19, 1992,Evening Post,4</i>)

>  He doesn't disagree that it fed his larrikinism. He was never far from the headlines for some outrage or other - seldom to do with his portfolio, but mostly scrapes just induced by his own bombast. <i>(Nov 3, 2002,Listener (New Zealand Listener),20</i>)

>  For the prevention of larrikinism there is nothing like a licking in season. <i>(Apr 2, 1887,Auckland Weekly News,21</i>)

>  Their good name and that of their school, and the convenience and comfort of other passengers are suffering from the undisciplined actions of the few, whose horseplay often amounts to larrikinism. <i>(May 26, 1955,Dominion,11</i>)

>  [Hdg] Larrikinism at Kaiwaka. <i>(May 31, 1879,Auckland Weekly News,14</i>)

>  Larrikinism was not wanted in Wellington,and it would be well for young fellows tobe careful. <i>(Jul 4, 1919,Evening Post,7</i>)

>  Larrikinism v. Public Entertainments <i>(Apr 27, 1871,Evening Post,2</i>)

>  Larrikinism [title] ... I was coming up Rattray St. today, wearing a bell-topper, and found about thirty boys preparing to pelt all comers. ... Nobody supposes that the masters are unaware of this larrikinism; ... <i>(1878,Evening Star</i>) [NOTES: 1878 Evening Star (Dunedin) quoted in 1974 Wood Victorian New Zealanders 31]

>  <b>HAY</b> Sometimes the youth finds himself in the police court, charged with `larrikinism', an offence that is sure to be severely punished. ... / He is not altogether bad - not so frequently thieving and breaking the law, as intent on simple mischief and practical jokes of the coarsest and roughest sort. <i>(1882,Brighter Britain,1/39-40</i>)

>  There is also a decrease in the number of offences that might be classed as acts of larrikins, and larrikinism ... is not so prevalent. <i>(1897,AJHR,6</i>) [NOTES: Police]

>  Such a teacher in a country school would ... be a great means of keeping [youths] from degenerating on leaving school, as well as preserving [134] them from larrikinism'. <i>(1893,TrNZI,29/133</i>) [NOTES: (Colenso, Presidential Address)]

>  <b>MORRIS</b> Larrikinism ... the conduct of larrikins(q.v.). <i>(1898,Austral English,262</i>)

>  We had not then [1852-3] the pestilential element of `larrikinism` which is so rife now and which makes many public meetings so objectionable. <i>(1879,Canterbury Sketches</i>) [NOTES: Mrs C.L. Innes]

>  <b>INGLIS</b> We saw [in Wellington] many deplorable cases of ... drunkenness ... of really well-dressed,respectable-looking women, ... probably mothers of families. Alas! alas! under such circumstances is larrikinism to be wondered at. <i>(1887,Our NZ Cousins,140</i>)

>  <b>COLENSO</b> What wonder is it that `Larrikinism` (as it is called) should flourish as it does among us. <i>(1888,Anniversary Address,9</i>) [NOTES: Anniversary Address (Hawkes Bay Philosophical Soc)]

>  Feeling often ran very high at local elections and the declarations of the result was [sic] usually accompanied by a good deal of larrikinism and frequently by mild disturbances. <i>(1914,History of North Otago,92,1978 ed.</i>)

>  larrikin, larakin n. a rough; a disorderly person; - a. rowdy.- larr'ikinism n. rowdy conduct. <i>(1929,Whitcombe's Etym. Dict. Aust-NZ Suppl.,7</i>)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> The horseplay became so rough that Andrew was chased into a cabin where he ... escaped the worst of the larrikinism. <i>(1939,First White Boy Born in Otago,181</i>)

>  TWO LARRIKINS PUT ON PROBATION For `irresponsible larrikinism,` two 17-year-old workmen were placed on probation for 12 months ... At a previous hearing the youths, ... pleaded guilty to breaking into an implement shed at the Shandon Golf Club, removing a tractor, and driving it round the grounds. `This is not an ordinary case of burglary, but rather irresponsiblelarrikinism,` the Magistrate told the youths. <i>(Sep 10, 1963,Evening Post,16/7</i>)

>  <b>LELAND</b> larrikin - larrikinism: A larrikin is a WILD YOUNG MAN. Larrikinism is what such a young fellow might engage in: VANDALISM ( see: yahoo, yob-yobbo) <i>(1980,Personal Kiwi-Yankee Dictionary, A,59</i>)
