## lash
 
 <i>v.</i> to fail to honour a debt or obligation.

>  LASH (v) Fail to honour a debt or obligation. From the English legal term laches (negligence). <i>(1982,Big Huey, The,250</i>) [NOTES: Glossary]



 
 <i>n.</i> In various transf. or fig. senses of `hit', or `go' (`have a go at', `give something a go'). 1. (WWI) give (something) a lash to leg, to undertake an action vigorously; to give something a good go (cf lash a screamer).

>  Said Martin of the Dinks, `Why, he was the greatest swi-up king in the `Inverteds', and he used to give the `vin rouge` a boscar lash to leg. <i>(Sep 5, 1917,NZEF Chronicles,28</i>)

>  .. have a lash at a man <i>(Sep 1, 1906,NZ Truth,63/4/4</i>)

>  Thereupon Smith caught him by the right arm and yelled to his mate. `Lash` he cried. And the other person `lashed!` Troon got it on the countenance and went down like a log. <i>(Nov 28, 1908,NZ Truth,180/5/1</i>)

>  <b>BALLANTYNE</b> ... hoping to get a lash at the Huns. <i>(1948,Cunninghams, The,37</i>)

>  lash ... 5. have a lash (at). Austral. & N.Z. inf. to make an attempt at or take part in (something). <i>(1982,Collins Concise English Dictionary,633</i>) [NOTES: nf. 1986 NZPOD]



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> A stroke of the cane or strap usu. or often pl. [(secondary schools') 1984 17 F E49 Pakuranga Coll. 23 LASH [16-17 M18 F2]]

## lasts
 
 <i>n.</i> [(marble players')]

>  <b>SUTTON-SMITH</b> Some of the typical terms used in marbles referred to the act of shooting and the movement of the marbles. Those related to ways of shooting marbles were cribbing, firsts, fudging, funks, funnicks, funnigans, flunks, forces, knee-high, knuckle-down-stiff, knuckles, and lasts. Terms connected with the movement of the marbles were: cannons, clears, dead-stick, double-hits, fires, funs, gees, haystacks, in smug, keeps, kills, kisses, lay-ups, manyes, ones, pink, ringer, rolls, scat, screws, skinned, skun, spiders, sported-off, stays, stops, tracks, throughs. <i>(1972,Folkgames of Children,175</i>)

>  ... wrap your laughing gear around that ... <i>(Jan 22, 1987,Ephemera</i>) [NOTES: Toyota advt Barry Crump]



## laughing jackass
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> <i>Sceloglaux albifacies </i>, a SI nocturnal bird, phs named by Australian-bred settlers, to be distinguished from morepork and from the little owl; =laughing owl, =whekau. also contrast jackass-bird.

>  <b>BUTLER</b> The laughing jackass is unlike the well behaved boy inasmuch as the latter is seen and not heard - the former is heard but has never yet been seen. I should never have supposed it resemble a laughing jackass, never having heard a jackass laugh, unless I had been told that it did so - but the parties who stood sponsors for the bird doubtless knew what they were about and I might be highly culpable were I to style them fanciful or misinformed. <i>(Jun 1860,Forest Creek MS,1960 ed.</i>)

>  <b>BUTLER</b> I have heard people talk, too, of a laughing Jackass (not the Australian bird of that name) but no one has ever seen it. <i>Ibid.,</i> 138 I have heard people talk too of a laughing jackass (not the Australian bird of that name), but no one has ever seen it. <i>(1863,First Year in Canterbury Settlement,122</i>)

>  [Attene albifacies, wekau of the Maoris, is known by some up-country settlers as the big owl or <i> laughing-jackass </i>.] Why it should share with one of our petrels and the great Dacelo of Australia the trivial name of `laughing jackass` we know not; if its cry resembles laughter at all it is the uncontrollable outburst, the convulsive shout of insanity. <i>(1870,TrNZI,3/63</i>) [NOTES: T.H. Potts]

>  <b>HUTTON</b> ATHENE ALBIFACIES. Gray. Laughing Owl. Laughing Jackass. Wekau. <i>(1871,Birds of New Zealand,2</i>)

>  <b>BULLER</b> SCELOGLAUX ALBIFACIES. (LAUGHING-OWL.) ... <i> Native names </i>. Whekau, Ruru-whekau, and Kakaha; `Laughing Jackass' of the colonists. <i>(1873,Birds of New Zealand,21</i>)

>  <b>BULLER</b> S. albifacies, Kaup, Laughing Owl, Laughing Jackass of the Colonists. <i>(1888,Birds of New Zealand,1/198,2nd ed.</i>)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> Jackass, Laughing ... There is another bird called a <i> Laughing Jackass <i/> in New Zealand which is not a <i> Kingfisher </i>, but an <i> Owl, Sceloglaux albifacies </i>, Kaup. (Maori name, <i> Whekau </i>). The New Zealand bird is rare, the Australian bird very common.... <i>(1898,Austral English,215</i>)

>  <b>POTTS</b> ' <i> Athene Albifacies </i>, wekau of the Maoris, is known by some up-country settlers as the big owl or laughing jackass.' -->`The cry of the laughing jackass ... Why it should share with one of our petrels and the great <i> Dacelo </i> of Australia the trivial name of laughing jackass, we know not; if its cry resembles laughter at all, it is the uncontrollable outburst, the convulsive shout of insanity; we have never been able to trace the faintest approach to mirthful sound in the unearthly yells of this once mysterious night-bird.' <i>(1882,Out in the Open,122</i>)

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> A bird we called [c1860s] the Laughing Jackass could also be heard screeching at night near the [Otago] coast. <i>(1939,First White Boy Born in Otago,121</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> any of several petrels and shearwaters (See [=laughing petrel](../=#=laughing-petrel))

>  <b>POTTS</b> Laughing Jackass is one of the names conferred on the wekau ..... This distinction is shared by an Australian bird as well as by some of our seabirds amongst the petrels or Procellaridae. <i>(1874,On Recent Changes in the Fauna,8</i>)

>  <b>STUDHOLME</b> Laughing Petrel (Laughing Jackass) --- Probably extinct. Last seen (dead) in 1916. <i>Ibid. 250 The kakas would screech and whistle, and on foggy evenings the laughing petrels, or laughing jackasses, as some people called them, would come rushing in from the sea, making for their nesting places in the hills, and start their hair-raising clatter close overhead. When I was a small boy, these shrieks gave me the creeps. I thought they sounded as if all the demons had got loose and were trying to frighten us, and I was always very thankful that there was someone nearby to protect me. I have not heard any of these birds for over thirty years, and presume that the stoats and weasels have accounted for them. <i>(1940,Te Waimate,240,1954 ed.</i>)

>  <b>HUTTON</b> 139. PRION TURTUR. Soland. Whale-bird. Laughing Jackass(?) <i> P. ariel </i>. GOULD. <i>(1871,Birds of New Zealand,48</i>)

>  <b>HUTTON</b> 125. PUFFINUS GAVIUS. Forst. Rain-bird or Wet-bird. Shearwater. Laughing Jackass(?). Hakoakoa. <i> P. opisthomelas </i>. COUES. <i> P. assimilis </i>. HUTTON <i>(1871,Birds of New Zealand,45</i>)



## laughing owl
 
 <i>n.</i> a large owl with various shrieking and chuckling notes; = laughing jackass, whekau

>  The cave had been the home of the laughing owls, a bird last sighted in 1914. <i>(Dec 7, 1996,Listener (New Zealand Listener),22</i>)

>  The introduction in 1906, of the European Little Owl, frequently called the "German Owl," and its subsequent establishment, chiefly in parts of the South Island, hardly compensates us for the loss of the Laughing Owl. <i>(1944,Korero (AEWS Background Bulletin),2(21) : 24</i>)

>  Capt. Hutton states that there is no evidence to show that the Laughing Owl was formerly more plentiful than it now is, <i>(1873,TrNZI,6/127</i>) [NOTES: Buller]

>  SCELOGLAUX ALBIFACIES (LAUGHING OWL).- The history of this bird remained, for many years, a desideratum to naturalists ..[87] The uncontrollable outburst of laughter, i.e. the call peculiar to this species, is only heard when the birds are on the wing and generally on dark and drizzly nights or immediately [88] preceding rain. <i>(1884,NZ Journal of Science,86</i>)

>  The name `laughing owl' is applied from the sound they make, which is a kind of ridiculous laugh in a descending key. <i>(1891,TrNZI,23/191</i>)

>  <b>GILLESPIE</b> A laughing owl was found dead on the roadside in 1914 and is believed to be the last specimen found in New Zealand. <i>(1958,South Canterbury,9,1971 ed.</i>)

>  232.a. <i>Sceloglaux albifacies rufifacies</i> Buller, 1904) <i>North Island Laughing Owl .</i>.. North Island, probably extinct ... 232.b. <i>Sceloglaux albifacies albifacies</i> (Gray, 1844) <i>Laughing Owl</i> (Whekau) <i>(1970,Annotated Checklist of Birds of NZ,61</i>)

>  <i>Extinct birds ... Laughing Owl Sceloglaux albifacies</i> (GRAY, 1844) OTHER NAMES: <i>Whekau, hakoke (North Island),rock owl</i>. The `doleful shrieks' of the laughing owl, which varied seasonally, were heard incessantly on rainy nights. Apparently it was easily kept in captivity, and reports say that it cooeyed in the evening, yelped like a young dog and mewed and chuckled. ... The most recent record of sighting was at Manapouri in 1950 ... <i>(1985,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,308</i>)

>  A friend is convinced that he heard there [sc. in Waipori Gorge] a laughing owl. <i>(Jun 29, 1922,Auckland Weekly News,15</i>)

>  He thinks that the bird was a laughing owl. <i>(Dec 14, 1922,Auckland Weekly News,74</i>)

>  <b>BULLER</b> SCELOGLAUX ALBIFACIES. (LAUGHING-OWL.) ... <i>Native names </i>. Whekau, Ruru-whekau, and Kakaha; `Laughing Jackass' of the colonists. <i>(1873,Birds of New Zealand,21,1888 2nd edn 1/198 ed.</i>)

>  <i> Athene albifacies </i>. - Whekau, Laughing Owl. South Island, and Kaimanawa Range in North Island. <i>(1886,TrNZI,18/112</i>) [NOTES: Martin]

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i> Whekau, whekaukau, ... Sceloglaux albifacies, laughing owl. </i> <i>(1917,Dictionary of the Maori Language,579</i>)

>  <i> Sceloglaux albifacies Gray. Laughing-owl. Mr. Clouston reports this species from one South Island locality. No other first-hand or reliable information is available. The undoubted diminution in the numbers of the laughing-owl is usually ascribed to the practical extinction of the `Maori' rat. </i> <i>(1923,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,6/84</i>) [NOTES: Myers Endemic Birds]

>  <b>GUTHRIE-SMITH</b> ... what are the chances of noting a single Thrush, or a single Laughing Owl <i>(1936,Tutira,102</i>)

>  <b>STEVENSON</b> The [bouquet] sachets were made from the skins of laughing owls (whekaus), birds that were once quite common in this locality, but now appear to be extinct. Occasionally their weird laughter could be overheard on dark ... nights up to about 1900 ... <i>(1947,Maori and Pakeha in North Otago,56</i>)

>  <i> laughing owl n [laughing </i> fr. pres. part. of <i> laugh</i>]: a reddish-brown owl (<i> Sceloglaux albifacies </i>) of New Zealand that is almost extinct <i>(1961,Merriam-Webster Third International,1278</i>)

>  <b>FALLA et al.</b> <i> Sceloglaux albifacies Maori name </i>: Whekau. ... According to early accounts the call from which this species received its name is heard mainly on dark nights accompanied by rain or drizzle or before rain; ... <i>(1966,Birds of New Zealand,184</i>)

>  <b>NATUSCH</b> The rare laughing owl (<i> Sceloglaux albifacies</i>) is larger than the morepork and has a pale facial disc. <i>(1967,Animals of NZ, The,275</i>)

>  `Hear the morepork?' asked the boy.`Laughing owl,' said the man. <i>(1973,NZ Short Stories III,211</i>) [NOTES: Henderson `The Dead Bush']

>  <i> laughing owl </i> large brown NZ owl. <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,422</i>) [NOTES: nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.]

>  At this time, other species were present which have since disappeared from Urewera, including the kakapo, laughing owl, huia, and stitchbird. <i>(1983,Land of the Mist,67</i>)

>  The laughing owl was the major predator of a whole range of smaller vertebrates (including tuatara up to 40 cm long) and large invertebrates. <i>(Jan 2003,NZ Geographic,No. 61/44</i>)

## laundry
 
 <i>n.</i> a room in a house used principally for washing and (since the advent of clothes dryers) drying clothes and linen. [Jim writes : In relation to places this is much the most common use of the word in NZ. Not in DNZE or - in this sense - NZPOD but recognised in NZ Oxford Paperback. NSOD defines <i>laundry</i> as an establishment where clothes etc are laundered, Collins as a place where clothes and linen are washed and ironed, which encompasses the NZ use but is unspecified. In most modern houses in England clothes are washed in the kitchen and there is no separate room. In NZ until recently bylaws prohibited washing clothes in a room used for preparing food. <i>Laundry</i> is known in England in the NZ sense but seems uncommon. In a 1987 Property supplement to a Midlands newspaper it rarely appeared in the detailed house descriptions and always as an attributive - <i>laundry area, laundry room</i>. The term <i>laundry room</i> appears in Ruth Rendell, <i>Harm Done</i> 2000. The Australian situation (and word) was similar to the NZ one according to Hudson <i>Modern Australian usage</i>. MAU p223. Macquarie 1981 is consistent with this. Salmond <i>Old NZ Houses</i>p. 200 suggests that the change of name from wash-house to laundry was associated with the advent of the bungalow style i.e. c1910-1920. Cf <i>porch</i>. But see <i>wash-house</i>. The same book implies (anachronistically?) a possible much earlier use "Until the 1880s... water was heated... in the laundry copper for washing clothes and for the children's weekly bath." p. 143. Two house plans (<i>Ibid.</i> p. 198-99) are of interest. One dated 1930 has a "washhouse" under the same roof as the house; the other dated 1924 has a basement "laundry".]

>  <b>ELDRED-GRIGG</b> I led her down the passage and into the kitchen, then through the kitchen and into the laundry. <i>(1995,Mum,43</i>)

>  <b>SALMOND</b> The very names of the rooms suggested a different lifestyle - "living room" for parlour, "breakfast room" for dining room, "kitchenette" for kitchen (now combined with scullery), "laundry" for washhouse. <i>(1986,Old New Zealand,200</i>)

>  <b>SALMOND</b> Until the 1880s.. water was heated over the kitchen fire, or in the laundry copper for washing clothes and for the children's weekly bath. <i>(1986,Old New Zealand,143</i>)

## laurel
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Myoporum laetum</i>, =ngaio.

>  <b>PAUL</b> <i>(1857,Letters from Canterbury, NZ</i>) [NOTES: (Andersen)]



 
 <i>n.</i> any of a number of plant of similar appearance found in various places including New Zealand; laurel(-tree), native, New Zealand laurel were formerlyapplied to various native trees perceived to be laurel-like; mainly to karaka, but also to taraire and tawa, pukatea, and occas. to ngaio, broadleaf, and mapau; see karaka, pukatea, taraire. The introduced laurel is often called cherry laurel: e.g.

>  <b>THOMSON</b> Gnaio shrubs (the native laurel) ... <i>(1867,Twelve Years in Canterbury,29</i>)

>  <b>POLACK</b> Many species of the laurel (laurus) exist, especially the philanthus, or sea-side laurel, and a species of the retiring mimosa, or sensitive plant, sheltered by the drooping leaf-branches of the chamerops, or dwarf palm. <i>(1838,New Zealand  (Polack),1/294</i>)

>  laurel n -s 4 : a tree or shrub that resembles the laurel: as h : any of several New Zealand and Australian evergreen trees of the genera Cryptocarya, Pittosporum, Corynocarpus, and Likea <i>(1961,Merriam-Webster Third International,1278</i>)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> Laurel, n. The English tree-name is applied in Australia to various trees, viz.... The New Zealand Laurel is Laurelia novae-zelandiae; called also Sassafras. <i>(1898,Austral English,263</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Beilschmiedia tarairi</i>, =taraire.

>  <b>DOMETT</b> laurel tree <i>(1872,Ranolf and Amohia</i>)

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> North Island, Auckland district. <i>(1940,Plants of NZ,183</i>)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> New Zealand Laurel.. .. taraire .. .. Beilschmiedia taraire -->tawa .. .. Beilschmiedia tawa (ii) Beilschmiedia tawa, =tawa. See cit. 1966 a. above <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/705-12</i>)

## law clerk
 
 <i>n.</i> a student studying law working for an established law firm [Probably differs from overseas use, especially in past years, because they were still students]

>  It took him six years to graduate, as from his second year he studied part-time while working as a law clerk. <i>(Apr 9, 1983,Listener (New Zealand Listener),16</i>)



## law office
 
 <i>n.</i> [Jim Cameron writes: This long-standing US synonym for a law firm or a solicitors' office is recorded as <i>NAmer</i> in NSOD. It is now quite common in NZ and seems to have become naturalised here. There is for instance at least one firm in Masterton and another in Featherston whose signs say "law offices". (Its use seems to have closely followed the modification of advertising restrictions on solicitors a few years back).]

>  The most important exotic timbers being grown on a commercial scale are: ... <i>Sequoia sempervirens</i> (Californian redwood), <i>Thuya plicata </i>(western red cedar), <i>Cupressus Lawsoniana </i>(Lawson's cypress or Port Oxford cedar). <i>(1936,Agricultural Organization in NZ,578</i>)

>  <i>cypress, Lawson's [s] Chamaecyparis lawsoniana</i> (A. Murr.) Parl.... <i>Ibid.</i> 43 <i>Lawson's cypress [s] Chamaecyparis lawsoniana</i> (A.Murr.) Parl. <i>(1969,Standard Common Names for Weeds,20</i>)



## lawsoniana
 
 <i>n.</i> short form of <i>Cupressus lawsoniana</i>, Lawson's cypress q.v. [ca1951 (Ruth Mason) `definitely a NZ use'.]

>  The hedge surrounding the house is alawsoniana, and already has reached aheight of five feet. <i>(Oct 1, 1945,NZ Dairy Exporter,75</i>)

>  Evergreens and Flowering Shrubs, &c.30,000 Coniferae Trees... 4000 CupressusMacrocarpas.. " Lawsoniana from 12s perdoz. to 24s. <i>(Jul 1, 1874,Otago Daily Times,4</i>)

>  2 year Lawsoniana, aGBP3 per 100. <i>(Sep 9, 1952,Nelson Evening Mail,1</i>)

>  <b>MATTHEWS</b> Chamaecyparis lawsoniana (Cupressus lawsoniana) - Lawson's Cypress, or White Cedar. Hardy in any part of New Zealand. <i>(1905,Tree Culture in NZ,106</i>)

>  [The Conservator of Forests for theRotorua Region states that Lawson'scypress continues to show the bestresults.. and generally has madeexcellent growth.] <i>(1933,NZ Journal of Agriculture,XXXIII/333</i>)

>  <b>MORTIMER</b> C[hamaecyparis] lawsoniana (LawsonCypress or Port Orford cedar) was firstplanted in New Zealand over a century agoand is one of the most valuableintroduced wind shelter trees. <i>(1984,Trees for the NZ Countryside,122</i>)

>  My grandmother's house is on a three-quarter acre section with an orchard and vegetable garden, and a tall windbreak of what we call Lawsonias. <i>(Sep 12, 2001,Sunday Star Times,A9</i>)

>  <b>BARRY</b> Applications were made to the Board for 50 Lawsoniana trees and for subsidy of [pounds]20 onGiant Stride. <i>(Jun 29, 1959,In the Lee of the Hokonuis,227,1966 ed.</i>)

>  <b>ASHTON-WARNER</b> Stoned fruit ... all tucked away securely behind the poplar and lawsoniana breakwinds. <i>(1960,Incense to Idols,237</i>)

>  <i>lawsoniana [=] Lawson's cypress [s] </i> <i>(1969,Standard Common Names for Weeds,43</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> ... dizzy with the taste of our lips and the smell of our sweat mixed with the overpowering scent of lawsoniana or tea-tree. <i>(1973,NZ Jack,126</i>)

>  <b>SUTHERLAND</b> Danny ... that afternoon hid in the lawsoniana hedge at the doctor's. <i>(1978,Elver, The,7</i>)

>  <b>WEBSTER</b> In the grounds adjacent to the road were great clumps of tall, healthy looking lawsonianas. <i>(1979,Rua,27</i>)

>  <b>JACKSON</b> Crouched under the lawsonianas by the Dental Clinic, a sense of loss ... seized me. <i>(1988,Rainshadow,61</i>)



## lawyer
 
 <i>v.</i>

>  . . . been very carefully lawyered. <i>(Oct 3, 2005,Radio NZ / National Radio</i>) [NOTES: Nine to Noon interview, 10 30am]



 
 <i>n.</i> short for bush lawyer [-->1867 LCN - 1911 EM (Andersen 9 entries) [Check cits with bush lawyer.]]

>  <b>HOOKER</b> Lawyer, <i> Rubus australis, Parsonsia <i/>, etc. <i>(1861,Handbook NZ Flora (List of Names),766</i>)

>  <b>HAAST</b> [We had] to scramble through a dense growth of wild irishmen, lawyers, and other prickly plants, which horribly retarded our progress. <i>(1861,Report on Topographical Exploration of Nelson,6</i>)

>  <b>PICKERING</b> So to cope with the rituals of tramping, its pains and camaraderie, a slang language has developed to suit. Lots of quick short words to fit in the breathless pauses as you pant uphill. `Crud' (cloud), `bush-bash', a `gunn' tramper (which must have originated from Davy Gunn's famous trek up the Hollyford Valley to help victims of a plane crash in 1936; he travelled 40 km by rowing boat, horse and foot in 21 hours), `windfall' (a collapsed pile of trees), `pit' (the tramper's true friend, a sleeping bag), `pog' (porridge), `bastard grass' and `lawyer' (two unpleasant plants), `piker' (one who stayed home), `Huey' (all-powerful god of weather), and many, many more. <i>(1988,Hills, The,8</i>)

>  They went through the thickest places matted with supplejacks and lawyers. <i>(Dec 3, 1887,Auckland Weekly News,8</i>)

>  The whole of this area was thick bush.. as far as St. David street, and from that locality along the edge of the stream impenetrable from creepers, lawyers, and supplejacks. <i>(Nov 23, 1920,Otago Witness,52</i>)

>  The fuschia and lawyers are all coming out in blossom. <i>(Sep 30, 1924,Otago Witness,71</i>)

>  <b>CRUMP</b> He hit a young horopito and it shot him across into a bunch of lawyer like a dirty great coil of winch-rope. <i>(1961,Hang on a Minute, Mate,189</i>) [NOTES: 1970 edition]

>  <b>NEWTON</b> It had one bad drawback for hoggets - it was bad with "lawyers". A "lawyer" is a dense bushy plant characterised by thousands and thousands of hooklike barbs along every inch of its creeper-like branches and tentacles. Even the backs of its leaves are covered with these barbs. <i>(1966,Boss's Story, The,29</i>)

>  <b>MORELAND</b> We entered on a dreary extent of moraine - a chaos of ridges and stones which filled the valley. In part it was overgrown by thickets of manuka scrub and tangles of hooked 'lawyer'. <i>(1911,Through South Westland,51</i>) [NOTES: Short for "bush lawyer"]

>  He duly arrived..covered in cuts from a vicious vine that could skin you alive. It is called "Lawyer", quite an appropriate name when you think about it. <i>(2001,Good Bastards: the larrikin's guide to success,223</i>)

>  <b>DEVANNY</b> Second in choice to eat were the berriesof the lawyer vine; next the heart, the"rolling pin" of the nikau palm. <i>(1988,Poems & Other Literary Remains,7</i>) [NOTES: Quoted from c1900]

>  A slim tendril of a lawyer vine had apparently pierced the stem of a young tree. <i>(Apr 16, 1910,NZ Truth,1</i>)

>  <b>CHAPPLE</b> If lawyer even gets mentioned in that [205] underslung farming way, then it won't be the lawyer you're used to, hanging occasionally across your path in the bush , occasionally raking you. No. This will be serious lawyer. <i>(2002,Te Araroa: the New Zealand Trail,204</i>)

>  <b>MUELLER</b> Ned was sitting by the fireside, his face and hands ... tremendously swollen on account of the scars made by the tearing of the lawyers (some prickly plant) through skin and flesh. <i>(Nov 19, 1865,My Dear Bannie,71,1958 ed.</i>)

>  <b>LINDSAY</b> In this condition [<i> Rubus australis </i>, Forst.] forms one of the most ... troublesome `Lawyers,' or `Supple-Jacks,' of the forest. It is known to the settler also as the `Wild Irishman,' and to the North Island Maori as `Tataramoa'. [fn] A term defined by Dieffenbach as `Thorns, Blackberry,' - a description at least partly applicable, and with greater propriety, to <i> Discaria Toumatou </i>. <i>(1868,Contributions to NZ Botany,54</i>)

>  The common lawyer Tataramoa (Rubus Australis) <i>(1870,TrNZI,3/206</i>) [NOTES: Webb (Otago)]

>  <b>MASTERS, Joeph</b> also a vine called by the settlers, `Lawyers', owing to the difficulty you have to extricate yourself from them, are hanging over your head ... <i>(1871,Autobiography,36</i>) [NOTES: ATBMS]

>  <b>BARKER</b> The impenetrable fence of matipo and goi, which were woven together by fibres of a thorny creeper called `a lawyer` by the shepherds. <i>(1873,Station Amusements in NZ,50</i>)

>  <b>HINGSTON</b> The New Zealanders funnily, but with a leaning to sarcasm, call them [sc. prickly vines] Lawyers. <i>(1879,Australian Abroad, The,293</i>)

>  <b>D'AVIGDOR</b> ... from the branches hang bunches of that wonderful lichen, `old man's beard', and slender lance-wood emerges ... from the convolvulus-leaved but thorny `lawyers' and the low manuca scrub. <i>(1888,Antipodean Notes,152</i>)

>  <b>HARPER</b> ... it may be explained that a `lawyer' is a bramble which grows in very dense masses, and is covered with small thorns. It is so named because, when once a man is unfortunate enough to getinto its clutches, he finds it hard to free himself. <i>(1896,Pioneer Work in the Alps of NZ,34</i>)

>  <i> LawaZ.yer </i> (laaZ.y&etilde.r), <i> n.</i> [Add.] 3. [Prov. Eng.<i>lawyer </i> a bramble, the thorny stem of a brier.] <i>(Bot.)</i> (a) In New Zealand, any one of various trailing brambles (esp. <i> Rubus australis </i>) which impede locomotion in the bush; - called also lawyer cane. (b) In Australia, the liliaceous plant Flagellaria Indica; - called also <i> lawyer vine </i>. Both plants are called also <i> bush lawyer</i>. <i>(1898,Merriam-Webster Third International,2026</i>)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> <i> Lawyer </i> ... One of the English provincial uses of this word is for a thorny stem of a briar of bramble. In New Zealand, the name is used in this sense for the <i> Rubus australis </i>, N.O. <i> Rosaceae </i>, or Wild Raspberry-Vine (Maori, <i> Tataramoa </i>). The words <i> Bush-Lawyer, Lawyer-Vine </i>, and <i> Lawyer-Palm </i>, are used with the same signification, and are also applied in some colonies to the <i> Calamus australis </i>, Mart. (called also Lawyer-Cane), and to <i> Flagellaria indica </i>, Linn., similar trailing plants. [Aus 1885] <i>(1898,Austral English,263</i>)

>  Climbing forest plants or `lianes' are abundantly represented by the lawyer [fn <i> Rubus australis.], Parsonsia albiflora </i>, Convolvulus, <i>(1900,Canterbury Old & New,184</i>)

>  <b>WHITE</b> Then other things are being smothered or strangled by ugly, worthless Lawyers and Supplejacks. <i>(1905,My NZ Garden,53</i>)

>  <i>(1906,AJHR,C-8</i>) [NOTES: (And) Report on Botanical Survey of Kapiti Island]

>  <i>(1909,AJHR,C-11</i>) [NOTES: Botanical Examination Higher Waimarino (And)]

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>(1910,NZ Plants</i>) [NOTES: (And)]

>  <i>(1911,Emu, The,vol. xi</i>) [NOTES: McLean M.B.O.U.]

>  <b>COWAN</b> ... almost trackless forest, your steps cumbered by rotting stumps, ... and twining supplejacksand prickly lawyer ... <i>(1916,Bush Explorers,10</i>) [NOTES: (TS:VUW)]

>  <i> lawyer n.</i>- law'yercane, palm, vine see BUSH-LAWYER. <i>(1929,Whitcombe's Etym. Dict. Aust-NZ Suppl.,7</i>)

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> A lofty climber ... in the bush or on its outskirts. <i>(1940,Plants of NZ,209</i>)

>  <b>SARGESON</b> (gloss.) lawyer: a thorny vine. <i>(1949,I Saw in My Dream,273,1974 ed.</i>)

>  <b>THOMSON</b> Going became fairly difficult, broken rocks retarding our progress, not to mention a mass of lawyer (a type of native rambling rose if it could be so called), high fern, and, at times, a densethicket of pepperwood shrubs. <i>(1952,Deer Hunter: The Experiences of a NZ Stalker,129</i>)

>  lawyer: 2.The New Zealand briar, or `bush-lawyer', a thorny climbing plant. <i>(1958,Whitcombe's Modern Junior Dictionary (8th edn),236,8th ed.</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> It comprised a series of good terraces around about 1,500 to 1,800 feet and a big stretch of steep nor'-west facings which was about the first country on the place to clear after snow. However it had one big drawback for hoggets - it was bad with `lawyers'. A `lawyer', perhaps I should explain, is a dense bushy plant characterised by thousands and thousands of hooklike barbs along every inch of its creeper-like branches and tentacles. ...How the plant came to get the name of Lawyer I don't know, but it is commonly said that, like those gentlemen of the legal profession, once they get their hooks into you, you've had it. <i>(1966,Boss's Story, The,29</i>)

>  <b>56</b> At the first bluff he sidled up the spur but he got off the discs and had to bash through the lawyers and windfalls. <i>(Apr 19, 1971,Listener (New Zealand Listener)</i>)

## lawyer bush
 
 <i>n.</i> = bush lawyer

>  Various species of <i> Rubus </i>, a briar-like plant which is a nuisance to men in the bush. It also grows on moist or shady hillsides in the open and is a great danger to hoggets, as they get caught up in it. When growing in the open it is often called a <i> l.-bush </i>. Except in Potts's `Out in the Open', to the best of my knowledge a <i> b.l.</i> has always meant an argumentative fellow. ([1898 Morris Austral-English]) [Lawyer = `briar` is recorded as English from the sixteenth century. The reason for the name is obvious.--A.W.] <i>(Nov 4, 1933,Press, The,15</i>) [NOTES: Acland Glossary]

>  <b>FERGUSSON</b> ... lawyer bushes, significantly so termed, from the clinging pertinacity of their nature, ... <i>(1893,Bush Life in Australia & NZ,250</i>)

>  ... the patch of scrub ... contains a few mapau (Pittosporum tenuifolium) and 'lawyer' (Rubus australis) bushes; some miko-miko (Aristotelia racemosa) and manuka ... <i>(1886,TrNZI,18/79</i>)

## lay
 
 <i>n.</i> a share (in phr. on a lay); a man's share in the proceeds of a whaling venture. in phr. to sail upon the lay.

>  <b>JOHNSTON</b> The whalers all sail upon the lay, ... or, every person on board receives, according to a graduated scale, a portion of oil which is to be given up as per agreement, to the employers at a certain price, not above half the amount they would obtain for it, were they to sell it themselves at Sydney. <i>(1840,Plain Truths,61</i>)

>  <b>JACOBSON</b> We were all on a `lay` .... If you are on 1/100th when 100 tons are got, you get one, and when you are on a `lay` they find you - that's the difference between a lay and going shares. If you are on shares you find yourself. <i>(1893,Tales of Banks Peninsula,207</i>)

>  <b>SATCHELL</b> Ain't there a lay of any kind? If so, I'm on it. <i>(1902,Land of the Lost,27</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>McARA</b> `Lay` of wire rope: Most of the winding-ropes were what was known as `lang lay', in which the strand wires were twisted in the same direction as the strands. They presented a larger surface to resist wear when made in this way and the rope was more easily inspected for internal wear. <i>(1978,Gold Mining at Waihi (Glossary),326</i>)

## lay on

We may go over and try it if we do not find a good opening on the river. <i>(Jan 4, 1863,Journal and Letters (Walker)</i>) [NOTES: (ATB:TS) (Central Otago]

>  <b>COTTLE</b> The kind (?) friends .... were always `laying him on` to these good things. <i>(1891,Frank Melton's Luck,25</i>)

>  <b>HISLOP</b> I am feeling pretty crook today, Jimmy, and I am going to get a tooth out. Can you lay me on to a good dentist. <i>(1943,Pure Gold,32</i>)

>  <b>BENNETT</b> A systems of hire-purchase called the `Lay-by' has resulted in the verb `layby', pronounced and written as one word. <i>(1943,English as it is spoken in NZ</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i>

## lay-by
 
 <i>n.</i> A siding on an (underground) mine tramway-system.

>  The driver takes the trucks through the mine races, and deposits them in lay-bys, places similar to ... railway sidings. Here the trucks are received by the hangers-on, boys who attach the trucks to an endless rope. <i>(1901,NZ Illustrated Magazine,IV/614</i>)

>  (Bell and Fraser: Waihi-Tairua) In the Grand Junction Mine ... electrically driven fans, placed in `lay-bys` in the underground road ways, have been found effective. <i>(1912,NZ Geol. Survey Bulletin,No 15 (N.S.) 18</i>)

>  <b>DEVANNEY</b> At the point where the bord left the dip a `lay-by` was put in. This was a double road [taking] full trucks on one side ... and [empties on the other]. <i>(1928,Dawn Beloved,169</i>)

>  <b>PEARSON</b> The four of them walked along the tunnel, past the main lay-by where another trucker was sending off the last boxes. <i>(1963,Coal Flat,28</i>)

>  <b>THOMPSON</b> Two shifts a day she [sc. the mine-horse] would drag upwards of 200 half-tonne boxes, six at a time, into the lay-by, where Frank ... would jig them down to the next level. [41] About once every three months she would ... [run] wide on one of the corners in such a way as to pull all theboxes off the line. <i>(1980,All My Lives,40</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> The reservation of an article by paying a deposit and `laying it by' for later full payment and collection. [[See Davin's gloss.]]

>  [Advt] No laybys. No rainchecks. Stock may vary from store to store. <i>(Jun 5, 2000,Dominion,17</i>)

>  <b>BURCHFIELD</b> Sometimes would have to be done about the word `lay-by.` In Britain it meant a roadside bay where vehicles could be pulled off the road. <i>(1954,Evening Post</i>)

>  <b>LELAND</b> lay-by: The LAY-AWAY with time to pay at your friendly department store. <i>(1980,Personal Kiwi-Yankee Dictionary, A,59</i>)

>  lay-by ... 4. Chiefly Austral. & N.Z. a system of payment whereby a buyer pays a deposit on an article, which is reserved for him until he has paid the full price. <i>(1982,Collins Concise English Dictionary,637</i>) [NOTES: 1986 NZPOD 424; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 279]

>  <b>MANTELL</b> Yes. That's it. A time payment card. ...So that the lower case letters `yby' could be part of the word `layby', not part of an address. <i>(1983,Murder to Burn,33</i>)

>  <b>WATSON</b> I can remember putting one [skirt] on layby and paying it off over the weeks. <i>(1986,Address to a King,63</i>)

>  Other definitions: telephone box (booth); layby (layaway); boarding cattery (kennel for cats); inward goods (deliveries); ... dormitory town (bedroom community) ... <i>(1986,Personal Communication</i>) [NOTES: 1986 B.G. Ostmann U.S. food-writer's written remarks: p.c.Tui Flower NZ Woman's Weekly (14 Jan 1987)]

>  layby small deposit securing goods from sale until depositor pays the balance (usually progressively) and uplifts the goods; generally operated by drapers and department stores. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,68</i>)

>  <b>TURNER</b> Goods may be bought on a layby system. <i>(1966,English Language in Aust. & NZ, The,172</i>)

>  There were no credit cards; we paid for our purchases with cash. If you didn't have enough cash you put the purchases on lay-by and paid it off. <i>(Jun 2004,NZ Memories,No. 48/6</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i> to put (goods) on lay-by

>  Layby Now [headline] We all want to beat GST, but with money invested we all want to wait until September 30, ... By paying a deposit now, we will set up your boat motor, dinghy, etc, fordelivery on or near September 30. <i>(Jul 13, 1986,Sunday Star (Auckland),D14</i>)

>  We went and lay-byed a fridge-freezer and a Gentle Annie automatic washing machine. <i>(Jun 1992,Metro,110</i>)



## lay-up
 
 <i>n.</i> (marbles term)

>  <b>SUTTON-SMITH</b> Some of the typical terms used in marbles referred to the act of shooting and the movement of the marbles. Those related to ways of shooting marbles were cribbing, firsts, fudging, funks, funnicks, funnigans, flunks, forces, knee-high, knuckle-down-stiff, knuckles, and lasts. Termsconnected with the movement of the marbles were: cannons, clears, dead-stick, double-hits, fires, funs, gees, haystacks, in smug, keeps, kills, kisses, lay-ups, manyes, ones, pink, ringer, rolls, scat, screws, skinned, skun, spiders, sported-off, stays, stops, tracks, throughs. <i>(1972,Folkgames of Children,175</i>)

>  <b>SUTTON-SMITH</b> Here are some [marble] terms from Taranaki around 1880-1890: firsts, lay-up, stakes, tips, sticking-taw, nothings, anythings, fade-it, knuckledown stiff, dubs, finking [sic, ?funking], sips, spanning, placing; and from Dunedin School in 1949: knee high, manyies, tracks, screws, nuts, sleets, kills, forces, dig, dubs, cheats. <i>(1981,History of Children's Play,269</i>)

## lead
 
 <i>n.</i> ?A continous deposit of alluvium; ? lode (cf leader n); An alluvial deposit of gold along the bed of an ancient river: also deep-lead; great-blue lead, etc: 1885 (Aust); 1880 deep lead. . [AND 1852.]

>  <b>WEKEY</b> The mere prevalence of blocks of `cement', as it is technically termed, ... forms no sure indication of the presence of a lead, but if the `cement' be found strewn over a limited tract, round which the bed-rock crops out ... it may then be taken as a guide in prospecting. <i>(1862,Otago As It Is,21</i>)

>  <b>HARPER</b> .. we came to several shafts sunk about eighty feet on a `lead' of gold lately discovered. Gold here [West Coast] is usually found in strata, about three feet thick of washdirt ... <i>(Feb 5, 1868,Letters from Canterbury, NZ,110,1914 ed.</i>)

>  Alluvial gold occurs in well-defined `leads', of which there are three distinct kinds, without including the local sluicing diggings <i>(1869,TrNZI,369</i>) [NOTES: Hector]

>  <b>MORRIS</b> ~ (pronounced leed), a mining term. In the Western United States and elsewhere, the term leadin mining is used as equivalent for lode. In Australia, the word leadis only used in reference to alluvial mining, and signifies the old river-bed in which gold is found. [1875] <i>(1898,Austral English,264</i>)

>  <b>PFAFF</b> These [black sand] leads yielded a richer return ... than probably any other alluvial workings in New Zealand ... <i>ibid.</i> 79 I worked a claim on the Scandinavian Hill ... This lead was about 600 yards long, and the wash varied from 1 foot to 4 feet. <i>(1914,Diggers' Story, The,29</i>)

>  ~ v.t.-n. the old river-bed in which gold is found.-v.t. to strike the lead, to find a river bed containing gold; to succeed. -lead'ers n. pl. of a bullock team, the leading pair. <i>(1929,Whitcombe's Etym. Dict. Aust-NZ Suppl.,7</i>)

>  <b>BLYTH</b> Lead: A defined gutter of auriferous wash. <i>(1935,Gold Mining Year Book,11</i>) [NOTES: Glossary]

>  <b>MILLER</b> I was on gold all the time. I had sunk my first prospect hole at the lower end of a shallow depression ... and had struck the deepest part of the lead. It was a narrow gutter, no more than afoot wide at the most, and rich in heavy gold. <i>(1946,There Was Gold,69</i>)

>  <b>MAY</b> LEAD: a well-defined bed of auriferous wash-dirt. Used narrowly, a lead was in a gully, creek, or terrace and its course could not be determined by the trend of the surface and the existing drainage system. <i>(1967,West Coast Gold Rushes,527,1962 ed.</i>)

>  Previously worked soil had been brought up to allow of further examination of the `lead' of bones under the rock. Notes Hamilton `Avian Remains' <i>(1893,TrNZI,226</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i> Of a sheepdog, to run ahead of a mob to keep them steady (cf lead n3., leading dog). [rural]

>  <b>LILICO</b> [The dogs] would head, lead, hunt away, force and back .... <i>(1934,Sheep Dog Memoirs,27</i>)

>  <b>HARTLEY</b> The dog `Trojan' ... would head, hunt, lead, back, heel cattle, catch a horse, pick up cast sheep, open and close gates, retrieve sacks ..., strike sheep with his paws, walk on his hind legs, ring a bell and perform many other minor tricks. <i>(1949,Shepherd's Dogs, The,2</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a sheep easily worked by a dog that sets an example for other sheep to follow. (See [lead cow](../L#lead-cow),[leader](../L#leader),[Judas sheep](../J#judas-sheep),[pet](../P#pet))

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> lead: the head of a mob of sheep on the move. Not necessarily physically the strongest beast, but a strong-willed one, will take the lead. <i>(1981,Loughnan Glossary,48</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>v.</i>

>  Lead. - (1) The head shepherd, or whoever is in charge of the musterers, is said to l. them. <i>(Nov 4, 1933,Press, The,15</i>) [NOTES: Acland Glossary]



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> a kind of cattle-race. [station]

>  The lead from the yards is circular and uphill just before entering the dip which is like a 60 ft. washing tray. <i>(Apr 11, 1865,Chudleigh Diary,173,1950 ed.</i>)

>  They made a `lead' in the stockyard for branding the cattle. This was something like a `race' for branding sheep with a swing gate ..... It had a wide entrance gradually getting narrower and narrower till it became a lane only just wide enough for one beast at a time to squeeze through ... Five or six would he jammed in at one time ... the gate at each end being closed .... in a few minutes each lot could be branded. <i>(1878,Boy Colonists,ELWELL,214 ed.</i>)



 
<b>3</b> <i>n.</i> the front part of a mob of sheep

>  <b>15</b> Lead. - (1) ... (2) The front part of a mob of sheep. (C.) <i>(Nov 4, 1933,Press, The</i>) [NOTES: Acland Glossary]

>  <b>SCRYMGEOUR</b> When the bailiffs arrived, with the bleating flock of sheep, she turned the lead, placed herself ... between the ford and the sheep, and invited them to cross. When dusk fell, Mrs. Parker still defied the stringing of the sheep across [the river]. <i>(1960,Memories of Maoriland,11</i>)

>  lead ...2 n ...NZ & Austral.the front of a mob of sheep; <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary</i>) [NOTES: [nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.]]

## lead-swinger
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  First heard it on the Maheno in 1915 from a `Tommy' who told me that before the war he was working at Day and Martin's, of blacking fame. `Ever heard of them?' he proudly asked me. `No, I can't say that I have,' I replied. He gave me such a look of pity and scorn that I could almost feel myself shrivelling up. `Gor blime, chum, I worked for them fifteen years,' he said. `Yus, fifteen years making blacking, and my old woman is deeping my end up. Never heard tell of them? `Leg swinger,' chum, that's what you are.' <i>(Nov 1, 1918,Quick March,15</i>)

>  An Educational scheme is under way for the benefit of :- (1) The Blue Boys. (2) Lead Swingers. <i>(1918,New Zealand At the Front,65</i>)

>  Sick parade in these days was, to many, a "leadswinger's" paradise... <i>(Dec 1916,Roll Call,5</i>)

>  That "Bill the Dunk" is a noted lead-swinger, but that he has the happy knack of feathering his own bed to advantage. <i>(1918,Link, The,29</i>)



## lead-swinging
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  P.U.'s. - See `Lead-Swinging.' Lead-Swinging. - See P.U.'s - they can tell you. The origin of this term is obscure - the destination, Blighty, or the clink. <i>(1918,New Zealand At the Front,32</i>) [NOTES: Digger's Dictionary]



## leadenall
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> FRIGATE MACKEREL <i> Auxis thazard </i> (Lacepede) ... This beautifully-shaped fish is known in Australia as the Frigate Mackerel. Fishermen there also call it Leadenall from the appearance of polished lead it presents when freshly caught. <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,304,2nd ed.</i>)



## leader
 
 <i>n.</i> a quartz-leader q.v. (cf. lead n.1)

>  <b>VICTORIAN MINER</b> Any reefs that may be found in this part of the country will not prove reefs in the true sense of the word, but mere `spurs' or `leaders', and these much broken and full of `faults'. <i>(1862,Rambles through Goldfields</i>)

>  Four rich leaders of gold-bearing quartz <i>(1865,Evening Post,1/245/2</i>)

>  LEADER.- A string or small vein which leads to the main reef, or is supposed to do so. <i>Ibid.</i> 82 BALLARAT, Moanatairi Creek. Four men's ground, ... Gold-bearing leaders. <i>(1868,Thames Miner's Guide, The,55</i>)

>  LEADER.- A string or small vein which leads to the main reef, or is supposed to do so. <i>Ibid.</i> 82 BALLARAT, Moanatairi Creek. Four men's ground, ... Gold-bearing leaders. <i>(1868,Thames Miner's Guide, The,55</i>)

>  The Golden Crown leader runs right through the claim; ... <i>(1869,Wit and Humour: Local Songs...,25</i>) [NOTES: Gold]

>  <b>BAINES</b> Our reef, or leader, measuring only four inches in thickness, a drive for a considerable distance following the course of the gold-bearing quartz had to be carried out, ... <i>(1874,Edward Crewe,262</i>)

>  <b>HOLLOWAY</b> The [West Coast] Quartz veins that have hitherto been worked are what are known as `leaders' or offshoots from the main reefs. <i>(Jun 20, 1874,Journal of a Visit,119</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a sheep that will easily be worked by a dog and set an example that other sheep will follow. (See [lead](../L#lead),[lead cow](../L#lead-cow),[Judas sheep](../J#judas-sheep),[pet](../P#pet),[yard sheep](../Y#yard-sheep))

>  <b>KERR</b> When we had the last mob of the day we'd try to batter them into submission, tire them out in the heat, march them up to the top of the hill and march them down again like Old King Cole, dog them constantly. We aimed for a nucleus of docile leaders. <i>(2000,High Times in the High Country,82</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  <b>HENDERSON</b> LEADER: Horses yoked in the lead; single leader. <i>(1961,Friends in Chains,187</i>) [NOTES: c1900-1920 A Teamster's Glossary in 1961 Henderson]

>  The leaders reached the shore, and the polers had just got their footing when the coach turned over. <i>(1914,History of North Otago,169,1978 ed.</i>)

>  ~ v.t.-n. the old river-bed in which gold is found.-v.t. to strike the lead, to find a river bed containing gold; to succeed. -lead'ers n. pl. of a bullock team, the leading pair. <i>(1929,Whitcombe's Etym. Dict. Aust-NZ Suppl.,7</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> leading bullock

>  <b>BUTLER</b> In the small river Ashburton ... we had a little misunderstanding [105] with the bullocks; the leaders ... slewed sharply round, and tied themselves into an inextricable knot with the polars [sic], while the body bullocks by a manoeuvre not unfrequent, shifted, or as it is technically termed, slipped, the yoke under their necks, and the bows over; the off bullock, turning upon the near side, and the near bullock upon the off. <i>(1863,First Year in Canterbury Settlement,ch vii 104</i>) [NOTES: 1860 in 1863]

>  <b>COOPER</b> The movements, so to speak, of the leaders are very eccentric. <i>(1867,Digger's Diary,9,1978 ed.</i>)

>  <b>MEREDITH</b> They [grasshoppers] actually stopped a bullock-team this week. The big-winged fellows kept flying into the faces of the leaders ...; and the `bullocky' had to take his team back to the homestead and unyoke them. <i>(1875,Adventuring in Maoriland in the Seventies,34,1935 ed.</i>) [NOTES: c1872 in c1875 Meredith Adventuring in Maoriland (1935)]

>  <b>WILSON</b> They yoked up the Bullocks today, after some trouble they got the leaders and two behind them in but drove the others. <i>(May 13, 1874,Diary</i>) [NOTES: 1874 May 13 J.G.Wilson Diary in 1973 Wierzbicka Wilson Family 190]

>  <b>WHITWORTH</b> I let my leaders go, and jumped down and cut wheelers [sic] traces and let them go too ... <i>(1875,Cobb's Box,6</i>)

>  <b>CHAMIER</b> ... he could [with a whip] ... tickle the ear of the leader, or flip a fly from off the rump of a poler. <i>(1891,Philosopher Dick,9</i>)

>  My ploughman upset his dray ... The leaders got away leaving the shafter on his back. <i>(Nov 20, 1905,Chudleigh Diary,430,1950 ed.</i>)

>  Leaders. - The two front bullocks of a team, which had to be specially broken in for this work. (Butler A First Year in Canterbury Settlement], p.95) <i>(Nov 4, 1933,Press, The,15</i>) [NOTES: Acland Glossary]

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> In 1867 there were many wagons on the road bearing huge freights from Dunedin to the diggings, but Thomson still adhered to his faithful dray with its tilt over it, and drawn by three horses, one shafter and two leaders. <i>(1947,Pioneer Recollections,2,1956 ed.</i>)

>  <b>OWEN & PERKINS</b> (Joe Gasparich, Northland kauri country) The pair in front [160]were the leaders; they answeredcommands immediatelly ... At the back of the team were the `chainers'. <i>(1986,Speaking for Ourselves,159</i>)

>  <b>OWEN & PERKINS</b> It was interesting to watch the leaders. The pair in front [sc. of the bullock team] were the leaders, they answered commands immediately, and the bullocky could get them to do all sorts of things. <i>(1986,Speaking for Ourselves,159</i>)

## leading dog
 
 <i>n.</i> a dog used to go in front of a mob to control its speed and stop it breaking or stampeding (cf. lead v and n3.) [station]

>  <b>SCOTT</b> We had no `leading' dog <i>(1897,How I Stole 10,000 Sheep,9</i>)

>  Leading Dog.- A dog trained to run ahead of a mob of sheep to keep them steady. Sheep drivers always use them; but hill shepherds do not like their dogs to learn to lead, as it is supposed to make them slack in pulling sheep. (C.) <i>(Nov 4, 1933,Press, The,15</i>)

>  <b>MASTERS</b> First of all, on account of his colour, I would pick up Snow, one of Athol's three grand leading dogs. <i>Ibid.</i> 219 Once all were in, Athol would give a low whistle; the leading dogs would vanish. <i>(1960,Back-Country Tales,218</i>)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> Leading Dog: Some `heading' dogs have a natural aptitude for leading sheep. They are trained to work at the head of a mob of sheep and keep them in check. This is a most useful dog when droving sheep. <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,1/493</i>)

>  Leading dog: Dog used to go in front of a mob to control its speed and stop it breaking or stampeding. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,24</i>)

>  leading dog NZ & Austral. dog used to go in front of mob of sheep to control its speed. <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,425</i>) [NOTES: nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.]



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) [droving or mustering] a dog that leads or works at the head of a mob.

>  <b>RICHARDS</b> At this stage I was able to put my good leading dog out to the front to control the pace of the leaders ... <i>(1989,Pioneer's Life, A,85</i>)

>  <b>MASTERS</b> When trucking time arrived the decoys would be put back inside the paddock and the wild cattle mustered to them .. The leading dogs would take up their position ... <i>(1960,Back-Country Tales,221</i>)

>  <b>WHYTE</b> A slack leading dog is worse than no dog in the lead, for he only 'sucks' the sheep along with him. <i>(1927,Sheep-Dog, The,156</i>)

>  <b>PARKER</b> I wanted to be like one of those digs the drovers call a 'leading dog', one who always runs in front of the flock of sheep ..minding the crossroads and gateways ... <i>(1937,Jack and Boxer,12</i>)

>  <b>SHARPE</b> With a leading dog ahead of the mob, on a road without traffic or open gates, droving was one of the best of fine-weather jobs. <i>(1962,Country Occasions,170</i>)

>  <b>MILLS</b> One day I came upon a large mob of cattle, weary and footsore. the leading dog had little to do. <i>(1966,Huntaway,194</i>)

>  <b>WATT</b> It was called a leading dog and was very clever, leading the sheep along, but holding them up if they went too fast. <i>(1990,Wild Horses & Me,149</i>)

>  <b>PULLEN</b> We had a lead dog now called Steel. [lead dog; leader dog] <i>(1996,On the Road Again,45</i>)



## leading-in
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>CARR</b> Stooking and Leading in. - The oat, wheat, and barley crops are ready ... Driving the binder requires experience. Men are wanted ... for stooking, i.e., picking up two sheaves and placingtheir heads together at an angle of about 45 degrees, 10 to 12 sheaves are generally put in one lot. ... There are ... good and bad stookers after years of experience... Leading in. - When the grain has been long enough in stook, it is loaded on to drays ... and carted to the stack or mill ... The man driving the dray is called the leader-in, and the man who leads him, the forker. <i>(1913,Country Work,13</i>)

## lean-to
 
 <i>n.</i> a small, makeshift dwelling; a rough shack (often with a non-ridged, sloping roof). [The Standard English meaning of `building whose rafters pitch against another, lean on another building or against a wall; a pent house' (OED, 1461) is extant in New Zealand, but equally or more in use is the development `a small, makeshift dwelling; a rough shack (often with a non-ridged, sloping roof).]

>  The house, with lean-to, contained seven appartments. <i>(1851,McLean Papers,ix/169</i>) [NOTES: (Campbell)]

>  <b>CARTER</b> The house [of most of the inhabitants in the 1840s] was divided into two rooms, one on the right the other on the left, and was frequently supplemented with the fashionable and useful lean-to, sometimes constructed at one end, but oftener in the rear of these truly convenient and comfortable colonial cottages, which being generally painted white, looked neat, clean andfresh. <i>(1866,Life & Recollections of an Early Colonist,2/7</i>)

>  <b>MEREDITH</b> He is a bachelor, and has a shack close to our boundary. The shack consists of a `lean-to' to which a house will presumably be added when he takes unto himself a partner for life. <i>(1875,Adventuring in Maoriland in the Seventies,32,1935 ed.</i>) [NOTES: 1872 Oct in c1875 Meredith]

>  <b>MANSFIELD</b> On one side the grass patch was bordered by the scullery and wash-house and over this whitewashed lean-to there grew a knotted vine. <i>(1920,Stories,236,1984 (Alpers) ed.</i>)

>  lean-to n. a room or shed built against the back or side of a house, with a separate sloping roof. <i>(1929,Whitcombe's Etym. Dict. Aust-NZ Suppl.,7</i>)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> `You know what a lean-to is?' ... `No,' they said ... `Well, it's bits you build on the back of a house to make it bigger. ... Lean a new bit on to what was already there.' <i>(1986,Shooting Through,19</i>)

>  They're building a sort of bomb-proof lean-to near the Beehive theatrette, so the PM has been kicked put. <i>(Jul 2, 2005,Listener (New Zealand Listener),20</i>)

>  <b>BAKER</b> They found shelter under a manuka pole and corrugated iron lean-to. <i>(2000,Kokopu Dreams,136</i>)

>  By the time Gordon reaches the rusty, corrugated iron lean-to that serves as the bar it seems nearly everyone has a suggestion, a question or a problem for him to solve. <i>(May 18, 1992,Listener (New Zealand Listener),59</i>)

>  Earlier a lean-to built of boulders at the side of the hut had been pushed over by the Lilybank cattle who smelled the rock salt stored there... <i>(May 1994,NZ Historic Places,47/36</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i>

>  I found Forlesse camped in a tohe-tohe lean-to with four men. <i>(Jan 25, 1851,Journal of Edward Ward,116,1951 ed.</i>)

>  <b>GODLEY</b> ... a funny little `leanto'. <i>(Nov 18, 1851,Letters,265,1951 ed.</i>)

>  Posts of lean-too[o2 struck out] put up. [Nov 9] lean to put up for shearing. <i>(Nov 4, 1859,Puketoi Station Diary</i>) [NOTES: Hocken TS]

>  <b>PHILLIPS</b> cobbing foundation of leanto. <i>(Oct 4, 1867,Journal Kept at Rockwood and the Point,127</i>) [NOTES: check page]

>  <b>BERRY</b> Sometimes the first erection is a `lean-to' - the back part only of that which will one day be a permanent residence. <i>(1880,Farming in North of NZ,24</i>)

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> Cockatoos', or cottage farmers, ... each ... living in a humble `lean-to'. <i>(1889,New Zealand After 50 Years,159</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS & REEVES</b> Their lovely leanto's aspect shocked them [here= a hut with a flat roof] <i>(1891,Double Harness,20</i>)

>  I gaze on the moon through the holes in the roof,/ Which shows that the `humpie' is not weatherproof, / .... some calls it a `lean-to', a `humpie', a `shack'. <i>(1926,Transport Workers' Song Book,123</i>)

>  <b>CHAMIER</b> The men's rooms were mostly `lean-to' sheds <i>(1891,Philosopher Dick,5</i>)

>  <b>ROBERTS</b> I went to a lean-to cottage. <i>(1895,Diary,9</i>)

>  When I was 20, I was enjoying one of the great summers of my life, sleeping on the beach at Karekare in a nikau lean-to on a brass bed I'd found on under a barn on a local farm. <i>(Jun 1999,North & South,66</i>)

>  When I was 20 I was enjoying one of the great summers of my life, sleeping on thebeach at Karekare in a nikau lean-to on a brass bed I'd found under a barn on a local farm. <i>(Mar 1992,Metro,59</i>)

>  There is a lean-to put to the back of the hall which is divided into three rooms - a supper-room in the centre, and a dressing-room at each end. <i>(Jul 21, 1900,Egmont Star,4</i>)

>  <b>GIBBONS</b> The shed had a cow-bail lean-to on one side and two lean-tos on the other. <i>(1988,Recollections (various authors/titles),8</i>)

>  Mr. Simpson added no "costly" buildings save a "lean-to" and a stable in the back-yard. <i>(Oct 8, 1887,Auckland Weekly News,13</i>)

>  The Herd Laddie's original sod whare had become a lean-to. <i>(Dec 7, 1897,Otago Witness,50</i>)

>  "The bathroom" was a euphemism for a roughly built lean-to whose iron roof covered the wash house also, and the bath was an unpainted zinc tub. <i>(Aug 12, 1924,Otago Witness,58</i>)



## leaner
 
 <i>n.</i> A high table, or built-out shelf, in a bar.

>  <b>MORRIESON</b> Sam ... was about to drink his beer when he caught sight ... of ... Joe Voot leaving one of the jug tables known as `leaners' and heading for the back door. <i>(1976,Pallet on the Floor,16</i>)

>  They had noticed a group of persons against a leaner at the rear of the bar. <i>(Nov 24, 1979,Evening Post,12/7</i>)

>  Customers might have been able to watch Sky television but they still downed their beers propped against Formica-topped leaners. <i>(Aug 5, 2004,Dominion Post,B7</i>)

>  The pub leaner - there's another national icon, or used to be.... It's difficult to find a leaner these days. <i>(May 22, 2005,Sunday (Sunday Star Times Magazine),p30</i>)

>  Up till then, New Zealand hotel patrons had a choice between spartan public bars - consisting of lino floors, bare walls and stand-up tables known as leaners - and what was known as the lounge bar, saloon bar or, even more quaintly, the ladies' and escorts' lounge. (In the trade it was commonly called the hens' bar.) <i>(May 12, 2001,Evening Post,31</i>)

>  <b>HAWES</b> The crowd that always half-mooned around the wee stage when Feefi was singing had disintegrated into individual people who had gone back to their leaners to be amazed at how good he was. <i>(2000,Dream of Nikau Jam, The,44</i>)

>  The drinking [in six o'clock swill days]was done standing up, at long bars andchest-high upright tables known asleaners. <i>(Feb 25, 1995,Evening Post,14</i>)

>  [The Paekakariki Hotel] still serves beer in jugs and customers still chew the fat propped against Formica-topped "leaners". <i>(Jun 21, 2003,Dominion Post,F3</i>)

>  <b>HAWES</b> Typical Coast Hotel. Sticky carpet, guacamole walls, grey newspaper portraits of dead race horses, dartboard with double twenty gouged out by use, wobbly formica leaners, waiting drums from a band called Double Fried Beans - and that reminds her of the smell, which reminds her of flatulent coackroaches. <i>(2000,Dream of Nikau Jam, The,76</i>)

## lear/leer
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [lair](../L#lair))



## learner
 
 <i>n.</i> [(shearing)]

>  <b>CHARLES</b> In the shearing shed there is a strict social order, ... Below [the gun shearers, the professionals] are the `learners' - men and boys in their first and second seasons. Then the shed-hands, in order of experience and ability, the `fleecies' who pick up the wool from the shearing board, and the `balers' who man the presses. <i>(1981,Black Billy Tea,27</i>)



## learner's chain
 
 <i>n.</i> [freezing works; cf. monkey chain]

>  The learners' chain is called the monkey chain, and the new chaps are called skulls, from the chaffing at the beginning: e.g., `Look at the skulls on them!', `They've got the right-shaped headfor it'. Learners retaliate by calling the old hands `lavatory chains' or more frequently `shithouse chains'. <i>(May 17, 1951,Personal Communication</i>) [NOTES: Feilding Freezing Works terms p.c. Colin Gordon]

>  ... he shifted Alasdair out of the learners' chain into the professional gang. <i>(Oct 1951,Here & Now,14</i>)

>  <b>SLATTER</b> I'd like to see them ... racing the chain, that remorseless heartless chain. And when the last is scrubbed and the chain is switched off, going down for a shower ... I'd like to see them on thechain. Even the learners' chain. In that narrow cell of concrete with the lambs swinging in quick time up the ramp from the sticking-pen. <i>(1959,Gun in My Hand, A,67</i>)

>  ... went Waingawa [freezing works] and got a job on the learners' chain. He worked his way on to the `gun' chain in a couple of seasons. <i>(Dec 18, 1973,NZ Truth,56/2</i>)

## leased
 
 <i>adj.</i> hired on contract, used of people as well as things

>  For one agency to have nearly half the leased executives is something which shows, I would have thought, not good planning... The Corrections Department was the only other government department in double figures, hiring 13 leased executives at a total cost of $1.05 million... Agencies should avoid leased staff being used in roles where they will be the public face of the agency - either internally or externally - over an extended period... The department had 41 leased contracts, 38 of which were in the IT areas... Generally, to source and retain these people the department has had to enter into leased contracts. <i>(Jul 11, 2001,Dominion,2</i>)

## leather plant
 
 <i>n.</i> Celmisia spp.; =cotton plant, also called leathery celmisia, see mountain daisy [from texture of leaf].

>  <b>HOOKER</b> Leather-plant. <i> Celmisia </i>, various sp. <i>(1867,Handbook NZ Flora (List of Names),766</i>)

>  <b>LINDSAY</b> The extreme toughness of the leaf and the presence of this cottony material [on the under surface] have given rise to the popular [53] colonial name applied to the more familiar species [of <i> Celmisia </i>], - the `Leather' or `Cotton' plant. They are also known partly as the `Native Aster,' - a term applied, however, equally to species of <i> Olearia </i> and <i> Mesembryanthemum </i>. <i>(1868,Contributions to NZ Botany,52</i>)

>  The various species of <i> Celmisia </i>, chiefly known by the settlers as cotton-grass orleather-plant, appear well adapted for our purpose. <i>(1873,TrNZI,6/56</i>) [NOTES: Kirk]



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> <i> C. coriacea </i>, mountain daisy. [1851 TCS. (Torlesse, Cby); 1906 CMN (Andersen); 1851 Torlesse Report Canterbury (And); -->1864 Hooker Handbook NZ Flora (And)]

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> (And) <i> Celmisia coriacea </i> leathery celmisia <i>(1906,Plants of NZ</i>)

>  <b>CHEESEMAN</b> (And) c. <i> C. spectabilis </i>. <i>(1906,NZ Flora</i>)

>  <i> Celmisia spectabilis </i>/ leather-plant <i>(1898,TrNZI,30</i>)

>  The trig. station lies at the south end of the [Hikurangi] mountain-top ... At the foot of these rocks the ground is soft with patches of leather-plant (<i> Celmisia spectabilis </i>), vegetable sheep (<i> Raouilia grandiflora </i>) ... <i>(1898,TrNZI,30/419</i>)

>  <b>KIRK</b> (And) <i> Celmisia spectabilis </i>/ leather-plant <i>(1899,Students' Flora</i>)

>  <b>CHEESEMAN</b> <i> C[elmisia] coriacea </i>, Hook.f. ... Cotton-plant; Leather-plant. <i>(1906,Manual NZ Flora,310</i>)

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> `Probably commonest sub-alpine form' <i>(1940,Plants of NZ,454</i>)

>  <b>MARTIN</b> What is regarded by many as the finest and noblest [<i> Celmisia </i>] species is that sometimes termed the COTTON PLANT (C. coriacea) or LEATHER PLANT, in several of its numerous forms. <i>(1929,Flora NZ,263,1961 ed.</i>)



## leather-leaf
 
 <i>n.</i> i> Senecio rotundifolius , =muttonbird scrub, also leathern-leaf. [[a1927 Ver. (A). =Vernacular names supplied by B.C. Aston (And) Senecio rotundifolius leathern-leaf; also leatherleaf, also leather leaf] (See [muttonbird scrub](../M#muttonbird-scrub))

>  <b>MATTHEWS</b> <i> Senecio rotundifolius </i> leather-leaf <i>(1905,Tree Culture in NZ</i>) [NOTES: AND]

>  <b>MATTHEWS</b> (Vernacular) (Andersen) <i> Senecio rotundifolius </i> leather-leaf <i>(1926,Tree Culture in NZ</i>)

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> S. rotundifolius is the mutton-bird scrub of Stewart Island. Its leaves are much used by tourists for post-cards, the white tomentum of the underside affording a suitable surface for writing. <i>(1940,Plants of NZ,477</i>)

>  <b>PICKERING</b> [caption] <i> A closely grown mat of leafs from a high alpine shrub known as `leatherwood' or `leatherleaf'. Ruahines. </i> <i>(1988,Hills, The,40</i>)

>  <b>MARTIN</b> Another daisy shrub growing in great abundance round the coasts of Stewart Island is that known as the LEATHERWOOD or MUTTONBIRD SHRUB (<i> S[enecio] puffini </i>), the leaves of which were at one time frequently used [211] as postcards ... . <i>(1929,Flora NZ,210,1961 ed.</i>)

>  The children attending the Pine Hill school assembled at 9.30 a.m., and, after a few remarks by their teacher (Mr J. Smith), they commenced the work of the day by planting a poplar, three native holly, two leatherleaf, a maple, two willows, and a grass tree. <i>(Aug 6, 1892,Otago Daily Times,3</i>)

>  <b>Mark Pickering</b> Leatherwood or leatherleaf or leatherjacket (Olearia colensoi) often gets a mention in tramping club newsletters. It is an endemic subalpine shrub in the Ruahine Range, and provides a formiddable barrier between the bush and the tussock tops. <i>(2010,Huts,319</i>)

>  <b>S.G. Macintosh</b> ..light green patches of leather-leaf fringing the bush line... <i>(Feb 15, 1930,Tararua Tramper,vol 2, no. 4</i>) [NOTES: per e-mail to Dianne from John Thomson 13 Dec 2010]

>  Apparently this has all been eaten out early, and the remarkable variety of vegetation attacked may be due to food shortage. On scrub line: Leatherleaf (Olearia colensoi) and pimelia. On tops: Tussock, Spaniard, flax, asteha, and danthonia. <i>(Jan 11, 1934,Evening Post,7</i>)

>  FARE, REYNOLDS, & CO are instructed to sell by auction: Apple, pear, and plum trees .. pampas grass, myrtle, escalonia, leather leaf, cryptomaria, wellingtonia, &c, &c. <i>(Jun 13, 1896,Otago Daily Times,8</i>)

>  H ERNEST LEIGHTON has received instructions from Mr. W. B. Hardy, grower, Lower Hutt, to sell, as above - Ake ake, clematis, broom .. lilac, honeysuckle, spiraea, deutzia, leather leaf, creepers, snowberry .. and other choice varieties of hedge and garden plants. <i>(Jun 3, 1908,Evening Post,12</i>)



## leather-leaf fern
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Pyrrosia eleagnifolia</i>

>  <i>Pyrrosia serpens</i> LEATHER-LEAF FERN ... Fronds thick, leathery, green above ... <i>(1982,Stewart Island Plants,456</i>)

>  <i>Blechnum blechnoides, B. durum, Asplenium appendiculatum</i>) and the shore spleenwort (<i>Asplenium obtusatum</i>) can all be found in damp seepages on exposed coastal cliffs, while, slightly further from the sea, the leather-leaf fern (<i>Pyrrosia eleagnifolia</i>) climbs over bare rocks by means of its long creeping rhizomes. <i>(Jan 2001,NZ Geographic,No. 49/73</i>)



## leatherjacket
 
 <i>n.</i> a fish; and a tree? (not DNZE)

>  <b>MEREDITH</b> My camp [on a Wairarapa Sheep-run] is in a small patch of `leather-jacket' or `lace-bark' trees. ... [28] Four corner posts (round leather-jacket) were sunk in the ground ... <i>(1875,Adventuring in Maoriland in the Seventies,27</i>) [NOTES: 1872 Oct in c1875 Meredith]

>  ~ n. a tree; a fish; a kind of pancake. <i>(1929,Whitcombe's Etym. Dict. Aust-NZ Suppl.,7</i>)

>  ~ n. small edible fish with thick skin; various trees with tough bark; the pigfish; kind of pancake. <i>(1966,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),619</i>)

>  leather-jacket n. small edible fish with thick skin; various trees with tough bark; pigfish; kind of pancake. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),12</i>)

>  <b>LORETZ</b> Leather jackets were a nuisance, as they are clever enough to take the bait without pulling the line. <i>(1976,Moments of Life,43</i>)

>  All around us the demoiselles and leatherjackets hover and watch. <i>(Nov 1, 2003,Dominion Post,E9</i>)

>  Leatherjackets, kina and skates. No, it's not some gang of spiky-haired youths, but fish species that could become lucrative export earners. <i>(Jan 28, 2003,Dominion Post,C1</i>)

## leatherwood
 
 <i>n.</i> A Stewart Island shrub with large leaves, helping to form muttonbird scrub. [AND <i> Eucryphia </i> spp. 1903.]

>  The track becomes a corridor through head-high leatherwood and coprosma... Colenso loved leatherwood's "muscular beauty". <i>(Dec 20, 2003,Dominion Post,E8</i>) [NOTES: [In the Manawatu]]

>  The ridge walk had become impassable andthey had to bush-bash through theleatherwood on the tops. <i>(Jan 9, 1994,Sunday Times (NZ),19</i>)

>  Among the several alpine plants named after him are a speargrass (<i>Aciphylla colensoi</i>), a shrub (<i>Leucopogon colensoi</i>), a grass (<i>Poa colensoi</i>) and the ubiquitous leatherwood (<i>Olearia colensoi). <i>(Sep 2001,NZ Geographic,No. 53/49</i>)

>  <b>MACLEAN</b> They had also come prepared to deal with the other major obstacle, the leatherwood; Jim Nichols wore hockey shin pads, Wally and Frank padded leggings made from sugar sack. <i>(1994,Tararua : the Story of a Mountain Range,154</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> tuupare... stiffly branching shrub or small tree. Leaves leathery...

>  <b>SEVERINSON</b> Above 3000 feet [in Ruahine Mountains] the black beech becomes much more stunted, giving way to belts of the despised leatherwood scrub. This tough bush [grows] at most to only ten feet. <i>(1962,Hunter Climb High,21</i>)

>  <b>SEVERINSON</b> Once I had fought up through the leatherwood out of the chasm the travelling was excellent. [Fiordland] <i>(1970,Hunt the Far Mountain,151</i>)

>  Strictly, there has not been a `leatherwood tree' on the top of Mt. Alpha in living memory, but in the 1930's the alpine shrub zone (consisting principally of Olearia colensoi `leatherwood trees') ... was in the process of being modified by red deer (Cervus elaphus). <i>(1971,VUWTC'71,54</i>)

>  <b>DENNIS</b> It is frequently made difficult by thick patches of hardy leatherwood and turpentine scrub (<i> Dracophyllum longifolium </i> and <i> D. uniflorum </i>) although respite is provided by ... the flowering of mountain heath. <i>(1981,Paparoas Guide,164</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> <i> Olearia colensoi </i> LEATHERWOOD ... Tuupare ...Stiffly branching shrub or small tree. Leaves leathery ... <i>(1982,Stewart Island Plants,98</i>)

>  <b>PICKERING</b> [caption] A closely grown mat of leafs from a high alpine shrub known as `leatherwood' or `leatherleaf'. Ruahines. <i>(1988,Hills, The,40</i>)

>  <b>DAWSON</b> Some of the other shrub species with relatively large thick leaves are <i> Griselinia littoralis </i> (broadleaf), <i> Pseudopanax colensoi </i> (mountain five-finger) and <i> Olearia colensoi </i> (leatherwood). <i>(1988,Forest Vines to Snow Tussocks,166</i>)

## leg
 
 <i>n.</i> [(building)]

>  <b>DEVANNY</b> ... the roof supported by sets of timber, bars placed across the roof, supported by `legs'. <i>(1928,Dawn Beloved,170</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> [(racing)]

>  leg ... 10. N.Z. & Austral. either one of two races on which a cumulative bet has been placed. <i>(1982,Collins Concise English Dictionary,642</i>) [NOTES: nf. 1986 NZPOD; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 281]]



 
 <i>n.</i> A section of a game of forty-fives. [See forty-fives 1979 cit]



 
 <i>v.</i> (Rural) [freezing works] to skin the legs of a sheep carcass.

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> leg: (v) i. in dressing a mutton carcass the initial operation is to flay, or open, the two hind legs. So: to skin the legs of a sheep carcass. <i>(1981,Loughnan Glossary,49</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i> (Rural) [sheepdogs] to nip the legs of sheep during yard work or to lame another dog in a fight.

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> leg: ii. Some dogs, working in the yards, develop the habit of nipping sheep's legs; and some will lame another dog in a fight. In either case this is called legging. <i>(1981,Loughnan Glossary,49</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>v.</i> (Rural) [shearing] to drag a sheep to the board by its leg.

>  Leg (3). To l[eg] a sheep is to haul him from the pen to the board by his hind leg ... <i>(Jan 20, 1934,Press, The  (Acland Glossary),15</i>)

>  <b>BOWEN</b> There are several ways of catching sheep.. Legging the sheep out on the board. <i>(1955,Wool Away!,19</i>)

>  <b>BOWEN</b> There are several ways of catching sheep: ... 2. Legging the sheep out on the board. The sheep is caught by the hind leg, and, with the other hand on the back of the sheep, it is dragged backwards. <i>(1955,Wool Away!,19</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>v.</i> (ref. to rabbits) ? to skin the leg; to release pelt from legs. [(Cf legger n, legging n2..)]

>  <b>SCRYMGEOUR</b> ... the trappers brought [for collection for freezing] on pack-horse their `catch' [of rabbits] of the previous night, slit down the belly line, freed of inside then legged to hamstring and hock, linked in pairs hung on a roughly improvised trestle, over which hessian kept the rays of the sun from contamination. This `underground mutton' was taken to a depot, frozen, then carted to railhead, then to the wharves, and exported to Britain, where ... a ready market was always assured for New Zealand rabbit. <i>(1960,Memories of Maoriland,39</i>)

## leg-rope
 
 <i>n.</i> a rope used to tie the back leg of a cow to restrain it from kicking

>  <b>BUTLER</b> She [the cow] was so quiet that we milked her there and then with no leg rope, and without putting her into the bail. <i>(Oct 4, 1862,Letter</i>) [NOTES: Butler to Acland in 1960 Maling Samuel Butler 58]

>  <b>ELWELL</b> [The cow] kicked out at Ernest, who was trying to get the leg-rope on. <i>(1878,Boy Colonists,235</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> After placing his pail outside the yard he returned and untied the leg rope that secured thebrown Jersey, which ... followed the boy from the bail. <i>(1952,Julian Ware,6</i>)

>  <b>DUGGAN</b> ... dried off all four tits and let the cow out into the race where, taking the legrope with her, she squittered off wild in the eyes. <i>(1981,Collected Stories,199</i>) [NOTES: `Along Rideout Road ...']

>  <b>TURNER</b> All the familiar details of the cowshed are named, the bails, the legrope with which the cow's near leg is tied ... <i>(1966,English Language in Aust. & NZ, The,149</i>)

>  ~ n. the rope itself. <i>(1966,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),619</i>)

>  <b>COWLEY</b> He took the cups from a cow, untied the leg rope, and opened the door of the bail. <i>(1979,Growing Season, The,72</i>)

>  <b>COWLEY</b> He took the cups from a cow, untied the leg rope, and opened the door of the bail. <i>(1979,Growing Season, The,72</i>)

>  leg-rope n. Austral. & N.Z. a rope used to secure an animal by its hind leg. <i>(1982,Collins Concise English Dictionary,643</i>) [NOTES: 1986 NZPOD 427; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 281]

>  <b>CHRISTIE</b> He rose, set the bucket aside, untied the leg-rope, and let the cow out. <i>(1987,Candles and Canvas,9</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) a rope used to fasten a cow's leg back during milking.

>  The adoption of a non-stripping technique and the elimination of leg-ropes from the shed will have other effects besides those mentioned. <i>(Aug 1, 1949,NZ Dairy Exporter,15</i>)

>  I made a rule then that if I couldn't milk without a backing chain or leg rope, I didn't want them. [leg rope] <i>(Sep 20, 2002,Taranaki Daily News,Farming Supplement  7</i>)

>  I consider there's no argument about leg ropes, but with back-chains, there's much more room for debate. <i>(Jun 1, 1957,NZ Dairy Exporter,66</i>)

>  <b>HAMMOND</b> The cow, an Ayrshire called Vivian, became quite hysterical in the end and bashed the bail door out in a marked manner, taking the leg-rope with her. <i>(1966,This Man's Father had my Father's Farm,21</i>)

>  Janet, the old house-cum-nurse cow needs neither leg rope nor bail: she is milked in the paddock. <i>(Aug 24, 1972,NZ Farmer,113</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i> (Rural) to keep one's spouse at home or to keep a person under control; to tie (one) into a relationship [figurative use]

>  She wanted to tempt more drivers to stop in the town. "We might have to leg-rope them yet," Mrs Rollinson said.. <i>(Aug 12, 1999,Taranaki Daily News,20</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> `You don't see much of women, Sim, do you?' `... You want to start picking them over for years before you leg-rope one of them. They're like any other possession. ...' <i>(1956,Sweet White Wine,63</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i> In ?general or US sense, to tie the legs of thrown cattle.

>  <b>ROBERTS, WHS</b> At Macfarlane's the men saddled fresh horses and rode in some cattle which we had a great bother to yard, as everything was new ... The yard was divided into three - one large and two small - but as there was no crush pen, or drafting race, we had to head rope those requiringbranding, and after hauling them up to a corner post, leg-roped and threw them on [44] their side, placing a long lever on their neck and under the lowest rail so that they would not move much ... When released the poor beast is not in an amiable temper ... So anyone in the yard has to climb the rails quickly ..., and the idea that it may catch you before your legs are safely over the cap rail certainly accelerates one's movements. <i>(Dec 18, 1856,Diary</i>) [NOTES: 1856 Dec 18 W.H.S. Roberts diary in 1947 Beattie Early Runholding 43-]



 
<b>1</b> <i>v.</i> (Rural) to attach a rope to a cow during milking.

>  <b>DONEY</b> .. the cow should be taken in hand at calving, baled [sic] and leg-roped ... <i>(1891,Essays on Bush-Farming,9</i>)

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> leg rope: a short rope, not needed bay all cows .. The term may also be used as a verb. <i>(1981,Loughnan Glossary,50</i>)

>  <b>ROBERTS</b> .. we had to headrope those requiring branding and after hauling them up to a corner post, leg-roped and threw them on their side. <i>(1856,Roberts Diary,43</i>) [NOTES: in Beattie (1947)]

>  In less time than it takes to leg-rope a cow, that meal of steak and onions disappeared. <i>(Sep 26, 1925,NZ Dairy Exporter,60</i>)

>  I leg-roped her, scraped the mud and water off her flanks.. <i>(Apr 11, 1960,NZ Dairy Exporter,124</i>)

>  We haven't leg-roped a heifer out of 125 calved and the cups always stay on. <i>(Sep 20, 2001,Taranaki Daily News,20</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>v.</i> to tie a back-leg of a bailed cow at milking to prevent it kicking the bucket. [(mainly dairy farming)]

>  When the milker has bailed-up and leg-roped the cow.. he should.. wash the cow's udder and teats and his hands. <i>(1895,Ann. Rep. Dept of Agriculture,III/88</i>)

>  <b>HENDERSON</b> I fear I would have defied to the death Correspondence School lessons, even had I been leg-roped. <i>(1983,Down from Marble Mountain,153</i>)

>  <b>SARGESON</b> I reminded him of nothing so much as an old cattle-dog, toothless and decayed, yet for ever snapping at the heels of a reluctant dairy-cow, one that persisted in refusing to be bailed-up and leg-roped for the milking. <i>(1984,Hangover & Joy of the Worm,243</i>)

>  <b>OWEN & PERKINS</b> I'd been allowed to leg-rope her - I was a bit nervous about the leg-rope thing - and I got a ;lovely froth on the milk. <i>(1986,Speaking for Ourselves,172</i>)

>  <b>STEWART</b> We were genuinely able to be of some usein the cowshed - helping Dick to bring upthe herd.. from the night paddock,hustling them into the shed, leg-ropingthem.. attaching the machine's strangecups to their fleshy teats. <i>(1983,Springtime in Taranaki,116</i>)

>  <b>FOSTON</b> Each cow was to be carefully leg-roped .... Ted was shown how to `leg-rope' a cow and bail up. <i>(1911,Bell Bird's Lair,36</i>)

>  <b>GUTHRIE</b> ... they first of all leg-roped her ... <i>(1930,NZ Memories,110</i>)

>  <b>FINLAYSON</b> He drives each cow in her accustomed turn from the yard into the bail, leg-ropes her, washes teats with a wet cloth,and settles himself on a stool to milk. <i>(1948,Tidal Creek,28,1979 ed.</i>)

>  <b>STEAD</b> <i>(1963,NZ Short Stories,98</i>)

>  v.tr. to tie an animal by the hind leg <i>(1966,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),619</i>)
