## legging up
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  A dodge by owners that should have been worked out ages ago is the legging up of unfashionable horsemen when they want a horse stopped, and then, later on, when they want to back him, they put up a `big gun.' It is manifestly unfair to the boys, but the latter are compelled to `hook 'em' as they get so little riding that four quid is often very handy to them. <i>(May 21, 1910,NZ Truth,256/2/7</i>)

## leggings-out
 
 <i>n.</i> [child marble players]

>  <b>SUTTON-SMITH</b> In the nineteenth century the ring game seems to have been the most popular marble game. (In more recent years marble games with holes have had the greater role.) There was Big Ring, in which a circle a yard or more in diameter was drawn on the ground; there was the mullibar variation, in which an outsize taw was catapulted amongst the marbles in this circle. There were Little Ring, Liney, Follows, Follow the Taw, Holey, and then, on a less important scale, Butcher's Board, in which the marbles were bowled through the holes in a board, Eye-Drop,Knock-Backs, Leggings-Out (or Leggin's-Out), Span-'Em, and Backits. The last was more usually a girls' game. <i>(1972,Folkgames of Children,174</i>)

## Legislative Council
 
 <i>n.</i> (historical) [AND 1825: now the upper house of a state Parliament]

>  <b>GREY</b> I propose to avail myself of the powers vested in me by the Act 11 & 12 Vict. c.5which empowers me, with the advice of my Legislative Council, to constituteProvincial Councils. <i>(1850,GB Parliamentary Papers (Irish Univ. series),VII 1420/56</i>)

>  Adverting to my despatch, No. 123, of the 24th October 1850, in which Itransmitted.. the draft of a Bill for the constitution of Provincial Councils whichI intended to introduce into the General Legislative Council of these islands. <i>(1851,GB Parliamentary Papers (Irish Univ. series),VIII 1475/18</i>)

>  Provincial Councils Ordinance (Passed the 9th day of July 1851). An Ordinance toprovide for the Establishment of Provincial Legislative Councils. <i>(1851,GB Parliamentary Papers (Irish Univ. series),VIII 179/34</i>) [NOTES: This was passed in Session XI, No.6 Ord. Gen. Ass. NZ.]

>  I issued, upon the 21st May last, the first of the enclosed proclamations forthe purpose of bringing the Provincial Councils Ordinance into full operation inthat province [sc. New Ulster]. <i>(1854,GB Parliamentary Papers (Irish Univ. series),IX 1779/132</i>) [NOTES: Dates from 1852]

>  I issued, upon the 21st May last, the first of the enclosed proclamations forthe purpose of bringing the Provincial Councils Ordinance into full operation inthat province [sc. New Ulster]. <i>(1854,GB Parliamentary Papers (Irish Univ. series),IX 1779/132</i>) [NOTES: Dated 1852]

>  And whereas ... it is further enacted, that in case we shall by any letters patent as aforesaid establish any such new colony or colonies as aforesaid, it shall be lawful for us ... to authorize any number of persons, not less than seven, including the governor or lieutenant-governor of any such new colony or colonies, to constitute a Legislative Council or Legislative Councils for thesame, and that every such Legislative Council shall be composed of such persons as shall from time to time be named or designated by us for that purpose, and shall hold their places therein at our pleasure, and that it shall be lawful for such Legislative Council to make and ordain all such laws and ordinances as may be required for the peace, order, and good government of any such colony as aforesaid, for which such Legislative Council may be so appointed; ... <i>(Nov 16, 1840,Charter for Erecting the Colony of NZ</i>) [NOTES: in 1949 Rutherford ed. Select Documents 8]

>  3. ... we did authorize the governor or the lieutenant-governor for the time being ..., and such other persons, not less than six, as were in the said charter designated, to constitute and be a Legislative Council for the said colony; ... <i>(Dec 5, 1840,GBPP</i>) [NOTES: Instructions to our trusty and well-beloved William Hobson ... in GBPP House of Commons 1841 (No.311) 35]

>  The legislative council of New Zealand is composed entirely of men interested in one single settlement, that of Auckland. <i>(1843,NZ Portfolio,128</i>)

>  <b>RUTHERFORD</b> ... it shall be lawful for Her Majesty ... to constitute ... a General Assembly in and for the Islands of New Zealand, to be called the General Assembly of New Zealand, which said General Assembly shall consist of and be holden by the Governor ..., and a Legislative Council, and a House of Representatives; ... <i>(1949,Selected Documents,20/VI</i>) [NOTES: 1846 Aug 28 An Act to make further provision for the government of the New Zealand Islands (9 and 10 Vict. c. 103) in 1949 Rutherford ed. Select Documents]

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> In May, Governor Fitzroy announced to the Legislative Council at Auckland, his scheme of `ways and means'. <i>(1848,Handbook for New Zealand, The,77</i>)

>  Under the 1852 Constitution Act a House of Representatives of thirty-seven members, elected on a broad franchise, and a Legislative Council of sixteen nominated members, were summoned tomeet at the seat of government in Auckland by Colonel Wynyard, the senior military officer, ... <i>(1856,Journal of Henry Sewell,I-72,1980 ed.</i>)

>  It gives me quite a chilly sensation, - the thought of you taking a seat in [168] that quiet, sleepy, unknown Legislative Council. <i>(1865,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,2/167,1960 ed.</i>) [NOTES: Mary Richmond]

>  <b>BARLOW</b> ... a Legislative Council, consisting at present ... of a Speaker ... and forty-six members, each member being appointed for life ... [fn] The Legislative Council is supposed to correspond with the House of Lords at home, but it is called out here by the irreverent, the Old Man's Refuge. <i>(1898,Kaipara and Waitemata Echo,177</i>)

>  <b>HOCKEN</b> So passionate elsewhere was the popular feeling that with difficulty did Sir George Grey equip the new Legislative Council at [117] Wellington with its non-official members - `nominees' theywere called, a term which indeed became one of reproach. <i>Ibid.</i> 140 For the government of the Colony as a whole there were constituted the two Houses which at present exist - the Legislative Council and the House of Representatives, together constituting the General Assembly. The councillors were nominated by the Governor, in this respect resembling the old nominee system. They held their seats for life. As showing the fear and detestation in which the word `nominee' was held, Mr. Fox and others who assisted in passing the measure viewed the relating clauses as the great blots of the Act. <i>(1898,Contributions to the Early History of New Zealand,116</i>)

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> The 1852 constitution was extremely democratic, more so than those then in force in the Australian colonies. The six Provincial Councils, the House of Representatives, and the Superintendents of the Provinces were all elective, though the members of the Legislative Council (the Upper House) were nominated for life by the Governor. <i>(1959,History of New Zealand,87</i>)

>  <b>BASSETT</b> Legislative Council: The upper chamber of the New Zealand Parliament, loosely resembling the House of Lords in Britain, to which members were appointed, not elected. It was abolished in 1949. <i>(1969,Sir Harry Atkinson,31</i>)

>  <b>McINTYRE</b> At 2 p.m. assembled in the Chamber of the Legislative Council both houses together; higgeldy piggeldy - an unseemly arrangement. <i>(1980,Journal of Henry Sewell,II/25</i>) [NOTES: Quote from SEWELL 1854]

>  <b>JACKSON</b> The Legislative Council.. is acknowledged to have fairly won for itself the appellation of the "Council of Old Ladies." <i>(1972,NZ Legislative Council,34</i>) [NOTES: Quote from nelson Examiner, 6 Sep. 1854]

>  No sane man can suppose that any one member of the Cabinet believed that in advising his Excellency to call Mr. Martin to the Legislative Council, he was securing an addition to that body of one who was more fitted than his fellows to fill so responsible and honorable an office. <i>(Jul 26, 1878,NZ Times,2</i>)

>  The Legislative Council had taken two days to consider whether or not a dog should be killed while they only took three minutes to consider whether or not the Constitution should be destroyed. <i>(1878,NZ Parliamentary Debates,29/274</i>)

>  The Legislative Council is the sort of red-herring which is sometimes found very useful. <i>(1885,NZ Parliamentary Debates,51/498</i>)

>  Mr. O'Conor asked the Premier, whether he will, this Session, introduce a bill to enable the people of the colony to elect members of the Legislative Council, either by popular election or by the local governing bodies. <i>(1892,NZ Parliamentary Debates,75/137</i>)

>  I think it is time we should make the Legislative Council a more useful body or else wipe it out altogether. <i>(1903,NZ Parliamentary Debates,127/61</i>)

>  This Bill is brought in for the purpose of creating a Legislative Council consisting of rich men, who are naturally Tories. <i>(1914,NZ Parliamentary Debates,170/630</i>)

>  The second clause is to abolish the Legislative Council. <i>(1949,NZ Parliamentary Debates,286/1341</i>)

>  I say now that it is a policy of the Government to abolish the Legislative Council as we have it today. <i>(1950,NZ Parliamentary Debates,289/282</i>)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> The Legislative Council Abolition Bill, introduced in August 1947 by the Leader of the Opposition, was defeated on second reading. <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,II/848</i>)

>  <b>JACKSON</b> The record of the Legislative Council is not one which is likely to inspire confidence in the role of nominated second chambers, particularly in small states. <i>(1972,NZ Legislative Council,212</i>)

>  <b>GISBORNE</b> The Legislative Council in New Zealand has long been looked at by many Radical eyes as a political deformity. <i>(1897,NZ Rulers & Statesmen from 1840 to 1897,228</i>)

>  <b>McLINTOCK & WOOD</b> The late Legislative Council was the second of that name... [The Governor in 1841] had a few officials.., an Executive Council.. and a Legislative Council, consisting of the same officials togetherwith three hand-picked settlers. <i>(1897,Upper House in Colonial NZ,16</i>)

>  From the outset our Legislative Council was fashioned on the British House of Lords, which arose from the Kings Council of the 14th century. <i>(Sep 28, 1992,Dominion,8</i>)

## lemon sole
 
 <i>n.</i> a common NZ flatfish. (See [also lemon](../A#also-lemon))

>  <b>BURTON</b> These much sought-after but relatively rare flatfish [flounder and sole] are generally found on sandy bottoms of shallow inshore areas. There are eight commercial species in New Zealand : the sand flounder, yellowbelly flounder, greenback flounder, black flounder, lemon sole, common sole, brill and turbot. <i>(1982,Two Hundred Years of NZ Food & Cookery,73</i>)

>  <b>SORENSON</b> <i>SOLE, ENGLISH </i> and <i> SOLE, LEMON </i>(a) Scientific name: (i) <i>Peltorhamphus novaezealandiae </i> (ii)<i> Pelotretus flavilatus </i>. (b) Suggested commercial name[s]: (i) English Sole (ii) Lemon Sole. (c) Other common names: None applicable. (d) Principal localities where taken: [south of Gisborne] <i>(1970,Nomenclature of NZ Fish,45</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> SOLE, LEMON Unlike Common Sole the Lemon Sole has no rostral flap, which in the Common Sole conceals the mouth when viewed from the right side of the head. Colour varies, but usually has a distinct yellowish tinge on the upper surfaces which have a camouflaged appearance. <i>(1981,Fisherman's Bible,73</i>)

>  Of the remaining species with two pelvic fish, the Lemon Sole (<i>Pelotretis flavilatus </i>) has a normal snout like the Flounder, and a broadly mottled upper surface, with lemon underside. The others have a rounded anterior end caused by an overgrowth of the snout which in the sole (Plate II. fig. 4), often but quite wrongly called the `English' Sole, entirely conceals the mouth from above, while in the last two to be mentioned the mouth is still visible from above. <i>(1927,Natural History of Canterbury,202</i>) [NOTES: 1927 Archey `Native Fishes of Canterbury' in Speight et al. Natural History of Canterbury 202]

>  Of the remaining species with two pelvic fish, the Lemon Sole (<i>Pelotretis flavilatus </i>) has a normal snout like the Flounder, and a broadly mottled upper surface, with lemon underside. The others have a rounded anterior end caused by an overgrowth of the snout which in the sole (Plate II. fig. 4), often but quite wrongly called the `English' Sole, entirely conceals the mouth from above, while in the last two to be mentioned the mouth is still visible from above. <i>(1927,Natural History of Canterbury,202</i>) [NOTES: 1927 Archey `Native Fishes of Canterbury' in Speight et al. Natural History of Canterbury 202]

>  <b>SHERRIN</b> LEMON SOLE (see TURBOT). <i>Ibid.</i> 304 <i>Ammotretis rostratus </i> Gunther Lemon Sole <i>(1886,Handbook Fishes of NZ,59</i>)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> In England, the name is applied to an inferior species of <i> sole </i>. In New South Wales, it is given to <i> Plagusia unicolor </i>, Macl., of the family <i> Pleuronectidae </i> or Flat-fishes. In New Zealand, it is another name for the New Zealand <i> Turbot </i>(q.v.). <i>(1898,Austral English,267</i>)

>  <b>DRUMMOND & HUTTON</b> The lemon sole and the common sole, however, have two, and the latter has a rounded snout. <i>(1902,Nature in NZ,73</i>)

>  <i>The Lemon Sole </i> (Ammotretis rostratus). This fish is popularly known as the lemon sole, to distinguish it from <i> Peltorhamphus novae-zealandiae, Gunther, which the local fishermen call the English sole. The names are somewhat unfortunate, seeing that they are applied to totally different species in Britain. </i> <i>(1906,TrNZI,38/543</i>) [NOTES: (Thomson, Otago)]

>  <b>38/551</b> <i>Ammotretis rostratus </i>, Gunther. Locally known as the `lemon sole'. <i>(1906,TrNZI</i>) [NOTES: (Thomson, Otago)]

>  Lemon Sole -<i> Ammotretis rostratus, Peltotretis flavilatus.</i> <i>(1911,Records of the Canterbury Museum,1/197</i>) [NOTES: Waite]

>  AMMOTRETIS GUNTHERI Hutton. Brill. Received in Wellington from Timaru and sold as `lemon sole.' The supply is small, increasing towards the end of summer. ... Distribution: South Island and Cook Strait. ... PELOTRETIS FLAVILATUS Waite.<i> Lemon Sole </i>. Sold as `brill' in Wellington. Mr. Adams informs me that it is common in Dunedin markets during midsummer, when examples are secured off Otago Heads in about 15 fathoms. <i>(Jun 1921,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,4/122</i>) [NOTES: Phillipps Notes on the Edible Fishes]

>  <i>Pelotretis flavilatus </i> Waite. A common species, usually known as `lemon sole'. <i>(1913,TrNZI,45/233</i>) [NOTES: (Thomson, Otago)]

>  21. <i>Pelotretis flavilatus </i> Waite. Lemon Sole. <i>(1929,TrNZI,61/143</i>) [NOTES: (Young)]

>  <b>PHILLIPPS</b> <i>Pelotretus flavilatus </i>Waite. LEMON SOLE. <i>(1927,Bibliography of NZ Fishes,29,1971 ed.</i>)

>  <i>Pelotretus flavilatus:</i> Lemon sole. <i>(1936,Handbook for New Zealand, The,72</i>)

>  <b>68/408</b> <i>Pelotretus flavilatus </i>Waite. Lemon sole. <i>(1938,TrRSNZ</i>) [NOTES: Graham Fishes of Otago Harbour]

>  <b>POWELL</b> A related species [to the SOLE (<i>Peltorhampus novaezelandiae </i>)] is known as the Lemon-sole,<i> Pelotretus flavilatus </i>. <i>(1947,Native Animals of NZ,67</i>)

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> Lemon Sole <i> Pelotretis flavilatus </i> Waite <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,405</i>) [NOTES: 1955 Whitley Namelist in 1956 Graham NZ Fishes 405]

>  [Flatfish submitted for opinion included] (1.) The flounder, or patiki, wrongly named `plaice,' about one-tenth adult size. (2) The turbot sole, wrongly [559] named `brill,' about one-fortieth its adult size: ... (3.) The lemon sole, wrongly named `flounder,' ... (4.) The New Zealand sole ... <i>(1901,TrNZI,33/558</i>)

>  <b>DOOGUE & MORELAND</b> LEMON SOLE ...<i> Other names: Pelotretis flavilatus </i>; New Zealand lemon sole. <i>(1966,Sea Anglers' Guide,219</i>)

>  <b>AYLING</b> <i>LEMON SOLE Pelotretis flavilatus </i> <i>(1982,Collins Guide,312,1984 ed.</i>)

>  More rounded than the New Zealand sole is the lemon sole which is found all around New Zealand but is more abundant in the South Island. <i>(Dec 8, 1982,Evening Post,37</i>)

>  <b>PAUL</b> <i>Lemon sole Pelotretis flavilatus </i> ... Known only from New Zealand, but named from a similar European species. <i>(1986,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,140</i>)

>  [species name] lemon sole .. [scientific name]<i> Pelotretis flavilatus </i> .. [other name(s)] - <i>(Jul 23, 1986,Codes for Species in NZ Waters</i>)

>  Take any white fish to taste (sole or lemon sole is the best) and fillet it. <i>(Aug 19, 1911,Evening Post,11</i>)

## lemon-squeezer
 
 <i>n.</i> A peaked hat formerly worn by all NZ soldiers and now occas. worn in ceremonial parades.

>  <b>IRELAND</b> [The Yanks] wore hats rather than the lemon-squeezers worn by our own troops. <i>(1998,Under the Bridge & Over the Moon,82</i>)

>  <b>STEWART</b> It may be noted that during this period of training [c.August 1916] the Division formallyadopted the method of wearing their felt hats with brim horizontal and crown peaked. <i>(1921,NZ Division,64fn</i>)

>  [Advt] Army lemonsqueezer Hats 1/- <i>(Oct 14, 1952,Evening Post,11</i>)

>  When news reached the New Zealand Troops in Korea that the Army proposed to experiment with the Canadian-type ski cap as a replacement for the beret, or `flying saucer' ... The men of K Force want the peaked felt hat or `lemon squeezer' when they are on leave. ... The officers themselves do not wear berets when they go on leave. They wear the field service cap, or `cheese cutter'. ... Members of K Force who were in the 2nd N.Z.E.F. particularly want the `lemon squeezer' for leave. They know it is the distinctive New Zealand badge. <i>(Feb 3, 1953,Evening Post,12</i>)

>  <b>SLATTER</b> ... the young soldiers with the neatly-pressed uniform and the level brim on his lemon-squeezer. <i>(1959,Gun in My Hand, A,48</i>)

>  lemon-squeezer n.(Coll.) N.Z. soldier's peaked hat. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),12</i>)

>  n.(colloq). New Zealand solider's peaked hat. <i>(1966,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),619</i>)

>  THERE were lemon squeezers, high ostrich feather busbies ... one horse hair wig and peakedhats ... <i>(Jul 19, 1977,Dominion,1/5</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> It looked like a lost soldier's hat. I can see it now, a stained old lemon squeezer made of khaki felt. <i>(1984,South Pacific Street,7</i>)

>  <b>PUGSLEY</b> Malone's Wellington Battalion wore their brimmed felt hats with four distinctive dents, laterknown as the `Lemon Squeezer'. Malone instituted this headdress in his Territorial Battalion, the 11th Taranaki Rifles, and on his appointment to command the Wellington Battalion of the NZEF made this the headdress in the battalion. In 1916 this was adopted by the New Zealand Division. It is still worn in the NZ Army on ceremonial occasions. <i>(1984,Gallipoli: the New Zealand Story,56fn</i>)

>  lemon-squeezer ... peaked hat (formerly) worn by NZ and Australian soldiers. <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,428</i>) [NOTES: nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 282]

>  There were actors with lemon squeezers. Weren't real soldiers, not like he was. <i>(1987,Metro Fiction,89</i>) [NOTES: Brown Beachcombers]

>  <b>JACKSON</b> ... I gazed at his placid face under the army lemon-squeezer ... <i>(1988,Rainshadow,5</i>)

>  <b>HALL</b> And we send over the boys in lemon squeezers and pretend we're fighting for freedom. <i>(1980,Prisoner of Mother England,61</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> lemon-squeezer: peaked Kiwi infantry hat, still worn in ceremonial parades. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,69</i>)

>  <b>McLEAN</b> As officer commanding the school cadet corps, 'Blobs", wearing the famous lemon-squeezer hat (sometimes back to front) was a force not to be reckoned with. <i>(1990,Otago Boys' High School,13</i>)

>  <b>HALL</b> And we send over the boys in lemon squeezers and pretend we're fighting for freedom. <i>(1980,Prisoner of Mother England,61</i>)

>  The first photograph is one taken at anannual territorial camp of the 11thTaranaki Rifles Regiment at Hawera about1912 and shows the C.O. of the Regiment,Lieut.-Colonel W.G. Malone wearing thepeaked slouch hat with the four dents init. This was our headdress on specialoccasions... We certainly entered AwapuniCamp wearing the Lemon Squeezer...[Malone] stated at Awapuni that all unitsof the Wellington Infantry Regiment wereto wear their slouch hats after the styleof his prior regiment, viz. the 11thTaranaki Rifles. <i>(May 1957,RSA Review,15</i>)

>  <b>IRELAND</b> We even chose to ignore your blushes / as you signed the girls' autograph books / in your khaki sandpaper-suit / and King-of-the-Mounties lemon-squeezer - <i>(1987,Kevin Ireland : Selected Poems,103</i>)

>  <b>HENDERSON</b> As I looked his clothes changed to.. hobnailed boots and an old army lemon-squeezer complete with airholes. <i>(1967,Return to Open Country,196</i>)

>  And so a monument of a World War I soldier - complete with "lemon squeezer" hat and rifle - stands on the hillside behind the church... <i>(Sep 2002,NZ Geographic,No. 59/24</i>)



## lemon-squeezer hat
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Lieutenant-General Sir Leonard Thornton in a "Mount Egmnont shaped lemon squeezer hat" is equalled by Major-General Brian Poananga.. parrying a soldier's difficult question in the presence of Field-Marshal Slim with a direct quotation from one of the field-marshal's books. <i>(Nov 29, 1986,Listener (New Zealand Listener),72</i>)

>  The majority of replies received have been in favour of the retention of what is now termed the Lemon Squeezer style, the hat which was formerly worn by the Territorials... This type of hat came into being at a Territorial camp held at Takapau in May 1914, when all units wore the slouch hat turned up at the left side... [Colonel Malone said] "You have a hat that.. resembles the peak in your district... You will now carry the badge of Mt. Egmont on your collar and hat and a replica of the peak on your head." <i>(May 3, 1948,Southern Cross (Wgton),3</i>)

>  <b>CAMERON</b> In the more opulent ...towns, the [war] monument is frequently a warlike, pensive, or courageous figure in the uniform of the World War of 1914-18, with a characteristic `lemon-squeezer' hat. <i>(1965,New Zealand (various authors/titles),1</i>)

>  <b>SUTHERLAND</b> Just then the door to the separator room opened and a thick-set man in palmer nap trousers, gumboots, grey flannel shirt and old army lemon-squeezer hat came in. <i>(1978,Elver, The,39</i>)

>  <b>KING</b> The New Zealanders acquired two new and distinctive features in France. Training in mid-1916 for the Battle of the Somme they were issued with the lemon squeezer hats that were to be their hallmark for the next 40 years; and they came to be widely known as `Diggers'. <i>(1981,New Zealanders at War,143</i>)

>  K-Force are disgusted with the changingof the lemon-squeezer hat. The hat issolely New Zealand's own. <i>(Jan 28, 1953,NZ Truth,19</i>)

>  <b>McKEON</b> Our uniforms consisted of the regulation tunic, slacks, the "lemon squeezer" felt hat. <i>(1971,Fruitful Years, The,255</i>)



## lemon-time
 
 <i>n.</i> orange time: half-time at rugby.

>  ... the practice of both teams leaving the field at half-time is a good one - at Home this being quite unknown, both sides merely taking a breather at `lemon-time' and crossing over. <i>(1908,British Rugby Team in Maoriland,138</i>)



## lemon-tree
 
 <i>n.</i> Pittosporum spp., esp. <i> engenioides, P. tenuifolium </i>; =lemonwood.

>  <b>BENNETT</b> <i> Pittosporum tenuifolium </i> lemon tree. <i>Ibid.</i> 416 The Tafiri, the lemon-tree of the settlers [P. tenui folium] <i> Pittosporum eugeniodes </i> <i>(1860,Gatherings of a Naturalist</i>)

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> <i> [Pittosporum eugenioides </i>] it is often cultivated for its beauty, and is sometimes - though not so often as P. [195] tenuifolium - used to form an ornamental hedge. It is known by a variety of names amongst the colonists, such as <i> Mapau, White Mapau </i>, and even <i> Maple </i>, and <i> Lemon-tree </i>. The Maori name seems to have been <i> Tarata </i>, but it is also called the <i> Mapau </i>. <i>(1907,Plants of NZ,194,1940 ed.</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> The multi-armed and insulator-ed telegraph poles spread round New Zealand by Lemon, the ex-Oamaru director of Telegraphs c. 1890. No usage to hand. [[c1890s] 1987 Apr 6 p.c. George Griffiths (Dunedin)]



## lemon-tree borer
 
 <i>n.</i> a native longhorn beetle, Oemona hirta, which attacks citrus and other trees.

>  <b>Crowe</b> The wood-boring grubs of the 'lemon-tree borer'...tunnel in the branches [of korokio taranga]; in summer these develop into a conspicuous brown native longhorn beetle over 2 cm long. <i>(2009,Which Native Forest Plant?,45</i>)



## lemonade apple
 
 <i>n.</i> [Royal Gala/Braeburn crossed apple type]

>  I was intrigued to see in your column something called a lemonade apple. .. Prove to me that the lemonade apple does indeed exist by telling me where I can find it. .. Ed - These apples are no hoax, they're a new Royal Gala/Braeburn cross by <i>Yummy (yummyfruit.co.nz</i>. This was their first commercial crop and only a few pallets were produced from young trees, but the might be back next season. <i>(Sep 19, 2010,Sunday (Sunday Star Times Magazine),00</i>) [NOTES: editorial and correspondence page]



## lemonfish
 
 <i>n.</i> A tradename for shark flesh used as food, esp. in fish and chips; =dog fish, shark (as fish for eating), =gummy shark, =school shark, =flake. [fishmongers']

>  Te Kawau-tu-Maro, island of the motionless shag, might better have been called Motumuru, for the small spotted shark prized by early Maori - and known today as lemonfish - which was plentiful in the sheltered local waters. <i>(Jul 1998,NZ Geographic,No. 39/99</i>)

>  <b>SORENSON</b> SMOOTHOUND (a) Scientific name: <i> Mustelus antarcticus </i>. (b) Suggested commercial name[s]: Small Flake Fillets (restricted to this species); Smoothound. (c) Other common names: Kini; Pioke; Lemons; Lemonfish; Dogfish; Rig; Silver Strip (fillets); Sweet William; Flake. <i>(1970,Nomenclature of NZ Fish,44</i>)

>  lemons .. [scientific name] -- [other name(s)] This may be RIG [Mustelus lenticulatus] or LEMON SOLE [Pelotretis flavilatus] <i>(Jul 23, 1986,Codes for Species in NZ Waters</i>)

>  <i>(Jun 1921,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,IV/118</i>) [NOTES: Phillipps Notes on the Edible Fishes]

>  <b>PARROTT</b> [Southern Kingfish, <i> Rexea Solandri</i>] is found also in southern Australian waters. In Sydney it is sold as `hake'. It is also known as `Hake' in Auckland and sometimes sold as `lemon-fish'. <i>(1957,Sea Anglers' Fishes of NZ,155</i>)

>  A MERCURY level seven times the legal limit has been found in a sample of shark destined for Wellington fish shops ... The samples would normally have ended up in someone's fish and chip parcel, or would have appeared behind a fish-shop window as `lemon-fish'. <i>(Nov 1977,Dominion,1/6</i>)

>  <b>BRAMBLEY</b> I lose patience with people who prattle about `gummy' sharks when they mean pioke, or dogfish - `lemon-fish' when it reaches the shops. Instead of teeth, the doggy has cross-hatched boney ridges ... <i>(1966,Sea-cockies of the Manukau,43</i>)

>  = sharkflesh <i>(1979,Heinemann NZ Dictionary,619</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> SCHOOLSHARK Not unlike a larger version of the Gummy shark (who grows to about fourfeet) the Schoolshark gets up to about eight feet and inhabits our / coastal waters ... The flesh, like that of the gummies, is quite edible and regularly marketed, usually as Lemonfish. The Schoolshark is usually dark-grey-green above and white below. Known also as Tope and Sand shark they are the only fish who close their eyes when they sleep. <i>(1981,Fisherman's Bible,224</i>)

>  <b>BURTON</b> Of the many sharks found in New Zealand waters, the gummy and school sharks are the most commonly eaten. Both are relatively small in size, and are marketed as lemon fish or flake. <i>(1982,Two Hundred Years of NZ Food & Cookery,79</i>)

>  Omissions [of NZ words from <i> The Macquarie Dictionary </i>] noted included <i> primers, showgrounds, lemon-fish, lolly scramble </i> and <i> section </i>(of land). <i>(Jan 21, 1984,Listener (New Zealand Listener),30</i>) [NOTES: Deverson]

>  [species name] rig .. [scientific name] <i> Mustelus lenticulatus </i> .. [other name(s)] spotted dogfish, gummy shark, kini, pioke, smooth hound, deepsea bream, lemonfish, spotted gummy shark, sweet william, dogi <i>(Jul 23, 1986,Codes for Species in NZ Waters</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> <i> lemonfish </i> euphemism for shark in fish and chip shops. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,69</i>)

## let down
 
 <i>n.</i> the release of milk into the udder system [farming]

>  I had prepared her by washing the teats and, if necessary the udder as well, from a small bucket of water with a cloth, then giving each teat a few pulls to encourage milk let down. <i>(Dec 2003,NZ Memories,No. 45/11</i>)

>  Let down: The release of milk into the udder system. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,40</i>)



## let drive
 
 <i>phr.</i> shoot

>  <b>THOMSON</b> Cocking my rifle and pointing in the general direction of his line of flight, I spotted him through the fern and trees and let drive. <i>(1952,Deer Hunter: The Experiences of a NZ Stalker,135</i>)



## let go
 
 <i>v.</i> to release a shorn sheep 1879.



## let me chat you
 
 <i>phr.</i> let me tell you; take my advice

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> <i>let me chat yer (or you)</i>! Let me tell you!: a New Zealand soldiers' c.p. in the G.W. <i>(1937,Dictionary of Slang,478</i>)

>  <b>BAKER</b> Among ... terms originated in the first Great War we may note ... <i>let me chat you</i>, take my advice! <i>(1941,NZ Slang,50</i>)

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> <i>Let me chat you! </i>Take my advice!: New Zealanders' and Australians': 1915 +. B., 1941, 1942. Cf.<i> chat</i>, v., 3. <i>(1949,Dictionary of Slang Addenda,1098</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> <i>let me chat you</i> allow me to tell you something, WWI Anzac c/p. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,69</i>)



## let on
 
 <i>v.</i> To admit; betray

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> let on . ... 2. Hence, mostly in Australia and New Zealand and from ca1880, occ. to pretend, make believe, give to understand: coll.: orig. dial. >, by 1828, Southern U.S. (Thornton). <i>(1937,Dictionary of Slang,478</i>)



## let one see the races
 
 <i>phr.</i> [cf. stand off the grass]

>  <b>WILSON160</b> `Righto. Righto.' [161] The man motioned with his hand. But the curious townspeople who were pressing up against the truck moved back a mere half inch, so he spoke again with quiet, lazy authority. `Come on. Let your mother see the races.' <i>(1965,Outcasts</i>)



## let rip
 
 <i>v.</i> to let go; to drop; to let (something, someone) go; often as an exclamation: `get cracking; begin!'

>  <b>PHILIPS ROCKWOOD</b> All hands tailing sheep let them rip at night. <i>(Dec 14, 1859,Journal</i>)

>  <b>BATHGATE</b> Most of the [diggers], when they found I would not buy, wouldthrow their picks down and say if I would not buy them I cound take them or let them rip as I pleased. <i>(1881,Waitaruna,139</i>)

>  <b>COTTLE</b> `Let 'em rip' [i.e. let them be; continue their quarrel] - This latter sentence, more brief than polite, was his favourite solution of a difficulty which proved to be a little out of his province. The word `rip', as doubtless most of my readers are aware, is formed of the initial letters of the words' requiescat in pace - rest in peace' <i>(1891,Frank Melton's Luck,133</i>)

>  ... so no further notice was taken of the matter, which was let rip. <i>(Aug 12, 1905,NZ Truth,8-7-5</i>)

>  <b>OWEN</b> I can rub along some how by letting the pressing rip. <i>(1909,Philip Loveluck,175</i>)

>  <b>DRESCHFELT</b> When he's shickered he drives full out the whole time, just lets her rip. <i>(1933,Broken Pattern,130</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> let her rip get something going, often machinery such as car engine; eg `Hang on the brakes with the revs up, then let her rip.' NZA. OED @v2 7 esp. Let her rip! Orig (1859) US; P. angl. ca1875: coll 20c. -->(P. only exclam. Let her rip! : and 19-20c : orig U.S.) <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,69</i>)



## let the hare sit
 
 <i>phr.</i>

>  <b>McGILL</b> let the hare sit ease up, take it easy; Jim Henderson traces to Timaru. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,69</i>)

>  <b>HENDERSON</b> What of the saying "Let the hare sit", and why Timaru? I wondered idly for years. Then someone brought it up, saying hares as well as wallabies abounded around Timaru, and "Let the hare sit" was another way of saying don't do your bun - take it easy - there is plenty of time - <i>piano, piano, si, si</i>, do it tomorrow. <i>(1998,From Top to Bottom : Reinga to Bluff,131</i>)



## let-away
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a free flourishing of a pasture species.

>  <b>LEVY</b> Any let-away of the paspalum during the summer will tend to smother out the rye-white and to give it a more-or-less pure paspalum pasture which then provides poor grazing in the winter and early spring. <i>(1970,Grasslands of NZ,131</i>)



## let-out pen
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Inspected the new wool shed [at Brancepeth] built of totara and black pine ... let out pens on battens 28 x 8. <i>(Sep 2, 1885,Chudleigh Diary,335,1950 ed.</i>)

## level
 
 <i>n.</i> horizontal mining tunnel

>  Level : The name given to the various horizontal drives. (tunnels) <i>(Sep 1999,Ohinemuri Regional History Journal,43 : 14</i>)

>  <b>McARA</b> Level: The `level' usually referred to one of the working levels at the different horizons in the mine. <i>(1978,Gold Mining at Waihi (Glossary),326</i>)

>  <b>BLYTH</b> Levels: Levels are horizontal passages in a mine usually cut at regular intervals as regards their depth from either adit tunnel, or surface, and numbered accordingly. <i>(1935,Gold Mining Year Book,11</i>) [NOTES: Glossary]

>  <b>ANDERSON</b> They led me along a tunnel (level, as they called it), which ran at right-angles from the main drive. At the coal-face two men were working up a ladder ... `They're blowing out tops,' Charlie explained. `Lumps of coal hanging from the ceiling are a danger to men working below'. <i>(1981,Both Sides of the River,45</i>)



## lever press
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a type of wool press.

>  <b>CAIRD</b> Behind the bins again is the press-room, where the wool is pressed into bales either by a screw-press or a lever press. <i>(1874,Notes on Sheepfarming in NZ,23</i>)



## Lewisham/Lewisham hospital
 
 <i>n.</i> [In Australia & NZ a hospital run by the Little Company of Mary, a Catholic order founded in Nottingham, England, by Mother Mary Potter. The name appears to be from the town where the first Australian hospital was established.]

>  He pointed out that the Lewisham hospitalwas a private hospital, not alone for thenecessitous poor, but for the relief ofthose who, if not poor, had no hospitalappliances at their own homes. <i>(Jan 28, 1916,Press, The,3</i>)

>  Lewisham hospitals in Australia and NewZealand have been recently placed on thelist of hospitals that are judged by theAmerican medical faculty to be efficientand up-to-date... This year a gigantic effort is to be madeto build and equip a hospital inWellington for the famous Lewisham nuns,whose splendid hospital record is welland favourably known throughoutAustralia. <i>(Jun 4, 1925,Evening Post,7</i>)

>  Veteran wrestling promoter WalterMiller... and his wife are ill inWellington's Lewisham Hospital. <i>(Apr 15, 1953,Evening Post,10</i>)

>  <b>POUND</b> The gas ring for the Lewisham "cup oftea"! <i>Ibid.</i> 40. A large number of clergy were also present, and for many it was their firstglimpse of the "Lewisham Sisters." <i>(1979,From Oak to Kowhai,13</i>)

>  <b>POUND</b> Three doctors must be mentioned at thisstage as having had an enormous influencein the early days of Lewisham.<i>Ibid.</i> 40. Every year hundreds of patients were treated free of charge at Lewisham. <i>(1979,From Oak to Kowhai,15</i>)

 <i>n.</i> (See [Compare lea](../C#compare-lea))

>  [I] remembered the spindly three-foot bamboos I'd put in four years ago with the Leylands, in the fond hope they would support them. <i>(Aug 8, 2004,NZ Catholic,19</i>)



## Leyner
 
 <i>n.</i> (trademark) a Leyner drill [(goldminers') (also below: Leyner water-feed-drill)]

>  <b>McARA</b> The first axial-water-feed hammer-drills were available about 1910. Leyner drills of this type were by then widely used in tunnels in the United States. They represented a great advance over the piston-type drills and besides proving more efficient reduced the dust problem underground. The Leyners were followed by other makes, notably Holman's, but the name persisted and the common name for the `big machine' or drifter at Waihi remained the `liner'. These drills were worked in a steel cradle about four-and-a-half-feet long, mounted on a feed-screw with a short handle at the rear ... <i>Ibid.</i> 326 Leyner water-feed-drill: The first axial-water-feed-drill used at Waihi. By controlling dust it marked the virtual end of the terrible scourage of phthisis, though many who already had the disease continued to die every year for some considerable time <i>(1978,Gold Mining at Waihi,185</i>)

## Lib-Lab
 
 <i>n.</i> Often pl the Liberal-Labour Alliance of ??. [hist]

>  I want to belong to something that has grit and can fight boldly for its rights, not a Lab-Lib.,Lib-Lab., half-and-half concern that allows it self to be tied up bound hand and foot by the employers' C. and A. Act. <i>(Oct 20, 1911,Maoriland Worker,33/17/1</i>)

>  <b>BELSHAW</b> Ballance's leadership of the `Lib-Labs', as the party was popularly known, until his death in 1893 ... <i>(1947,New Zealand (various authors/titles),92</i>) [NOTES: Airey NZ in Evolution]

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> It [the United Labour Party] comprised a group of Trades and Labour Council officials andex-`Lib-Labs' (Liberal-Labour supporters). <i>Ibid.</i> 303[Colonial-N.Z. glossary]Lib-Lab Member of Liberal-Labour Federation, 1899 (A term also used in England) P: applied to the Liberal-Labour alliances of c1915-1922: 1916+, by 1925 merely hist; cf EDD sb ob. a jelly-like substance; the smooth superstratum of cream, etc. the result of much beating: e.g. all froth and liblab. <i>(1959,History of New Zealand,197</i>)

 <i>n.</i> [hist.]

>  Before the worker decides to support a candidate he should consider the candidate's platform and record. As to Mack's platform, it is a nauseous mixture of Lib.,-Labism and Cold Tea. <i>(Oct 30, 1911,Maoriland Worker,33/17/2</i>)



## Liberal
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> The New Zealand Liberal Party was a world apart from its British namesake. ... The political ideals of the New Zealand Liberals were similar to those of the Fabians and other socialist groups in England; the Nationalist Clubs, the Knights of Labour, and the Populists in the United States; the Labour Parties in Australia. <i>(1959,History of New Zealand,169</i>)



## liberal land policy
 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) a policy requiring the division of large land-holdings and the implementation of a graduated land tax [late 1800s].

>  <b>GREENWOOD</b> What is referred to as the liberal land policy, the compulsory breaking-up of big estates, started with a graduated land tax and then in 1892 a Land for Settlement Act was forced through Parliament against bitter opposition. <i>(1986,Te Waimate,31</i>)



## Liberal-Labour
 
 <i>adj.</i>

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> In 1898 a Trades and Labour Conference resolved that a Labour Party should be set up. Seddon tried to forestall them by founding the Liberal-Labour Federation, but he was unable to satisfy Labour's demands for more radical legislation without offending his middle-class and farmer supporters. <i>(1959,History of New Zealand,197</i>)

>  [Hdg] Roskill Electorate. A Liberal-Labour Breakaway. <i>(Jun 4, 1925,Evening Post,6</i>)



## liberate
 
 <i>v.</i> [WW2. wouldn't it liberate you!, Constr: `Wouldn't it (rock you) etc'; and WW2 `liberate': apparently popular in Italy when `liberate' first became fashionable.]

>  Wouldn't it liberate you? <i>(1946,Johnny Enzed in Italy,45</i>)

>  <b>SLATTER</b> `You know, Spandau, this Welsh team isn't at full strength,' I tell him. `Lili Maleesh,' says Spandau, when he's downed another swig. ... `Where were they when I liberated that plonk factory back of Cesena?' <i>(1970,On the Ball,137</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>v.</i> (Rural) [dog trials] to set free three sheep for a competition run. (See [slip](../S#slip))

>  I spent most of my time liberating on the long pull, a course we shorten for our local maiden day. <i>(Jun 1984,NZ Journal of Agriculture,13</i>)

>  <b>GORDON</b> The young fellas were liberating this day and they were waiting for W.V. <i>(1998,Three Sheep and a Dog,206</i>)



## liberating
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) [dog trials] the freeing of three sheep for a competition run.

>  <b>GORDON</b> Liberating in a group can be quite fun and it certainly was at Martinborough when I was one of several involved in the job. <i>(1998,Three Sheep and a Dog,197</i>)



## liberation
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) [dog trials] the freeing of sheep from a pen in a competition run.

>  <b>GORDON</b> The three sheep stand waiting up to 800 metres away, in the liberation area, which is also known as the hook or 'the strings.' <i>(1998,Three Sheep and a Dog,107</i>)

>  <b>GORDON</b> When the veterans scrutinized the hill next morning they saw that the hook reached almost to the thistle, so all was well. As a result, the liberation worked much better for the rest of the trial. <i>(1998,Three Sheep and a Dog,197</i>)



## liberation pen
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) [dog trials] a small yard where competition sheep are kept before use in a run.

>  <b>GORDON</b> I remember the zigzag course, no longer used, where the liberation pen was jammed under a tree ... <i>(1998,Three Sheep and a Dog,151</i>)



## liberator
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a person who sets free sheep for use during dog trial contests [mainly N.I.] (See [slipper](../S#slipper))

>  I remember hearing a liberator at Rotorua on the short head telling me about the run-outs of all the dogs while he was putting the sheep out on the mark. <i>(Sep 27, 1973,NZ Farmer,71</i>)

>  <b>GORDON</b> Liberators get good views too, and those include a brief stint on top of Kyeburn's very tall and well-tussocked long pull course. <i>(1998,Three Sheep and a Dog,222</i>)

>  Seeing this [the disappearance of the dog] the liberators quickly grabbed the dog, put it on the landrover and tore down the track round to the bottom of the course. <i>(Mar 1984,NZ Journal of Agriculture,31</i>)

>  Meg went out, had a good look at one of the liberators as she went by.. <i>(Aug 9, 1984,NZ Farmer,90</i>)

>  Liberators (slippers) must ensure that every competitor is given sheep that are sound and in good order. <i>(1991,Constitution of NZ Dog Trial Assoc,32</i>)

>  Behind the scenes the liberators - those people who with help from their dogs put three sheep out on the mark for every competitor - had to contend with some testing weather conditions. Weather and sheep aside, the weekend's liberators did a fine job. <i>(Apr 19, 2001,Taranaki Daily News,19</i>)

## licensing trust
 
 <i>n.</i> an elected body set up to sell alcoholic liquor and to distribute the profits to worthy causes. [The Licensing Trusts Act, 1949, seems to be the relevant Act, although the Invercargill Trust seems to have been already in existence [see NZEF Times cit below].]

>  This Act may be cited as the Invercargill Licensing Trust Act, 1944... There is hereby established for the purposes of this Act, a Trust, to be called the Invercargill Licensing Trust. Ibid. 1944/4 23 The functions of the Trust shall be to provide accommodation and other facilities for the travelling public within the Invercargill Licensing District, to establish and maintain hotels and suitable places within the district for the sale or supply of refreshments, to sell and supply intoxicating liquor within the district and establish and maintain premises for that purpose.. and to do all such other acts and things as may in the opinion of the Trust be necessary or desirable having regard to the general purposes of this Act. <i>(1944,NZ Statutes,1944/4 : 19</i>)

>  An Act to Provide for the Constitution, Pursuant to the Determination of the Electors of No-licence Districts, of Licensing Trusts to Conduct Hotels and Sell Liquor; and to Constitute theAshburton and Geraldine Licensing Trust. <i>(1949,NZ Statutes,43/743</i>)

>  Back from Parliament came the Invercargill Licensing Trust Act constituting the Invercargill Licensing Trust - a body corporate to run the hotels of Invercargill on distinctly different lines. <i>(1944,Korero (AEWS Background Bulletin),2(17) : 4</i>)

>  Back from Parliament came the Invercargill Licensing Trust Act constituting the Invercargill Licensing Trust - a body corporate to run the hotels of Invercargill on distinctly different lines. <i>(1944,Korero (AEWS Background Bulletin),2(17) : 4</i>)

>  The two City Council members of the Invercargill Licensing Trust.. were rebuked by the Chairman.. when the desirability of having hotels in the suburbs was being discussed in the city. <i>(Jul 3, 1944,NZEF Times,IV/158 : 2</i>)

>  My friend.. explained that he had in mind something he had seen concerning the Invercargill Licensing Trust. <i>(May 1, 1946,People's Voice,4</i>)

>  There has been some desire expressed up and down the country for licensing trusts. Ibid. 287/2466 The functions of the Trust shall be to provide accommodation and other facilities for the travelling public within the Trust district, to establish and maintain hotels and suitable places within the district for the sale or supply of refreshments, to sell and supply intoxicating liquor within the district and establish and maintain premises for that purpose.. and to do all such other acts and things as may in the opinion of the Trust be necessary or desirable having regard to the general purposes of this Act. <i>(1949,NZ Parliamentary Debates,287/2464</i>)

>  Members were welling up with tears, worrying about licensing trusts. <i>(1990,NZ Parliamentary Debates,505/682</i>)

>  It is generally believed that the Bill will provide for the setting up of a licensing trust on the lines of the Carlisle system. <i>(Mar 22, 1944,Evening Post,6</i>)

>  <b>HALL-JONES</b> In Southland the first brewery in New Zealand was established - and the first Licensing Trust. <i>(1945,Historical Southland,6</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> Invercargill has the oldest and biggest licensing trust. <i>(1985,G'day Country,175</i>)

>  A petition to force a by-election for two new trustees for the Terawhiti Licensing Trust never got off the ground. Organiser Norm Thomas says the petition is a `blue duck'. <i>(Oct 11, 1988,Karori and Western Suburbs News,1</i>)

>  ... seek election to one of New Zealand's licensing trusts ... all members of all trusts seem to get to the annual conference. <i>(Mar 26, 1963,NZ Truth,12</i>)



## lichen moth
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  S.I. Zebra Moth (Daclana egregia) 1970: 3c: definitive issue. (Named Lichen Moth on stamp.) [headlines] The first is the South Island Zebra Moth, also known as the Lichen Moth, a member of the family selidosemidae. This is one of several families of moths whose caterpillars are popularly known as `loopers' or "measuring worms' because they progress with a looping gait. ... <i>(Sep 16, 1972,Press, The,11</i>)



## lick
 
 <i>n.</i> a block of mineral material, dietary supplement; usually a block, that is placed in the paddock for the animals to lick. It may be in the form of a drum with a rotating ball that the animals lick; =cattle-lick; = salt-lick [farming]

>  <b>MACDONALD</b> [Sheep] should ... be given a regular supply of salt or a lick ... <i>Ibid.</i> 121 A salt lick, consisting of 40lb. Liverpool salt, 1lb. sulphated iron, is useful. <i>Ibid.</i> 122 Several licks are recommended as being effective for worms. Thirty parts of Liverpool salt, 3 parts lime, 3 parts flowers of sulphur, and 1 part powdered bluestone, is good ... <i>(1915,NZ Sheepfarming,120</i>)

>  `Bush sickness' is now combated entirely successfully by giving the animals a lick of limonite (hydrated oxide of iron) ground to the finest powder and mixed with salt. <i>(1936,Agricultural Organization in NZ,82</i>)

>  <b>STAPLEDON</b> It is the belief of the animal nutritionists that extreme mineral deficiencies may be made good by providing `licks' containing the ingredients missing. <i>(1928,Tour in Australia and NZ,84</i>)

>  As the sheep exhibited typical symptoms of bush-sickness, a limonite-salt lick was recommended. The use of this lick gave very satisfactory results. <i>(1938,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,20/194A</i>)

>  <b>LEVY</b> It has always been a source of wonderment to me that specialists in animal nutrition frown on proprietary mineral licks. <i>(1951,Grasslands of NZ,336,1970 ed.</i>)

>  <b>FINLAYSON</b> `Perhaps the cows need a bit of lick, Jake. See how they're chewing sticks and other trash lately.' <i>(1948,Tidal Creek,135</i>)

>  ~ A lick is a block of material containing minerals or dietary supplement that is placed in the paddock for the animals to lick. It may be in the form of a drum with a rotating ball that the animals lick. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,5</i>)

>  lick ... NZ & Austral. block of material containing minerals etc. placed in paddock for animals to lick (salt lick). <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,431</i>) [NOTES: see Collins Concise Eng. Dict. 1019, SALT LICK; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 284]



 
 <i>v.</i> to speed, to move at full speed. [intransitive]

>  <b>LANCASTER</b> Lick back along over the hill. <i>(1905,Sons o' Men,195</i>)

>  <b>GASKELL</b> He got the new Ford V8 eh? ... He speed [sic] her along. Boy, can she lick. <i>(1947,Big Game,80</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a proprietary mineral feed block for farm stock.

>  This [dispensing of Epsom salts] is augmented by home-made licks in the paddock and the entrance to the shed. <i>(Apr 1980,NZ Dairy Exporter,15</i>)

>  This cow is very keen on the lick and often puts most of the time she is in the yard licking it...The cow is constantly licking the lick in the yard. <i>(Mar 15, 1943,NZ Journal of Agriculture,161</i>)

>  In the paddock the best way to administer salt is to let the animals have ready access to a lick. <i>(Jun 17, 1909,Weekly News,44</i>)

>  Will you kindly inform me what is used most by experienced flockmasters as a lick for sheep? <i>(Apr 1920,NZ Farmer,558</i>)

>  <b>COOP</b> Many farmers feel much happier if a lick is provided for their stock... <i>(1949,Animal Nutrition,30</i>)

>  I keep shifting the [ewes] around and give them lick but they still di.e. <i>(Jan 27, 1955,NZ Farmer,21</i>)

>  The value of licks is limited, as many animals may fail to take them.. However, licks have a place on hill country properties where topdressing has not hitherto proved feasible. <i>(Mar 1956,NZ Journal of Agriculture,15</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> [hard work'; also adverbial `by hard work'; `great guns': Aust coll ca1888 (Bold) from US (-1861); nf W3 P8 LICKS 3. Big licks hard work: Aust 1888; also adv. by hard work (going it big licks) Aust.] big licks, go in for big licks, to attack or proceed in a vigorous way, to go great guns: some time's derogatory as implying `biting off more than one can chew', putting on a false appearance.]

>  [He] went in for bigger licks. <i>(1865,Dunedin Punch,128</i>) [NOTES: ca1935 common, though perhaps old-fashioned]



 
<b>4</b> <i>n.</i> temper [ca1938 (Havelock) Keep that dirty Scots lick of yours under control.]



 
<b>5</b> <i>n.</i>

>  <b>CROSS</b> ... there were licks of flat country. <i>(1960,Backward Sex, The,8</i>)



 
<b>7</b> <i>n.</i>

>  <b>McGILL</b> lick an ice cream, among 1960s teens. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,69</i>)

Had a discretionbeen left to myself, I should have dsignated an officer by the term `Superintendent'; and I would still recommend the adoption of this designation for the officer administering the government of a province. <i>(Aug 30, 1851,Handbook for New Zealand, The</i>) [NOTES: Governor Grey to Earl Grey in 1949 Rutherford ed. ## lift
 
 <i>v.</i> (Rural) [wool grease] to rise and render fleece ready for shearing.

>  <b>STEVENS</b> It is a traditional opinion that sheep are ready to shear when the wool has lifted and the grease has risen. <i>(1958,Sheep.  Part 1, Sheep husbandry,73</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i> (Rural) to gather stock.

>  <b>RICHARDS</b> I lifted the sheep from the County paddock shortly after daylight. <i>(1989,Pioneer's Life, A,85</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i> (Rural) [sheepdogs] to move sheep on a hillside.

>  <b>KERR</b> Otairi's huntaways were used to lifting sheep; mine to slewing from above. <i>(2000,High Times in the High Country,173</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> a blow [ca1935 I'll give you a lift under the ear. P: 4.punch: colloq.: (-1923); also as verb ca1935 I'll lift you one under the ear. P: Austr. (B 1942)]



 
<b>1</b> <i>v.</i> (Rural) [heading dogs] to move sheep towards a shepherd or handler.

>  <b>HARTLEY</b> The system I have devised ... in the case of excessively stong-eyed dogs, ... gives the trainer greater command where there is likelihood of the dog `setting' sheep and failing to lift or pull. <i>Ibid.</i> 33 Much will depend upon the manner in which the sheep are lifted. ... allow the dog to stand a few moments to familiarize the sheep with his presence before being brought on to lift. <i>(1949,Shepherd's Dogs, The,3</i>)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> see entry HEADING (CLASS) <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,1/493</i>)

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> lift: a dog lifts his sheep when he has completed the head and then approaches them to start them moving. <i>(1981,Loughnan Glossary,50</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) [sheepdogs] the working of sheep from a standing position.

>  <b>HARTLEY</b> A nice steady `lift' with the huntaway standing head-on to his sheep. <i>(1949,Shepherd's Dogs, The,20</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> Such was that dog's way with sheep that when running out - say on the right - he could make his lift from round about three o'clock and yet turn his sheep directly towards me. I have, of course, often heard the argument that the run-out cannot be judged until the dog has lifted his sheep. In other words, provided the sheep turn directly towards the man when `lifted', the dog has finished his head correctly regardless of whether it is short or otherwise. <i>Ibid.</i> 168 (Glossary) Lift: When a dog has completed his head his next job is quietly to start them on the `pull'. This, the first movement in the pull, is known as the lift. To pull is to bring the sheep back to the man. <i>(1971,Ten Thousand Dogs,67</i>)

>  Lift: Action of a dog to move sheep from a standing position. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,25</i>)

>  <b>MILLS ET AL</b> Frequently it is essential that you be able to steady your dog either in his lift or during the pull. <i>(1964,Practical Guide to Handling Dogs and Stock,37</i>)

>  He cast the dog, headed well and after a nice lift had them moving nicely down off the netting. <i>(Jun 1984,NZ Journal of Agriculture,13</i>)

>  Next comes the lift - when the dog begins to move the sheep, to begin the pull. <i>(1991,Constitution of NZ Dog Trial Assoc,5B</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>v.</i> to take (gold) from a lode. [? a development of OED 12 (Scottish) to take up out of the ground (esp potatoes), 1884 [This spec. use is still current in NZ]]

>  <b>DEVANNY</b> They had `lifted' a fair amount of gold [from the lode] <i>(1927,Old Savage,97</i>)



## lifter
 
 <i>n.</i> [Nf OED, EDD, W3.]

>  Seven shots were laid - four lifters and three easers - and 150 lb. of gelignite was used. <i>(Feb 17, 1965,Evening Post,18/10</i>)

>  <b>McARA</b> Lifters: The bottom holes of a round, drilled close to the floor and pointing slightly downwards. Lifters were timed to fire last and threw the heap of rock up into a convenient pile. <i>(1978,Gold Mining at Waihi (Glossary),326</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a shearing comb attachment that prevents a sheep being shorn too close its skin (affording protection to sheep from exposure).

>  The lifter has never been tested in extreme conditions and is detrimental to fine wool harvesting due to length variation (it cuts the wool about). <i>(2000,Wool Grower,Spring 5</i>)

>  Genuine winter combs or winter combs plus lifters must be used. <i>(Aug 2001,In the Paddock,2</i>)

>  Depending on the region, genuine winter combs, winter combs plus lifters, and blades will leave sufficient wool to protect ewes from all except the worst weather. <i>(May 2002,In the Paddock,2</i>)

>  Lifters comprise a plate with sleds that is screwed underneath the winter comb, raising it by 2.5 cm. Lifters are replacing blades in some areas. <i>(Jun 2002,In the Paddock,2</i>)

>  The use of winter combs with attached 'lifters' is becoming more common in colder regions. <i>(2002,Wool Grower,Spring 22</i>)



## lifting the bones
 
 <i>phr.</i> (See [hahunga](../H#hahunga))

>  <b>ANGAS</b> The body is enclosed in a mausoleum of carved woodwork within the `pah' for several months, and at the expiration of this period, the ceremony of `lifting the bones' takes place, which is performed by the nearest relative of the diseased. The bones, after being well scraped and cleaned, are then deposited in a box, and buried. <i>(1866,Polynesia,150</i>)



## light country
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) land with a shallow covering of soil and nutrients [often susceptible to both flooding and drought.]

>  <b>ACLAND</b> Dagnam was very light country and only carried about 1000 sheep. <i>(1946,Early Canterbury Runs,58</i>)

>  The country is light, rainfall abundant. <i>(Nov 5, 1953,NZ Farmer,13</i>)

>  <b>GORDON</b> Light country, it's also drought-prone; in other words, it's a demanding place to run, especially at a substantial stocking rate. <i>(1987,People, Places & Paddocks,70</i>)



## light fires
 
 <i>phr.</i> [(whalers')]

>  <b>GRADY</b> [glossary] Fires, to light - Signals to the whalers at sea in whale-chasers telling them in which direction to go to pursue whales sighted from shore vantage-points. The Peranos had a code of signal fires. <i>(1982,Perano Whalers,227</i>)



## light land
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) land with a shallow covering of soil and nutrients [often susceptible to both flooding and drought.]

>  <b>GUTHRIE-SMITH</b> Tutira It was likewise impossible to change light lands into good lands. <i>(1921,Tutira,III  220</i>) [NOTES: [1999]]

>  Light land is more quickly broken in than a stiff heavy soil; it is much easier to manage at any time, and is usually warm and "early". But it is very "hungry" - that is, it consumes humus and other manures quickly. <i>(Mar 20, 1933,NZ Journal of Agriculture,182</i>)

>  <b>STEVENS</b> The third farm is also in mid-Canterbury and is light land in a light rainfall area but is not under irrigation. <i>(1958,Sheep.  Part 1, Sheep husbandry,25</i>)



## light on
 
 <i>adj.</i> lacking, short of

>  light ... 21. light on. Austral. & N.Z. inf. lacking a sufficient quantity of (something). <i>(1982,Collins Concise English Dictionary,651</i>) [NOTES: nf. 1986 NZPOD]



## light-mantled sooty albatross
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Phoebetria palpebrata</i>, a small dark albatross with a characteristic black eye-ring, breeding on NZ subantarctic islands. [also sooty albatross]

>  <b>Light-mantled sooty albatross</b> <i>Phoebetria palpebrata</i> A 2.75 kg albatross that breeds on high-latitude islands in the Atlantic, Indian and Pacific Oceans. It is rarely seen in coastal waters. <i>(2007,NZ Geographic Poster,No 84 Mar-Apr</i>)

>  Visitors to the Auckland Islands.. usually see sooty albatrosses sitting on their nests, which are in clefts in perpendicular cliff faces, or are on ledges some 30 feet high. <i>(Aug 3, 1922,Auckland Weekly News,17</i>)

>  <b>FORSTER</b> We saw a great Variety of bird this day viz. A white & sooty Albatross (<i>Diomedia exulans & palpebrata</i>he black & sooty Petrel, (<i>Procellaria aequinoctialis & fuliginosa</i>) ... <i>(Jan 28, 1773,Resolution Journal of J.R. Forster, The,2/220</i>)

>  <b>FORSTER</b> We likewise [in the s. Indian Ocean] saw ... a third [species of albatross], less than the others, which we named the sooty, and our sailors called the [92] quaker bird, from its having a greyish-brown colour. <i>(1777,Voyage Round the World,1/91</i>)

>  <b>BULLER</b> DIOMEDEA FULIGINOSA. (SOOTY ALBATROSS.) ... Native name.- Toroa-pango. ... [`This well-known species (the `Cape hen' of sailors), ... is comparatively common in the New-Zealand seas.'] <i>(1873,Birds of New Zealand,296</i>) [NOTES: 1888 2nd edn 2/205]

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> Koputu, ... <i>Phoebetria fuliginosa</i>, sooty albatross. <i>(1917,Dictionary of the NZ Language,103</i>)

>  <b>FALLA et al.</b> LIGHT-MANTLED SOOTY ALBATROSS <i>(1966,Birds of New Zealand,34</i>)

>  26. Phoebetria palpebrata (Forster, 1785) Light-mantled Sooty Albatross <i>(1970,Annotated Checklist of Birds of NZ,20</i>) [NOTES: 1980 edition]



## light-wood
 
 <i>n.</i> Aristotelia serrata (formerly A.racemosa), ?because it grows in the light, when heavy bush cleared =makomako (See [makomako](../M#makomako))

>  The makomako [Aristotelia serrata], or settlers' `light-wood', springs up over the clearings on the Mount Egmont slope. <i>(1899,TrNZI,31/475</i>) [NOTES: Walsh]



## lightbulb changer
 
 <i>n.</i> [basketball]

>  As basketball in this country is growing so is the terminology to describe the sport. ... The guys that really jump, really get high, are called lightbulb changers, nose bleeders and sky walkers, and the best of them can turn the 360 (full circle) or take the alley-oop pass (ball thrown high up by the basket). They can also throw the rock (ball) down or stuff the pill (ball). <i>(Mar 18, 1984,NZ Times,18</i>) [NOTES: J. Dybvig]



## lighter
 
<b>1</b> <i>v.</i> (Rural) to transport wool and other supplies between coastal stations and coastal freighters [1860s to 1940s.]

>  <b>TAIT</b> Supplies were lightered ashore from a small coastal vessel. <i>(1956,Farms & Stations of NZ Vol I,103</i>)

>  <b>TAIT</b> Wire came by sea to Porongahau, where it was lightered ashore and then carried by bullock dray the 16 miles south-west to the farm. <i>(1961,Farms & Stations of NZ Vol III,131</i>)

## lightning fence

## lightning rod
 
 <i>n.</i> (obsolete) a mixture of methylated spirits and whisky and/or raspberry cordial

>  <b>THORNTON</b> [reference Taranaki bush] Bill had had some lightning-rod last night. [FN] Adulterated spirits. <i>(1916,Wowser, The,26</i>)

>  <b>HUTT</b> Also prominent in the newspapers of the time were sensationalist reports that the King Country prohibition was being widely flouted and that "lightning rod" (a mixture of methylated spirits and whisky and/or raspberry cordial) was being hawked in bush shanties. <i>(1991,Maori & Alcohol,103</i>) [NOTES: See Bollinger <i>Grog's Own Country</i> 1967, p. 104; Eldred-Grigg <i>Pleasures of the Flesh. Sex and Drugs in Colonial NZ</i>, 1984, p. 202,216]



## lights
 
 <i>n.</i> liver

>  The gutter or gutty guts, that is , extracts the guts from the carcase, which is hard because he may get maggots under the nails, that is, fat under the nails which affects them. The gutter drops guts and throws the lights down. <i>(May 17, 1951,Personal Communication</i>) [NOTES: Feilding Freezing Works terms p.c. Colin Gordon]



## lignum
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <i>Lignum </i>, n. (I), or <i> Lignum-Viteae </i>. The name is applied to several trees, as <i> Myrtus acmenioides </i>, F. v. M., called also <i> White Myrtle; Acacia falcata </i>, Wild., N.O. <i> Leguminosae </i>, called also <i> Hickory </i> and <i> Sally </i>; but chiefly to <i> Eucalyptus polyanthema </i>, Schau., N.O. <i> Myrtaceae </i>. <i>(1898,Austral English,269</i>)



## lignum vitae
 
 <i>n.</i> An early, descriptive name for Dodonea viscosa , =ake(-ake); or Metrosideros spp. [The name was applied to very hard, durable woods in other countries; and to various trees in Aust.; see Morris.]

>  <b>YATE</b> Aki - called the <i> Lignum vitae </i> of New Zealand, from its hardness, weight, and colour: is useless for all common purposes, and is very difficult to work. <i> Dodonoea viscosa </i> <i>(1835,New Zealand (various authors/titles),47</i>)

>  <b>SAVAGE</b> Their weapons of war prove the existence of a hard wood somewhat similar to lignum vitae growing in this country. This tree is more than a foot in diameter.] <i>(1807,Some Account of NZ,8</i>)

>  <b>POLACK</b> The <i> Akki, (lignumvitae,) </i> when young is much used for boat timber, and, when cut fresh from the bush, it can easily ... put into any shape; ... <i>(1838,New Zealand  (Polack),2/398</i>)

>  [see AKE] <i>(1840,NZ Journal,1/11/139</i>)

>  Ake-ake, (<i> Metrosideros buxifolia </i>.) This is a variety of the Rata, No. 10. It is often called the <i> Lignum vitae </i> of New Zealand, and resembles that wood in hardness, weight, and colour. <i>(1848,Handbook for New Zealand, The,143 14</i>)

>  <b>JAMESON</b> [see AKE] <i>(1841,New Zealand, S. Aust. and NSW,330 ed.</i>)

>  [see AKE] <i>(1869,TrNZI,1/47</i>)

>  <b>FEATON</b> DODONOEA VISCOSA (Forst.) ... The `AKE-AKE'.- This small tree is abundant in dry woods throughout the Islands. ...By the settlers it is sometimes called `Lignum Vitae' on account of its density and weight, being the heaviest of New Zealand woods. <i>Ibid.</i> 177 Ake-ake[Maori name] .. Lignum vitae [Settlers' name] .. Dodonea viscosa .. Small tree <i>(1889,Art Album NZ Flora,96</i>)

>  <b>KIRK</b> [Puriri] has been appropriately styled the New Zealand teak:it is ... closely related to the Asiatic teak, and affords a timber of great density and durability, closely resembling lignum vitae in general appearance. <i>(1875,Durability NZ Timbers,15</i>)

>  The Ake, or New Zealand Lignum Vitae, (<i>Dodonaea viscosa</i>,) is a small tree or large shrub ... [48] Its wood is very hard and very heavy ... It is used for Sheaves, Axe-handles, &c. <i>(1869,TrNZI,1/III/47</i>) [NOTES: (Colenso Botany North Isd)]

>  <b>MARKHAM</b> Pouriedee [<i>puriri</i>] is a fine hard wood like Lignum Vitae, grows into immense trees, but you can not planks out of it as the Worms ... make places like Nail holes .... <i>(1834,New Zealand (various authors/titles),33,1963 ed.</i>)

>  ... one in particular whose wood is almost as hard as Lignum Vitae and as beautiful as Cedar. [fn 2] Hardness suggests first the Southern Rata ..., <i>Metrosideros umbellata</i>... [or] Matai . <i>(May 10, 1773,Cook's Journals,2/785,1961 ed.</i>) [NOTES: Wales Journal in Cook's Journals]

## liitle grebe
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>P. rufopectus</i>, dabchick.

>  ... the light-eyed Teal slunk silently from view; or ... the little Grebe with rosy breast, dived and sported with restless activity; close by a group of sober Grey Ducks; whilst the watchful Paradise Drake basked on the sunny bank above ... <i>Ibid.</i> 2/73 PODICEPS RUFIPECTUS, Gray. Totokipio. Dab-chick, Little Grebe. <i>(1870,TrNZI,2/47</i>) [NOTES: (Potts Birds)]

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i>Weiweia, ... Podicipes rufipectus, little grebe. </i> <i>(1917,Dictionary of the Maori Language,565</i>)

>  The little grebe (or dabchick) was not uncommon in the quiet reaches of the river or deeper lagoons. <i>(1922,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,IV/279</i>) [NOTES: (Bathgate, Otago)]

>  <b>6/81</b> <i>Poliocephalus rufopectus Gray. Little Grebe; Dabchick. This species probably eludes observation in many localities where it is not uncommon. </i> <i>(1923,NZ Journal of Science & Technology</i>) [NOTES: Myers Endemic Birds]

>  <b>GUTHRIE-SMITH</b> Another lake bird to whom the future is secure is the Little Grebe (<i>Podiceps rufipectus</i>), whose nest is practically undiscoverable, ... <i>(1926,Tutira,214</i>)

>  <b>GUTHRIE-SMITH</b> Another lake bird to whom the future is secure is the Little Grebe (<i>Podiceps rufipectus</i>), whose nest is practically undiscoverable, ... <i>(1926,Tutira,214</i>)

>  <b>REISCHEK</b> On the west side, at the foot of the Pass, lay a little lake, the Lyndon. Paradise duck, grey duck, little grebe, and other water dwellers were enjoying life <i>(1930,Yesterdays in Maoriland,22,1933 ed.</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i>Weiweia ... Podiceps rufopectus, dabchick (little grebe). </i <i>(1957,Dictionary of the Maori Language,481</i>)



## like a bush fire
 
 <i>phr.</i> travelling quickly or fiercely or noisily etc.

>  <b>HOGG</b> Of nuggety, and round, and shotty gold./ The news like a bush fire through the diggings ran,/ <i>(1875,Lays and Rhymes,176</i>)

>  The news spread round ... Gore like a bushfire. <i>(Oct 28, 1905,NZ Truth,19/6/3</i>)

>  <b>DAVIN</b> ... the flu came raging south like a bushfire. <i>(1959,No Remittance,146</i>)

>  <b>DAVIES</b> You'd have gone roaring round the place like a bush fire ... <i>(1975,Outback,136</i>)



## like a cut cat
 
 <i>phr.</i> emphasising a speedy (or vigorous) action, an apter and more usual rendering of 'like a scalded cat/a cat on hot bricks'. [ca1938 Havelock.]

>  <b>SHADBOLT</b> They might have gone on for hours like a pair of cut cats, but then Jim walked into the argument carrying his crate; ... <i>(1965,Among the Cinders,263</i>)

>  <b>SLATTER</b> Off down the road like a cut cat. Pandebloodymonium - I told him to go and get intercoursed - <i>(1968,Pagan Game, The,162</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> <i>go like a cut cat </i>go or depart fast; eg `Whatever you think of motor scooters, these new Vitas go like a cut cat. True!' <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,51</i>)



## like a headless chicken
 
 <i>phr.</i> aimlessly

>  He's also come across a few new species - the Australian white-footed ant, another species that "tears around like a headless chicken", and the tiny sugar ants which he's heard can find their way into Tupperware. <i>(Dec 1, 1999,Evening Post,22</i>)

>  "I felt I achieved something rather than running round like a headless chicken." <i>(Jun 22, 2003,Sunday Star Times,B2</i>)



## like a headless chook
 
 <i>phr.</i> variant of like a headless chicken [ca1949 at least]

>  The Blue play like headless chooks and everybody calls for the coach to be sacked. Peter Sloane has never coached any team to play like headless chooks. <i>(Apr 23, 2004,Dominion Post  Sport & RaceForm,2</i>)

>  Then for one month in every three years they all rush around like headless chooks asking each other where the money is going to come from. <i>(Jul 28, 1984,Listener (New Zealand Listener),29</i>)

>  From a journalist's point of view, the Year of the Headless Chook beckons. <i>(Jan 2, 1999,Listener (New Zealand Listener),17</i>)

>  It's headless chook territory... Everyone knows politics is fought in the middle ground. <i>(Nov 3, 2003,Dominion Post,B5</i>)

>  I'll enjoy watching them run round like headless chooks. <i>(Feb 24, 1992,Listener (New Zealand Listener),38</i>)

>  All this has totally confused the medical profession currently running around like headless chicks wondering what to do about complementary and alternative medicines. <i>(Nov 22, 2003,Dominion Post,E8</i>)

>  The Nats are running like a bunch of headless chooks. <i>(Dec 20, 1991,Dominion,9</i>)



## like a madwoman's shit
 
 <i>phr.</i> [Common in the 1940s in the phrases : like a madwoman's shit, like a madwoman's spew e.g This car's steering is all over the road like a madwoman's shit or Don't leave your clothes lying all over the place like a madwoman's spew.]

>  <b>McGILL</b> madwoman's shit, all over the place like A Mess <i>(1998,David McGill's Complete Kiwi Slang Dictionary,82</i>)



## like a one-armed paperhanger
 
 <i>phr.</i> (work, go, etc) LIKE A ONE-ARMED PAPERHANGER, very busily (with often a connotation of unnecessary action, fuss or (occas.) inefficiency) Charles Ransom remarks (1986): Connotation in US is different. There it suggests being barely able to cope with all the work one has to do, having to work frantically to keep ahead of things. Nothing to do with unnecessary action or inefficiency. [ca 1940 at least (Les Cleveland)]

>  <b>CRUMP</b> I had to work like a one-armed paperhanger to keep up with Harry's tally. <i>(1960,Good Keen Man, A,112</i>) [NOTES: nf P.]



## like a packet of salts
 
 <i>phr.</i> go through (usu. somebody, occ. something) like a packet (Br. dose) of salts, to go through (somebody, something) very quickly (eg. spend money extravaganty; run, penetrate, pass through opposition players etc)

>  The discovery acted like a packet of salts on the Maoris, who made haste to get out. <i>(Mar 25, 1911,NZ Truth,4</i>)

>  <b>SLATTER</b> 'Struth, did ya see him go. Went through them like a packet of salts. His blood's worth bottling. He won't be beat that bloke. cf P. <i>dose</i>: like a dose of salts, very quickly; espec. <i>go through </i>(sthg) like .... : low, mostly Austr: 20c. <i>(1959,Gun in My Hand, A,147</i>)

>  <b>SLATTER</b> Played a blinder against Maungaraki [rugby team]. Went through them like a packet of salts. <i>(1968,Pagan Game, The,155</i>)



## like a possum caught in the headlights
 
 <i>phr.</i> (simile)

>  The move came as local government consultant Larry Mitchell observed that some councils were like possums trapped in the headlights. <i>(Aug 12, 2004,Dominion Post,B6</i>)

>  Hadlow is a great sook, while Griffiths, left like a possum in the headlights by his mad co-stars, executes an awesome tumble down a flight of stairs. <i>(May 19, 2002,Sunday Star Times,F5</i>)

>  Roxanne Cross remains a rather spoilt "pocket Venus" with an angel's voice, and Mr Hosokawa rather like a possum caught in the headlights of her talent and fame. <i>(Sep 17, 2002,Listener (New Zealand Listener),58</i>)

>  "I was like a possum caught in the headlights". <i>(Aug 14, 2001,Independent Herald (Wellington),23</i>)

## like one thing

## like one's steak well browned

## like smoke in a wheelbarrow
 
 <i>phr.</i> indicating strong disbelief

>  <b>SUTTON-SMITH</b> Some of the [slang] expressions listed by children in two schools are: `Up the shoot, going eyes out, bullswool, go jump in the lake, ... shivery dick, shiver me timbers, ... you're telling I, got it on the brain, like smoke in a wheelbarrow' (Kaitaia), `Sheila, crow, bud, frig it, grub, holy moses, blast it, mole, balls to you, shut your face, ... nerts to you, go bite your back, have a roll, fluff off, scram, ants in your pants, rag bag, ... you're bats, dronk, drongo, dill, dip, dippy, ya big lug, greetings gruesome, who dented your beak. (South Wellington). <i>(1953,Unorganized Games,2/677</i>)

>  <b>SUTTON-SMITH</b> [c1920-1950] there are vigorous <i>Slanging Contests</i> in which the contestants try to outdo each other by using phrases like the following: `You're up the shoot.' `Oh, bullswool.' `Go jump in the lake'. .... `Shivery dick'. `Shiver me timbers.' ... `Like smoke in a wheelbarrow.' ... `Mole balls to you.' ... <i>(1972,Folkgames of Children,138</i>)



## like the hammers of hell
