## likeness
 
 <i>n.</i> liking (?a solecism)

>  At first they tasted the ensilage without showing any great likeness for it. <i>(Apr 24, 1952,NZ Farmer,3</i>)



## lillipee
 
 <i>n.</i> A porridge or gruel made of flour (occas. sugar), or grain boiled; cf also stirabout.

>  The Poatini [Poutini] (West Coast) natives arrived. Feasted them on Lilipee. <i>Ibid.</i> 1854 Apr 25 8/171 Several large canoes were filled with what they [at Poarikaino] called Riripi, i.e. flour and water boiled and sweetened with sugar. <i>(Jan 3, 1850,Torlesse Papers,122</i>)

>  <b>HAAST</b> ... I found that [our provisions] would only last us ten days by confining ourselves to a small pot of lillipe (or boiled flour) twice a day, with the hoped-for addition of wekas or eels... <i>Ibid.</i> 38 After a hearty meal of lilipi our other provisions being exhausted.... <i>(1861,Report on Topographical Exploration of Nelson,22</i>)

>  <b>KENNAWAY</b> The poor children, who lived principally on heavy bread and `lillippee' (its) (a mixture containing nothing more nor less than paper-hanging paste) cried at intervals ... <i>(1874,Crusts: a settler's fare due south,147</i>)

>  To this [Maori c1855] feast I received a special invitation, and ... I accepted it, especially as the fare was to consist of Lipi, dried shark and potatoes. Upon arriving at the Pah I found the `Lipi' mixed in a canoe. It consisted of flour, sugar, and water. The men were sitting round, helping themselves by means of pannikins, mussel shells, and their hands. <i>(1900,Canterbury Old & New,116</i>)

>  [The Maoris] were also [fond] of wheat and sugar boiled together, to which they gave the name of `Lilipa'. <i>(1915,Reminiscences of Earliest Canterbury,34</i>)

>  <b>HENDERSON</b> The most important dish ... was a kind of gruel made out of flour, sugar, and water, mixed in a canoe, and called lilipi. <i>(1948,Taina,111</i>)

>  <b>VON HAAST</b> Haast having inspected the provisions found they would last ... by reducing his party to a small pot of lillipi (boiled flour) twice a day, with a hoped-for addition of wekas or eels. <i>(1948,Julius Von Haast,81</i>)

>  <b>BOSWELL</b> She made what we called `lillipy', a corruption of the Maori rerepe, a dish made by boiling the pollen of the raupo in water to a porridge. Mother's `lillipy' was made by stirring plain flour into boiling milk to a thick porridge, when it was poured out on to a huge meat plate and dotted and dabbed with butter and sugar, with a good sprinkling of nutmeg. It also was a fine lunch on a cold day. <i>(1955,Dim Horizons,129,1956 ed.</i>)

>  <b>BURTON</b> '...[sic] twenty pots of stirabout'. (Stirabout, otherwise known as lillipee, consisted simply of flour and sugar mixed together with a little a boiled water.) [`part from Marianne Williams's journal] <i>(1982,Two Hundred Years of NZ Food & Cookery,17</i>)

>  One day the native cooks opened the bag of lime, thinking it was flour, and proceeded to mix it with water and sugar, in order to provide "stirabout" or "lillipee," dear to the palate of the Maori. <i>(Apr 13, 1912,NZ Free Lance,5</i>)

>  <b>MEAD</b> Lillipee feasting... So much lillipee (a mixture of flour, hot water, sugar and something else) was prepared [sc. in 1844] that a fishing canoe was cleaned out and used as the communal cup. <i>(1990,Taranaki Tales,11</i>)

>  Mr Punch will recommend that the guardians of little Master N.-d, when they next treat certain not over-respectable people with lilipi, shall at the same time treat Mrs Eng...d's brave-hearted soldiers - not exactly with lilipi, but.. "lilly pay". <i>(Dec 5, 1860,Taranaki Punch,8</i>)

>  The hon. member for the Hutt had.. repeated that witty.. performance.. about.. a miserable resolution which, while leaving to the Governor all real power, reserved to the Ministry such great questions as the right of deciding whether Paora should have a new blanket, or Matia an extra bucket of lillipee. <i>(Aug 23, 1862,Otago Witness,2</i>)



## lilliput cards
 
 <i>n.</i> small or miniature playing cards (?from trade name).

>  <b>BEATON</b> SANDY produces some lilliput cards. <i>(1984,Outside In,59</i>)



## lillypilly
 
 <i>n.</i> monkey apple

>  Then there are plans to build a tree hut in the lilly pilly tree. <i>(Aug 17, 2002,Dominion Post,F11</i>)

## LIM report
 
 <i>n.</i> a document issued by a local authority identifying special characteristics of a piece of land eg liability to erosion or slippage, drains, the uses to which it may be put, rates owing, etc. [Jim Cameron writes : A purchaser of land may require a LIM (pron.<i>lim</i>) which is analogous to a copy of the title. (Local Government Official Information and Meetings Act 1987, s. 44A)]

>  They have argued that land information memorandum (LIM) reports have to flag the possible contamination ... <i>(Dec 23, 2004,Dominion Post,A6</i>)

>  LIM reports, one of the key protections for house buyers trying to avoid buying "lemons", are not the protection many people believe, an investigation shows... LIMs are intended to provide certain basic information about a house, such as whether it complies with the building code, is prone to flooding, or has a protected view. <i>(Oct 10, 2004,Sunday Star Times,D5</i>)

>  When the owner.. bought the house in 1993, a Land Information Memorandum (LIM) said the pool complied but a council officer later admitted he had not done a thorough inspection... When we do a LIM report we ensure our inspectors chase everything up. <i>(May 30, 1999,Sunday Star Times,A4</i>)

>  Farmers, he says, don't think about Lim (Land Information Memorandum) reports if there is no housing involved. <i>(Jan 17, 2002,Dominion,9</i>)

>  It paid to get a Land Information Memorandum (LIM) from the council to indicate what restrictions might be placed on development. <i>(Apr 9, 2001,Evening Post,20</i>)

>  A LIM report is [a] handy tool for property hunters. A Land Information Memorandum (LIM) provides information held by the local authority on a property... A LIM report might include information about potential erosion, contamination or flooding; structural requirements; where the street water pipes and sewerage run; rates details; consents and notices that have been issued relating to this or neighbouring properties; the zoning that says what can be done with the land; likely future use such as new roads; information from other utility providers; and any other information the local authority regards as relevant. <i>(Nov 19, 2001,Evening Post,22</i>)

>  I'm still amazed at the number of people who buy houses without independent builder's assessments, LIM reports or engineer's surveys. <i>(Sep 22, 2002,Sunday Star Times,A11</i>)

>  A Land Information Memorandum (LIM).. gives information on land and buildings as they exist, and it can be applied for at any time without the need for a building consent. <i>(Oct 18, 2000,Norwester,9</i>)

## lime-juice
 
 <i>n.</i> [P: Lime-juice (sb) = `new-chum' as in Austr (-1886, ob 1896, obsolete 1910); OED lime-juicer n Austr.: f. 1859 are not attested but were probably used: [OEDS2] - covered by OED] cf WF n. (1), (2) + (3) 1892, 1935 - connected with `English']

>  <b>BATHGATE</b> `This is no go, and we must give it best! ... I think it's time to cry `Smoke oh' and then we'll go up, for I'm full on this shooting racket.' `You are adept at slang, Arthur, and no mistake', ... `And yet a fellow told me the other day, he could tell at once that I had not been long in the country, or, as he phrased it, `that I had not got the lime-juice out of me yet;' so it can't be colonial slang that I speak.' Well, I don't know that colonials are worse than other people for speaking slang, but they have, of course, many words and phrases peculiar to themselves.' <i>(1881,Waitaruna,151</i>)

>  <b>DAVIDSON</b> Jim had a mate ... who looked with contempt upon my brother as being quite a new chum, and savouring strongly of lime juice. <i>(1889,Stories of NZ Life,41</i>)

>  <b>TREGEAR</b> I see that you are a new arrival and if you will kindly stand on one side (for the smell of limejuice is unpleasant) I will tell you. <i>(1884,Southern Parables,10</i>)

## Limited, the
 
 <i>n.</i> the Limited Express, formerly running between Auckland and Wellington.

>  <b>SMITH</b> The `Limited' is the train which runs daily between Wellington and Auckland (and vice versa). It leaves Wellington at 7.10 p.m. ... <i>(1947,From N to Z,81</i>)

>  `You can put Mac on with Monty', I told Dawson, `and young Jimmy can spell Arthur.' I wasn't going to stand there arguing ... and I wanted a ride fifty miles to the township to get the Limited ... <i>(Mar 3, 1950,Listener (New Zealand Listener),8</i>) [NOTES: P. Llewellyn]

>  New Zealand has developed many phrases that depend on local interpretation: e.g. `The Limited', `the six oclock rush', `off the bitumen', `motor camp', `to farewell', `to pencil in', `to check up on', `return (at meals)'. All these are immediately understandable to New Zealanders, but not always to others. <i>(1950,Post Office English Course [TS],14</i>)

>  We pulled up to let both the three o'clock express to Auckland, and the seven-fifteen `Limited', pass us. <i>(Apr 1954,NZ Geographer,(x/1) 62</i>)

>  <b>SUTHERLAND</b> The train that was carrying me south from Auckland into the night bore an exciting title - the Limited. <i>(1956,Green Kiwi,75,1960 ed.</i>)

>  The decision to run the "limited" at night has been arrived at after very careful consideration. <i>(Dec 2, 1924,Otago Witness,34</i>)

>  The introduction of the Limited train between Auckland and Wellington made an immediate appeal to the popular imagination... The introduction of a summer running train, which took advantage of the daylight, was also 'limited in its accommodation'... It brought about a desire for more specific titles, so "Daylight Limited" and "Night Limited" became the terms commonly used in referring to them. <i>(Jul 18, 1928,NZ Free Lance,35</i>)

>  ... you were drawn beneath the wheels of a train if you stood too near the edge of the platform. That was the terrible danger when the Limited came rushing in ... <i>(1966,NZ Short Stories,121,1976 ed.</i>) [NOTES: (Frame `Reservoir')]

>  <b>JOHNSON</b> .. with second-class tickets and a pillow each we boarded the Limited at seven on the evening for the northern terminus <i>(1970,Life's Vagaries,70</i>)

>  Well, they took me on as fireman on the Limited Express,/ And I thought that she'd be jake but now it's just a flaming mess; <i>(1964,Book of New Zealand, A (J.C. Reid),223</i>) [NOTES: Peter Cape `Taumarunui']

>  <b>WILSON</b> ... I got all packed up and went off on the Limited express from Frankton Junction one night at ten o'clock. <i>(1973,NZ Jack,178</i>)

>  You wouldn't expect the 7.50 from Taitato perform like the Limited (even thoughthe Limited sometimes performs like the7.50 from Taita). <i>(1995,She'll Be Right Record Sleeve,00</i>)

>  <b>IRELAND</b> Many is the time I would wake up in the morning, feel an itch to be moving on, and decide to catch the Limited Express or hit the road and move to another town. <i>(1998,Under the Bridge & Over the Moon,150</i>)

>  In the heyday of rail travel in the 1940s, 50s and 60s, the Limited express, as it was called then, regularly carried 250 to 300 passengers a night. <i>(Oct 30, 2004,Dominion Post,A 1</i>)

## limpet
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  (Queen Charlotte Sound) Of other shellfish there are ten or twelve sorts such as periwinkles, wilks, limpets, and some very beautiful sea Ears and another sort that sticks to the weeds , with some other things as sea eggs, starfish ... ed.fn Periwinkles, <i> Melarhapa </i> spp. (2), and probably <i> Zediloma </i> and <i> Melagraphia </i> spp. Whelks, <i> Cominella </i> sp. Limpets, <i> Cellana </i> spp. The `very beautiful sea Ears' were certainly the Paua, <i> Haliotis iris </i> ; ... <i>(Feb 1777,Cook's Journals,3/808,1967 ed.</i>) [NOTES: Anderson's Journal in Cook Journals (1967)]

>  <b>M'DONNELL</b> The rivers are well stocked with fine fish ... - the very creeks swarm with them. There are abundance of lobsters, crawfish, oysters, prawns, and shrimps; besides clams, peppies, muscles, limpets, and cockles. <i>(1834,Journal,15,1979 ed.</i>)

>  <b>POLACK</b> fn Among the piscivorous tribes, are those well known in Europe under the appellations of snapper, tench, haddocks, pollock, salmon, gurnets, john dory, hakes, bream, sword-fish, elephant-fish, diodons, pipe-fish, cod, parrot-fish, flounders, skate, cat and dog-fish, sting-rays, eels. - Among the larger tribes that sport in shoals in the vicinity of the shore, are, sperm-whales, called <i> para </i> [as-acute] <i> paraua </i> [a3-acute] (physeter macrocephalus); black whale, <i> mungu nui </i> (black physeter); right whale, <i> tohora </i> (balaena mysticetus); finback, (balaena physalis); sun-fish (diodon mola); large-lipped whale (balaena mysticetus); large-lipped whale (balaena musculus); hump-back (balaena gibbosa); shark, <i> mango </i> (squalus); hammer-head (zygaena); pilots, (scomber); flying-fish (exocaetus volitans); and an infinity of others, besides a great variety of testaceous and crustaceous fishes that almost cover the rocks under water, such as clams, muscles, oysters, crays, cuttles, limpets, wilks, gigantic muscles, a foot in length, cockles, sea-ears, sea-eggs, star-fish, mutton-fish, &c.; from the latter the pearl is procured which they insert in the eyes of their carved images and boxes. <i>(1840,Manners & Customs of the NZers,1/202</i>)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> Limpet .. .. ngakihi .. .. <i> Cellana </i> <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/705-712</i>)

>  <b>BELL</b> We never caught any Flat Fish - and the only Shellfish besides small Musells and Limpets were Cray-fish - <i>(1791,Journal of Voyage in HMS 'Chatham',51</i>) [NOTES: (MS ATB)]

>  It was a tiny freshwater limpet. Moving the stones caused it to release luminous mucus - a defence mechanism against predators such as eels. The glowing goo is carried away by the current and diverts the predator's attention. Found only in the North Island, the limpet is one of the very few bioluminescent animals in the world. <i>(Nov 2002,NZ Geographic,No. 60/51</i>)



## linaria
 
 <i>n.</i> toad flax

>  <b>HILGENDORF & CLADER</b> TOAD FLAX (Linaria [vulgaris]), often called linaria, and sometimes yellow toad flax, is frequent on roadsides and waste land in ] both islands. It is [perennial] with trailing stems up to 30 inches long. ... Four other linarias, all called toad flax, and all with spurred flowers, are found in a few localities. <i>(1926,Weeds of New Zealand,157,1967 ed.</i>)

## line
 
 <i>v.</i> (of a male dog) to mate with (a bitch)

>  <b>AYTON</b> ... numerous entries about dogs - ... bitches being `lined' by male dogs (`Lining' was a slang term for marrying, and a genteel term for mating), ... <i>Ibid.</i> 14 NANNY got lined by Tom Cambridge's brindle and white dog ... should have pups Jan 24. 1900. [15] Nov 27 Rover lined Nanny once. <i>(Nov 22, 1899,Diary,xii,1982 ed.</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> i.e. the main trunk railway line (in phr. down or up the line for travel south or north inNZ)

>  <b>DAVIS</b> Remember me telling you I went up to my uncle's place [near Taihape] but she wasn't a roaring success. Well, after I shot through I headed down the line to Palmerston and got me a job as postie. <i>(1964,Watersiders,108</i>)

>  <b>PICARD</b> .. no thanks to this party [26] who had been smoodging to a clina up the line instead of slinging the roaring lion his hash. <i>(1906,Some Ups and Downs in NZ&A,25</i>)

>  <b>BATISTICH</b> Paddy lived in a shack 'up the line'. <i>(1987,Sing Vila in the Mountain,82</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> esp. in cut a line, i.e. a survey line, or line of road (road line); a bush track or road; cf. road line [very freq. in early days in expression `cut a line', that is, clear a strip through scrub to mark boundaries, place pegs, and to walk along; very quickly came to connote almost `bridle track' and in more recent times (Marlborough, Lower Hutt, Manawatu) named subsidiary access roads; development of the use surveyor's line; see track OED2 base line 1750 (see also base line). OEDS sense 2. NZ and Canadian 1828: AND 1. line of road 1828-1898 2. abbrev. of line of road (also attrib) 1837 ... ]]

>  .. am now employed with John Lye, by the Company, cutting lines for surveyors. <i>(1843,Letters from New Plymouth,27,1968 ed.</i>) [NOTES: (1842 Mar 8) 1843 (1968)]

>  <b>DIEFFENBACH</b> .. the difficulties presented by the heavy forest and underwood, although surveyors' lines had been cut for about twenty miles up the valley, ... <i>(1843,Travels in NZ,1/72</i>)

>  FOREST BIVOUAC. ... Strengthened by the usual New Zealand food of roasted potatoes and `damper', we set off ... but soon found ourselves bewildered ... in the surveyor's lines which crossed one another in all directions in the wood. We then resorted to the usual expedient of torches made of `karehao' (supple Jack) or `katoa'; ... it was [agreed] ... that we should light a fire and bivouac for the night. <i>(Aug 30, 1848,New Zealand (Selwyn),Part V (Church in the Colonies XX) 76,1851 ed.</i>)

>  <b>PHARAZYN</b> ...cutting a line to the back hills. <i>(1849,Journal,293</i>)

>  <b>HAAST</b> He conducted us ... through the bush, where we found a good blazed line, which brought us ... to the open terraced fern flat ... <i>(1861,Report on Topographical Exploration of Nelson,13</i>)

>  <b>BARFLOW</b> Before one surveyor's pegs here had time to ... decay, and the lines cut, become grown up withtea-tree scrub ... <i>(1888,Kaipara and Waitemata Echo,135</i>)

>  The simple pastime of chopping `lines' through forest giants ... inspired deathless poetry. <i>(Feb 10, 1921,Quick March,35</i>)

>  The so called road between French's & Barriball's clearing ...[sic] is really the worst part of the line as it is so thickly overgrown [96] with makos &c. The tracks were destroyed by the pigs but there were still traces [of Maori] - one a mako stick cut, & others some hairs of a mat caught in a stick. <i>(Mar 15, 1864,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,2/95-96,1960 ed.</i>) [NOTES: (A.S. Atkinson)]

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> I walked some distance along the surveyor's line and made the acquaintance of ... the newcomers. <i>(1845,New Zealand Half a Century Ago,?,1892 ed.</i>)

>  <b>WAITT</b> We followed up the base line until we reached the Waimakariri. <i>(1856,Progress of Canterbury, NZ, The,2</i>)

>  Col. Wakefield is also about to direct a line or bridle road (the basis of the future road) to becut. <i>(1841,NZ Journal,2/43/224</i>)

>  Captain Thomas and Cass came over from Gibies ..., Cass remaining to cut line of road to Terapi. <i>(Feb 9, 1849,Torlesse Papers,56,1958 ed.</i>)

>  <b>MARSHMAN</b> ... as the population spreads, and the country becomes enclosed, and vehicles are confined to the reserved lines of roads, it becomes necessary to form them and lay on metal. There are several trunk lines through the settled districts. <i>(1864,Canterbury, NZ, in 1862,24</i>)

>  <b>BELL</b> Roads, as the term is generally understood, were for a time unknown. At first, a pathway through the bush or along the felled road line, diving ... into a deep gulley, or skirting a slippery sideling, and barely passable for a horse; then a formed clay track, ... <i>Ibid.</i> 58 [He] found his swag ..., struck down to the road line, and stepped out ... along it towards Bloomsbury. <i>(1899,In the Shadow of the Bush,1</i>)

>  [check and collate with following] There is what is called a `good' bush road to Rata Nui, but from there on, what is called a `line'; a line is made by cutting the supple jacks and small shrubs with a bill-hook, but a good walker cannot get two miles done in less than an hour. <i>(1853,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,I-306</i>)

>  There is what we call a good bush road to Rata Nui but but beyond it there are two miles of bush walking along what is called `a line'; a line is made by cutting the supple jacks and small shrubs with a bill-hook, but a good walker cannot get two miles done in less than an hour. <i>(1853,Richmond-Atkinson Papers,1/133,1969 ed.</i>) [NOTES: J. Maria Richmond. Check date.]

>  <b>HAAST</b> He conducted us ... through the bush, where we found a good blazed line, which brought us ... to the open terraced fern flat ... <i>(1861,Report on Topographical Exploration of Nelson,13</i>)

>  <b>BROOKES</b> [The packers] had a rough country to travel in with nothing but a rough exploring line to pack upon. <i>(1892,Frontier Life,151</i>)

>  <b>SEARLE</b> This track was known to the neighbourhood as `Mount's Line' <i>(1890,Angela,2</i>)

>  <b>BRUCE</b> I could easily have refused to take them ... - but seeing that I had never refused a line yet, we counted them out and handed them to the drover. <i>(1977,Life in the Hinterland of the South Island,47</i>)

>  The line is surveyed, and it would be a great convenience to Tauhoa. <i>(Sep 13, 1879,Auckland Weekly News,7</i>)

>  This space was a grave - the last resting place of a pioneer surveyor who in heroic service, came in tragic circumstances, just there to "the end of the line." <i>(Jun 1, 1922,Auckland Weekly News,14</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> a poison line laid in possum hunting

>  <b>CRUMP</b> We laid cyanide lines up every ridge within reach of the camp ... We'd do this for three days, then go back over the lines [32] cutting the ears off the dead possums for tokens. Sometimes we got 300 off one line. <i>(1960,Good Keen Man, A,31</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) a group or mob of stock, often for sale. (See [station line](../S#station-line),[hill country line](../H#hill-country-line))

>  Line.- A mob of sheep when sold, or offered for sale. `Will you take the l., or a run off?' asks the auctioneer, meaning, `Will you take them all, or part of them?' (C.) <i>(Nov 4, 1933,Press, The,15</i>) [NOTES: Acland Glossary]

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> When a mob of sheep or cattle is put up for sale it is then referred to as a line. <i>(1981,Loughnan Glossary,54</i>)

>  <b>BELL</b> Has nobody a line of broken-mouthed ewes that could be palmed off to him as four-tooths. <i>(1899,In the Shadow of the Bush,26</i>)

>  In addition to the fat sheep coming forward, a few large lines of boilers are waiting their turn. ibid. Although large lines have been booked for boiling-down purposes, boilers have not yet been handled in any quantity as yet. <i>(Mar 1907,NZ Farmer,121</i>)

>  The down-country cockies like nothing better than a nice line of blacks and will pay accordingly. <i>(Sep 10, 1970,NZ Farmer,35</i>)

>  .. in the store pens the best line made $67.20. <i>(Aug 8, 1996,NZ Farmer,35</i>)

>  Plus many lines of shorn well bred hill country lambs. <i>(Apr 13, 2002,Dominion,33</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> in phr. down or up the line [[Cf P: line, go (for a trip up the): To go to prison: 20 c (quot 1934).] used as euphemism for mental hospital in phrases (depending mainly on geographical location of observer). 1949 R. Mason. up the line in Porirua Mental Hospital, Wellington only; e.g. He has been up the line. -->1981 Feb Avondale College (Auckland) Slang Words in Use (Goldie Brown Collection) down the line]

>  <b>FRAME</b> The train drew into the station. Yes, the loonies were there; everyone looked out at the loonies, known in Oamaru as those who were sent `down the line', and in Dunedin, `up the line'. <i>(1984,Angel at My Table, An,15</i>)

>  <b>ADSETT</b> Once Mrs Baker had gone away and Mrs Tonkin hinted that she knew where to. It was a place nobody mentioned much and was called `down the line'. But it turned out to be only temporary. <i>(1963,Magpie Sings, A,64</i>)

>  <b>BATISTICH</b> Paddy lived in a shack 'up the line'. <i>(1987,Sing Vila in the Mountain,82</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> (West Coast, obs.) Development of line-->, `boundary'; over the line, over the boundary of a no-licence (prohibitionist) licensing district.

>  <b>SCOTT</b> ...the first hotel `over the line' <i>(1937,Barbara Prospers,76</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> A number of bales of the same sort of wool.

>  <b>PERRY</b> ... the hogget, wether, and wet ewe fleeces are best made into separate lines, provided ... at least three bales of each can be made. <i>(1922,Sheep Farming in NZ,121</i>)

>  The broker has a good binning system and can handle most of the oddments. If a line of fourbales cannot be made, send the wool in for binning. <i>(Oct 16, 1950,NZ Journal of Agriculture,313</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> a furrow

>  <b>WRIGHT</b> We had kept the lines in the winter time / On the wing of a poisoning gang. <i>(1897,Station Ballads,18</i>)

>  <b>STRONACH</b> Carrots, cut with a knife, or minced, or even cut up in a box with a spade, are scattered along the furrow, or `line'. <i>(1953,Musterer on Molesworth,60</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> harpoon rope [nautical, whalers']

>  <b>GRADY</b> Line - The rope that links the harpoon with the whale-chaser. <i>(1982,Perano Whalers,227</i>) [NOTES: glossary]



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a tracing through bush, to establish a fence, route or road.

>  <b>RICHMOND</b> There is what we call a good bush road to Rata Nui but there are two miles of bush walking along what is called a 'line' - a line is made by cutting the supplejacks and small shrubs ... <i>(Sep 24, 1851,Letter,133</i>) [NOTES: in Scholefield (1960) Richmond-Atkinson Papers I]

>  <b>MOWAT</b> .. we will fix up what is to be done with the unfenced portion of the line. <i>(Oct 1914,Alexander Mowat Diaries,29</i>)

>  <b>MORGAN</b> .. the government wish for a line of road through the interior of the country ... <i>(Jul 1857,Letter,14</i>) [NOTES: in Scholefield (1960) Richmond-Atkinson Papers I]

>  <b>MOWAT</b> Cleared a line through scrub for de Blois to run a break fence down to the netting. <i>(Sep 1913,Alexander Mowat Diaries,5</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> a formed road, often extant in modern street or road names [ca1960 There are `lines' (i.e. `roads') in many districts e.g. White's Line (Hutt Valley) and very frequent in the Manawatu.]

>  I mean by the Purau line the road from Akaroa to Purau. <i>(Sep 22, 1864,Press, The</i>) [NOTES: 1864 Sep 22 Press (Christchurch) in 1966 Turner English Language in Australia and NZ 194]

>  <b>BENNETT</b> In some country districts (the Manawatu, for example) the roads are named lines- McDonell's Line, Richardson's Line, Union Line - presumably from early boundary or surveyors' lines. <i>(1943,English as it is spoken in NZ,87</i>)

>  <b>Jane Godfrey</b> Ten or so years ago a lot of the names of the Lines round Palmerston were changed to `Road' (etc.) because `It was more up-to- date, to the horror of many of us. <i>(1984,Personal Communication</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>v.</i>

>  <b>BROWN</b> The land at the junction of the Matakitaki and Buller which was not heavily bushed was lined and later surveyed in sections ... <i>(1976,Difficult Country,47</i>)

## line-up
 
 <i>n.</i> A gang rape (cf gang(ie), gang-splash, cp Aust. `queue'). [slang]

>  ...Murray said she was familiar with the term `line-up'. Mr Bennetts: So this rape was in fact aline-up? Murray: Yes. It was against my will. You have been a party to line-ups on several occasions? I probably have, but if so I was under the influence of alcohol and I can't rememberthem. <i>(Feb 3, 1970,NZ Truth,39</i>)

>  Sergeant: What is a `gangie'? - It's a line-up. It's a group of males having intercourse with a girl or girls one after the other. Isn't that what happened? - Yes - but I wouldn't call that a gang splash. <i>(Mar 30, 1971,NZ Truth,7</i>)

## linesman
 
 <i>n.</i> [The context seems to refer to cutting lines (i.e. clearing scrub etc) for a tunnel (p128) poss. short for `railway linesmen' - in Northland; a roadman; one who clears edges of roads, gutters, etc; a (county or MOW) road worker (? from one who looks after a line n1.); ? lines- used mainly in or felt as a pluralising agent linesmen.]

>  The road was impeded by a landslip....When we had cleared one slip away another came into view. I asked the driver why the linesmen had not attended to the matter. <i>(1888,Geysers and Gazers,7</i>)

>  <b>HYDE</b> The linesmen lived in tents with a board cookhouse. Timothy had been there six months, and liked the life. <i>(1938,Godwits Fly, The,128,1970 ed.</i>)



## liney
 
 <i>n.</i> a marble game

>  <b>SUTTON-SMITH</b> In the nineteenth century the ring game seems to have been the most popular marble game. (In more recent years marble games with holes have had the greater role.) There was Big Ring, in which a circle a yard or more in diameter was drawn on the ground; there was the mullibar variation, in which an outsize taw was catapulted amongst the marbles in this circle. There were Little Ring, Liney, Follows, Follow the Taw, Holey, and then, on a less important scale, Butcher's Board, in which the marbles were bowled through the holes in a board, Eye-Drop,Knock-Backs, Leggings-Out (or Leggin's-Out), Span-'Em, and Backits. The last was more usually a girls' game. <i>(1972,Folkgames of Children,174</i>)

>  <b>KEITH & MAIN</b> Marbles became `in' or `out' capriciously but someome always knew the rules of the various games - `holey,' `liney' and, seen here, `ringey' - which could be played for `funs' or `keeps.' <i>(1984,NZ Yesterdays,174</i>)



## ling
 
 <i>n.</i> A large sea fish used as food; also called hokarari, = cod, cloudy bay.

>  The bather took a dive into the wave andhit something which turned out to be adead ling. <i>(Dec 24, 1935,Christchurch Star-Sun,8</i>)

>  <b>WEEKES</b> The emigrants have been engaged in fishing over the vessels side [in Cloudy Bay] with a hook and line, catching a number of ling and rockcod. <i>(Mar 24, 1841,Journal</i>) [NOTES: Weekes Journal in 1940 Establishment of New Plymouth 39]

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> Ling <i> Genypterus blacodes </i> (Bloch and Schn.)... Ling <i> Genypterus microstomus </i> Regan... Ling <i> Hypolycodes haastii </i> Hector (Syn. <i> Pseudolycodes haastii </i> Hector, 15th. Ann. Rept. Colon.Mus. 1880, p. 20) <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,112</i>) [NOTES: 1955 Whitley Namelist in 1956 Graham]

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> LING (HOKARARI) <i> Genypterus blacodes </i> (Bloch and Schneider) [ibid. 340] In some parts of Nelson Province I have heard Ling referred to as Cloudy Bay Cod on account of its `cloudy' appearance and its being not unlike Red Cod. In Australia, this fish is known as Rockling. <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,337,2nd ed.</i>)

>  <b>PARROTT</b> It [rock cod, <i> Lotella rhacina </i>] occurs not uncommonly in Cloudy Bay, and Mr. W. J. Phillipps records that, in 1924, small numbers were sold in Wellington as `Cloudy Bay Cod.' It may be noted that Ling [No. 55] which were caught in Cloudy Bay were also sold in Wellington as `Cloudy Bay Cod'. <i> 158 THE LING <i> Genypterus blacodes </i> (Bloch & Schneider) ... Maori name: HOKARARI. <i>Ibid.</i> 169 Ling <i> Genypterus blacodes Cloudy Bay Cod <i>(1957,Sea Anglers' Fishes of NZ,43</i>)

>  <b>DOOGUE & MORELAND</b> LING ... <i> Other names: Genypterus blacodes </i>; rock ling (Australia); hokarari, hoka (Maori). <i>(1966,Sea Anglers' Guide,285</i>)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> Ling .. .. hoka, hokarari .. .. <i> Genypterus blacodes </i> <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/705-12;</i>)

>  <b>MORELAND</b> LING [<i> Genypterus blacodes </i>] ... Also known as rock ling (Australia) and hokarari and hoka (Maori). <i>(1967,Marine Fishes,36</i>)

>  <b>NATUSCH</b> Family OPHIDIIDAE is represented by, among others, the ling (<i> Genypterus blacodes </i>, ... ) a large omnivorous fish, some-what tadpole-built, which is of some nuisance value to the fisherman after something `better' <i>(1967,Animals of NZ, The,231</i>)

>  <b>SORENSEN</b> LING (a) Scientific name: Genypterus blacodes. (b) Suggested commercial name[s]: Ling. (c) Other common names: Not known. (d) Principal localities where taken: [Tauranga to PortChalmers] <i>(1970,Nomenclature of NZ Fish,33</i>)

>  <b>AYLING</b> <i> LING </i>(Hokarari) <i> Genypterus blacodes </i> ... <i>(1982,Collins Guide,152,1984 ed.</i>)

>  <b>BURTON</b> An offshore species which is most abundant from Cook Strait southwards, ling (or hokarari) is a long, slender fish which weighs up to 14 kilograms. <i>(1982,Two Hundred Years of NZ Food & Cookery,77</i>)

>  The hoki are sent down first and are by far the major species caught in this area designated G in New Zealand's exclusive economic zone. But there are also ling and school shark, headed and gutted before they are boxed, squid and silver dory, fat blue nose and silver warehou, hake and sharp-toothed southern kingfish, ugly green monkfish and slimy flathead, electric skates and others with saw toothed snouts. <i>(Aug 22, 1984,Marlborough Express,6</i>)

>  [species name] ling .. [scientific name] <i> Genypterus blacodes </i> .. [other name(s)] kingklip <i>(Jul 23, 1986,Codes for Species in NZ Waters</i>)

>  <b>PAUL</b> <i> Ling Genypterus blacodes </i> ... Known as ling in Australasia, and also occurs off southern South America. A similar South African species is called kingclip. Not related to the Northern Hemisphere ling. <i>(1986,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,64</i>)

>  <b>PAUL et al.</b> [index to common names 283-293] ling 863-9, 873 <i>(1987,Fishes: A Bibliography</i>)

>  <b>PARROTT</b> The Ling was known to the Maoris as Hokarari. Some years ago large catches were made in Cloudy Bay, and were placed on the Wellington market as `Cloudy Bay Cod'. The scientific name <i> Genypterus blacodes </i>, freely translated, means `a flabby fish, with a fin on the chin.' The New Zealand Ling is also found in Australia where it is known as the `Rockling.' <i>(1957,Sea Anglers' Fishes of NZ,159</i>)

>  Their diet included ling, crayfish, blue cod, red cod, paua, hoki, blue fin and yellow-eyed penguins <i>(Jul 26, 2000,Evening Post,14</i>)

>  <b>HEAPHY</b> The bream, ling, gurnard, herring, sole, rock-cod, and a species of mackarel, are all in abundance, as are also many fish unknown elsewhere [than Wellington]. <i>(1842,New Zealand (Heaphy),49</i>)

>  <b>TAYLOR</b> On the Kairiki beach ... we found a large ling which had been cast on shore. <i>(1959,Early Travellers,244</i>) [NOTES: 1846 Heaphy Exped. to Kawatiri in 1959 Taylor Early Travs]

>  <b>WAKEFIELD</b> There are also several other kinds of fish, which are called by the sailors, from some slight resemblance to the English sorts, Herrings, Horse-mackarel, Cole-fish, and Ling. The latter isoften taken of the weight of forty pounds. <i>(1848,Handbook for New Zealand, The,161</i>)

>  Many ... varieties of sea-fish are caught round the coast, such as barracouta, groper, rock-cod, ling, flounders, soles, gar-fish, and others, all which are plentiful; ... <i>(1868,Province of Otago in NZ, The,32</i>)

>  Many ... varieties of sea-fish are caught round the coast, such as barracouta, groper, rock-cod, ling, flounders, soles, gar-fish, and others, all which are plentiful; ... <i>(1870,TrNZI,2/325</i>) [NOTES: Travers]

>  <b>HECTOR</b> LING. ...(Genypterus blacoides). This fish, also known as the Cloudy Bay Cod, is exceedingly common in Cook Strait, <i>(1872,Edible Fishes,116 77.</i>)

>  [Native or Common Name] Ling .. [Scientific Name] Genypterus blacoides <i>(1877,TrNZI,9/485</i>) [NOTES: P. Thomson, Otago]

>  <b>SHERRIN</b> LING (Genypterus blacodes). ... This fish is also known as the Cloudy Bay cod, and is exceedingly common in Cook Strait and on other parts of the coast to the southward. <i>Ibid.</i> 60 But, while writing of the Genypterus blacodes as ling, it must be remembered that the European ling belongs to quite a different family, and is not at all allied to the fish sought to be described; <i>Ibid.</i> 304 Genypterus blacodes Frost. Ling, or Rari <i>(1886,Handbook Fishes of NZ,59</i>)

>  Ling (<i>Genypterus blacodes</i>) <i>(1890,Handbook Fishes of NZ,22/375</i>) [NOTES: (G.M. Thomson)]

>  Of the edible [pelagic] fishes ... such as the frostfish, barracouta, horse-mackerel, kingfish, dory, warehou, mackerel, and garfish; while only the ling, hake and haddock ... are among those of more southern types ... <i>(1892,NZ Official Handbook,167</i>)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> <i>Ling n</i>. a fish. The name is given in England to various fishes, from their length. In New Zealand and Tasmania, it is applied to <i>Genypterus blacodes, </i>Forst.; also called <i>Cloudy Bay Cod. Lotella marginata</i>, Macl., is called Ling, in New South Wales, and Beardie. <i>Genypterus</i> belongs to the <i>Ophidiidae</i> and <i>Lotellae</i> to the next family, the <i>Gadidae. </i> <i>(1898,Austral English,271</i>)

>  <b>DRUMMOND & HUTTON</b> The snapper, the mullet, and the trevally are essentially northern; while the trumpeter, the red cod, and the ling are just as essentially southern. <i>(1902,Nature in NZ,71</i>)

>  <i>Genypterus Blacodes </i>... Ling. <i>(1911,Records of the Canterbury Museum,1/248</i>) [NOTES: WAITE]

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i>Hokarari, n. Genypterus blacodes, ling-fish.</i> ... Hoka (iii), n. 1. Lotella bacchus, red cod. 2. Genypterus blacodes, ling-fish. [so 1957/56 ... ling</i>, a fish.] <i>(1917,Dictionary of the Maori Language</i>)

>  <b>DRURY</b> FISH [MET WITH IN PELORUS]. Ling - about four feet long, resembling the European fish of the same name. <i>(1855,Sailing Directions,67</i>)

>  Ling. <i>Genypterus blacodes</i>... <i>(1918,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,1/269</i>) [NOTES: Phillipps]

>  GENYPTERUS BLACODES (Bloch and Schneider). <i>Ling; Hokarari</i>. A deep-water fish, to be secured during all months of the year. Common around Stewart Island and the South Island generally. Distribution: Taken as far north as the Bay of Plenty and Hauraki Gulf where it is considered rare. <i>(Jun 1921,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,IV/123</i>) [NOTES: Phillipps Notes on the Edible Fishes]

>  At the Portobello Marine Fish - hatchery the swimming form has beentaken from the stomachs of the following fishes: ... Spotty (<i>Pseudolabrus celidotus</i>), parrot-fish (<i>Pseudolabrus miles</i>). ling (<i>Genypterus blacodes</i>), and Leather jacket (<i>Pseudomonacanthus scaber</i>). <i>(Jun 1923,NZ Journal of Science & Technology,6/111</i>) [NOTES: Thomson and Thomson]

>  <b>PHILLIPPS</b> <i>Genypterus blacodes</i> (Bloch & Schn.). LING; HOKARARI. <i>(1927,Bibliography of NZ Fishes,52,1971 ed.</i>)

>  <i>Genypterus blacodes </i>Bloch and Schneider. Ling. Maori: Hokarari <i>(1929,TrNZI,61/148</i>) [NOTES: Young]

>  <i>Genypterus blacodes:</i> Ling. <i>(1936,Handbook for New Zealand, The,72</i>) [NOTES: ANZAAS Conf. Jan. 1937]

>  <i>Genypterus blacodes </i>(Bloch and Schn.). Ling. <i>(1938,TrRSNZ,68/416</i>) [NOTES: Graham Fishes of Otago Harbour]

>  LING (<i>Genypterus Blacodes</i>), Hokarari of the Maoris, grows up to 4 feet in length, with a weight of 30 lbs, and looks just like a giant tadpole. <i>(1947,Native Animals of NZ,72</i>)

>  <b>174</b> <i>ling </i>: edible fish. <i>(1947,Reeds' School Dictionary: NZ Suppl.</i>)

## linnet
 
 <i>n.</i> 1. In early SI use, Otago use `Blacksmith bird'; =bellbird. [[??Where does the following note derive: ??Begg's Boultbee or Starke's; nf at creeper.] [ed. fn: The linnet, brown creeper (Finschia novaeseelandiae), was occasionally called brown linnet in the earlier days; ... `parroquets', probably the red-crowned parrakeet ...]]

>  <b>BOULTBEE</b> The birds are not remarkably musical - the linnets are rather so; The Bell-Bird, is of a purple colour, with a white spot on the breast, shaped like a bell. The Saddle back, is a brown bird with a bright red spot on its back like a saddle, these two last species, are about the size of a thrush. There are green parroquets, large hawks, and several kinds of wild ducks, besides Emus, greenbirds and woodhens, which are birds of excellent flavour. <i>(1835,Journal,104,1986 ed.</i>)

>  <b>BUTLER</b> Our linnet is a little larger than the English, with a clear bell-like voice, as of a blacksmith'shammer on an anvil. Indeed, we might call him the harmonious blacksmith. <i>(1863,First Year in Canterbury Settlement,ix/137</i>)

>  <b>BOULTBEE</b> The birds [in West Southland] are not remarkable musical (the linnets are rather so). The Bell-Bird is of a purple colour, with a white spot on the breast, shaped like a bell. The Saddleback is a brown bird, with a bright red spot on its back like a saddle, these last two species are about the size of a thrush. <i>(1828,Journal,222</i>) [NOTES: in 1979 Begg]



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> brown creeper, toitoi

>  <b>HUTTON</b> CERTHIPARUS NOVAE ZEALANDIAE. Gml. Brown Creeper. Brown Linnet. Toitoi. <i> C. Maculicaudus. </i> Gray. <i>(1871,Birds of New Zealand,11 27.</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> (introduced) greenfinch

>  <b>FALLA et al.</b> GREENFINCH <i> Chloris chloris Local name:</i> Green Linnet. <i>(1966,Birds of New Zealand,225</i>)



## linseed
 
 <i>n.</i> Applied to spp. of Linum , more commonly to the Australian or wild linseed (`Australian flax') than to the native linseed rauhuia <i> L. monogynum.</i>

>  <b>HILGENDORF</b> AUSTRALIAN LINSEED (<i> Linum marginale </i>), is also called wild linseed and wild or Australian flax. It is common all over the islands and is a twiggy and slightly branched perennial [up to 20 inches tall] much like the cultivated variety. <i>(1926,Weeds of New Zealand,117,1967, p. 119 ed.</i>)

>  <i> Australian linseed [=] Australian flax [s]... Australian blue linseed [=] Australian flax [s]</i> <i>Ibid.</i> 44 <i> linseed, Australian blue [=] Australian flax [s]</i>... <i> linseed, Australian [=] Australian flax [s] </i> <i>(1969,Standard Common Names for Weeds,3</i>)

>  <i> linseed, [s] Linum usitatissimum </i> L. <i>(1969,Standard Common Names for Weeds,44</i>) [NOTES: [Nf OEDS, EDD]

>  <i> linseed, New Zealand [=] rauhuia [s] Ibid. 53 <i> New Zealand linseed [=] rauhuia [s] </i> <i>(1969,Standard Common Names for Weeds</i>)

>  <i> linseed, wild [=] Australian flax [s] </i> <i>Ibid.</i> 86 <i> wild linseed [=] Australian flax: rauhuia [s] </i> <i>(1969,Standard Common Names for Weeds,44</i>)

## lionfish
 
 <i>n.</i> a inshore tropical fish

>  A good example [of a tropical stray] is the lionfish - a gaudy species with large poison-tipped pectoral and dorsal fins - seen in 1990 off Ngaio Rock in the Poor Knights. It had probably arribved on the East Auckland current as a planktonic larva. <i>(Oct 2004,NZ Geographic,No. 69/65</i>)



## lionhead
 
 <i>n.</i> Diplocrepis puniceus

## liquid heroin

Detective-Sergeant Malcolm Burgess of the Auckland drug squad said the decision to use the term `liquid heroin' had been made because `homebake tends to trivialise the whole thing'. <i>(Jul 6, 1986,Sunday Star (Auckland),A3</i>)



## liquid laugh
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Just a small update on 'helicopter heave'; my brother recently spoke to a member of the Massey University Students' Assn, and he confirmed that the term is still in use (at Massey anyway). It is particularly associated with the "chunder-mile," a rather ,messy capping event... "Technicolour yawn" is used for an instance of vomiting, as is "liquid laugh', I believe. <i>(Aug 24, 1998,Letter</i>)

## Liquor Licensing Authority
 
 <i>n.</i> an independent statutory body created in 1989 whose principal function is the administration and supervision of the liquor licensing system. [Jim Cameron writes : This is the successor to the <i>Licensing Control Commission</i> 1948-1989. Subordinate to the Authority are the territorial local authorities <i> sub.nom. district licensing agencies</i>. See Sale of Liquor Act 1989.]

>  <i>(Sep 1, 1922,NZ Education Gazette,100/1</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> party list [(attrib. uses) The Royal Commission on Electoral Reform was reported in 1886. This probably saw the first use of these terms. The Electoral Bill of 1993 saw the first debate on the principles of MMP.]

>  He had been bumped from No 8 on the list to No 9 to make way for Metiria Turei. <i>(Sep 30, 2002,Listener (New Zealand Listener),11</i>) [NOTES: (nb: entered originally into the database with date of 31/9/2002)]

Party list members would be selected from ordered party lists nominated by each registered political party.<i> Ibid.</i> 17089 Lange... I will be supporting the proposal in the Bill for the party lists. <i> Ibid </i> 17098 17098 We need to have 40 list seats available, preferably more than 40. <i> Ibid. </i> 17108 It is my view that.. list members will typically reside in Wellington. <i> Ibid.</i> 17158 It would be possible to amend the Bill to have 100 MPs under MMP, with 40 of those being list MPs. <i>(1993,NZ Parliamentary Debates,17084</i>)

>  Her list seat would normally pass as of right to former MP Gilbert Myles, who in a single term mutated from National to Liberal to Alliance, and then NZ First. <i>(Dec 3, 1998,Dominion,8</i>)

>  Deputy Prime Minister Wyatt Creech announced yesterday that he would not contest his Wairarapa electorate at the next election and would stand only for list selection.<i> Ibid.</i> She said Mr Creech's decision meant Wairarapa would have a range of representatives in Parliament - list, constituency and Maori MPs. <i>(Dec 5, 1998,Dominion,2</i>)

## little blue penguin
 
 <i>n.</i> (Environment) [aka blue penguin, korora]

>  There are about twenty different kinds of penguins, and all are inhabitants of the Southern Hemisphere, amidst the ice of the Antarctic, in South Africa, South America, Australia, and New Zealand, whilst they vary in size from the large Emperor penguin, which measures 3ft 6 inches in height, and weighs about 80lb, to the little blue penguin, which stands only about 16in in height. <i>(Sep 18, 1901,Hawera & Normanby Star,3</i>)

>  Little blue penguins may look cute and cuddly, but they have sharp beaks and don't take kindly to being pulled out of their burrows. <i>(Aug 2009,Forest & Bird,333/9</i>)

>  ".. we were given permission to visit the spotted shag and little blue penguin nesting sites," says Angela. <i>(Nov 2006,Footnotes,26 / 1</i>)

>  Well-meaning people who find little blue penguins or any marine animals in "holes" in the ground should leave them there, says the education officer at Marineland in Napier. <i>(Nov 4, 2004,Dominion Post,A4</i>)

>  <i>Blue Penguin Eudyptula minor</i> (FORSTER, 1781) ENDEMIC <i>Northern Blue Penguin E.m. iredalei </i>MATHEWS, 1911. <i>Cook Strait Blue Penguin E.m. variabilis</i> KINSKY & FALLA, 1976. <i>White-flippered Penguin E.m. albosignata</i> FINSCH, 1874. <i>Southern Blue Penguin E.m. minor</i> (FORSTER, 1781). <i>Chatham Island Blue Penguin E.m. chathamensis</i> KINSKY & FALLA, 1976. OTHER NAMES: <i>Little blue penguin, little penguin, fairy penguin, korara.</i> <i>(1985,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,46</i>)

>  <b>BULLER</b> EUDYPTYLA UNDINA. (LITLE BLUE PENGUIN.) <i>(1873,Birds of New Zealand,349,1888 2nd edn 2/302 ed.</i>)

>  <b>POWELL</b> LITTLE BLUE PENGUIN (<i>Eudyptula minor</i>). Common all around the coasts of the North and South Islands, <i>(1947,Native Animals of NZ,77</i>)

>  <b>NATUSCH</b> The little blue penguin, <i>Eudyptula ? minor</i> .. up to twenty inches long, nests in caves and crannies of rugged coasts ... <i>(1967,Animals of NZ, The,252</i>)

>  Canterbury has its own distinctive penguin, the white-flippered penguin, which is a local variant of the Little [sic] blue or fairy penguin found only in New Zealand and Australia. It is slightly heavier than the northern Little blue penguin, and has a distinctive white band along both edges of the flippers. They breed only on Banks Peninsula, Motunau Island and the neighbouring coasts. <i>(Aug 24, 1988,Press, The,21</i>)



## Little Bosnia
 
 <i>n.</i> popular local name for a half-completed and for a time abandoned building project in central Christchurch (since completed as Cathedral Junction)

>  Meanwhile, in Christchurch where they are usually more committed to saving buildings than wrecking them, locals hate the shopping area in the central city nicknamed "Little Bosnia". <i>(Jun 17, 2001,Sunday Star Times,A6</i>)

## Little Enemy
 
 <i>n.</i> (historical) [Otago hist.]

>  <b>BATHGATE</b> The few Englishmen who had the hardihood to `locate' themselves in Otago in those days [c1853] were united together for purposes of self-defence [against the intolerant `Cameronian' spirit] and were known as the `little enemy'. <i>(1874,Colonial Experiences,31</i>) [NOTES: [c1853]]

>  <b>BARR</b> Not that there was entire unanimity [at the meeting]. The noisy and always ridiculous `little enemy' was there. [ie, the English minority]. <i>(1879,Old Identities, The,89</i>)

>  <b>HOCKEN</b> It was at this time [c1851], that the smart and appropriate sobriquet of the `Little Enemy' was affixed to a small but obstructive body of gentlemen in the community, mostly of standing and intelligence, and supporters of Sir George Grey. English and Episcopalian, they were viewed as intruders ... and reciprocated by seeing in the special scheme of the settlement and its leaders all that was narrow and intolerant. In a superior way they stood aloof from the rest, and their attitude was one of opposition. ... No quarter was given, no consideration shown on either side. The advent of the beneficent constitution finally ended these bickerings ... and though the term `Little Enemy' clung long, it was rather as a memory than a badge. <i>(1898,Contributions to the Early History of New Zealand,119</i>)

>  <b>LOCKE</b> The `Little Enemy' made use of the first newspaper, the Otago News, to attack and ridicule Captain Cargill. <i>(1978,Gaoler, The,25</i>)

## little pigs in blanket(s)
 
 <i>n.</i> a dish of oysters and bacon; aka angels on horseback [also pigs in blankets/a blanket (a sausage dish)]

>  <i>LITTLE PIGS IN BLANKETS.</i> Miss Hawker Season the quantity of oysters required ... Wrap each oyster in slice of bacon, ... <i>(1915,Amuri Cookery Book,7,1927 ed.</i>)

>  <b>MILLER</b> [the same recipe on file 4CBKAUS 30 is termed `ANGELS ON HORSEBACK'] <i>`LITTLE PIGS IN BLANKET' </i>Bacon ... Oysters ... roll an oyster in each piece ... <i>(1917,Economic Technical Cookery Book,38</i>)

>  <i>LITTLE PIGS IN BLANKET</i> ... bacon ...oysters ... wooden skewers ... buttered toast ... hard boiled egg ... parsley and pepper ... cook the little pigs long enough to crisp the bacon. Place one on each piece of toast. Serve on a hot dish garnished with egg ... parsley ... <i>(1928,Everybody's Cookery Book of Tested Recipes,68</i>)

>  [no oysters in this recipe] <i>PIGS IN BLANKETS </i>... pork sausages out of their skins ... chopped onion ... sage leaves ... wrap small portions in ... bacon ... bake ... Serve on hot fried bread. <i>(1936,Recipe Book [St Mary's],60</i>)

>  <b>BASHAM</b> <i>LITTLE PIGS IN BLANKET</i> ... oysters ... bacon ... roll ... up ... Don't put butter or fat in pan, but see it is quite hot before the `pigs' are put in ... Serve on fingers of hot toast. <i>(1939,Aunt Daisy's Cookery Book,40</i>)

>  PIGS IN A BLANKET/ Season two cups mashed potatoes ... Fry ... 1 dozen small sausages ... Roll each sausage in the mashed potatoes ... then ... in breadcrumbs, and fry a golden brown. <i>(1955,New Home Cookery,33</i>)

>  Little Pigs in Blankets. Season some large oysters with salt and pepper, have ready some slices of bacon cut rather thin, put one or two oysters on each slice and roll it up, fasten each one with a small skewer, fry them for about two minutes - just the time to let the bacon become crisp; serve at once on fried toast. <i>(Sep 19, 1900,Otago Witness,61</i>)



## little ring
 
 <i>n.</i> [marble players]

>  <b>SUTTON-SMITH</b> In the nineteenth century the ring game seems to have been the most popular marble game. (In more recent years marble games with holes have had the greater role.) There was Big Ring, in which a circle a yard or more in diameter was drawn on the ground; there was the mullibar variation, in which an outsize taw was catapulted amongst the marbles in this circle. There were Little Ring, Liney, Follows, Follow the Taw, Holey, and then, on a less important scale, Butcher's Board, in which the marbles were bowled through the holes in a board, Eye-Drop, ... <i>(1972,Folkgames of Children,174</i>)

## little shag
 
 <i>n.</i> (Environment)

>  Six kinds of shag fish in Wellington harbour: the big black shag, or common cormorant, which is about the size of a goose, the little black shag, the pied shag, the spotted shag and, recently, a long shag was seen here. <i>(Aug 18, 2003,Dominion Post,B5</i>)

>  LITTLE SHAG <i>Phalacrocorax melanoleucos brevirostris Maori name</i>: Kawaupaka. The name White-throated Shag is also in general use for the most abundant phase of a rather variable species which, at its other extreme, is identical with the Little Pied Shag of Australia. However, as intermediates occur, and the Little Pied form is relatively uncommon, it seems more convenient to treat the New Zealand population as a variable one under a distinctive name. <i>(1966,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,67</i>)

>  <b>32</b> <i>Phalacrocorax melanoleucos brevirostris</i> Gould, 1837 <i>Little Shag</i> (Kawaupaka) <i>(1970,Annotated Checklist of Birds of NZ,1980</i>)

>  <i>Little Pied Cormorant Phalacrocorax melanoleucos</i> (VIEILLOT, 1817) ... OTHER NAMES: <i>Kawaupaka, white-throated shag, frilled shag, little river shag, little cormorant, little shag.</i> <i>(1985,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,117</i>)

## little spotted kiwi
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  We have been transported to this location to see if the rare little spotted kiwi still exists on mainland New Zealand. <i>(Jul 5, 2003,Listener (New Zealand Listener),28</i>)

>  <b>BAKER</b> It wasn't killing rabbits any more.. and nor was it affecting cats and dogs, and not the native bat nor the little spotted kiwi. <i>(2000,Kokopu Dreams,4</i>)

>  Little spotted kiwi behave very similarly to North Island browns, but the parents allow the young to remain in their territory for up to a year. <i>(Jan 2000,NZ Geographic,No. 45/91</i>)

>  <b>FALLA et al.</b> LITTLE SPOTTED KIWI <i>Apteryx oweni </i> <i>(1966,Birds of New Zealand,19</i>)

>  <b>NATUSCH</b> There are three species of kiwi, divided into five races: the North Island kiwi (<i>Apteryx australis mantelli </i>... ); the South Island tokoeka (<i>A.a. australis</i>); the Stewart Island Kiwi (<i>A.a. lawryi </i>... );the little spotted kiwi of west ad southern South Island (<i>A. owenii</i>);and the great spotted kiwi from more northern parts of the South Island (<i>A. haastii</i>). <i>(1967,Animals of NZ, The,250</i>)

>  <i>2. Apteryx owenii</i> Gould 1847 <i>Little Spotted Kiwi (Kiwi-pukupuku)</i> South Island ... <i>(1970,Annotated Checklist of Birds of NZ,15,1980 ed.</i>)

>  <i>Little Spotted Kiwi Apteryx owenii </i>GOULD, 1847 ... OTHER NAME; <i>Little grey kiwi. </i> <i>(1985,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,39</i>)

>  In two years' time little spotted kiwi could be prodding their way through the kidney ferns and bog moss in the Never Never land of Anchor Island, along with yellowhead, saddleback and perhaps even another dozen kakapo. <i>(Aug 3, 2002,Listener (New Zealand Listener),34</i>)

>  A fourth species of kiwi had been discovered... Till now scientists had recognised three species - the great spotted kiwi or roa in the northwest of the South Island, the little spotted kiwi on Kapiti and a few other islands, and the brown kiwi, scattered from Northland to Stewart Island. <i>(Apr 23, 1993,Dominion,3</i>)

>  Twenty endangered little spotted kiwis, extinct on the North Island for about 125 years, were moved from Kapiti Island to the fenced sanctuary in early July. <i>(Aug 9, 2000,Dominion,7</i>)

## little teal
 
 <i>n.</i> (Environment)

>  <b>HUTTON</b> 97. QUERQUEDULA GIBBERIFRONS. S. Muller. Little Teal <i>Anas gracilis</i>. BULLER. <i>(1871,Birds of New Zealand,36</i>)

>  (Black Stewart Island) The following is a list of the birds shot ... <i>Querquedula gibberifrons</i>, little teal; <i>Anas superciliosa</i>, grey duck; ... <i>Graculus carbo</i>, cormorant or black shag; <i>Graculus varius</i>, pied shag; <i>Graculus brevirostris</i>, white throated shag; also another species of shag, locally called the `blue shag' ... pretty highly esteemed by the sailors as an article of food; <i>(1872,Appendix Proc. Otago Prov. Council,39</i>)

>  <i>Querquedula gibberifrons</i>. - Tete, Little Teal. Both Islands; Australia, New Caledonia, Indian Archipelago. <i>(1886,TrNZI,18/117</i>) [NOTES: (Martin)]

>  Amongst the b[i]rds of the district Mr Smith notes the morepork, the kaka .. the little teal, the crested grebe, the dabchick .. and several species of sea-birds. <i>(Oct 4, 1918,Grey River Argus,4</i>)

>  I picked them up again, and dried them as well as I could and took them home, and kept them in a warm place all night, but I am sorry to say two succumbed - the little teal and one of the ducks. <i>(Jul 26, 1894,Otago Witness,34</i>)

## Livestock Incentive Scheme
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a programme to encourage land development [in which farmers are paid in cash or tax relief for each additional stock unit held in a year.]

>  <b>MACMILLAN</b> The Livestock Incentive Scheme is a .. For every stock unit carried above a specified base number calculated over the preceding two winters, there is a payment of $12 cash or $24 taxation relief. The farmer can use payment from the scheme to buy in extra stock or alternatively receive a payment for any extra animals he retains. <i>(1980,Land Development in the Chatham Islands,11</i>)

## lizard
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [kakariki](../K#kakariki))

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i> Kakariki, n </i>. ... 2 <i> green lizard </i>. <i>(1892,Dictionary of the Maori Language,49</i>)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> <i> Green Lizard </i>, ...sometimes called the <i> Spotted Green Lizard </i>, a New Zealand reptile, <i> Naultinus elegans </i>, Gray. <i>(1898,Austral English,175</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i> Kakariki </i>... 2. <i> Naultinus elegans </i>, green lizard. <i>(1917,Dictionary of the Maori Language,108</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i> Moko-kakariki ... Naultinus elegans, green gecko. </i> <i>Ibid.</i> 244 <i> Papa ... Hoplodactylus pacificus, brown gecko </i> a lizard = <i> moko-papa. </i> <i>(1957,Dictionary of the Maori Language,207</i>)

>  <i> green lizard n.</i> New Zealand reptile. <i>(1966,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Whitcombe's),616</i>)

>  <i> green lizard n.</i> N.Z. reptile. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),9</i>)

>  <b>BUICK</b> [The Waitaki-mouth site's] principal inhabitants are skinks, or large green rock-lizards, and rabbits ... <i>(1937,Moa-Hunters of NZ,162</i>)

>  <i>Brown tree-lizard </i> ... of New Zealand, <i> Naultinus pacificus. </i> <i>(1898,Moa-Hunters of NZ,58</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i> Papa, ... 2. Dactylocnemis pacificus, tree-lizard. = moko-papa, narara-papa, teretere. </i> <i>(1917,Dictionary of the Maori Language,284</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i> Papa ... Hoplodactylus pacificus, brown gecko </i> a lizard = <i> moko-papa. </i> <i>(1957,Dictionary of the Maori Language,244</i>)

>  <b>HUTCHINSON</b> [A lizard] called `karaiki' [?sic] by the Maoris, and `manuka lizard' [c1909] by the settlers ... <i>(1947,At Omatua's Fireside,124</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a high-country musterer or shepherd.

>  Lizard.- Slang for musterer, I suppose because they both crawl over the hills. (C.) <i>(Nov 4, 1933,Press, The,15</i>) [NOTES: Acland Glossary]

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> Lizard. A sheep-musterer: New Zealand and Australian rural: since ca, 1880. B., 1941, 1942. He has to move both slowly and quietly. <i>(1949,Dictionary of Slang,1101</i>) [NOTES: Addenda]

>  <b>McGILL</b> lizard sheep musterer, by association with shearing handpiece of same name. - idler; short for `lounge lizard', a lounge bar idler after women. Both ANZ. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,70</i>)

>  <b>CARR</b> Shepherds are frequently known as Lizards' - the other station hands declare that all the work they do is to crawl over the hills and go to sleep in the sun. <i>(1913,Country Work,33</i>)

>  <b>MOORE</b> They [stockmen] were known as 'the lizards' (because they were rock-crawlers I suppose.) <i>(1969,From Forest to Farm,19</i>)

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> lizard: a musterer. Perhaps the term has arisen from his having to sidle through steep bluffs, because sometimes one would prefer to crawl like a lizard than stay upright. <i>(1981,Loughnan Glossary,50</i>)

>  In the old days they used to say that men who had gone mustering had 'taken up the burnt chops'. Also called a lizard from crawling round the hills. <i>(Nov 15, 1983,NZ Journal of Agriculture,20</i>)

>  <b>MCGILL</b> lizard: sheep musterer, by association with shearing handpiece of same name. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,70</i>)

## loaf
 
 <i>n.</i> In esp. NI towns, the standard `loaf' size was usually the two-pound, with a half-loaf being a pound. In country districts, esp. in the South Island, the standard loaf size was usually four-pound; with a half-loaf, two-pound, and a quarter-loaf, one pound. `Tourists' and holiday makers from towns were and are often thus confused when ordering country bread: the town's `half' was the country's `quarter' loaf.

>  <b>LELAND</b> <i>bread</i>: It comes in standard-sized loaves (government regulation). 350g (about 12 oz), 500g (about 1 lb), 700g (about 1 and a half lb). Unfortunately, common parlance for these loaves differs between the North and South islands. Many of the 700g loaves are made like a camel with two so that they can conveniently be split in half. Usually, if you ask for a loaf in the South Island it is one of these two humps (350g) that you get. In the North Island, if you want the same amount of bread (350g) you must ask for a half loaf. The other thing about bread, is that in this land of abundant food, where restaurant servings are usually generous, it is very hard to get more than one piece of bread (or one roll). Unlike North America, where they seem to hope you will settle for smaller servings if you fill up with bread, in New Zealand restaurants appear afraid that you will spoil your appetite with the stuff. <i>(1980,Personal Kiwi-Yankee Dictionary, A,18</i>)



## loaf on
 
 <i>v.</i> To stay with (free); (? to bludge on) (cf loafer).

>  <b>LADY BARKER</b> At first [new chums] simply `loaf' (as it is called here) on their acquaintances and friends. <i>(1873,Station Amusements in NZ,17</i>)

>  Loaf on.- An expression I have often heard used to signify `stay with'. (C.) <i>(Nov 4, 1933,Press, The,15</i>) [NOTES: Acland Glossary Press]



## loafer
 
 <i>n.</i> A cadger; a beggar (cf loaf on).

>  <b>CAIRD</b> These gentlemen [who wander from place to place with no particular object] are generally known in the colony by the significant name of `loafers' <i>(1874,NZ Sheepfarming,26</i>)

>  <b>THATCHER</b> In London the swells in the park drive all round, But the diggers drive here with a pick underground; And the props that they use, surely everyone knows Ain't the same used in London for hanging up clothes. The women use fans* there, the men use them here, Though to cockneys that statement may sound very queer, And the fellows that frequently hang round hotels, Out here are called loafers, at home are called swells. <i>(1875,NZ Songster,3/88</i>) [NOTES: London & the Diggings]

>  <b>WADDINGTON</b> ...`loafers' who do not care to work, but prefer begging. <i>(1887,Notes of a Tour in the England of the Antipodes,34</i>)

>  <b>ELKINGTON</b> Every man who doesn't work, be he rich or poor, is termed a loafer. <i>(1906,Adrift in NZ,13</i>)



## loafer cow
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a high-consuming, low-producing dairy cow. (See [boarder](../B#boarder))

>  The cow is on her trial. Her milk production and the butter-fat it contains are all carefully recorded. The 'loafer' cow finds her way sooner to the butcher than she would do if no particulars were kept of what she is yielding. <i>(Mar 21, 1919,Hawera Star,2</i>)



## loafing area
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) an area where farm stock congregate when they are not grazing.

>  Best results are obtained by placing [mineral ] blocks in loafing areas. These are usually adjacent to the best feed areas within the paddock. <i>(Sep 1973,NZ Journal of Agriculture,110</i>)

<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a shed where dairy cows are fed or kept during winter.

## loafing pad
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a stand-off area where stock rest, in order to save pasture in winter.

## loafing yard
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a solid-based area used to house stock during winter.

>  For the relatively small outlay of just over aGBP100, Mr Steve Algie, a town supply dairyman on the low-lying Taieri Plain, has provided a partly-covered loafing yard to keep his milkers off the pastures during the wet winter months. He decided to use a loafing yard. <i>(Sep 28, 1961,NZ Farmer,46</i>)

## loaming
 
 <i>n.</i> [goldminers']

>  Frequently he may find on hill slopes quartz fragments intermingled with soil or clay. ... the wise man will wash the attached soil and if a tail of gold is obtained proceed to prospect the areasystematically. This method is called `loaming'. <i>(1952,Prospecting for Gold,20</i>)

## lobster
 
 <i>n.</i> crayfish (See [cf. crawfish](../C#crawfish))

>  [Natives] sold us some Lobster Mussels and two Conger ells. [sic] [fn2] The common N.Z. salt water crayfish is <i> Jasus lalandi..</i>. <i>(Nov 1, 1769,Cook's Journals,I-189,1955 ed.</i>)

>  <b>McDONNELL</b> The rivers are well stocked with fine fish ... - the very creeks swarm with them. There are abundance of lobsters, crawfish, oysters, prawns, and shrimps; besides clams, peppies, muscles, limpets, and cockles. <i>(1834,Journal,15,1979 ed.</i>)

>  In order to ascertain the possibilities of lobsters [European lobster, <i> Homarus vulgaris </i>] and crayfish living peaceably together, one of each, both accustomed to confinement, were placed in an observation-tank. But there can be no question as to the lobster being `boss,' as it chased the crayfish about the tank, snapping at it with its powerful claws, the crayfish having to ascend to the surface to get out of its way, so that they had to be at once separated. <i>(1892,TrNZI,24/487</i>)

>  <b>MORRIS</b> <i> Lobster, n.</i> The name is often carelessly used in Australia for the <i> Crayfish </i>(q.v.). <i>(1898,Austral English,271</i>)

## local option
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  The decision of the recount in the Invercargill local option issue has been announced. <i>(Feb 7, 1944,NZEF Times,III/137 : 4</i>)

>  <b>SINCLAIR</b> Since it was distinctly possible that Stout might split the Liberal Party over this issue [temperance], Seddon was in 1893 obliged to forestall him by introducing a `local option'. This gave the voters the right to decide on the continuation or reduction of liquor licences in their electorates; a three-fifths majority could prohibit the sale of liquor altogether. <i>(1959,History of New Zealand,182</i>)

>  local option n. (esp. in Scotland, New Zealand, and the U.S.) the privilege of a municipality, county, etc., to determine by referendum whether a particular activity, esp. the sale of liquor, shall be permitted there. <i>(1982,Collins Concise English Dictionary,661</i>) [NOTES: nf. 1986 NZPOD; 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 288]]

>  Local Option is, and. always has been, a help to iniquity. <i>(Apr 16, 1904,NZ Free Lance,12</i>)

## loci
 
 <i>n.</i> A small locomotive engine, esp. for hauling timber on private tram-lines; narrow-gauge team locomotive for hauling logs from native bush cf loco). [cf 1854 Richmond Letters 1/313 The speed of a bullock approaches that of a locomotive railway.] -->ca1900 Havelock and Pelorus; and King Country loceys - timber-hauling locomotive engines. The embankments for these can still be seen in places, and there is a loci outside the Havelock museum.]

>  <b>GASKELL</b> She often saw wisps of smoke rising against the bush ... Sometimes she heard a lokey puffing, ... <i>(1978,All Part of the Game,84</i>) [NOTES: 1947 Gaskell `School Picnic' in 1978 All Part of the Game]

>  .. gang of chamberhands ... crossing the loci tracks. <i>(1950,Landfall,14/125</i>) [NOTES: Middleton]

>  <b>HILLIARD</b> Through gaps ... she could see the railway yards, ... locis were hauling [check ?mauling] rakes of trucks. <i>(1974,Maori Woman,25</i>)

>  (Westland) Bill Oats with his lokey, which provides cheap and reliable transport over boggy ground. -->The first task was construction of a small `lokey'. After Oats had sketched out the general arangement , construction was undertaken by a Whataroa firm. Oats already had a setof wheels suitable for wooden rails, and the engine from an old Ford 100E was at hand. It took 2 [and a half] days to build, and with half a 44-gallon drum as a bonnet and a coast of green paint, was soon looking a picture. An old four-wheel railway trolley coupled on behind completed therolling stock <i>(Aug 14, 1982,Listener (New Zealand Listener),60</i>)

>  <b>BEARDSLEY</b> lokey locomotive <i>(1984,Blackball 08,244</i>) [NOTES: Glossary]

>  Whilst working for Smyth Bros & Boryer, Arohena, 1942 to 1945, then Ngaroma 1945 to 1956, the Lockie was turned around with the smoke box facing the load... My guess is that the photo was taken at Taurewa prior to 1942 when Egmont Box owned the Lockie. <i>(Dec 2001,NZ Memories,No. 33 (Dec/Jan 2002) :9</i>)



## lock-up
 
 <i>n.</i> a sealed-room briefing

>  It debased the budget in the place where it ought to matter most and underlined the growing importance of the late 80s phenomenon called the lock-up. Lock-ups are sealed-room briefings for journalists and analysts two nor three hours before the budget presentation... The opposition is locked out of the lock-up, and the government's own backbenchers get only a cursory briefing just before the budget's delivered. <i>(Jul 25, 1992,Listener (New Zealand Listener),28</i>)

>  If only the Greens had staged their silent protest a day later. The press gallery and a phalanx of analysts and technicians could have been saved the boredom of the budget lock-up to concentrate on the only issue on political minds - the election date and anything that may precipitate it. But no, Thursday's lock-up went, as it always does, without a hitch. <i>(May 26, 2002,Sunday Star Times,C2</i>)

>  [The government] assembled a Budget-sized mob of officials and held a lock-up. This is a thing whereby journalists and hangers-on are detained for a couple of hours under guard, minus cellphones - burly security escorts to and from the toilet, the works - while they are briefed about the documents. The ideas being, if let loose too soon, they might derange the Dow Jones with half-cocked ideas of the government's brilliant blueprint for worl domination. <i>(Feb 23, 2002,Listener (New Zealand Listener),16</i>)

>  <b>It's tradition</b> that the guys and gals from the money markets and the scribes from the parliamentary press gallery get an advance peek at the budget in special "lock-ups" held before the finance minister reads the document in the House. <i>(May 21, 2005,Dominion Post,B4</i>)

## locks
 
 <i>n.</i> The short wool which has either fallen through the wool table or been swept from the shearing board Farming: (usu. pl.) pieces of short wool from the edges of fleeces, sweepings of the shearing board, etc.

>  <b>FITTON</b> NEW ZEALAND. - Scoured fleece, 1s. 8-1/2d. ...; average flocks, 1s. 5d. ...; pieces and locks, 1s. 1d. ... unwashed, 10-1/2d. ... <i>(1856,New Zealand (various authors/titles),234</i>)

>  ... the latter is always skirted and classed, whereas the black fleece was rolled up just as taken from the sheep's back, the belly-pieces being also left in. ... For example, white wool divides, when skirted by the roller at the wool-table, thus: Belly pieces, say, 6d. per pound; first pieces, 8d. per pound; ... fragments or locks, 4d. per pound. <i>(1901,TrNZI,33/196</i>)

>  <b>McDONALD</b> The wool table, about 7ft. x 5ft., is made of battens about 1-1/2in. wide ... to permit the locks or second cuts to fall through. <i>(1915,NZ Sheepfarming,69</i>)

>  <b>McDONALD</b> The big stations may make classing a very simple business, ... and may even divide up the skirtings or pieces from the fleece into different classes, besides keeping the bellies, locks, and stained wool separate. <i>Ibid.</i> 148 The belly wool and the pieces or locks are kept separate from the fleece when rolling it, ... <i>(1920,NZ Sheepfarming,144</i>)

>  <b>SMITH</b> There are also baskets for the bellies and locks ... The `picker up' is the name given to the young man or boy whose duty it is to [75] carry the wool from the shearer to the skirting table ... <i>(1927,Sheep & Wool Industry,74</i>)

>  `Locks' consist mainly of short pieces of wool such as second cuts, sweepings and short wool that falls on to the table during handling. <i>(1936,Agricultural Organization in NZ,710</i>)

>  Boards which fit between cleats on opposite legs of the [wool] table keep the locks from being trampled on. <i>(Oct 16, 1950,NZ Journal of Agriculture,310</i>)

>  <b>FERNANDEZ</b> `What's 2nd Pcs H?' `Grade 2, pieces - that's skirtings - hogget wool.' `And Bls E?' `That's belly wool. E grade bellies.' `Lox?' `Oh, that's floor sweepings.' `What about Lbs Wigs?' `Lambtop-knots - off the head.' <i>(1973,Tussock Fever,166</i>)

>  Locks: Short wool which has either fallen through the wool table or been swept from the shearing board (also spelt lox). <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,56</i>)

>  Locks see skirtings. <i>Ibid.</i> 17 ... colonial woolscourers who removed grease, sticks and thistles from the locks and pieces - the dirty, inferior quality skirtings trimmed from the fleeces. <i>(1985,Woolscours of NZ,9</i>)

>  lock ... (in pl.) NZ & Austral. small pieces of wool that fall off fleece during shearing. <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,440</i>) [NOTES: [nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.]]

>  <b>OGONOWSKA-COATES</b> Fleece. The main part of the sheep's wool when shorn. It is picked up in one piece and carried to the wool table, where it is skirted and classed. The small bits and pieces that are cut off separately by the shearer are the locks, bellies and pieces. <i>(1987,Boards, Blades & Barebellies,96</i>)
