## lodge
 
 <i>n.</i> originally a fishing lodge, later a luxury guesthouse, offering good food, wine, recreational fishing and/or shooting, and privacy

>  <b>PARSONS</b> Alan Pye's hands physically shaped a small paradise in New Zealand called Huka Lodge, to which first anyone, and later chiefly a select few, were admitted, many of them again and again. <i>Ibid.</i> 11 By 1974 the Lodge had passed into the hands of Mrs and Mrs H.P. Harland-Baker, who subsequently carried out a series of improvements including the upgrading of the row of chalets facing the river bank... In the mid-1980s, Huka Lodge became the property of Mr. Alex van Heeren, who built a modern lodge and individual villas along the river-bank. <i>Ibid.</i> 45 Long before his dream began to come true, Alan had determined that his fishing-lodge would be only for men. <i>(1998,Pye's Kingdom of Huka Lodge,8</i>)

>  The luxury Puka Puka Lodge is an under-performing asset... The tribe was unhappy with the lodge's performance, he said. <i>(Dec 2, 2000,Dominion,6</i>)

>  Puka Puka Lodge, nestled in bush overlooking the exclusive coastal community of Pauanui, is a top-dollar resort attracting privacy-seeking guests who fly in to its helicopter pad. <i>(Dec 1, 2000,Dominion,3</i>)

>  Lilybank Lodge, an exclusive South Canterbury hunting resort built by the youngest son of disgraced former Indonesian president Suharto, has been sold. <i>(Dec 1, 2000,Dominion,8</i>)

>  <i>Pavement</i> is regarded as a style magazine and the next step is to focus on style associated topics like food, restaurants, interior design, wine, New Zealand travel and great places to stay. They should be covering Huka Lodge. <i>(Nov 1999,North & South,92</i>)

>  We tracked down 3 diverse properties, set on some of the country's finest sites, where the surrounding region is a natural source of leisure activities, fresh local food and wines, and guests can relax and recharge their spirits: Arthur's Pass Wilderness Lodge perched in the foothills of the Southern Alps, Paratiho Farms Lodge set in a stunning rolling green valley between Nelson and Motueka, and the Cavalli Beach House which nestles between two ridges with spectacular views over the jewel-like Cavalli Islands. Each offers very personal service overseen by owners who live on the properties and are constantly on hand to look after and pamper their guests. <i>(Sep 2000,Cuisine,120</i>)

>  Lodges range from luxurious ro rustic and guests can be assured of individual hospitality from hosts. A number of lodges are the private homes of husband-and-wife hosts. Other places have been purpose-built. <i>(Sep 1986,Traveller,12</i>)

>  Takaro Lodge, which opened in 1970.. was the first luxury lodge in the South Island. <i>(May 2000,North & South,16</i>)

>  [Hdg] neighbours may fight country lodge resource consent <i>(Dec 19, 2000,Independent Herald (Wellington),3</i>)

>  Eastwick says the initial idea behind a corporate lodge was to give busines people a place to kick back and relax without being watched. <i>(Jan 20, 2001,Evening Post,13</i>)

>  Queenstown-based mountainside retreat Nugget Point has been voted second in "Top Places to stay in New Zealand"... First was Taupo's Huka Lodge. <i>(Jan 29, 2001,Evening Post,18</i>)

>  At cocktail hour each evening, guests mingle and help themselves to drinks in the lodge room where a gigantic log-fire usually blazes and tartan carpets adorn the wooden floors.. The lodge's initials are even engraved on the soap, and embroidered discretely on pillow cases and the duvet cover. <i>(Apr 9, 2001,Evening Post,17</i>)

>  Tired of diary farming after 30 years, a Manawatu couple have built a flash lodge instead. <i>(Jun 1, 2002,Evening Post,17</i>)

>  This conflict was sown in the early 1970s when Minister of Lands Duncan McIntyre approved selling public lands to a rich American for a luxury fishing/hunting lodge near Te Anau. <i>(Aug 11, 2002,Sunday Star Times,A6</i>)

>  Since he arrived in New Zealand, the American has been lodge-hopping, starting in Northland where he stayed at Kauri Reefs, before he put his head down at Huka Lodge while trout fishing in Taupo. <i>(Jun 16, 2002,Sunday Star Times,C13</i>)

>  The couple, who ran Zanzibar restaurant in New Plymouth for three years, decided to build the lodge - designed by local architect Ian Pritchard - after they had trouble finding anywhere nearby to escape for a night or two of pampering. <i>(Apr 29, 2003,Dominion Post,A12</i>)



## lodgepole pine
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Pinus contorta</i>, lodgepole pine, [<i>Pinus contorta</i> spreads and invades, considered a pest]

>  Lodgepole pine, Pinus contorta, is a relative of the familiar radiata pine. <i>(2001,NZ Geographic,53/54</i>) [NOTES: Date 0 / 9 / 2001 Source NZ Geographic]

>  Lodgepole pine, <i>Pinus contorta</i>, is a relative of the familiar radiata pine... In the south-eastern Kaweka Ranges contorta has marched across the southern ridges, shading out many species, and in places is so thick that pushing through it has become virtually impossible. Each year, the Department of Conservation tackles areas of contorta for removal, but it is an ongoing and expensive battle. In the eastern South Island ,there is no control, and contorta threatens to change tussockland into exotic forests. <i>(Sep 2001,NZ Geographic,No. 53/54</i>)



## log
 
 <i>n.</i> = loggerhead [whalers']

>  <b>McNAB</b> <i>(1838,Old Whaling Days</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> short for log of wood (the Ranfurly Shield) (See [log of wood](../L#log-of-wood))

>  <b>HOWITT</b> The following week they defended the log against Wanganui. <i>(1975,NZ Rugby Greats,20</i>)

>  And the log holders are becoming accustomed to Tayler's cross country course ... <i>(Apr 14, 1974,Sunday Times (NZ),19/1</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> a marijuana cigarette.

>  <b>COX</b> `Best dope right here in my pocket. You smoke?' [43] ... The log Bill produced was a fat one. He set it alight, had a quick blow then passed. <i>(1987,Dirty Work,42</i>)



## log bogie
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>MAHONEY</b> Log bogies were the wagons that carried the logs on bush trams. They were a form of rail vehicle distinctive to bush trams. The typical bogie had a simple wooden chassis riding on four wheels. <i>(1998,Era of the Bush Trams in NZ, The,21</i>)



## log country
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) land on which bush has been felled and logs remain.

>  <b>NEWTON</b> Out behind Huia the country runs higher and much of it is 'log country.' This type of country was invariably left littered with logs ('log country') after a bush burn ... <i>(1969,Big Country of the North Island,208</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> .. it winds its way through that wild log country common to the higher land in this area. <i>(1969,Big Country of the North Island,236</i>)



## log fence
 
 <i>n.</i> a (?temporary) fence made of piled up logs.

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> To make a log fence you drove in two stakes here and there opposite each other and a few inches apart, and filled up with sticks lying flat and lashed with karewao vine. <i>(1939,First White Boy Born in Otago,98</i>)



## log hauler
 
 <i>n.</i> a stationary engine and winch used to haul logs by logging line from bush to mill

>  <b>CARR</b> Log-haulers, worked from an engine, are used in some mills. Hooking the winding rope on to suitable trees ... demands ... quickness of judgment. Wages. ... bullocky 10s to 12s a day, hooker-on 9s to 12s a day. <i>(1913,Country Work,40</i>)

>  One shop [at Thames] specialized in the manufacture of sawmilling and bush-working machinery, log-haulers in particular. <i>Ibid. </i> 26 Boilers, giant timber-haulers, and stone-crushers are just a matter of course [for the foundry]. <i>(1948,Our Own Country,26</i>) [NOTES: from Korero, AEWS magazine]

>  <b>SMYTH</b> They saw the oil-driven winch, or log-hauler, as it is called. <i>(1930,Wooden Rails,35</i>)

>  <b>MAHONEY</b> The first log hauler constructed and put to use in Southland - if not the first in New Zealand - was made in July of 1888 for Mr John Murdoch, sawmiller of Woodend near Invercargill. <i>(1998,Era of the Bush Trams in NZ, The,19</i>)



## log hut
 
 <i>n.</i> (contrast frame hut)

>  <b>HENTY</b> [The house] should be of the class known as log-huts .... They are much cooler .... in the heat of the summer than frame-huts. ... You easily face them outside and in the with match board. <i>(1891,Maori & Settler,196</i>)



## log of wood/log o' wood
 
 <i>n.</i> the Ranfurly Shield; the interprovincial rugby union challenge trophy. (See [also log](../A#also-log))

>  Wellington will field the same team in its first defence of the Ranfurly Shield .... as lifted the `log of wood' from Auckland <i>(Sep 2, 1963,Evening Post,12</i>)

>  This is only the third time Taranaki have won the Shield. In 1913 ... they lifted the `log of wood' from Auckland 14-11 .... <i>(Sep 7, 1963,Evening Post,23</i>)

>  <b>SLATTER</b> The rep games when the log of wood has gone. <i>(1968,Pagan Game, The,174</i>)

>  <b>McMENAMIN</b> Since then 'the log of wood', or simply 'the log', as the shield [sc. The RanfurlyShield] became affectionately known, hasprovided some of the most gloriouschapters in the history of New Zealandrugby. <i>(1986,Glory Days,13</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> We ... won the Ranfurly Shield from Auckland. But Auckland's team might have been weakened ... the Saturday we took that Log of Wood off them. `You think Waikato will keep the Shield the whole of this season?' Don asked. <i>(1973,NZ Jack,82</i>)

>  <b>GLEN</b> Apart from the thousands ... who attend the New Zealand religion of the rugby variety, it was doubtful if the competition for the `log of wood' would have matched the numbers at McNab onthat day. <i>(1980,Bush in Our Yard,105</i>)

>  the log of wood inf. see RANFURLY SHIELD <i>(1985,Collins Compact Dictionary,289</i>) [NOTES: [nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.; nf. 1986 NZPOD]]

>  [Kirton, rugby rep coach says:] `I will certainly do one more year [rep rugby coaching] if I am wanted. I'll give that log of wood a real crack and then get out.' <i>(Oct 11, 1986,Dominion,17</i>)

>  [Advt.] The Trouble with Rugby Today is.. Ye Old Log of Wood. <i>(May 26, 1938,NZ Observer,21</i>)

>  At the end of the month the vast horde of 29 players descend from Hawkes Bay to drag back the "Old Log of Wood." <i>(Aug 18, 1938,NZ Observer,26</i>)

>  [Hdg] After Twenty-Two years... 'Log of Wood' comes back to Wellington. <i>(Aug 3, 1953,Evening Post,14</i>)

>  Having defended the Log o' Wood effectively for the second straight season, it was appropriate that it was defence which kept Waikato in touch during a first spell in which it rather had to batten down the hatches. <i>(Oct 17, 1999,Sunday Star Times,B5</i>)

>  Mehrtens did admit he felt unusually fresh for this time of the year, adding the Log o' Wood helped that: "There's a buzz there and the shield helps immensely." <i>(Oct 14, 2001,Sunday Star Times,B3</i>)

>  While Canterbury and Auckland were battling for the log o' wood on Saturday night, I was at the intimate Silo theatre in central Auckland, watching a play about a man who has an affair with a goat. <i>(Oct 10, 2005,NZ Herald,C2</i>)

>  The last time Auckland challenged Canterbury for the shield - successfully in 2003 - Koonwaiyou was on the bench for the visitors but he felt the emptiness of losing the Log of Wood last season to Bay of Plenty. <i>(Oct 7, 2005,NZ Herald,Supersport D3</i>)

>  Jones, one of the surviving members of the Otago team that last lifted the Log o' Wood in 1957, said he would have been lousy company during the match. "I'd be far too anxious to talk," he elaborated. <i>(Sep 3, 2006,Sunday Star Times,A1</i>)

>  [Caption] The captains in last Saturday's Ranfurly Shield match... Between them is the "log of wood". <i>(Sep 11, 1935,NZ Free Lance,19</i>)

>  <b>STONE</b> Many a fine game he played in defence of the old "log of wood." <i>(1938,Rugby Players Who Have Made NZ Famous,13</i>)

## log stain
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) charcoal or bush-burn stain on fleece wool. (See [bush stain; burn stain](../B#bush-stain;-burn-stain))

>  <b>MAF</b> Bush stain: Discoloration of wool with charcoal from bush burns. More commonly referred to as log stain. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,53</i>)

>  <b>SQUIRE</b> Log stain - charcoal discoloured wool from sheep grazing in burnt-over areas. <i>(1991,A to Z Glossary of Wool Terms,25</i>)

>  <b>WOOLPRO</b> Log stain - discoloration of wool with charcoal from bush burn. <i>(2001,Woolpro Glossary of Wool Terms,20</i>)

>  <b>MOORE</b> [caption] Sheep in a new clearing - note the tangle of timber which will cause `logstain' in their wool. <i>(1969,From Forest to Farm,65</i>)

>  Log stain: Discoloration [sic] of wool with charcoal from bush burn. Also called bush or charcoal stain. <i>(1982,MAF Agricultural Glossary,56</i>)



## log the lizard
 
 <i>phr.</i> to gain sexual entry. [also lodge the l. -->ca1951 p.c. Don Singe (RNZAF) -->ca1943-45 RNZAF Pacific (ca1952 D. Singe) Log prob. because lizards go up or into logs to hide: but prob. also assoc. with `lodge"; (cf P. go up a log (as a lizard or snake does: Aust: 20c)]



## log up
 
 <i>v.</i> To pile up (logs) for burning (cf logging up). [v.t. (usu. in passive use)]

>  <b>WALLACE</b> Stumping and logging-up [bold] the remaining trunks and stumps of trees is a common practice. ... <i>(1891,Rural Economy of Aust. & NZ</i>)

>  So, also, the handling of axe and bill-hook so as to avoid danger in under-scrubbing, felling, and clearing, is not learnt in a day ...`Logging up' the great scorched logs on the burnt spaces, and sowing down the clearings with grass-seed follow the felling; <i>(1892,NZ Official Handbook,156</i>)

>  <b>WALLACE</b> When the [bush] burning is badly done ... the rubbish lies thick over the ground and the whole has to be gone over again and `logged-up'. <i>(1891,Rural Economy of Aust. & NZ,232</i>)

>  There is very little logging-up done now-a-days, and with a good burn it is quite unnecessary. ... two or three chains around the site should be logged up and burnt. <i>(1902,Brett's Colonists Guide,21</i>)

>  <b>THOMSON</b> These [big unburned trees] are `logged up' afterwards, that is rolled together and piled round the stumps, so as to dry thoroughly preparatory to `firing' them again. <i>(1905,Bush Boys of NZ,32</i>)

>  (Aroha, Hauraki) When the milling-timber has been removed the undergrowth is cut down and, when sufficiently dry is burned, the fire killing any large trees still standing. ..... Later the unburned wood is `logged up' and burnt ... <i>(1913,NZ Geol. Survey Bulletin,No 16 (NS) 27</i>)

>  <b>BAUGHAN</b> The Stars are its shining logs; / Here, sparse and single, but yonder, as logg'd-up for burning, /Close in a cluster of light. <i>(1908,Shingle-Short,84</i>)

>  <b>ANTHONY</b> I logged up about 10 acres ..., and had three blistering, heart-rending days burning it all at once. I went around every heap with a fire-stick first, <i>(1924,Follow the Call,30,1975 ed.</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i> (Rural) to gather fallen bush into a pile to dry before firing.

>  They can be felled and logged up or used for firewood. <i>(1883,Brett's Colonists Guide,18</i>)

>  <b>STEVENS</b> Laurie was working at the track and also did some logging up and burning. <i>(1914,Diary,21</i>)

>  <b>STEVENS</b> Laurie & H Cooley were logging up and burning. <i>(Sep 23, 1914,Diary</i>)

>  <b>CHINN</b> I rode through miles of country simply littered with burnt stumps and big boles of trees left to rot away or to be logged up and burnt before the land could be cultivated for cropping. <i>(1963,Packtrack to Highway,51</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>v.</i> To pile up logs.

>  <b>WILSON</b> He ... put in the ... morning and evening on his own land, and `logged up' by the light of his fires half the night. <i>(1955,Land of My Children,80</i>)

>  <b>ANTHONY</b> Sam used to hook his horse on to big trees when he was logging up, and then hammer the horse over the head with a batten when it couldn't pull the load. <i>(1924,Follow the Call,7,1975 ed.</i>)

>  <b>MULINDER</b> After tea [Dad] was at work logging up and burning to clear the ground. <i>(1977,Pioneer Family, A,39</i>) [NOTES: A. Anderson's Reminiscences, Wairarapa 1870's]

 <i>n.</i> A bush-clearing which has not been logged up.

The logging-delivery tram, mill log-skids, engine, breakdown bench. <i>(1923,Small Sawmills,6</i>)



## log-way
 
 <i>n.</i> [sawmilling]

>  <b>R GILBERD</b> Remains of old bullock-tracks in old Kauri bush-country down which logs were snigged. Called simply `roads' by old bushmen, but are now called `log-ways' to distinguish from roads proper and tracks still in use. <i>(1950,Letter</i>) [NOTES: Northland]

>  NL, fr. <i>James Logan</i> 1751 Irish botanist + NL <i>-ia] 1 cap</i> : the type genus of the family Loganiaceae comprising Australian and New Zealand herbs and subshrubs that are sometimes cultivated for their clusters of pentamerous white to flesh-colored flowers 2 -s : any plant of the genus <i>Logania <i>(1961,Merriam-Webster Third International,1330</i>)

## loggerhead

## logging
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [logging up](../L#logging-up))

>  <b>JOSEPH MASTERS</b> On fine nights we used to work late, loging [sic], that is rolling larger trees together, to burn them out of the way, to get rid of them. <i>(1871,Autobiography,50</i>) [NOTES: (ATBMS)]

## logging truck
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Imagine twice the number of logging trucks using both internal forest roads and district roads. <i>(Nov 29, 2002,Dominion Post,C6</i>)



## logging up
 
 <i>n.</i> the gathering together of bush-felled logs for firing (from log up q.v.).

>  <b>WILLIAM MORGAN</b> Finished fencing on Wednesday, and since then have been busy at the occupation of `logging up'. <i>(Apr 21, 1860,Journal,18,1963 ed.</i>)

>  `Logging-up is generally done in the autumn, when there are strong gales of wind blowing. The bush which has been felled in winter, is set fire to, and in a day or two, when the ground is sufficiently cool for walking on, the still-burning logs are rolled together and piled up with rubbish, so that they may be burnt clear away <i>(1889,Collinson's Colonial Magazine,26</i>)

>  <b>BELL</b> He was employed ... to assist in the work of the farm, in attending to the stock, in logging up and clearing ... and in doing a little bushfalling, though the latter work was usually let by contract. <i>Ibid.</i> 200 Robinson's house was ... not threatened [by the bushfire], and the logging up and clearing ... rendered it unlikely that it would be endangered. <i>(1899,Shadow of the Bush,9</i>)

>  There is very little logging-up done now-a-days, and with a good burn it is quite unnecessary. ... two or three chains around the site should be logged up and burnt. <i>(1902,Brett's Colonists Guide,21</i>)

>  The process of improving the lands by the gradual `logging-up' and burning of the fallen tree-trunks is a long one, ... <i>(1902,Settler's Handbook of NZ, The,13</i>)

>  <b>LEVY</b> Removal of logs and standing stumps by logging-up and burning eliminates areas where fern and other growths may establish, and facilitates grazing and treading by cattle. <i>(1951,Grasslands of NZ,249,1979 ed.</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> At this time of the year every settler kept fires burning continuously to help clear surplus timber. This was termed `logging up'. <i>(1955,Land of My Children,90</i>)

>  <b>KEENE</b> The children liked to help with the logging up [ca1900], as indeed they could have done by picking up small pieces of wood .... <i>(1967,Tang of The Bush,72</i>)

>  <b>LEVY</b> Following the initial primary forest burn, secondary scrub fires and logging-up burns continue the breaking-in process. <i>(1951,Grasslands of NZ,250,1970 ed.</i>)

>  <b>RICHARDS</b> Farm work, scrub cutting, logging up and burning went on as usual. <i>(1989,Pioneer's Life, A,49</i>)

>  <b>SINCLAIR & HARREX</b> The commonest method of clearing bush..was simply to burn it off... The subsequent process of 'stumping' and 'logging up' could be prolonged. <i>(1976,Looking Back,10</i>)

## lokey
 
 <i>n.</i> (See [loci](../L#loci))

>  <b>BEARDSLEY</b> On to the wharf, stilling the fussing ofa lokey with a rake of coal trucks. <i>(1984,Blackball 08,12</i>)

>  The 16-wheel lokey <i>Lady Mary</i>.. operated between 1915 and 1917 on a logging line on the south side of the Tahakopa Valley. <i>(Jul 2003,NZ Geographic,No. 64/36</i>)

>  <b>MAHONEY</b> The biggest tram... was operated from 1904 to 1944 by the Taupo Totara Timber Company and required a fleet of six lokeys. <i>Ibid.</i> 79 The word 'lokey' is bushman's slang for a steam locomotive used on a bush tramway. From interviews with several hundred old bushmen it is apparent that the term was widely used throughout the ountry. <i>Ibid.</i> 83 New Zealand made an early start in introducing lokeys to bush tramways from the 1870s onwards, but an economic depression considerably slowed the process. <i>(1998,Era of the Bush Trams in NZ, The,12</i>)

>  <b>BIRD</b> The small lokey chuffed its way wearily home along the track towards Port Craig. <i>(1998,Viaducts against the Sky,69</i>)

## lolly
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> a sweet [(in miscellaneous combinations)]

>  Perhaps Phil Judd. when he was working on the dole in 1974 (and working in a lolly factory as a result), should have abandoned his dreams for Split Enz and set his sights on a career in confectionery. <i>(Dec 13, 2001,Dominion,10</i>)

>  <b>PICARD</b> [A] pale consumptive comp ... who couldn't fight his way out of a lollie bag ... <i>(1906,Some Ups and Downs in NZ&A,3</i>)

>  Irate city councillors complained that the large canopy erected for the Royal Visit at the Wellington Town Hall made the entrance look like a barber's shop or a lolly counter. <i>(Dec 23, 1953,NZ Observer,4</i>)

>  In addition to a lollie, fruit and cordial stall, the ladies will have a stall for.. disposing.. the goods remaining over from the recent sale of work. <i>(Jan 29, 1910,NZ Truth,1</i>)

>  We had counted our lolly collection so often that the jaffas had turned white and the pineapple lumps had lost their chocolate coating on the side of the jar. <i>(Apr 22, 2001,NZ Catholic,8</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> a sweet (cf. lolly-pop). [Spg occas. lollie. [Early uses are mainly pl.] 1953 13-15 M A2 Thames DHS 22A LOLLIES [15] -->1960 17C M B6 St Bede's, Chch 22A LOLLIES -->1960 18C F B12 Horowhenua C. 22A LOLLIES -->1968 NZ Contemp. Dict. Supp. (Collins) 12 lolly n sweet or candy in N.Z. (not money) -->1984 15-16 F E1 Pakuranga Coll. 22A LOLLIES [F15 M5] -->1984 14 F E123 Wgton Girls C. 22A LOLLIES [F7]]

>  It's in her book, written plain and simple: "To live to 90 years old and be able to spoil my great-grandchildren with lollies." <i>(May 4, 1992,Listener (New Zealand Listener),47</i>)

>  Once when she couldn't have me Dad let me go to the flicks by myself. He gave me some extra money, so I went to the milkbar and bought a gobstopper and some other lollies. I stuffed my face, and felt crook all the way home. He bought new footy boots for my eldest brother too, and an Art Union ticket for Mum. He must have had a beaut time up the RSA that day, I reckon. <i>(1988,University Entrance Board Bursaries Exam</i>)

>  <b>LUSH</b> ... Tui gave me a lolly and in biting it I broke one of my false front teeth - <i>(Nov 16, 1864,Waikato Journals,34,1982 ed.</i>)

>  Quamdin se bene gesserit. - Lollies for children who behave themselves. <i>(Aug 12, 1865,Punch in Canterbury,87</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> It consists of a few stores ... buying a few lollies for the Captain's daughter ... we returned on board to get our traps and come on shore. <i>(Jan 23, 1873,Diary</i>) [NOTES: 1873 Jan 23 G.M.Wilson Diary in 1973 Wierzbicka Wilson Family 145]

>  <b>PYKE</b> One of them filled her lap with cakes and `lollies' for the `bairnie'. <i>(1874,George Washington Pratt,164</i>)

>  [Give him] `baccy and lollies, and plenty of prog./ And prime the old beggar with lashions [sic, ?= lashin's] of grog. <i>(1879,Political Ballads from Free Lance,8</i>)

>  ... lollies and other juvenile delicacies. <i>(Apr 23, 1880,Evening Post,2/4</i>)

>  <b>BLAIR</b> It does not follow that the taste for lollies in the rising generation is dying out. <i>(1884,Industries of NZ,16</i>)

>  <b>BUTLER</b> [Wellington pilot] A friend of mine goes to the stores ... and says `I want some lollies.' <i>(1886,Glimpses of Maori Land,42</i>)

>  <b>BARLOW</b> He would risk his liberty for a bottle of lollies ... <i>(1888,Kaipara and Waitemata Echo,160</i>)

>  LolaZ.ly (l&obreve.laZ.l&ybreve.), n. [Cf. LOLLIPOP.] A lollipop; also, in general, any kind of sweetmeat or confectionery. <i>(1898,Merriam-Webster Third International,2026</i>)

>  <b>DAVIDSON</b> Although his wee Davie worried him when he was going to his work in the mornings for pennies, and in default of them coming, a general order for a wheelbarrow, spade, some lollies, or some crackers, he still thought in his heart ... <i>(1889,Stories of NZ Life,73</i>)

## lolly scramble
 
 <i>n.</i> the term now has wider application, as describing any kind of undignified attempt by groups of people to `get theirs'. The playing off of one political candidate against another in terms of pre-election promises has been described as the `election time lollyscramble' [cf. 10 / 11 / 1999 Independent (Auckland) Page 1 [Hdg] Govt v govt in scramble for govt lolly]

>  <b>WEBBER</b> In an effort to sustain this difficult role, it [sc the State] attempts the exercise of conducting a lolly scramble with one hand and snatching back the lolly with the other. <i>(1962,Look No Hands!,69</i>)

>  [Headline] Business outlook : no lolly-scramble Those still hoping the finance minister would be master of ceremonies at the sort of sectoral lolly-scramble which has characterised many New Zealand budgets were disappointed. <i>(Aug 26, 1985,National Business Review,28</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> lolly scramble children rucking for sweets. - any unseemly pursuit of power, often applied to political promises at election time. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,70</i>)

>  Lolly scramble time. The Hillary Commission and the Sports Foundation this week dished outthe dosh. <i>(Jul 16, 1999,Evening Post,32</i>)

>  .. this lolly-scramble election comes in a lull in the transition between two wrenching changes in our economy. <i>(Aug 23, 2005,NZ Herald,A11</i>)

>  In the lolly scramble of next week's election, many stay-at-home parents feel they are missing out. <i>(Sep 5, 2005,NZ Herald,A6</i>)

>  In the electoral lolly scramble, National is shovelling more sweets off the back of its truck than Labour - about $7 billion more by June 2009. <i>(Sep 8, 2005,NZ Herald,C2</i>)

>  It's a fact also that the primary school over our back fence lost trees, walls, roofs, corrugated-iron changing sheds, and had broken window-glass slung arcoss its soccer field like some sinister lolly-scramble. <i>(Oct 15, 1990,Listener (New Zealand Listener),94</i>)

>  Certainly there was no lolly scramble. There was an extra $35 million for lower income families. <i>(Jul 3, 1993,Evening Post,2</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> [(literal sense) also lollie scramble]

>  <b>FRAME</b> The lolly scramble ... was held both for the amusement of the staff ... and for the pleasure of the patients. <i>(1961,Faces in the Water,97</i>)

>  <b>HILLIARD</b> He was late for the lolly-scramble and the only lolly left was in a cow-plop. <i>(1965,Power of Joy,62</i>)

>  <b>ANDERSON</b> While the ladies did the washing up, the men organised games, and the afternoon [of the picnic] was spent in treasure hunts, paper trails and lollie scrambles. <i>(1972,Letter from James,144</i>)

>  <b>LELAND</b> lolly-scramble: Originally an activity engaged in at kids' parties where double handfuls of candy are thrown on the floor and the kids scramble for them. <i>(1980,Personal Kiwi-Yankee Dictionary, A,60</i>)

>  <b>NEAVE</b> Our school picnics were held at Otekaieke Station [c1900] ... The Duntroon School also joined us, and we looked forward to the refreshments and soft drinks provided, lolly scrambles etc., and small silver coins for winning races and competitions. <i>(1980,Land of Munros, The,48</i>)

>  (Deverson) Omissions [of NZ words from The Macquarie Dictionary] noted included primers, showgrounds, lemon-fish, lolly scramble and section (of land). <i>(Jan 21, 1984,Listener (New Zealand Listener),30</i>)

>  <b>GEE</b> `What are you eating'? she said. `Lollies'. `We won them in a lolly scramble'. <i>(1972,In My Father's Den,21</i>)

>  <b>LOCKE</b> Then there were sports, with lolly-scrambles and merry-go-rounds and boat swings for the small children. <i>(1984,Inky Way, The,102</i>)

>  Beneath the veneer of pikelets and lolly scrambles, there is something in this country not altogether nice. <i>(Oct 20, 2001,Listener (New Zealand Listener),46</i>)

>  Luncheon and afternoon tea were servedround in the hall, and scrambles forlollies for the children eventuatedoutside just prior to the rain.] <i>(Dec 24, 1919,Northern Advocate,6</i>)

>  Every child present was the happyrecipient of a toy, while free ice-creamsto the prize-winners, and the usual lollyscramble, served to add to the enjoymentof the kiddies. <i>(Jan 7, 1929,Northern Advocate,7</i>)

>  [After lollies had been scrambled with a liberal hand, . . the teacher addressed the children.] <i>(Jan 7, 1885,Wairarapa Daily,2</i>)

>  <b>GIBBONS</b> We had a lolly scramble. <i>(1988,Recollections (various authors/titles),24</i>)

>  <b>STEWART</b> We sneaked off after the lolly-scrambles and went birdsnesting. <i>(1983,Springtime in Taranaki,39</i>)

>  <b>BEARDSLEY</b> Oh joy oh boy, the lolly scramble, ahellashun big tin and Mr Stevens shoutingand hurling them into the air, a greatcoloured fan, plop, plop, plop into thegrass. <i>(1984,Blackball 08,62</i>)

>  <b>ELDRED-GRIGG</b> Shepherds and cockatoo farmers came from miles away.. and amusements.. included a 'lolly scramble' and a few boxing matches. <i>(1980,Southern Gentry,80</i>)

## lolly shop
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> a sweet shop

>  <b>PARK</b> And Mrs Bedding, the lolly-shop lady, poured out all the wonders she had stored ... in the back of the shop. Black gelatinous bears ..., the blackballs, aniseed balls, ... Hundreds and thousands,musk lollies hard as bullets ... green, white, yellow conversation lollies with inscriptions of `Oh, you kid!' and `Is that so?' and sometimes, blushingly, `You're the cat's pyjamas.' <i>(1951,Witch's Thorn, The,78</i>)

>  <b>FRAME</b> In Thames Street my chief place of call was the lolly shop with its notice High Class Confectionery which I read as High Glass confectionery,... <i>(1982,To the Is-Land,65,1984 ed.</i>)

>  <b>ALLEN</b> ... his favourite `lolly' shop. <i>(1936,Poor Scholar,17</i>)

>  <b>HYDE</b> ... a glorified lolly-shop. <i>(1938,Nor the Years Condemn,324</i>)

>  I got interested in a dear little flapper who worked in a lolly shop on Stratford <i>(1977,Gus Tomlins,102</i>) [NOTES: c1924]



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> (figurative) a brothel [(further from Dalley's article 10) The lolly shops and tobacconists behind which some one-woman brothels operated were more than a convenient facade or 'legitimate' reason for men to gather. Many of the one-woman brothel keepers ran their front shops, as well as their prostitution trade, as regular business enterprises, selling merchandise as any other retailer would. The association between the legitimate frontage and the activity in the back rooms also reflected what Ann-Louise Shapiro has termed the 'polymorphous world of commerce and sexuality' which infused urban life in the nineteenth and twentieth centuries.]

>  [Hdg] Sex, Lies & Lolly Shops... On February 10, 1916, Truth published an article entitled 'A Grave Problem, Vile Disease and Dreadful Truth' about the so called 'lolly shops' in Cambridge Tce that were actually facades for brothels... Dalley has investigated the existence of these lolly shops, which were legitimate after a law was passed in 1908 that allowed prostitutes to operate from brothels providing they didn't employ anyone else... During World War I troops from Trentham came into town and lined up outside the shop... Particularly because it was opposite the Queen Victoria statue - it became known as Gallipoli. <i>(Jun 24, 1998,Capital Times,5</i>)

>  [Title] Lolly shops 'of the red-light kind' and 'soldiers of the King'. Suppressing one-woman brothels in New Zealand, 1908-1916. <i>Ibid.</i> 15 <i>Truth</i>, in its usual melodramatic style of making sex news, reported evocatively of one-woman brothels: 'the confectionery bagnio . . . "lollie shops" in Cambridge-terrace [which] are houses of crimson vice'. In its 'Warning to Warriors when in Wellington,' it describes in meticulous detail a lolly shop, 'one of the "red light" kind', which, the tabloid claimed, would receive as many as 40 clients of an evening, all 'soldiers of the King.' <i>(Apr 1996,NZ Journal of History,30(1): 3</i>)

## lomu
 
 <i>v.</i> to charge like Jonah Lomu

>  Jonah Lomu is such a darling of the British tabloids that he is within cooee of turning into a verb. Harry Orsman reports that a mystery yarn by Hilary Bonner, A Fancy to Kill For, includes the immortal line, "He came at her like Jonah Lomu. His bulk catapulted itself through the doorway... If this catches on, it won't be long before Jonah's name will become lower case. We'll have the hero poised to lomu through the door and everyone will.know instantly what the author means. <i>(Apr 10, 1999,Dominion,18</i>)

## long acre
 
 <i>n.</i> The strip of grass at each side of a formed country road used for free grazing; esp. to graze the long acre, to turn stock out to graze along a road; =long paddock, long meadow

>  LONG ACRE, the long narrow roadside strip of grass illegally grazed by farmers. How Partridge came to muddle this about I can't understand. <i>(Jul 29, 1966,Letter</i>) [NOTES: Lett. fr. I. Davey (Whangarei)]

>  <b>FRY</b> Over there/ The long acre/ Sustaining the stray;/ The well-fenced flock feed seven strands away. <i>(1978,Brushed Words,7</i>)

>  Cattle reared on East Coast hill country are driven on hoof to Waikato for fattening, feeding on grass growing on roadsides. Farmers call it `grazing the long acre'. <i>(Jun 27, 1978,Dominion,7/4</i>)

>  <b>CHARLES</b> A big flock of sheep and no blooming hay!/ I have tried to sell 'em and I can't get a taker,/ So I am goin' to go grazing on the long, long acre!/ <i>(1981,Black Billy Tea,45</i>)

>  <b>STEWART</b> It's against the law to allow animals on the road at night, including grazing `the long acre' behind a temporary fence. <i>(1985,Gumboots and Goalposts,39</i>)

>  under ACRE] long acre NZ grass verge betweeen roadway and fence (farm the long acre crop this grass for fodder). <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,442</i>) [NOTES: [see Collins Concise Eng. Dict. 10 & 1985 Collins NZ Compact Dict. 5,]

>  <b>OWEN & PERKINS</b> (Bob Edwards, bushman) And also, there was a bit of living off the `long acre' [in the early 1930s]. The long acre was the roadside ... but you seemed to think that if there was too many sheep wandering the long acre, one wouldn't be missed. <i>(1986,Speaking for Ourselves,77</i>)

>  <b>BAKER</b> When Sean was fifty metres away, riding unseen down the "long acre" with his crossbow unslung and cocked, they managed to drag the fighting, cursing woman from the car. <i>(2000,Kokopu Dreams,215</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) grass on the sides of roads. (See [long paddock; between the fences](../L#long-paddock;-between-the-fences))

>  <b>ANDERSON</b> .. then off to find the horses grazing the 'long acre', most being hobbled. <i>(1962,Life's Roundabout, A,27</i>)

>  The 'long acre' on New Zealand road sides must total in fact many thousands acres of wasteland. <i>(Feb 13, 1963,Weekly News,38</i>)

>  <b>LOUGHNAN</b> long acre or long paddock: the roadside. To farm the long acre is to gain free, and often excellent, grazing there or even droving along so slowly they can graze. <i>(1981,Loughnan Glossary,51</i>)

>  We also graze the 'long acre' where we can. <i>(Jul 1983,NZ Dairy Exporter,17</i>)

>  <b>TINSLEY</b> Cow cockies were feeling the pinch through the lack of grass and several were starting to graze the 'long acre.' <i>(1985,Cheese Punchers' Ball, The,59</i>)

>  Some cockies can get pretty possessive about their rights to the Crown's long acre. <i>(Nov 1987,NZ Journal of Agriculture,35</i>)

>  <b>STIRLING</b> In winter, when many farms were short of grass, it was common practice for stock to be turned out on to the roadside verges or the 'long acre' as it was called. <i>(1990,From the Hills Above Taiamai,89</i>)

>  The only spat I can ever remember was over grazing the 'long acre' or roadside in a severe drought. <i>(Mar 1992,NZ Dairy Exporter,74</i>)

>  The 'long acre' often comes to the rescue of many stock during winter as bonus feed which has all been eaten out on the block. <i>(May 2001,Lifestyleblock.co.nz,29</i>)

## long blow
 
 <i>v.</i> (Rural) to use a long shearing stroke with a blade or handpiece, from the tail to the neck of a sheep.

>  <b>RICHARDS</b> Six shearers 'long blowing ' together would cause [the engine] to grunt and groan. <i>(1989,Pioneer's Life, A,51</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a long shearing stroke, with either shears or handpiece [usually to open the back fleece-wool.]

>  <b>BOWEN</b> As long as you get to the flank, you are all right, for when you come to the long blow, with the sheep laid out correctly - you can get the flank quite easily at that time - at the start of the long blow. <i>Ibid.</i> 30 ... the shearer still has a long awkward long blow, almost from the tail to the head, and tends to make more second cuts than with the new style. <i>(1955,Wool Away!,27</i>)

>  (H.A.L. Laing) Long blow: The shearing strokes, usually five in number, along the sheep's back as it lies on the board. After completing the long blow the shearer moves on to the `last side'. <i>(Feb 26, 1965,Listener (New Zealand Listener),15</i>)

>  LONG BLOW: The shearing strokes, usually five in number, along the sheep's back as it lies on the board. `The finest blow in shearing'. <i>(Feb 21, 1968,Straight Furrow,20</i>) [NOTES: [Shearer's language]]

>  <b>NEWTON</b> Laying his sheep full length he swung into the 'long blow' from rump to neck. <i>(1949,High Country Days,49</i>)

>  <b>RICHARDS</b> It made a fast neck and long blow. <i>(1986,Off the Sheep's Back,97</i>)

>  Now you are ready to lie her out for the long blows. <i>(May 2002,Growing Today,63</i>)



## Long Depression
 
 <i>n.</i> the Depression of 1870-1896

>  ...[Simpson] goes further and argues that the Long Depression, which he dates 1870-96, the Great Depression and the present crisis or depression are pretty much identical phenomena and that we could return to prosperity and full employment if only we had the wit to realise that Vogel and Nash had shown the way... <i>(Aug 13, 1990,Listener (New Zealand Listener),110</i>)



## long drop
 
 <i>n.</i> an outside toilet built over a hole in the ground

>  <i>(Sep 26, 1972,NZ Truth,25/3</i>) [NOTES: [see cit NINGNONG]]

>  long-drop: outside toilet <i>(1981,Avondale College Slang Words in Use</i>) [NOTES: (Goldie Brown Collection)]

>  long-drop: outside toilet <i>(1981,Avondale College Slang Words in Use</i>) [NOTES: (Goldie Brown Collection)]

>  <b>JOHNSTON</b> `Dunny box for a new long drop,' Hec grunted and continued with his work. <i>(1981,Fish Factory,175</i>)

>  The toilet was a `long drop' in a little tin hut down the gardenpath, near the woodshed <i>(Jan 3, 1984,Dominion,10</i>) [NOTES: Diary, Wairarapa farm, 1948]

>  And at the river plateau settlement of Te Waiti where big blowflies dive-bomb the long drops, 150 invited guests clutched cans . <i>(Nov 16, 1986,Sunday News,5</i>)

>  He stressed the dark side of the national force: macrocarpas, child molestation, murder in the long drop. <i>(Aug 12, 1999,Dominion,8</i>)

>  Our little climbing community dug a long drop and mowed lawns in exchange for tent space. <i>(Apr 16, 2003,Capital Times,4</i>)

>  Their [sc. Greenie frumps'] greatest pleasure should be long tramps into the bush on cold, wet days to worship mauka, and doing maypole dances around long drops. <i>(May 8, 2003,Dominion Post,B4</i>)

>  We have philosophy and higher mathematics, and hip-hop, and the motor car. We did not invent these things so that we would need, ever again, to experience the long drop, and its close and equally stinky relation, the chemical loo. <i>(Aug 8, 2004,Sunday Star Times,C8</i>)

>  Still, on this day in 1901, Annie Taylor wedged herself inside a wooden keg and set off over Niagara Falls to become the first person to ride the proverbial long-drop. <i>(Oct 23, 2001,Evening Post,2</i>)

>  There's only a makeshift shower (usually just for the kids) out the back and a long drop for a toilet. You get used to the blowflies. <i>(Dec 1996,Mana,No. 14 : 30</i>)

>  "Deep, like a long drop ... Not this time, Davy boy." <i>(May 3, 1997,Listener (New Zealand Listener),64</i>)

>  House and telephone are solar-powered, a spring gives water, a composting toilet replaces the ruined longdrop beneath a huge totara. <i>(Jul 3, 2004,Listener (New Zealand Listener),23</i>)

>  The toilets were of the long-drop type, with sprung seats. They were only to be used while the train was in motion. <i>(Jul 2003,NZ Geographic,No. 64/36</i>)

>  One job she didn't do, a pleasure that awaited my father on his next visit, was cleaning the longdrop. <i>(Mar 4, 2002,Evening Post,15</i>)

>  [My body] loathes uncertainty, sudden change or long-drops. <i>(Jul 21, 2002,Sunday Star Times,A13</i>)

>  The ablution department is rudimentary and open to the sky, the marine equivalent of the Kiwi longdrop, but entirely private and adequate. <i>(Aug 29, 2000,Dominion,18</i>)

>  A long drop was a dwelling where the great outdoors merged with the great indoors and caused the person occupying it to meditate not only on the marvels of nature but also on the marvels of modern sanitation. <i>(Aug 2005,NZ Memories,55/  51</i>)

>  I was pleased right away, because the long drop dunny, instead of being out of sight of the house as at our last place, was in the backyard, separated only by a spreading plum tree from the living quarters. It became a favourite place for my father to read. Notes <i>(Dec 28, 2003,Sunday Star Times,D3</i>)

## long green wrasse
 
 <i>n.</i> [Not exclusively NZ: <i>Pseudojuloides elongatus</i>, found in Japan, New South Wales and Western Australia in Australia, and the Poor Knights Islands off the east coast of Northland in New Zealand, in weedy reef areas at depths of between 10 and 30 m. Te Papa names as 'elongate green wrasse']

>  <b>PAUL</b> <i>Long green wrasse Pseudojuloides elongatus </i>... Seen around the Poor Knights Islands since the early 1970s, but not common. <i>(1986,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,109</i>)



## long head
 
 <i>n.</i> (Rural) the course over which the long head trial for heading dogs is conducted. (See [long pull](../L#long-pull))

>  <b>THOMSON</b> The long head is unusual in the sense that the dogs can either run up the creeek, or up the guts but have a difficult piece of ground to cover to clear the sheep cleanly, and not flatten their head. <i>(1993,Taranaki Dog Trialing,8</i>)

>  <b>GORDON</b> To get to the long head, the track follows a clear and busy river ... <i>(1998,Three Sheep and a Dog,221</i>)

>  <b>GORDON</b> On no course is the challenge more obvious than the long head or long pull. <i>(1998,Three Sheep and a Dog,108</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a dog trial competition for heading dogs. (See [long pull](../L#long-pull))

>  The other eye grabber was what they call in the south the long pull and in the north, the long head. <i>(Jul 15, 1986,NZ Journal of Agriculture,60</i>)

>  Locals Bernard Murphy and Gold won the long head and Ken Lobb and Star took out the short head and yard. <i>(Feb 24, 2000,Taranaki Daily News,20</i>)

## long meadow
 
 <i>n.</i> [[Here a Briticism either in mistake for long paddock by a writer not completely at home with NZ idiom; or recalling Brit. dial. long meadow): cf the Eng. dial. use.] =long acre, long paddock. ->Cf 1980 Rose Reflections of a Life in Haddenham 16 The grass margins alongside the roads were the only remaining waste after the enclosure of 1830. It was called the `Long Meadow' by the villagers [of Haddenham] who regarded it as valuable source of food for livestock. The grass was considered the property of the farmers who held land on either side.]

>  <b>ARMFELT</b> Overgrazed, the bastards! No wonder the poor girl [ewe] sought the long meadow <i>(1971,Catching Up,55</i>)

## long paddock
 
 <i>n.</i> The grassed borders of a public road used by farmers for grazing stock; the grass or grazing along roadsides; also called long acre.

>  ~ Slang for the road. People turn stock out on it, or travel them on it, to get cheap grazing. (C.) <i>(Nov 4, 1933,Press, The</i>)

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> Long paddock, the. The open road: New Zealand and Australian coll.: C. 20. B., 1941 and 1942. <i>(1949,Dictionary of Slang,Addenda 1101</i>) [NOTES: [P8 at long acre]]

>  ... the time must come when New Zealand will have to give consideration to what has becomeknown as `the long paddock' - that is to say the long acres of land which flank either side of the roads and railway lines of New Zealand. <i>(1949,Report of Royal Comm. Sheep-Farming Industry,87</i>)

>  <b>CHERRILL</b> Cooper has let out all his stock into the long paddock. Rachel had learnt by now that the `long paddock' was the ample grass verge on either side of the back country roads. <i>(1950,Story of a NZ Sheepfarm,113</i>)

>  <b>REED</b> I went on to Kaiapoi by the side road leading through a scattered farming district, and there I saw, almost for the first time on this journey, cows grazing in `the long paddock'. <i>(1961,North Cape to Bluff,198</i>)

>  The spotlight has been trained once again on to the `long paddock', that vexed question thatliberal-minded, trail-blazing surveyors passed on to posterity. Their bequest made provision for a five-figure mileage of main roads and a chain-wide vastness in secondary roads longer than the distance from New Zealand to Europe and back again. The purpose of this article is not to take liberties with statistics, for nobody has measured accurately the potential that the Hawke's Bay County Council spoke of in its recent decision to press for action on the shameful wastageperpetuated by the sanctity of the long paddock. <i>(Mar 5, 1965,Dominion,11</i>)

>  long paddock n. the open road; applied particularly when cows are put out to graze on roadside. <i>(1968,NZ Contemp. Dict. Suppl. (Collins),12</i>)

>  <b>BOYD</b> As more land was occupied, more horses and cows were turned out into `the long paddock', especially at night when gates were deliberately left open. <i>(1984,City of the Plains,84</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> long paddock roadside, where free grazing available. NZA. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,70</i>)



 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) grass on the sides of roads, often grazed by stock. (See [between the fences; long acre](../B#between-the-fences;-long-acre))

>  Long paddock, the.- slang for the road. People turn stock out on it, or travel them on it, to get cheap grazing. <i>(Nov 4, 1933,Press, The  (Acland Glossary),15</i>)

>  <b>CHERRILL</b> Cooper has let out all his stock into the long paddock. <i>(1950,Story of a NZ Sheepfarm,113</i>)

>  The pattern of the day no longer included grazing the long paddock; there was little left on the roadside that was edible. <i>(May 1982,NZ Journal of Agriculture,52</i>)

>  .. they'd spend the night in the cow paddock and in the morning the road gate'd be opened and they'd spend the day on the 'long paddock', the roadside. <i>(Apr 1986,NZ Journal of Agriculture,40</i>)

>  The nuisance at the present time is very much aggravated by the fact that there is a bull amongst the frequenters of the "long paddock"... <i>(Jan 8, 1908,Tuapeka Times,3</i>)

## long pull
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a dog trial competition for heading dogs.

>  Mr R. Guthrie did most of the judging - i.e., classes 2, 3, and 4. - and, on the whole, his verdicts were well received, but it must be said that his placing of the first dog in the Long Pull was a bit of a surprise to those who closely watched the running. <i>(Jun 13, 1906,Otago Witness,35</i>)

>  <b>McNAUGHTON</b> When Bluey finally got him [a sheepdog under training] started on what is known as the `long pull', on flat ground ... he would naturally take a wide run out - or `cast' - to head his sheep. <i>(1970,Tat,88</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> There are five standard classes [in dog trialling], each incorporating three events. The standard classes are as follows: Class I (the Long Pull); Class II (the Drive and Yard); Class IIA (a SouthIsland event); Class III (the Zig-zag or Slew) and Class IV (The Straight Hunt.) The three events in each class are: The maiden, for dogs which have not won a maiden event; the Intermediate, open only to dogs which have won a maiden but have not won an open; and lastly, the open itself. The standard events are usually held on hill courses. <i>(1971,Ten Thousand Dogs,11</i>)

>  More especially in the "long pull" have the prizes been increased. <i>(Jan 18, 1900,Otago Witness,42</i>)

>  Last year's programme with slight alterations was adopted, the principal difference being the lengthening of the "long pull" from 680 yards to about half a mile, and Class IV. youths to be over 18, and their task to be same as Class II. yarding omitted and sheep to be taken off the course to the satisfaction of the judge. <i>(Feb 16, 1899,Timaru Herald,4</i>)

>  .. in 1981 Bob Bariss sold his heading bitch Pearl to Alan Dever, just after Pearl and Bob came second in the New Zealand long pull. <i>(1984,NZ Journal of Agriculture,Jan/Feb 13</i>)

>  The other eye grabber was what they call in the south the long pull and in the north the long head. <i>(1986,NZ Journal of Agriculture,60</i>)

>  <b>HALL</b> The long pull is where a shepherd stands in a ring marked with stones, about ten paces in radius, and brings the sheep down the hill to him; it finishes when the shepherd, the dog and the sheep are all perfectly stationary for at least a second. <i>(1987,Shepherd's Year,  A,59</i>)

>  We achieved a complete in the long pull. <i>(1987,Shepherd's Year,  A,79</i>)

>  <b>KERR</b> John Harris, a hard doer, was sending his dog off for the Long Pull at the Waitaki club grounds, one of the nation's biggest hills for the sport. <i>(2000,High Times in the High Country,158</i>)

>  Class I., long pull, about half a mile, and hold in ring until the judge is satisfied, was proceeded with. <i>(Jun 2, 1914,Poverty Bay Herald,3</i>)

>  Long Pull for All-comers, head, pull, hold in the ring and yard, about half a mile - First aGBP8, second aGBP4, third aGBP2. <i>(Apr 15, 1924,Otautau Standard & Wallace County Chronicle,4</i>)

## long tom
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  We found, about thirty men engaged in the digging and washing operations, the latter being carried on by means of a small stream of water, carried by means of leads or small channels into the "claim" of each party, and carried by each through their "long tom," or into tubs or buckets for use in the cradle. <i>(Feb 28, 1855,Nelson Examiner,2</i>)

>  The gold accumulated by one week's work by the long-tom process will amount to about 1 oz. per man for the week's work. <i>(Feb 18, 1855,Nelson Examiner,2</i>)

>  <b>SINCLAIR & HARREX</b> The contents [sc. the auriferous pay dirt] were then tipped into a 'cradle' or a 'long tom' - both types of sluice boxes - to separate the gold from the soil. <i>(1978,Looking Back,75</i>)

 <i>n.</i> [goldmining]

## long wave
 
 <i>n.</i> an extra large ocean wave

>  Analysis of data from a recorder at Timaru has revealed six "long wave" events, with the largest on April 4, 2002, peaking at 1.2 metres. <i>(Apr 29, 2003,Hawke's Bay Today,2</i>)

>  Port's long waves to be monitored .. The Hawke's Bay Regional Council installed the recorder as part of a study by the National Institute of Water and Atmospheric Research into long waves that turn up on the East Coast. .. Scientist Derek Goring said the long waves, which came at intervals of seven to 11 minutes, were the bane of port operators and ship owners at open coast ports such as Napier, Gisborne, Timaru and, to a lesser extent, Lyttelton. .. Scientists want to know why, and how often, long waves turned up, so they could provide more-informed warning procedures. .. While a lot of work had still to be done, he hoped the research would allow NIWA to predict when the long waves would occur. <i>(Apr 29, 2003,Hawke's Bay Today,2</i>)

## long-finned boarfish
 
 <i>n.</i>

## long-finned cod
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Lepidion microcephalus</i>, a deepwater morid cod, also called small-headed cod.

>  <b>AYLING</b> <i>LONG-FINNED COD</i> (Small-headed cod) <i>Lepidion microcephalus</i> ... The long-finned cod is similar in body shape and size to the red cod ... This species has a chin barbel and two [145] dorsal fins, the first of which is long and filamentous. <i>(1982,Collins Guide,145,1984 ed.</i>)

>  <b>PAUL</b> <i>Long-finned cod Lepidion microcephalus</i> An Australasian deepwater species. <i>(1986,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,59</i>)

## long-tailed cuckoo
 
 <i>n.</i> a migratory cuckoo, Eudynamys taitensis, with a tail longer than the body (also called kawekawea, koekoea, kohoperoa).

>  Affordable, close to the Rimutaka Forest Park (cabins, swimming holes, the rumoured arrival of the rare long-tailed cuckoo), one undertaker, three pubs, population 116,860... And so the one road remains, with its median barrier and skid-resistant bauxite, a ramp leading up and over the hills, towards intelligent finance and the long-tailed cuckoo. <i>(Oct 25, 2003,Listener (New Zealand Listener),94</i>)

>  The harsh notes of the long-tailed cuckoo were heard for the first time last year on October 27. <i>(Jan 26, 1922,Auckland Weekly News,15</i>)

>  <b>Crowe</b> The long-tailed cuckoo...never builds a nest, but lays its eggs instead in the nests of whitehead, brown creeper or yellowhead. <i>(2001,Which New Zealand Bird?,16</i>)

>  Eudynamys taitensis. Long-tailed Cuckoo. Koekoea. <i>(1869,TrNZI,1/160</i>) [NOTES: (Hutton Birds Great Barrier)]

>  During the quiet summer nights the deep rich notes of the Koheperoa, or Long-tailed Cuckoo, may be heard at intervals till break of day. ... The Pipiwarauroa, or Shining Cuckoo, is of milder disposition ... <i>(1869,TrNZI,1/3/13</i>) [NOTES: (Buller Ornithology)]

>  <b>HUTTON</b> 54. EUDYNAMIS TAITIENSIS. Sparrm. Long-tailed Cuckoo. Koekoea <i>(1871,Birds of New Zealand,20</i>)

>  <b>BULLER</b> EUDYNAMIS TAITENSIS. (LONG-TAILED CUCKOO.) ... <i>Native names.</i> Koekoea, Kawekawea, and Koheperoa, [and Kohaperoa]. <i>(1873,Birds of New Zealand,75,[1888 2nd edn 1/127] ed.</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i>Koekoea, n. the larger cuckoo.</i> [sic: long-tailed nf] <i>(1892,Dictionary of the NZ Language,63</i>)

>  <i>The Kohoperoa or Koekoea, Long-tailed Cuckoo </i>(Urodynamis taitensis) <i>(1904,TrNZI,36/113</i>)

>  <b>GUTHRIE--SMITH</b> The Long Tailed Cuckoo was very plentiful and very noisy. <i>(1914,Mutton Birds & Other Birds,15</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i>Koekoea</i>, n.<i> 1. Urodynamis taitensis, long-tailed cuckoo. </i>[so 1957/111 ... <i>Eudynamis taitensis </i>...] <i>(1917,Dictionary of the Maori Language</i>)

>  <b>COWAN</b> The shining cuckoo, or <i>pipi-wharauroa,</i> and the long-tailed cuckoo, called koekoea and also kohoperoa, are regular summer migrants from the tropics, ... <i>(1926,Travel in NZ,(Vol II South Island) 170</i>)

>  <b>REISCHEK</b> Here I also got a specimen of the long-tailed cuckoo. <i>(1930,Yesterdays in Maoriland,70,1933 ed.</i>)

>  <b>STUDHOLME</b> The Long-tailed Cuckoo is now extremely rare, if not extinct, in South Canterbury ... <i>(1940,Te Waimate,238,1954 ed.</i>)

>  <b>BUICK</b> Feathers of the albatross (<i>toroa</i>), long-tailed cuckoo (<i>koekoea</i>), and heron (<i>kotuku</i>) were also valued.. <i>(1949,Coming of the Maori, The,284</i>)

>  <b>FALLA et al.</b> LONG-TAILED CUCKOO <i>Eudynamis taitensis Maori names:</i> Koekoea, Kawekawea, Kohoperoa. <i>(1966,Birds of New Zealand,181</i>)

>  <i>228. Eudynam[y]s taitensis </i>(Sparrman, 1787) <i>Long-tailed Cuckoo (Koekoea) </i> <i>(1970,Annotated Checklist of Birds of NZ,60,1980 ed.</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i>Koohoperoa</i> n. <i>Eudynamis taitensis,</i> long-tailed cuckoo. = <i>koehoperoa.</i> <i>(1971,Dictionary of the Maori Language,126</i>)

>  <i>Long-tailed Cuckoo Eudynamys taitensis </i>(SPARRMAN, 1787) ... OTHER NAMES: <i>Koekoea, kohoperoa, screecher, screamer. </i> <i>(1985,Reader's Digest Book of NZ Birds,255</i>)

>  Long-tailed Cuckoo - koekoea, <i>Urodynamis taitensis </i> <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/705-712</i>)

>  <b>PHILLIPPS</b> <i>Chlorophthalmus nigripinnis</i> Guenther. CUCUMBER-FISH. <i>(1927,Bibliography of NZ Fishes,15,1971 ed.</i>)

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> Blind Cucumber Fish <i>Bathymicrops sp.</i> Bruun <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,400</i>) [NOTES: 1955 Whitley Namelist in 1956 Graham NZ Fishes 400]

>  <b>MORELAND</b> He told me, too, of the shining, or long-tailed, cuckoo, who also hails from New Guinea. It is a larger bird, mottled brown, and both [sc. cuckoos] have the habit of laying an egg in other birds' nests. <i>(1911,Through South Westland,111</i>)

## long-tailer
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a sheep that misses having its tail docked as a lamb [possibly a hermit or straggler. ]

>  <b>MACNICOL</b> I'd tell them where the maid went - where no dog had ever been/ And brought out some long-tailers that no man had ever seen,/ <i>(1965,Beyond the Skippers Road,18</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> .. what sheep had been left there had bred up, and when I went it was alive with long-tailers of all ages. <i>(1971,Ten Thousand Dogs,32</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> Among them [rough sheep] were several long tailers which had never seen the inside of a yard and some had wool almost dragging on the ground. <i>(1947,Wayleggo,94</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> .. longtailers and woollies which have escaped muster and gone semi-wild are known as 'rough' sheep. <i>(1966,Boss's Story, The,154</i>)

>  <b>NEWTON</b> When he took over, the only domestic sheep were 'roughies' - longtailers and doubles that had multiplied from those which had escaped the wind-up muster four years ago. <i>(1973,Big Country of the South Island,86</i>)

>  <b>ASPINALL</b> By now, most of the lambs have grown quite big, but there are always small ones as well as a number of wild "long tailers" that were missed in the earlier docking muster. <i>(1976,High Country Sheep Station,26</i>)

>  <b>ASPINALL</b> .. there are always small ones as well as wild 'long tailers' missed in the earlier docking muster. [longtailer; long tailer] <i>(1993,Farming Under Aspiring,153</i>)



## long-thinker
 
 <i>n.</i> One of the later 2NZEF echelons, the members of whom were considered to have spent a long time thinking before volunteering; also "deep thinkers"

>  I.. can look anyone in the face, knowing I wasn't a 'long-thinker'. <i>(Sep 8, 1916,Evening Star,3</i>)



## long-toed skink
 
 <i>n.</i> (Environment) a pretty and elusive skink; striped, sparsely resident in inland Canterbury and Marlborough, characterised by unusually long toes. [<i>Oligosoma longipes</i>]

>  The long-toed skink and the scree skink, both threatened species, reach the northern limit of their distributions in the Kaikoura ranges. <i>(Aug 2002,Forest & Bird,305/34</i>)

>  Its native biodiversity includes a number of lizard species, including the nationally-threatened scree skink and long-toed skink. <i>(Dec 22, 2003,Dominion Post,B6</i>)

>  This long-toed skink's three-pronged tail speaks of an eventful life. <i>(Jul 2000,NZ Geographic,No. 47/75</i>)



## long-wool
 
 <i>n.</i> A long-woolled sheep

>  The staple of New Zealand wool, especially the long-wool and cross-bred, is remarkable for its freedom from breaks and other imperfections ... <i>(1902,Settler's Handbook of NZ, The,92</i>)

>  <b>BURDON</b> In the same year [1874] we find [Tripp] engaged in an argument with his neighbour [116] John Grigg ... about the best breed of longwool to mate with the Merino. <i>(1938,High Country,115</i>)

>  The two qualities have been best combined by the judicious crossing of Down rams with long-wool ewes in the North Island; ... <i>(1902,Settler's Handbook of NZ, The,93</i>)

## longfin
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  [T]he longfin (<i> Caesioperca lepidoptera </i>. . .), a red fish, is smaller [than the groper] (rarely more than a foot long). <i>(1967,Native Animals of NZ,221</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> Caprodon longimanus , =pink maomao, =mata (maataa) [check longfin right]. (See [pink maomao](../P#pink-maomao))

>  <b>INGLIS</b> The whare with its idle group of Crimean-shirted stockmen ... bullock-punchers, horse-breakers, fencers and general rouseabouts ... came back to us. <i>(1887,Our NZ Cousins,163</i>)

>  <i> Caprodon longimanus </i> (Guenther). LONGFIN; MATA. <i>(1927,Bibliography of NZ Fishes,33,1971 ed.</i>)

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> Longfin <i> Caprodon longimanus </i> (Gunther) (Syn. <i> Scorpis fairchildi </i> Hector) <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,406</i>) [NOTES: 1955 Whitley Namelist in 1956 Graham NZ Fishes]

>  <b>PARROTT</b> THE LONGFIN <i> Caprodon longimanus </i> (Gunther) ... The Longfin, so called on account of the long pectoral fins, has a rather interesting distribution, being found in New Zealand, Australia and Chile. This is not a common species in New Zealand, but it is occasionally caught in moderately deep water off the North Island. <i>(1957,Sea Anglers' Fishes of NZ,75</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i> Mata, (iii) ... 2. Torpedo fairchildi, electric ray, and Caprodon longimanus, longfin </i>; fish. <i>(1957,Dictionary of the Maori Language,185</i>)

>  <b>DOOGUE & MORELAND</b> PINK MAOMAO or LONGFIN ... -->Although commonly called pink maomao, particularly inthe far north, this species is a member of the groper and butterfly perch family (Serranidae), as is not strictly as maomao. <i> Other names: Caprodon longimanus </i>; mata (Maori). <i>(1966,Sea Anglers' Guide,227</i>)

>  <b>MORELAND</b> PINK MAOMAO [<i> Caprodon longimanus </i>] ... Longfin and mata (Maori) are alternative names. <i>(1967,Marine Fishes,44</i>)

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i> Maataa (iii), ... 2. Torpedo fairchildi, electric ray, and Caprodon longimanus, longfin </i>; fish. <i>(1971,Dictionary of the Maori Language,185</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> LONGFIN <i> Alternate name for Pink Maomao. </i> <i>(1981,Fisherman's Bible,143</i>)

>  [species name] pink maomao .. [scientific name] <i> Caprodon longimanus .. [other name(s)] longfin, pinkfish <i>(Mar 27, 1986,Codes for Species in NZ Waters</i>)

>  <b>PAUL et al.</b> [index to common names 283-293] longfin 1260, 1266 <i>(1987,Fishes: A Bibliography</i>)



## longfin(ned) eel
 
 <i>n.</i> the New Zealand longfin eel; <i>Anguilla dieffenbachii</i> [aka tuna kuwharuwharu; aka New Zealand longfin eel; <i>Anguilla dieffenbachii</i>; An eel whose dorsal fin is longer than its ventral fin, one of two common NZ freshwater eels or tuna. Compare short-finned eel]

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> NEW ZEALAND EELS Long-finned Eel (Tuna): <i>Anguilla dieffenbachii </i>Gray, Short-finned Eel (Tuna Heke): <i>Anguilla australis schmidtii</i> Phillipps. [ibid. 125] The Short-finned Eel varies from dark brown to very light brown on the back, with silvery sides and belly, and is known as the Silvery-eel or Silver-belly. <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,123,2nd ed. ed.</i>)

>  <b>AYLING</b> LONG-FINNED EEL (Tuna) <i>Anguilla dieffenbachii</i> <i>(1982,Collins Guide,89,1984 ed.</i>)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> Long-finned Eel - kuwharuwharu, reherehe, <i>Anguilla dieffenbachii </i> <i>(1986,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/705-712</i>)

>  <b>SORENSON</b> <i>EEL, LONGFIN EEL, SHORTFIN </i> (a) Scientific name:<i> Anguilla dieffenbachi</i>and <i>Anguilla australis</i> respectively. (b) Suggested commercial name[s]: (i) Longfin Eel - Eel (ii) Shortfin Eel - Silver belly; Silver eel. (c) Other common names: Freshwater eel. (d) Principal localities where taken: Auckland, Whakatane, Raglan, Lake Ellesmere, Riverton (Southland). <i>(1970,Nomenclature of NZ Fish,21</i>)

>  [species name] longfinned eel .. [scientific name] <i>Anguilla dieffenbachii </i>.. [other name(s)] yellow eel, silver eel <i>(Jul 23, 1986,Codes for Species in NZ Waters</i>)

>  <b>SPEIGHT et al.</b> They are the long-finned (<i>Anguilla aucklandii</i>) and the short-finned (<i>A. australis</i>) the first of which frequents quick-running rivers, while the latter is found more often in lakes or sluggish streams. <i>(1927,Natural History of Canterbury,197</i>) [NOTES: Archey `Native Fishes of Canterbury' in Speight et al. Natural History of Canterbury]

>  <i>Anguilla dieffenbachii</i>: [European Name] Long-fined eel [Maori Name] Tuna. <i>(1936,Handbook for New Zealand, The,73</i>)

>  <i>Anguilla aucklandii </i>Richardson. Long-finned eel. Tuna. <i>(1938,TrRSNZ,68/403</i>) [NOTES: Graham Fishes of Otago Harbour]

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> Longfinned Eel <i>Anguilla dieffenbachii</i> Gray `Syn. <i>A. aucklandii</i> Richardson and <i>A. waitei</i> Phillipps) <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,401</i>) [NOTES: 1955 Whitley Namelist in 1956 Graham NZ Fishes]

>  <b>WILLIAMS</b> <i>Orea</i>, n. <i>Anguilla dieffenbachii, long-finned eel. =arokehe, kuwhauwharu. </i> <i>(1957,Dictionary of the Maori Language,241</i>)

>  The principal species in the park with sea access are likely to be the long-finned eel, koarao, and red-finned bully. <i>(1983,Land of the Mist,72</i>)

>  <b>MORELAND</b> LONGFINNED EEL [<i>Anguilla dieffenbachi</i>] ... Mud-eel and black eel are alternative names, while a variety of Maori names are in use. Tuna is widely used. <i>(1967,Marine Fishes,30</i>)

>  <b>NATUSCH</b> There are two species of freshwater eels in New Zealand, one. the short-finned <i>Anguilla australis schmidtii</i> ... having the dorsal fin about the same length as the ventral fin, and the other. <i>A. dieffenbachii</i> ... , having the dorsal fin continuing well forward of the point where the ventral fin stops. The long-fin is usually predominant in cold water, ... <i>(1967,Animals of NZ, The,209</i>)

>  <b>PAUL</b> Longfinned eel <i>Anguilla dieffenbachii </i>... Alternatively, longfin. Occurs throughout New Zealand, but not elsewhere. <i>(1986,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,42</i>)

>  The short--finned eel (<i>Anguilla australis</i>) is found in New Zealand and south-east Australia, while the similar long-finned eel (<i>Anguilla dieffenbachia</i>) is found only in this country. [43] A good supply of wild eels, both the short-finned and the long-finned types, has long been taken for granted. <i>(Aug 2, 2003,Listener (New Zealand Listener),42</i>)

>  Two other species ply our fresh waters - the shortfin eel (<i>A. australis</i>), and the Australian longfin (<i>A. reinhardtii</i>), a recent arrival currently restricted to the northern part of the North Island. Of the three the New Zealand longfin ins the most impressive. Described by those who have seen them a "logs that moved", there have been confirmed sightings of specimens up to two metres in length ... <i>(Sep 2010,NZ Geographic,97</i>)

>  Both our varieties of eel - the short finned and the long finned - are good to eat. There's a slight variation in the colour of the flesh (pinkish in the case of the long-finned, greyish in the case of the short) but it takes an expert to tell the difference between the two. <i>(Jan 24, 2004,Dominion Post,E7</i>)

>  New Zealand longfin eels, among the largest freshwater eels on Earth, breed just once and may live for more than 90 years. They spend most of their lives in fresh water before making a 6000 km journey across the Pacific ocean, to a particular site thought to be near Tonga, where they breed and die. <i>(Sep 2010,NZ Geographic,97</i>)

>  Local Maori called the long fin eel kuwharuwharu, and the short fin eel tuna hao or tuna heke. <i>(Feb 2011,Wairarapa News,Supplement: pukaha Mount Bruce  p 8</i>)

Common names include Southern Bluefin, Longfin-Tuna and Longfin Albacore. <i>(1981,Fisherman's Bible,19</i>)

## longshoreman
 
 <i>n.</i> poss. equivalent to some senses of beachcomber: the ref. is to the Wellington west coast near the Turakina.

>  <b>POWER</b> Since I have been in New Zealand, I have never seen a white man so utterly isolated and removed from all comforts of life. It is ten times worse for a person in Dr. C -'s condition, than it would be for the whalers and `long-shore men' usually found in such localities, ... from his general unhandiness ... <i>(1849,Sketches in NZ,169</i>)



## longtailed bat
 
 <i>n.</i> longtailed bat .. pekapeka .. <i>Chalinolobus morio </i> (See [pekapeka](../P#pekapeka))

>  <b>POWELL</b> BAT, LONG-TAILED (<i>Chalinolobus morio</i>), Pekapeka of the Maoris, shelters in caves and hollow trees. <i>(1947,Native Animals of NZ,92</i>)

>  <b>McLINTOCK</b> longtailed bat .. pekapeka .. <i>Chalinolobus morio </i> <i>(1966,Encyclopaedia of New Zealand, An,3/705-712</i>)

>  [caption] Long-tailed bats are rarely seen in winter when they go into hibernation. <i>(1983,Land of the Mist,75</i>)



## longwall method
 
 <i>n.</i> [(coalmining)]

>  The system of work [in the Grey coalfield] is mainly by bord and pillar, a small portion only of this coal having been worked by the longwall method. In the bord-and-pillar system the bords are 18ft. wide, and pillars 14 yards thick. The pillars are left intact until the boundary of a section is reached, when they are worked back. The roof has in [418] main proved good, enabling a large percentage of the pillar-coal to be won. <i>(1906,NZ Mining Handbook,417</i>)

## look
 
 <i>v.</i> look over, inspect (country, land, stock, etc) (cf. crawl v.). [(transitive use) station use, ?obs]

>  Look.- I am told that on some South Canterbury stations they l. the sheep or paddocks, i.e., go and see that they are all right. I have never heard the word used myself. ([1898 Morris Austral-English]) <i>(Nov 4, 1933,Press, The,15</i>)

>  <b>STUDHOLME</b> [Grass-thieving] quickly came to an end, one old Scot [shepherd] looking his country with a catching dog and a sharp knife. <i>Ibid.</i> 132 The grass thieves [sc. farmers who ran their sheep on others' country as if by accident] thought we were hard on them, but after the Station disposed of the blocks adjoining them, they found our successors were far more severe, and this sort of thing quickly [133] came to an end, one old Scot looking his country with a catching dog and a sharp knife. Dead sheep, like dead men, tell no tales. <i>(1940,Te Waimate,116</i>)

## looking-glass plant
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> <i> C [oprosma retusa]</i> is much used for hedges in Wellington and Melbourne. In the former place it is generally known as <i> taupata </i> (sometimes <i> naupata </i>); in the latter it is called the <i> looking-glass </i> plant. <i>(1907,Plants of NZ,392,1940 p. 420 ed.</i>)

>  <b>DAWSON</b> In the North Island particularly, prostrate shrubs of the shiny-leaved taupata (<i> Coprosma repens </i>, known as the `looking glass plant' in California where it is cultivated) are often prominent. <i>(1988,Forest Vines to Snow Tussocks,155</i>)

## lookout
 
 <i>n.</i> [Jim writes : In the sense of an elevated place from which the landscape may be viewed, this is in NZPOD but not DNZE. It appears not to be a British use (except perhaps in the form <i>lookout point</i>); the equivalent is <i>viewpoint</i> which is the term in British road atlases etc. Lookout in this sense is also AusE - see Macquarie 1981 and MAU 236.]

>  <b>GEE</b> Wre stopped at the lookout and Auckland lay spread flat, winking its lights. <i>(1992,Going West,74</i>)

## loopy
 
 <i>n.</i> Usu. pl. a tourist.

>  (Griffiths Collection) [in Aug 1975 it was used on TV1 without explanation] A television news item about the visit to Stewart Island by the Prime Minister (Mr Rowling) referred to the islanders' word for tourists, `loopys.' An islander has told the `Southland Times' that the word originated in another corner of Southland, Milford Sound, about 1970. `The name was coined by hotel staff who appeared to have a great contempt for `loopys',' said the islander. `It refers to the peculiar looping motion of the hand while swatting sandflies and to the `loopy' questions they ask.' <i>(Jul 28, 1975,Press, The,30</i>)

>  <b>LELAND</b> loopies: In the resort areas of New Zealand (particularly Central Otago), the residents and transient workers have a name for those of us who only come to sun, ski, climb or boat. I haven't been able to find the origin of this ungenerous appellation for tourists and I'm not sure I want to. <i>(1980,Personal Kiwi-Yankee Dictionary, A,60</i>)

>  Loopies: the local [Golden Bay] word for tourists. I didn't understand it, but maybe, as ourtail-lights ascended, the locals were in the settlements below, pointing: There child, that red fire fly light on the mountain, there go the loopies. <i>(Jun 16, 1984,Listener (New Zealand Listener)</i>)

>  In the same Listener Aucklander Geoff Chapple, last heard of hiding behind a bush off the Heaphy Track, wondered why the South Island word for tourists is `loopies'. (At first he thought it was `loonies'.) We thought it must have come from `loopy', meaning wrong in the head, but sources in the southern heartland assure us that the term almost certainly derivesfrom tourists' propensity for taking the loop road in any given area, with the general idea of seeing more of the countryside. How diverting. <i>(Jun 30, 1984,Listener (New Zealand Listener),44</i>)

>  Loopies [headline] While leafing through the Listener of June 16 I came across the headline `Look, child, there go the loopies'. I quickly scanned the article to see if `loopy' meant what I thought it did. ... One of the words used by Mr Chapman was `loup', from the Norse `hlaupa' meaning `leap'. Orkney residents would describe tourists as `loupers', meaning `leapers', presumably because they jumped from place to place. <i>(Jul 7, 1984,Listener (New Zealand Listener),10</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> loopies tourists; dismissive term by locals, possibly because tourists do a quick loop and off. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,70</i>)

>  <b>No.3 : 6</b> LOOPIES ON ICE [headline] Tourists have in the past visited Antarctica in the comfort of vessels like Linblad Explorer and in jet aircraft. Few ever spent a night ashore. <i>(1986,Antarctic News</i>)

>  <b>IRELAND</b> Not a thing was smarmied over or concealed, in the way the teaching loopies of today take it on themselves to 'protect' our children's imaginations by distrusting and debasing them [sc. Grimm's Fairy Tales] <i>(1998,Under the Bridge & Over the Moon,67</i>)

>  Anyway, sand, sun, scenery, hair-stylists on the beach, rent-a-bikists, cheap pseudo-spirits, Aussie one-pounder steaks, billion-mozzie swimming pools - all had been sacrificed to "loopies" so that Indonesia could get on with the chore of starving quietly to death. <i>(Oct 20, 2002,Sunday Star Times,F9</i>)

>  In the cities it is relatively harmless as the infrastructures that exist to service the regular working population are well able to cater for a few loopies as well. <i>(Oct 3, 1999,Sunday Star Times,C5</i>)

>  I now looked deeply into Heather's eyes, trying to detect whether she was treating me as a "Loopie", an expression South Islanders have for outsiders - foreign tourists, that is, and "foreigner" down there includes anyone from north of Cook Strait, especially Aucklanders. <i>(Nov 1999,AA Directions,33</i>)

>  I learned a new word at Te Anau - "Loopies." It refers to the thousands of tourists who in two weeks flock to Christchurch, cross over to the West Coast, "do" Queenstown and Fiordland, and then return via Mt Cook. <i>(May 11, 1997,Sunday Star Times,C1</i>)

>  NOTE: That the interdepartmental leaking roster is now fully extended, providing photocopies of government secret documents to Labour, Alliance, Act, New Zealand First, independent loopies, trade unions, radical charities, journalists and assorted nutters in the community. <i>(Dec 26, 1998,Listener (New Zealand Listener),19</i>)

>  They like the way Foveaux Strait acts as a deterrent to "loopies" (the local word for tourists). <i>(Mar 2002,NZ Geographic,No. 56/77</i>)

>  Besides, in the end, and the loopies notwithstanding, the people are usually right. <i>(Nov 21, 1992,Listener (New Zealand Listener),63</i>)

>  But it is Christmas that brings the hordes to Golden Bay. 'Loopies', the locals call them - holidaymakers, mostly from Christchurch, who descend like migrating birds on the beaches and cram the campgrounds to capacity. <i>(Apr 1991,NZ Geographic,102</i>)

>  The derogatory name [on the Chatham islands]for visitors is Loopies. <i>(Jun 26, 2004,Dominion Post,E1</i>)

>  The reality is that Bluff needs a complete facelift and Stewart Islanders don't particularly like tourists. They call them loopies. <i>(Apr 18, 1993,Sunday Times (NZ),7</i>)

## loppy
 
 <i>n.</i> A station rouseabout.

>  <b>SCRYMGEOUR</b> Proceeding just fair, but with their regularity that follows a shearer getting his back used to the arduous job, the advancement went on but with a different and a rougher cut than was usual. More frequently application of the loppies galloping down the board, to the ever increasing cry of `Tar'. The cut was becoming rougher and numbers spoilt good work, and slowly the gore of the classer, the shepherds, and certainly that of William Steuart, rose... Conferences would be held, a slight glistening on the fibre of the fleece, when sunlight decreed `wet sheep' and then the shearers would declare a break, whilst the `loppies', paid wet or fine, would be put on varying jobs to fill in the time until the Lords and Masters of the situation agreed to work. <i>Ibid.</i> 50 The bell rang, the whole procedure stopped, shearers trooped out, half shorn sheep were pushed back into the catching pens. Jimmy Greave the classer hurriedly whispered to William Steuart todesist, but loppies, rousies, and even young Jim, who was assisting to pen up, followed out from the side door to where the shoots from the stands gave the nearest exit, to the level space of the outside turf, that made eventually the [boxing] ring. <i>(1960,Memories of Maoriland,49</i>)
