## lot
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  lot ... NZ & Austral. group of bales of wool of a similar type offered for sale. <i>(1986,NZ Pocket Oxford Dictionary,444</i>) [NOTES: [nf. 1982 Collins Concise Eng. Dict.]]



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i>

>  <b>CRUMP</b> that little lot =a girl (only ex) ? add to OED 7 <i>(1961,Hang on a Minute, Mate,146, 91</i>)



 
<b>3</b> <i>n.</i> ?Town lot : cf town section : and allotment cf allotment.

>  The memorial [from Mr.Sinclair, Wellington] states that certain town lots ... had been advertised for sale by auction, but that several allotments, comprising some of the most valuable lands in the township, ... had been reserved from such sale ...; that these sections were to be paid for at the average price of half the town sections ... <i>(Sep 10, 1841,GBPP,1842 (No.569) 130</i>) [NOTES: Villiers and Lefevre : Colonial Land and Emigration Office in GBPP]

>  By the Government advertisement for the sale of town lands at Auckland, it was stated that sections ... would be exposed to sale by auction, but we have since learned that several allotments, comprising some of the most valuable lands in the township, have been reserved from such sale ...; and that these sections are to be paid for, not according to the price ... of the less valuable lots in their vicinity ... but at the average price of half the town sections ... <i>(Feb 10, 1841,GBPP,1842 (No.569) 131</i>) [NOTES: D.Sinclair and others, Wellington to Sir George Gipps in GBPP]

>  <b>THOMSON</b> At Auckland the people were living on the government expenditure, and awaited the arrival of immigrants to occupy the houses they had built, and re-purchase the town lots they had bought. <i>(1859,Story of New Zealand, The,2/60</i>)

>  Glossary. B-Ech: contraction for B-echelon reserve section of a unit - Lotus Eaters. -->From Tennyson's poem `The Lotos Eaters' (with popular confusion with lotus): They sat them down upon the yellow sand,/... And sweet it was to dream of Fatherland,/ Of child, and wife, and slave; but evermore/ Most weary seem'd the sea... In the afternoon they came into a land/ In which it seemed always afternoon. <i>(1946,Johnny Enzed in Italy</i>)

## Louise cake

## lounge
 
 <i>n.</i> In public buildings: a hotel lounge (usu. where drinks are served by waiters); a lounge in a picture theatre: (?formerly) a public area with a few seats, at the entrance to an expensive part of the theatre.



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> in domestic houses: an `up-market' word, esp. in land-agents jargon, for `sitting-room', `living-room', `front room', etc, where a `lounge-suite' (=couch, sofa, settee, armchairs) is found. [Aust. lounge-room]

>  <i> lounge ... 4. Chiefly Brit.</i> a living room in a private house. <i>(1982,Collins Concise English Dictionary,688</i>)



## lounge suite
 
 <i>n.</i> a set of lounge furniture [Jim Cameron writes : OED defines the word as "a set of furniture of the same pattern, frequently with reference to 2 armchairs and a sofa". NSOD & Collins define the word in this sense as "esp. of 2 armchairs and a settee." NZPOD following English POD gives "a sofa and armchairs of the same design." This is usually called a <i>lounge suite</i> in NZ, a term not recorded in the British dictionaries. The NZ use of suite ordinarily encompasses also a <i>dining suite</i> and a <i>bedroom suite</i>. (I have seen bedroom suite used in an English mystery novel published a few years ago). See furniture store ads in any newspaper or circular. In NZ, the meaning of <i>suite</i> by itself would be uncertain except in context.]

>  <b>BAKER</b> The sight of him made Sean think of lounge suites and 'Some Enchanted Evening' on the stereo. <i>(2000,Kokopu Dreams,138</i>)

>  I think the day when poeple will buy a $2000 lounge suite straight off the Net is quite far away, though we have sold a few this way. <i>(Feb 13, 2001,NZ Infotech Weekly,No. 476 : 23</i>)

## Low Maria
 
 <i>n.</i> lomaria

>  `Why can't you people leave names alone?' I had asked Falla one day when showing him a fern plant at the Auckland Museum. `Leave them alone and lose our jobs?' he had replied. ...[156fn] `but sooner than see our wives ... begging bread ..., we will trot out every animal and plant in New Zealand and adamise them afresh. Yes, just as Adam did and just as arbitrarily.' He wasnot in a pleasant mood. `Take your old fern ... and go on calling it `Low Maria' to the end of time.' <i>(1936,NZ Naturalist,155</i>) [NOTES: Guthrie-Smith]

## low pressure system
 
 <i>n.</i> [1989 Charles Ransom notes: Low pressure system - Not found elsewhere, but as this is a descriptive phrase in common English,I doubt it deserves an entry -- could even refer to weather.]

>  Low-pressure system- Term applied to a water system in which the pressure is at or below 28 m head (280 kPa). System supplied either from storage tanks or via pressure-reducing valve. <i>(1979,SANZ Glossary Building Terminology,72</i>)



## low voltage
 
 <i>n.</i> [1989 Charles Ransom notes: Low voltage - This is also common English, but it is defined in wiring regulations of NZ, which it would be appropriate to quote.]

>  Low voltage- Any voltage normally exceeding 32 V a.c. or 50 V d.c., but not exceeding 250 V in either case. <i>(1979,SANZ Glossary Building Terminology,72</i>)

<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) a cowshed with a milkline that runs beneath cows as they are milked.

## low-rider
 
 <i>n.</i> shorts or trousers several sizes too large so they hang below the waistline (See [also lo-ryder](../A#also-lo-ryder))

>  It's called the "low-rider" - trousers or shorts worn on the hips and exposing a generous amount of boxer short. It's mostly adopted by boys, and it's a fashion some principals say is skirting with indecency... He believed students were following blindly a fashion that began in American jails where prisoners did not have belts. <i>(Jun 21, 2002,Dominion,3</i>)

>  A school principal reckons he has got the low-down on low-riders and has a plan he hopes will banish them from his school forever. The term "low-riders" describes the trend for young males, and occasionally females, to wear their shorts or trousers several sizes too large so that they hang below the wastline... For the past two years the low-riding antics of some of his students has had Whangarei Boys' High School headmaster Al Kirk tearing his hair out in frustration. <i>(Jun 20, 2002,Evening Post,18</i>)

## lowland ribbonwood
 
 <i>n.</i> <i>Plagianthus betulinus / regius</i> [lowland ribbonwood; aka manatu, and variously <i>Plagianthus betulinus</i>, <i>Plagianthus regius</i>, <i>Plagianthus betulinus regius</i>]

>  A New Zealand native that isn't widely recognised, but which I think should be, is the ribbon wood <i>Plagianthus regius</i> which can be found over much of the country, including Stewart Island and the Chathams... The common name, ribbonwood, was given by the early settlers because of the tree's inner, ribbon-like bark. These layers are very tough and were once used by Maoris for making rope and twine for fishing nets. <i>(Feb 25, 2000,Dominion,13</i>)

>  English Name Native name Botanical Name Ribbonwood Houi Plagianthus Betulinus <i>(1889,Catalogue NZ Exhibition,35</i>) [NOTES: 1889 Canterbury [Catalogue Canterbury Court, Exhibition) 35]

>  <b>DAWSON</b> The species ... are not confined to such sites, but some are most frequent there, including ribbonwood (<i>Plagianthus regius</i>), and species of lacebark (<i>Hoheria</i>) of the family Malvaceae. <i>(1988,Forest Vines to Snow Tussocks,154</i>) [NOTES: [contrast LACEBARK]]

>  (And) <i>Plagianthus betulinus</i> ribbon wood <i>(1877,TrNZI,9</i>)

>  Houi (ribbonwood) .. Plagianthus betulinus .. Timber white and straight grained; suitable for manufacturing paper <i>(1890,PWD Catalogue of Timbers,9</i>)

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> <i>Plagianthus [betulinus]</i>, however, has much larger leaves than <i>Betulus</i> [the English birch]. Owing to the presence of an inner, ribbon-like bark, it is generally called the Ribbonwood by the colonists. <i>(1907,Plants of NZ,254,1940,. p. 270 ed.</i>)

>  <i>Plagianthus betulinus</i> <i>(1915,AJHR,C-6</i>) [NOTES: Cockayne Plant Covering Kennedy's Bush]

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>Plagianthus betulinus</i> <i>(1919,NZ Plants & Their Story</i>)

>  <b>COCKAYNE</b> <i>Plagianthus betulinus</i> <i>(1924,Cultivation of NZ Plants, The</i>)

>  <b>LAING & BLACKWELL</b> <i>Plagianthus betulinus</i>(The Lowland Ribbonwood) <i>(1940,Plants of NZ,270</i>)

>  <b>MARTIN</b> 8. Lowland ribbonwood (<i>Plagianthus betulinus</i>) ... <i>(1961,Flora NZ,1929 ed.</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> <i>Plagianthus betulinus</i> LOWLAND RIBBONWOOD ... Maanatu ... Small deciduous tree. <i>(1982,Stewart Island Plants,92</i>)

 <i>v.</i> (1960 below) 'get', shoot? [ca1935 Havelock.]

>  You're lumbered at last? <i>(Nov 16, 1942,NZEF Times,II/73 : 15</i>)

>  <b>CRUMP</b> We lumbered 28 [goats] in one mob and had to wait for our rifles to cool before going on to the next. <i>(1960,Good Keen Man, A,173</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i> To arrest and charge with an offence: usu. as in Brit. slang, be/get lumbered; to pot, report.

>  ...do sometimes lumber a `prominent resident'. <i>(Aug 26, 1905,NZ Truth,10/1/4</i>)

>  <b>CRUMP</b> We were sneaking into the church to bunk down last night when the johns lumbered us. <i>(1961,Hang on a Minute, Mate,136</i>)

>  `I was lumbered by the police who were so aggressive and foul-mouthed that they could teachme a thing or two, although I have been bad enough,' said Barker. <i>(Jan 6, 1965,NZ Truth,3</i>)

>  The father was lumbered by the cops on a sex charge-... and was sent up. <i>(Oct 17, 1972,NZ Truth,7/1</i>)

>  <b>NEWBOLD</b> Being silent is doing you no good at all because when the shit hits the fan, boy, it's you who's going to get lumbered <i>(1982,Big Huey, The,23</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> `I can lumber you, Jack, any time I want to. ... I can pot yer like a sitting duck.' <i>(1957,Strip Jack Naked,29</i>)



## lump
 
 <i>v.</i> to carry; (spec. on wharf, to unload cargo) (cf lumper).

>  Lumping was casual work, paid by the hour. <i>(1993,Wharfie,p11</i>)

>  For a month or so [S] was lumping on the wharf at the Spit. <i>(1911,Kiwi on the Swag,14</i>)

>  <b>McNEISH</b> He lumped in a short hut with a remittance man, a Cork tailor and an aborigine calledAndy. <i>(1970,Mackenzie,62</i>)



## lumper
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>STEWART</b> Those working on the wharves were called 'lumpers' or 'Holy Rollers' because so much of the cargo, especially wool, was lumped or rolled about... By now unions had been formed. A 'Lumpers' Society' was set up first in Port Chalmers in 1873. <i>(1973,Days of Fortune,41, 58</i>)

>  <b>BOWMAN</b> Through 1885 there was much friction between the lumpers and their employers in the four main ports. <i>(1948,Port Chalmers,42</i>)

>  The Wellington lumpers are still out and some inconvenience will be experienced today as an unusual number of steamers are in the harbour. <i>(Jan 5, 1885,Wairarapa Daily,2</i>)

>  In 1871 the Provincial Council set up an Auckland Harbour Board with representatives of the port's commercial interests, but the men who loaded and unloaded ships, the "lumpers", as they ere called, had no voice on the board. <i>(1993,Wharfie,p11</i>)

>  A lumper, called Angelo, met with a nasty accident on Dec. 23. <i>(Jan 1, 1887,Auckland Weekly News,20</i>)

>  <b>C.W.ADAMS 1</b> An emigrant ship on the eve of her departure presents an extraordinary spectacle ... the gangs of `lumpers' hoisting in the huge casks etc. <i>(1853,Spring in the Canterbury Settlement, A</i>) [NOTES: Griffiths Collection]

>  The Lumpers `went out' in support of the coal-heavers. <i>(Jan 7, 1885,Evening Post,2</i>)

>  <b>GRACE</b> When she landed, she tottered up the wharf in such a manner as to cause the `lumpers' to imagine she was drunken & disreputable. <i>(1895,Maoriland Stories,93</i>)

>  <b>BOWMAN</b> The term `lumpers' came to denote the men who manhandled the cargo [in 1870's-80s]. <i>(1948,Port Chalmers,43</i>)

>  A lumper in the employ of Messrs Gannawayand Thompson, stevedores.. had themisfortune to have his leg broken.. whileworking in No. 3 hold of the Delphic. <i>(Jun 16, 1899,NZ Times,5</i>)

>  The oaths of a number of mere lumperswere of no avail. <i>(Jan 14, 1905,NZ Observer,16</i>)



## lumpfish
 
 <i>n.</i> Trachelochismus pinnulatus (Bloch and Schneider)

>  <b>FORSTER</b> We got some other new fish, viz. a small Lumpfish [[ed. fn] <i> Cyclopterus pinnulatus </i> of <i> Descr. Anim., p.30], a small Blenny, both caught under the rocks at low water mark; some Starfish, Coats </i> of mail water spouts, Centipus, Muscles & Turbines, besides in the Evening a Lamprey ... [[ed. fn] <i> Petromyzon cirratus </i> of <i> Descr. Anim.</i> p.112] was caught. We described all this and drew a new Sole.[ed. fn] <i> Pleuronectes scapha </i> of <i> Descr. Anim </i>. p.130] <i>(Apr 8, 1777,Resolution Journal of J.R. Forster, The,2/250</i>)

>  <b>PHILLIPPS</b> <i> Trachelochismus pinnulatus </i> (Forster). LUMP-FISH. <i>(1927,Bibliography of NZ Fishes,55,1971 ed.</i>)

>  <i> Trachelochismus pinnulatus </i> (Forster). Lump-fish. <i>(1938,TrRSNZ,68/418</i>) [NOTES: Graham Fishes of Otago Harbour]

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> Lumpfish <i> Trachelochismus pinnulatus </i> (Bloch and Schn.) (Syn. <i> T. guttulatus </i> Hutton, <i> Crepidogaster simus </i> Hutton, and <i> Diplocrepis tumidus </i> Griffin) <i>(1955,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,413,1956 ed.</i>) [NOTES: 1955 Whitley Namelist in 1956 Graham NZ Fishes]

>  <b>GRAHAM</b> LUMP-FISH <i> Trachelochismus pinnulatus </i> (Bloch and Schneider) <i>(1956,NZ Fishes: An Identification Guide,370,2nd ed. ed.</i>)

>  <b>NATUSCH</b> [The cling fish's] relative <i> Trachelochismus </i>, the lump fish, has a rather more pointed snout and no incisors. The lump fish lives in slightly less exposed parts of [rocky coasts.] <i>(1967,Animals of NZ, The,233</i>)

>  <b>PAUL et al.</b> [index to common names 283-293] lumpfish 743-54 <i>(1987,Bibliography of NZ Fishes</i>)



## lumps
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>SLATTER</b> There was [postal] work [in the 1960s], hard satisfying work. Unloading parcel bags, huge cumbersome containers nicknamed pre-fabs, from a three-door Z. There was slots, there was lumps, there was packing fragies in bottlos, there was the tying down of the north line for a clearance, the scissors, lead seals and squeezers. The sorters on call-back flicking circulars withprecision. The volcanoes of letters on the facing-up table, the checker chanting the reds onto the lorry, the checker calling the overseas in, London 45 ... The queue of men working at the parcel depot or the postal siding ... Tieing down the cargon. ... The sorters' school at the posties' pub known colloquially as Box 44 or the B.B.C., the Back Bar of the Cecil. The cheerful abuse of cobbers. <i>(1968,Pagan Game, The,85</i>)



 
<b>2</b> <i>n.</i> mumps [1951 14 M 14 St Bede's, Chch 31 LUMPS (MUMPS)]

## lunatic soup
 
<b>1</b> <i>n.</i> (Rural) methylated spirits [used by swaggers and station cooks.]

>  <b>PETER</b> lunatic soup: methylated spirits to those who could not afford the real thing. <i>(2000,Peter Glossary,6</i>)

>  <b>PARTRIDGE</b> Lunatic soup. Strong drink: New Zealand: C. 20. B., 1941, `Lack of vigour or colour could not ... be a charge levelled against such terms for strong drink as lunatic soup, Africa speaks, plonk, steam, red Ned, or sheep wash,'. In Australia it specifically = `cheap red wine' (B., 1942). Elsewhere (Michael Harrison, Reported Safe Arrival, 1943) we find the variant lunatic'sbroth. <i>(1949,Dictionary of Slang,Addenda 1103</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> lunatic soup alcoholic drink, including meths (methylated spirits). NZA. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,70</i>)

## lunchbox
 
 <i>n.</i> ["Lunchbox" is gay slang for genitals according to Avis Coxhead; the penis being the "lunch"]

>  Mr Smith.. has not returned calls asking for an interview the following day about Dunedin's renowned "tartan mafia", of which he is a leading light... Though it may sound like it, the "tartan mafia" is not a derogatory term for the city's close-knit business community, which on occasions also uses the term... Mr Marsh is one of Dunedin's richest men, an acountant who invested wisely in car-dealing, property and manufacturing companies. He says there is no "formal tartan mafia". <i>(May 19, 2004,Dominion Post,C11</i>)

>  The signs have been there for years. They've been in underwear ads where pouting boys, lunchboxes packed, gaze out of the pages of women's glossies... My current copy of <i>Vogue</i> has similar ads for perfume, all nudity and smoulder, though none is as lovingly explicit as a recent ad which featured a male model with his whole lunchbox unpacked. <i>(May 20, 2004,Dominion Post,B4</i>)

## lunge
 
 <i>v.</i> [Sc. horsebreaking (also lunging); see quotation 1947.]

>  <b>BEATTIE</b> The old process of breaking-in horses is mentioned on Nov 5th, 1860, where a young horse was `lunged and mounted'. <i>Ibid.</i> 108 The diary mentions a `lunged horse'. This is never seen now. Mr. Kennard described the process as using a halter or `cavison' [cavesson], with a rein or rope, and running the horse round a circle until it was tired. This was for breaking in and training purposes, and was called `lunge-ing' <i>Ibid.</i>108 The diary mentions `lunged horse.' This is never seen now. Mr. Kennard described the process asusing a halter or `cavison,' [cavesson], with a rein or rope, and running the horse round a circle until it was tired. This was for breaking in and training purposes, and was called `lunge-ing.' <i>(1947,Early Runholding in Otago,83</i>) [NOTES: c1860s Puketoi Station Diary]

>  <b>BATHGATE</b> They brought [the colt] out to an open piece of ground where they `lunged' [it] for some time. <i>(1881,Waitaruna,236</i>)

>  <b>LELAND</b> lux: (A) TO VACUUM one's carpet (B) a VACUUM CLEANER. This, like hoover, is a corruption of a brand name. In this case, Electrolux, which appears to be the most widely usedvacuum cleaner in New Zealand. <i>(1980,Personal Kiwi-Yankee Dictionary, A,61</i>)

## lurk
 
 <i>n.</i> Cunning, scheme (to avoid work, make money); a cunning dodge; a racket.

>  If he doesn't break away from [the conditions] Through some underhanded `lurk'. <i>(1926,Transport Workers' Song Book,87</i>)

>  <b>SARGE</b> Lurk - (Army slang) A scheme for dodging work or duty. <i>(1950,Excuse my Feet!</i>) [NOTES: Glossary.]

>  lurk ... n. 3. Austral. & N.Z. sl. a scheme for success. <i>(1982,Collins Concise English Dictionary,672</i>) [NOTES: nf. 1986 NZPOD]



 
 <i>n.</i>

>  <b>COOZE</b> After years of hard work/ He had learnt all the lerk/ And prayed hard for a chance to get rich. <i>(1946,Ten Bob Each Way,8</i>)



 
 <i>n.</i> An easy or slightly dishonest job; sinecure; a racket; often as (be on to) a good lurk

>  <b>HILLIARD</b> Yet unless he got into some `lurk' like that he'd be a casual labourer, a wage-worker for the rest of his days. <i>(1960,Maori Girl,238</i>)

>  <b>McGILL</b> lurk job, plan, ruse, hiding place, hangout (also Brit.); a frequent example: `You're on to a good lurk', meaning a cushy job; c. 1916. NZA. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,70</i>)

>  <b>SLATTER</b> Stamping was a good lurk. <i>(1959,Gun in My Hand, A,20</i>)



## lush
 
 <i>n.</i> (hard) liquor [cf lushington obs.] (See [cf lushington](../C#cf-lushington))

>  I'll raise my Ebenezer. And I'll have `down with all the lush'. <i>(1869,Punch or the Auckland Charivari,83</i>)

>  <b>ANZAC</b> ...Whisky that takes the lining of your throat down with it. ... the kind of lush that gives you a sixty-horse dose of the jim-jams while you wait. <i>(1916,On the Anzac Trail,77</i>)

>  <b>COOPER</b> <i>(1966,Drown Him Deep,165</i>)



 
 <i>v.</i> drink heavily?

>  <b>FARJEON</b> ... he used to lush - my eye! ... Well, when he was pretty well screwed, he used to lay intous ... <i>(1866,Grif: a Story of Colonial Life,3</i>)



## lush up
 
 <i>n.</i> a drinking spree

>  His [the gumdigger's] surplus money may be banked, though in nine cases out of ten, it goes in a `lush up' <i>(1882,Brighter Britain,2/27</i>)



## lush-thrush
 
 <i>n.</i> A rhyming word for `a (pretty, well-proportioned) girl', among, esp. University and Training Coll. students in the 1950's (cfslick chick). Perhaps from U.S. slang, influenced by reading primers' characters: Fox-Lox, chicken-licken, Goose-loose etc. [rhyming with 'brush'?? ---- OEDS2 attributes a use to C. Brasch.]



## lushington
 
 <i>n.</i> A drunkard.

>  ...the present Police custom, when lushingtons have money about 'em, to bail 'em. <i>(Oct 28, 1865,Dunedin Punch,159</i>)

>  <b>FERGUSON</b> Teddy was a Lushington, Teddy was a man/ Who ne'er refused .../ To have a round or stand a shout .../ <i>(1883,Castle Gay,185</i>)

>  <b>CHAMIER</b> The commodore had no liking for his wife's brother, whom he denounced loudly as a worthless `lushington'. <i>(1895,South Sea Siren,89</i>)

## lux
 
 <i>v.</i> To clean by vacuum cleaner (See [Compare hoover](../C#compare-hoover))

>  <b>HALL</b> Refused to 'lux' the venetians. <i>(1911,Prisoner of Mother England,21</i>)

>  (A Male Au Pair) My conditions were spelt out - ... the house must be cleaned twice a week, vacuumed, mopped, dusted, polished and jiffed, the dishes washed ... - in two hours a day. [134] I jiffed the bath, handbasin and loo, polished the mirrors, ... then luxed the carpet. <i>(1986,More Magazine,133</i>)

>  lux to vacuum; short for electrolux, used to puzzling effect on English immigrants in Roger Hall play Prisoners of Mother England; lux the Venetians vacuum clean the Venetian blinds. <i>(1988,Dictionary of Kiwi Slang, A,70</i>)



## luxing
 
 <i>n.</i> vacuum cleaning [cf. electroluxing]

>  Shifting from the South Island to theNorth - from Invercargill all the way upto Morrinsville... Things that stick outmost is we roll our R's, we call'luncheon' 'belgium' and we call 'vacuumcleaning' 'luxing'. <i>(Jan 21, 1996,NZ Tablet,8</i>)



## lycopodium
 
 <i>n.</i> OED: 1. Bot. A plant of the cryptogamous genus Lycopodium: a club-moss. In earlyuse, L. clavatum.

>  <b>MACKIE</b> We were glad now and then to throw ourselves under a Manuka bush and partake of some bread and cheese provided us by our friends. I saw a small bittern and gathered an excellent Lycopodium half trailing half climbing plant. <i>(Mar 31, 1853,Traveller under Concern,95,1973 ed.</i>)

>  <b>WILSON</b> Trails of lycopodium and red berries decorated a table covered with ... food fit for the gods. <i>(Mar 7, 1890,In the Land of the Tui,103</i>)

>  (And) Lycopodium volubile giant lycopodium. <i>(1917,Duncan & Davies' Catalogue</i>)

>  <b>MANDER</b> The stunted manuka was brown, the crawling lycopodium was brown, the very fern had a rusted look. <i>(1925,Allen Adair,42,1971 ed.</i>)

>  <b>HOWE</b> [Lyc. fer] Pohutukawa ... and lycopodium fern made an enchanting display on the adjoining hills and added to the splendour of our Christmas decorations. <i>(1964,Stamper Battery,97</i>)

>  <b>DEMPSEY</b> Linking the mingi-mingi and the tree trunks, the curling trails of a lycopodium made a vignette of delicate beauty. <i>(1964,Little World of Stewart island,73</i>)

>  <b>SANSOM</b> lycopodium, a beautiful [143] wiry green ground-creeper is lush; the now rare native brown duck is still to be seen ... <i>(1970,Stewart Islanders,142</i>)

>  <b>DOMETT</b> Of fragrant broom and ferny plume/ And winding woven lycopod/ Close-creping - ... <i>Ibid</i> 2/337 Lycopodium volubile, or L. densum. <i>(1883,Ranolf and Amohia,2/63</i>)



## lympho
 
 <i>n.</i> caseous lymphadenitis [ORIGIN: abbreviation, freezing works]

>  Caseous lymphadenitis is referred to as `C.L.A.' or `lympho' in the works killing sheets. ... There is substantial economic loss ... as <i>(Oct 16, 1950,NZ Journal of Agriculture,345</i>)

>  <b>NEAVE</b> [Colin Hercus's] achievements ... in such matters as feeding sheep on oats in the winter, the control of lympho, the use of sulphur in top dressing, ... the battle against white muscle disease with delenium - these ... are part of New Zealand's Agricultural History. <i>(1980,Land of Munros, The,123</i>)



## Lynn of Tawa
 
 <i>n.</i>

>  Ginette McDonald's Lyn of Tawa act - an urban female Fred Dagg take on the township - mortified him. He could only cringe when Lyn's Royal Variety Concert performance included an invitation to the Duke of Edinburgh to open Tawa's new piddling pool. <i>(Jan 30, 2003,Dominion Post,B5</i>)

>  The voice was speaking pure New Zild of a type rarely encountered, in a flow of mangled vowels and consonants that made Lyn of Tawa sound like an Oxbridge graduate by comparison. <i>(Jun 22, 2002,Listener (New Zealand Listener),67</i>)

>  I think Lynn of Tawa's monologues play to two different audiences. The natives laugh at the feeble jokes but see nothing unusual about the accent in which these are delivered; we migrants don't think much of the jokes but keenly appreciate Miss McDonald's brilliant mimicry of country Kiwi dialect. <i>(Apr 15, 1991,Listener (New Zealand Listener),10</i>)

>  And talking like that Lyn of Tawa on the tv. <i>(Aug 29, 1992,Listener (New Zealand Listener),30</i>)
